# APAH Connections Study Guide

A cross-reference of all 250 AP Art History image-set works and the connections between them. Each entry includes a concise summary (Historical Context / Function / Content / Form), the "Possible Connections with other works in image set" text from the student snapshot, and — when available — the outside work from the compare-and-contrast (LO 3.5) exercise.

**Contents.** 225 works with connections, followed by 25 works without connections.

---

## Works with connections

## #1 — Apollo 11 Stones

**Early Homo Sapiens (San peoples)** · c. 23,000 B.C.E. · Charcoal on slabs of rock · Apollo 11 Caves, Namibia · ~4.25 × 5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Apollo-11-cave-therianthrope.jpg/250px-Apollo-11-cave-therianthrope.jpg" width="240" alt="Apollo 11 Stones">

### Summary

Among the **oldest known figurative artworks in Africa** — portable stone slabs depicting animals.

- **Historical Context.** Found in the **Apollo 11 Caves, Namibia**, and named by German archaeologist Wolfgang Wendt after the 1969 moon landing. Attributed to early Homo sapiens (ancestral to the **San peoples**). Carbon-dated roughly 25,000–27,000 years old, among the earliest figurative art yet found on the continent.
- **Function.** Unknown, but likely ritual or mnemonic; the animal images may have recorded species of importance to hunter-gatherers, or served a shamanic/identification purpose.
- **Content.** Seven small slabs carrying charcoal traces of animal figures — a feline-like creature with human hind legs is the most famous, suggesting an early **therianthrope** (human-animal hybrid).
- **Form.** Pigments of black charcoal, reddish ochre, and white kaolin on portable stone. Outlined animal forms use **pictorial definition** — the clearest identifying silhouette rather than naturalistic shading.

**Vocabulary:** prehistory · cave painting · conceptual realism · pictorial definition
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 3.1

---

## #2 — Great Hall of the Bulls

**Paleolithic artists (multiple hands)** · c. 15,000–13,000 B.C.E. · Mineral pigment (ochre, charcoal, animal fat) on rock · Lascaux Caves, Montignac, France · largest bull ~17 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Lascaux_painting.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Hall of the Bulls">

### Summary

One of the most famous concentrations of **Upper Paleolithic cave painting**, with monumental animal figures built up by successive generations of artists.

- **Historical Context.** Painted deep inside the Lascaux cave complex over a long period; overlapping images show multiple hands and styles, suggesting the site was revisited across generations by hunter-gatherer cultures. The cave was closed to the public in 1963 and later replicated as **Lascaux II**.
- **Function.** Unknown, but the remote, hard-to-reach location and absence of daily-life traces point to **ritual or shamanic use** rather than domestic display. Likely tied to hunting magic or symbolic communication in a pre-literate society.
- **Content.** Large aurochs, horses, deer, and the enigmatic "unicorn" figure, arranged in two facing rows along the calcite chamber walls. The largest bull runs about 17 ft long — the biggest figure in known cave art.
- **Form.** Mineral pigments (ochre, charcoal, manganese) suspended in groundwater or animal fat and blown or daubed onto the wall. Figures combine **profile body / frontal horns** (twisted perspective). No true ground line, no background, and no linear perspective.

**Vocabulary:** shamanism · hunting-and-gathering · ritualistic · communication
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#1](#1--apollo-11-stones) — Apollo 11 Stones (San peoples, c. 23,000 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Apollo-11-cave-therianthrope.jpg/250px-Apollo-11-cave-therianthrope.jpg" width="180" alt="Apollo 11 Stones">

**Theme:** Animalism

> The Apollo 11 Stones bear major connections to the paintings in the Great Hall of the Bulls in that it uses painted images to convey the importance of animals in the hunting-gathering societies of that time period.

---

#### → [#5](#5--beaker-with-ibex-motifs) — Beaker with Ibex Motifs (Susa, c. 4,200–3,500 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Bushel_with_Ibex_Motifs.jpg" width="180" alt="Beaker with Ibex Motifs">

**Theme:** Animalism

> The Bushel with Ibex Motifs is another example of the use of animal figure in distorted proportions to exemplify certain themes. The horns of the ibex on this vase are especially pronounced, adding to the ideals conveyed through artwork, diverging from realism and focusing on expression.

---

#### → [#3](#3--camelid-sacrum-in-the-shape-of-a-canine) — Camelid Sacrum in the Shape of a Canine (Tequixquiac, c. 14,000–7,000 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Animalism, Religion

> The Camelid sacrum, like other works of art, including the paintings in the Lascaux Caves, serve to set a precedent for the importance of animals in ancient human cultures.

---

## #3 — Camelid Sacrum in the Shape of a Canine

**Prehistoric Mesoamerican (unknown culture)** · c. 14,000–7,000 B.C.E. · Carved camelid sacrum bone · National Museum of Anthropology, Mexico City (found at Tequixquiac, Mexico) · ~6.8 × 6.7 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Camelid_sacrum_in_the_shape_of_a_canine.jpg/250px-Camelid_sacrum_in_the_shape_of_a_canine.jpg" width="240" alt="Camelid Sacrum in the Shape of a Canine">
### Summary

**One of the earliest known figurative artworks in the Americas**, reshaping a fossil bone to suggest a canine head.

- **Historical Context.** Unearthed in **1870 at Tequixquiac, Mexico**, during drainage works some 40 ft deep; the bone is the sacrum of an extinct camelid. No controlled archaeological context survives, but the object is among the oldest artworks yet recovered in the region. Later Mesoamerican cultures treated the sacrum as a "**sacred bone**" or "second skull," and the name itself derives from Latin *os sacrum*.
- **Function.** Unknown. Speculative purposes include ritual worship of animal spirits or use as a portable mask connecting the sacrum ("second skull") to the head. Possibly tied to hunting ritual.
- **Content.** A canine or other mammal head, rendered through the bone's natural paired hollows (read as eyes) plus humanly carved nostril holes and snout grooves.
- **Form.** **Subtractive sculpture in the round** in fossilized bone, no added pigment. The roughly triangular form is compact and symmetrical; the sculptor's minimal intervention exploits the sacrum's natural skull-like cavities.

**Vocabulary:** sacrum · camelid · prehistoric
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#237](#237--pisupo-lua-afe-corned-beef-2000) — Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000) (Michel Tuffery, 1994)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg/330px-Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Pisupo Lua Afe">

**Theme:** Identity, individual vs. industry

> They both depict an animal that is (presumably, in the Camelid Sacrum) used for food, one hunted and the other is farmed.

---

#### → [#117](#117--the-horse-in-motion) — The Horse in Motion (Eadweard Muybridge, 1878)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Eadweard_Muybridge-Sallie_Gardner_1878.jpg/250px-Eadweard_Muybridge-Sallie_Gardner_1878.jpg" width="180" alt="The Horse in Motion">

**Theme:** Progress of technology, movement

> They both relate to the depiction of animals, The Horse in Motion depicts a horse, while the Camelid Sacrum depicts a canine-like mammal. They show how different mediums can be used to illustrate animals.

---

#### → [#165](#165--painted-elk-hide) — Painted Elk Hide (Cotsiogo (Cadzi Cody), c. 1890–1900)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Story_robe_Shoshone_Amerind.png/250px-Story_robe_Shoshone_Amerind.png" width="180" alt="Painted Elk Hide">

**Theme:** Cultural traditions, identity

> They both depict animals and could have both been used in relation to hunting. The Painted Elk Hide is depicted traditional culture of hunting and the Camelid Sacrum could have been used for hunting rituals.

---

## #4 — Running Horned Woman

**Unknown artist** · c. 6,000–4,000 B.C.E. · Pigment on rock · Tassili n'Ajjer, Algeria · ~8 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/ba/Running_Horned_Woman%2C_6%2C000%E2%80%934%2C000_B.C.E.%2C_pigment_on_rock%2C_Tassili_n%E2%80%99Ajjer%2C_Algeria.jpg/250px-Running_Horned_Woman%2C_6%2C000%E2%80%934%2C000_B.C.E.%2C_pigment_on_rock%2C_Tassili_n%E2%80%99Ajjer%2C_Algeria.jpg" width="240" alt="Running Horned Woman">

### Summary

A Saharan rock painting often read as a **goddess or ritual figure**, painted when the region was green savanna rather than desert.

- **Historical Context.** Found deep in a canyon at **Tassili n'Ajjer, Algeria**, a UNESCO site rich in Neolithic rock art. Long known to local inhabitants before being documented by Lieutenant Brenans (French Foreign Legion) and publicized by ethnographer **Henri Lhote**, whose early Egyptian-influence reading has since been discredited. Belongs to the **Round Head period** of Tassili rock art.
- **Function.** Unknown, but the figure's ceremonial dress, horns, and scale suggest a **religious or ritual role** — perhaps a goddess, priestess, or dancer. Its remote, hard-to-access location hints at sacred use.
- **Content.** A large horned female figure in full stride (or dance), dotted with white markings, surrounded by smaller, less-detailed figures — an early use of **hierarchical scale**.
- **Form.** Pigment on stone, sharply outlined in white. The body is rendered in **twisted perspective** (frontal torso, profile legs). Flat, overlapping figures create no sense of depth.

**Vocabulary:** pictograph · hierarchical scale · twisted perspective
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#178](#178--aka-elephant-mask) — Aka Elephant Mask (Bamileke, c. 19th–20th century)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/60/Elephant_cap_mask%2C_Bamileke_people%2C_Southern_Grassfields_region%2C_western_Cameroon%2C_mid_20th_century_AD%2C_cotton_fabric%2C_glass_beads_-_Krannert_Art_Museum%2C_UIUC_-_DSC06161.jpg" width="180" alt="Aka Elephant Mask">

**Theme:** Tradition, Performance, power

> visually similar, Similar pattern with dots, Shows tradition

---

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houten_beeld_van_een_man_met_ramshoorns_TMnr_5112-1.jpg/250px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houten_beeld_van_een_man_met_ramshoorns_TMnr_5112-1.jpg" width="180" alt="Ikenga">

**Theme:** Religious, Power/strength

> Horns, visually similar, religious in nature

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Malanggan-Masken_Berlin-Dahlem.jpg/250px-Malanggan-Masken_Berlin-Dahlem.jpg" width="180" alt="Malagan Display and Mask">

**Theme:** Ritual, sacred

> both are sacred, both could have been ritualistic

## #5 — Beaker with Ibex Motifs

**Susa I culture** · c. 4,200–3,500 B.C.E. · Painted terra cotta · Louvre, Paris (found at Susa, Iran) · 28.9 × 16.4 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/79/Bushel_with_Ibex_Motifs.jpg" width="240" alt="Beaker with Ibex Motifs">

### Summary

A Susa I funerary beaker whose **geometrically abstracted animals** anticipate later Near Eastern ornament.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the earliest inhabitants of **Susa** (modern Shush, Iran), near the Zagros mountains — a site whose archaeology ties it to Uruk, the proto-Elamites, Sumerians, Akkadians, and later Achaemenids. The depicted animals (ibex, wading birds, saluki-like dogs) all lived in the surrounding mountains and grasslands.
- **Function.** Excavated from the **Susa acropolis burials**; one of many similar beakers thought to hold food for the deceased in the afterlife. The abstract symbol inside the ibex horns may be a **clan emblem** identifying the deceased.
- **Content.** Three stylized ibex with exaggerated sweeping horns encircling an abstract symbol; above them, a band of long-necked wading birds and another of elongated running dogs.
- **Form.** Wheel-thrown **terra cotta** (unglazed, from red clay). Animals built from modified geometric shapes — circles, triangles, rectangles — producing biomorphic silhouettes that dissolve into pattern at a distance. Simple two-color scheme: natural clay with dark brown paint. No background, no depth.

**Vocabulary:** abstraction · animal style · biomorphic · twisted perspective

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#2](#2--great-hall-of-the-bulls) — Great Hall of the Bulls (Lascaux, c. 15,000–13,000 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/1e/Lascaux_painting.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Hall of the Bulls">

**Theme:** The power and beauty of animals throughout the natural world

> Both of these sites, while separate mediums, depict a variety of animals that are not simply food sources, highlighting certain attributes and suggesting a sense of awe and admiration

---

#### → [#11](#11--terra-cotta-fragment) — Terra Cotta Fragment (Lapita culture, c. 1000 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PortVilaLapita.jpg/250px-PortVilaLapita.jpg" width="180" alt="Terra Cotta Fragment">

**Theme:** The power and beauty of animals throughout the natural world

> Both of these pieces capture animals in 2-dimensional representations
>
> Both pieces were created through the crafting of terra cotta and the use of paint

---

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, 1830–1833)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg/500px-Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg" width="180" alt="Under the Wave off Kanagawa">

**Theme:** The triumph of inanimate nature over man; beauty of geometric aspects of the natural world

> Both pieces highlight the importance of an aspect of nature (one is focused on living creatures while the other highlight the non-living)
>
> Both pieces use subdivision and arrangement to represent nature and symbolism (under the wave uses fractal-like cascades where the bushel with ibex motifs uses geometric figures within other geometric figures)

---

## #6 — Anthropomorphic Stele

**Unknown pre-Islamic Arabian culture** · Fourth millennium B.C.E. · Sandstone · Found near Ha'il, northwest Saudi Arabia · ~3 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Stele_from_Kerma_Nubia.jpg/250px-Stele_from_Kerma_Nubia.jpg" width="240" alt="Anthropomorphic Stele">
### Summary

A **pre-Islamic Arabian grave marker** in low relief, human in shape but sharply geometric.

- **Historical Context.** Found in a small village near **Ha'il, northwestern Saudi Arabia**. Two similar steles are known in the region — shared features suggest contact between mobile groups as the Arabian savanna was drying into desert and hunter-gatherers shifted toward domestication. The Arabian Peninsula was a **commercial crossroads** between East and West through the Bronze Age and Antiquity, which helps explain the stele family's shared iconography.
- **Function.** Likely **funerary** — a grave marker in an open-air sanctuary, thought to capture the essence of the deceased.
- **Content.** Simplified human figure seen only from the front: trapezoidal head with closely spaced eyes and a flat nose, square shoulders, a **necklace with an awl** across the chest, and a **double-bladed dagger hanging from a belt** at the waist — the weaponry pointing to an elite male.
- **Form.** Carved from a single slab of sandstone in **low relief**; never painted. Stiff outer contours, simple curved interior features. Frontal, motionless, abstract — recognizably human without any naturalistic modeling.

**Vocabulary:** anthropomorphic · stele · low relief

---

## #7 — Jade Cong

**Liangzhu culture (Neolithic China)** · c. 3,300–2,200 B.C.E. · Carved nephrite jade · Liangzhu, China · ~10 × 2.75 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Jade_Cong_King%2C_2018-06-09_01.jpg/250px-Jade_Cong_King%2C_2018-06-09_01.jpg" width="240" alt="Jade Cong">

### Summary

A **Liangzhu jade ritual object** — square outside, round inside — central to Neolithic Chinese burial practice.

- **Historical Context.** Produced by the **Liangzhu culture** of the lower Yangtze region during the late Neolithic. Neolithic Chinese practiced **ancestor veneration**, burying the dead with jades; jade was already seen as the epitome of beauty and virtue, a status Confucius would later codify. Patterns on congs parallel those on jades found elsewhere across Central Asia, reflecting river-network exchange.
- **Function.** **Funerary and ritual.** Jade congs were buried in large numbers, arranged around the body (often the best pieces near the chest and stomach), presumably to aid or accompany the soul into the afterlife.
- **Content.** Stylized **face patterns** combining human-like and beast-like features — likely spirits or deities in the Liangzhu animist / pagan religious system, later absorbed into Buddhist, Taoist, and Confucian traditions.
- **Form.** Three-dimensional carving in **nephrite jade** (the harder, more durable of the two true jades), ranging from cream to green depending on iron content. A characteristic **square outer profile around a circular inner tube** — a form read later as symbolizing earth (square) and heaven (round circle).

**Vocabulary:** bi · cong · nephrite

---

## #8 — Stonehenge

**Neolithic Britons** · c. 2500–1600 B.C.E. · Sandstone (sarsen) and bluestone · Wiltshire, UK · outer ring ~97 ft diameter; tallest stone 24 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/250px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg" width="240" alt="Stonehenge">

### Summary

A Neolithic-to-Bronze-Age **megalithic monument** whose post-and-lintel ring was built in phases over nearly a millennium.

- **Historical Context.** Built across about 1,000 years in **Wiltshire, England**, spanning the transition from stone tools to Bronze Age metallurgy. Nearby **round barrows** (burial mounds) and the lavishly furnished **Amesbury Archer** grave mark a shift to individual burial with grave goods. Later cultures — Celts, Anglo-Saxons (one decapitated man buried there), medieval chroniclers crediting **Merlin** — reused and reinterpreted the site.
- **Function.** Unknown and probably multiple. Evidence of **cremation burials** in the Aubrey holes points to funerary use; the alignment of the trilithon horseshoe with the **midsummer sunrise and midwinter sunset** points to an astronomical/ceremonial calendar function.
- **Content.** A circular bank and ditch (~320 ft across) enclosing an outer ring of **sarsen post-and-lintel trilithons** and an inner horseshoe of five larger trilithons; smaller **bluestones** set between and within.
- **Form.** Massive sarsen sandstone blocks (25–50 tons each) capped by horizontal **lintels** locked with mortise-and-tenon joints — the structure's defining **post-and-lintel** system. Bluestones brought from as far as the **Preseli Hills in Wales** (~150 miles away).

**Vocabulary:** megalith · trilithon · henge · post · lintel
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 3.3, 3.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#17](#17--great-pyramids-menkaura-khafre-khufu-and-great-sphinx) — Great Pyramids of Giza (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2550–2490 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Pyramids of Giza">

**Theme:** Tombs/burial sites

> Both (potentially) served as burial grounds and were constructed over long periods of time, as well as being made of materials that were hard to obtain.

---

#### → [#12](#12--white-temple-and-its-ziggurat) — White Temple and its Ziggurat (Sumerian, c. 3500–3000 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/250px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg" width="180" alt="White Temple and its Ziggurat">

**Theme:** Religious, important to the community, and towered above flat plain

> Both (potentially) were religious, and important to the people who constructed them, and both were also tall structures that towered above their surroundings.

---

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis / Parthenon (Iktinos and Kallikrates, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/330px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg" width="180" alt="Parthenon">

**Theme:** Religious, had columns, and cultural significance

> Both were significant to the people who constructed them, both were (potentially) religious, and the columns in the Parthenon were constructed in (a more advanced) trilithon style

---

## #9 — Ambum Stone

**Unknown (ancestors of the Enga peoples)** · c. 1500 B.C.E. · Greywacke · Enga / Ambum Valley, Papua New Guinea · 7.9 × 3.0 × 5.5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8c/Ambum_Stone.webp/250px-Ambum_Stone.webp.png" width="240" alt="Ambum Stone">

### Summary

A finely carved Papua New Guinean **zoomorphic pestle**, probably representing a fetal echidna and venerated as a "bone of the ancestors."

- **Historical Context.** Carved in the **Enga (or Inga) highlands of Papua New Guinea**, in a culture whose **"big man" power system** has remained relatively stable for millennia. Enga society was largely **animistic**, attributing power to nature and ancestors; authority was negotiated through control of pigs and other resources redistributed at ritual events.
- **Function.** Dual purpose. Stones like this were thought to be imbued by "big men" with supernatural power, **buried on ancestral land**, and fed periodic pig sacrifices to keep the ancestors favorable and the land fertile. It also served as a **mortar** for pounding food or herbs — a ritual object that was also a tool.
- **Content.** A stylized **fetus of an echidna** (spiny anteater), valued for its fat and hunted in the region. Enga call such stones **"simting bilong tumbuna"** — "bones of the ancestors."
- **Form.** Hand-carved **greywacke**, a hard volcanic sandstone. Bulging ears/eyes, a snout closer to a fruit bat than an anteater, arms and legs tucked around a round belly in a fetal pose — an **amalgam of animal and human features**. Vertically symmetrical, curving throughout with almost no straight lines; the elongated neck shows wear/patina consistent with use as a pestle handle.

**Vocabulary:** mortar · pestle · zoomorphic · greywacke · animism · echidna · fetal position
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#237](#237--pisupo-lua-afe-corned-beef-2000) — Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000) (Michel Tuffery, 1994)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg/330px-Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Pisupo Lua Afe">

**Theme:** Depiction of animals

> Depiction of an animal native to an island that is important to its people

#### → [#218](#218--buk-mask) — Buk (Krar) Mask (Torres Strait, mid-late 19th century)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Secret_Society_Mask%2C_turtleshell_and_human_hair%2C_early_c19%2C_Torres_Straits_Islands_-_Bristol_Museums_object_no._E1013.jpg/250px-Secret_Society_Mask%2C_turtleshell_and_human_hair%2C_early_c19%2C_Torres_Straits_Islands_-_Bristol_Museums_object_no._E1013.jpg" width="180" alt="Buk (Krar) Mask">

## #10 — Tlatilco Female Figurine

**Tlatilco culture, Central Mexico** · c. 1200–900 B.C.E. · Ceramic · Princeton University Art Museum · 3.8 in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b3/Tlatilco_female_figurine.jpg" width="240" alt="Tlatilco Female Figurine">

### Summary

A small ceramic "**Pretty Lady**" figurine from the Early Formative period, notable for its **two heads** (or two faces) and exaggerated hips.

- **Historical Context.** Made at **Tlatilco** in the Valley of Mexico during the **Early Formative period**, as settled village life was taking hold in Mesoamerica (c. 2000 B.C.E. onward). Dozens of related figurines have been excavated from **burials**, where they were the most common grave good. The culture was contemporary with — and influenced by — the early Olmec.
- **Function.** Unclear, but likely tied to **women's roles in fertility and the cosmos**; the **double-head / dual-face** variant may invoke ideas of duality (life/death, seen/unseen) central to later Mesoamerican thought, or record actual cases of conjoined twinning.
- **Content.** A standing nude female with **tiny breasts and waist but dramatically wide hips** and carefully rendered hair. The figurine is **two-headed** (two faces sharing a body), arms stiffly extended.
- **Form.** **Hand-modeled ceramic**, originally painted; colors now lost to weathering. Rounded, voluptuous volumes; fine incised marks in the hair; stylized, symmetrical facial features repeated across the figurine corpus.

**Vocabulary:** shamanism · Mesoamerica

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WLA_metmuseum_Power_Figure_Male_Nkisi.jpg/250px-WLA_metmuseum_Power_Figure_Male_Nkisi.jpg" width="180" alt="Power Figure (Nkisi n'kondi)">

**Theme:** Human figure

> Both depict the human figure in a non naturalistic style and exaggerated certain parts of the body (the hips in the Tlatilco figure and the shoulders on the power figure)

#### → *Venus of Urbino* (Titian, 1538) — *attribution unclear*

## #11 — Terra Cotta Fragment

**Lapita culture** · c. 1000 B.C.E. · Clay · Solomon Islands / Reef Islands (Pacific)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/PortVilaLapita.jpg/250px-PortVilaLapita.jpg" width="240" alt="Lapita pottery fragment">

### Summary

A shattered **Lapita** ceramic piece notable for its stylized human face among geometric bands — early evidence of the design tradition that would shape Micro- and Polynesian art.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Lapita culture**, ancestors of the Micronesian and Polynesian peoples who dispersed across a vast network of Pacific islands.
- **Function.** Uncertain because of the fragment's condition. Likely pottery, but Lapita terra-cotta forms may also have served as **stamps** to imprint patterns onto bark cloth or human skin for tattoos.
- **Content.** A stylized **human head** set among dense, incised geometric patterns. Lapita burial custom of separating heads from bodies (replaced by cone-shell rings) suggests religious importance placed on the head.
- **Form.** Dense, repeating **geometric ornament** framing an abstracted face; flat, stylized, low-relief incision.

**Vocabulary:** stylization · abstraction · ornamental
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 3.1

---

**Theme:** Prehistory artifact with unknown function, but likely of religious significance

## #12 — White Temple and its Ziggurat

**Sumerian** · c. 3500–3000 B.C.E. · Mud brick · Uruk (modern Warka, Iraq) · ~40 ft / 12 m high

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg/250px-White_Temple_ziggurat_in_Uruk.jpg" width="240" alt="White Temple and its Ziggurat">

### Summary

One of the earliest monumental religious structures: a whitewashed temple set atop a stepped **ziggurat** in the world's first major city.

- **Historical Context.** Built at **Uruk** (modern Warka, Iraq), a pioneer of urban life in Mesopotamia and birthplace of **cuneiform** writing. Uruk society was stratified, headed by **priests**, and polytheistic. The complex towered over the city's defensive walls.
- **Function.** A tribute to the **Mesopotamian sky god Anu**. Access was restricted to a small group of priests and, likely, the ruling elite; it also served administrative and economic roles, since religion and government were intertwined. The height brought worship physically closer to the sky.
- **Content.** A rectangular temple with a **tripartite plan** (long central cella flanked by side rooms) sitting atop a high ziggurat; the corners orient to the cardinal points. A stepped **altar** sat in the cella where, symbolically, Anu was believed to descend.
- **Form.** Mud brick covered in **white plaster**, producing a luminous light-catching exterior. Sloping sides with recessed bands; a single stairway ramp climbing the north side; a "**bent-axis**" plan requiring a ninety-degree turn to reach the altar.

**Vocabulary:** ziggurat · Anu · cuneiform · cella · bent-axis plan
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.1, 3.3, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#17](#17--great-pyramids-menkaura-khafre-khufu-and-great-sphinx) — Great Pyramids and Great Sphinx (Egyptian Old Kingdom, c. 2550–2490 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Pyramids of Giza">

**Theme:** Honorary, similar structure

> Both the Great Pyramids at Giza, Egypt and the White Temple and its Ziggurat were honorary—the White Temple to the Mesopotamian sky god Anu, and the Pyramids to the Egyptian kings Menkaura, Khafre, and Khufu. The Pyramids, however, served a funerary purpose. Both structures have a similar sloping structure, though the Pyramids are significantly taller.

---

#### → [#157](#157--templo-mayor-main-temple) — Templo Mayor (Mexica/Aztec, 1375–1520 C.E.)

**Theme:** Pay homage to key deities, similar structure

> Both the White Temple and the Templo Mayor are dedicated to key deities in their respective cultures. The White Temple is dedicated to Anu, while the Templo Mayor is a set of twin temples, one of which is dedicated to Tlaloc (Aztec god of water, rain, and agriculture) and the other to Huitzilopochti (war, fire, and sun god). Additionally, both temples have a very similar structure, positioned at the center of the city, and accessible by two sets of stairs.

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (India, c. 300 B.C.E.–100 C.E.)

**Theme:** Associated with key figures/deities, similar materials used

> The Great Stupa at Sanchi and the White Temple both show respect and importance of key religious figures. However, they differ in that the Great Stupa represents Buddha himself, while the White Temple is a place to worship the sky god Anu. The two structures are made out of the same material—mud brick, though the stupa has not been gone over with white plaster.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

#### Great Stupa at Sanchi (Madhya Pradesh, India, c. 300 B.C.E.–100 C.E.) — *not in image set*

**Prompt:** Students analyze relationships between works of art based on their similarities and differences. What are some similarities and differences between the White Temple and its Ziggurat and the Great Stupa at Sanchi?

> Both the White Temple and its Ziggurat and the Great Stupa at Sanchi are dedicated to important figures in each of their religions/belief systems. The White Temple is dedicated to Anu, the Mesopotamian sky god, while the Great Stupa at Sanchi is dedicated to Buddha (Buddhism). However, a key difference here is that while the White Temple is dedicated to Anu, the Stupa is considered to *be* Buddha himself—the base represents his crossed legs, the middle portion his torso, and the top his head. The two structures are also similar in that they were constructed with consideration to the presence of a heavenly realm in the sky. The Mesopotamians believed that deities resided in the sky, and therefore sought to make their temples as tall as possible in order to be closer to the sky. Similar, a spire atop the Great Stupa symbolizes an axis of rotation through the earth's center, through which a connection between Earth and the heavens was established. Both these structures were constructed from mud brick. However, the White Temple was done over in white plaster. Additionally, the White Temple and its Ziggurat could be entered, while the Great Stupa was a solid structure and could only be circumambulated around.

---

## #13 — Palette of King Narmer

**Predynastic Egypt** · c. 3000–2920 B.C.E. · Greywacke / siltstone · from the temple of Horus, Hierakonpolis · 25 in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Narmer_Palette.jpg/330px-Narmer_Palette.jpg" width="240" alt="Palette of King Narmer">

### Summary

A ceremonial **cosmetic palette** commemorating (or ceremonially enacting) the **unification of Upper and Lower Egypt** under **King Narmer** — foundational to Egyptian pictorial conventions.

- **Historical Context.** Made in Predynastic Egypt and deposited as a **votive object** in the temple of Horus at **Hierakonpolis**. Narmer is traditionally credited as the unifier of Upper and Lower Egypt; the palette is among the earliest works to fuse **Egyptian pictorial conventions** that would persist for three millennia.
- **Function.** Nominally a **palette for grinding eye makeup** (used to decorate cult images of gods), but monumental in scale and far too elaborate for daily use — it is a **ceremonial / votive** object buried beneath the temple floor.
- **Content.** Narmer appears twice, once in the **White Crown** of Upper Egypt smiting an enemy, once in the **Red Crown** of Lower Egypt inspecting beheaded foes. Hathor heads flank the top; two long-necked **serpopards** frame the central grinding circle; a falcon-Horus perches above papyrus (Lower Egypt).
- **Form.** Low-relief carving in **greywacke** organized into horizontal **registers**. **Hierarchical scale** (Narmer is the largest figure); **composite view** (head and legs in profile, torso frontal); a flat, two-dimensional idealization that sets the template for later Egyptian art.

**Vocabulary:** votive object · hierarchical scale · register · composite view
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#15](#15--seated-scribe) — Seated Scribe (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2620–2500 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg/500px-Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg" width="180" alt="Seated Scribe">

**Theme:** Funerary

> Much like the Narmer Palette the seated scribe is meant for religious and funerary purposes. Both pieces utilize Egyptian styles such as thin angular figures and non individualized portraits.

---

#### → [#24](#24--last-judgment-of-hu-nefer-from-his-tomb-page-from-the-book-of-the-dead) — Last Judgement of Hu-Nefer (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1275 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary

> The palette of Narmer is much like The Book of the Dead because it is a votive object, depicts scenes related to death, represents several gods, utilizes two-dimensional art, utilizes hierarchical scale, and evokes the divinity of the king.

---

#### → [#22](#22--akhenaton-nefertiti-and-three-daughters) — Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and three daughters (Amarna, c. 1353–1335 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious shift

> The Palette of Narmer is similar to this depiction because they both focus mainly on Egyptian religious values. The King is also shown in association with the god.

---

## #14 — Statues of Votive Figures, from the Square Temple at Eshnunna

**Sumerian** · c. 2700 B.C.E. · Gypsum inlaid with shell and black limestone · modern Tell Asmar, Iraq · ~11 5/8 in (largest male)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Standing_male_worshiper_MET_DT850.jpg/250px-Standing_male_worshiper_MET_DT850.jpg" width="240" alt="Votive Figures from Tell Asmar">

### Summary

Twelve small Sumerian figures with **oversized inlaid eyes** — eternal stand-ins for their patrons, meant to pray forever in a god's presence.

- **Historical Context.** Found buried beneath the floor of a temple (possibly dedicated to the god **Abu**) at ancient **Eshnunna** (Tell Asmar, Iraq). Access to Sumerian shrines was restricted; elites likely commissioned these statuettes to place themselves permanently before the god.
- **Function.** **Votive substitutes**: the figures embody their patrons and offer perpetual prayer. Some hold small cups or sprigs of vegetation for libation.
- **Content.** Men in **belts and fringed skirts**, some bald, some bearded; women in long robes, varied hair. All stand **erect, hands clasped**, staring upward with wide eyes fixed on the cult image.
- **Form.** Geometric **gypsum** bodies — flattened torso, cylindrical lower body — carved for a **frontal view**. **Inlaid shell and black limestone eyes** with painted hair exaggerate the gaze.

**Vocabulary:** votive · inlay · incising · subtractive sculpture
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#6](#6--anthropomorphic-stele) — Anthropomorphic Stele (Arabian Peninsula, 4th millennium B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Burial, religious

> Both have relatively geometric, simplified forms, though the stele is much more minimalistic than the votive figures. Both are meant for a frontal view, and were created for religious purposes.

---

#### → [#18](#18--king-menkaura-and-queen) — King Menkaura and Queen (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2490–2472 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="180" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

**Theme:** Funerary, religious, depiction of royals

> Though this depicts royals and may imply their divinity (contrasted to votive figures), their depiction is less idealized compared to other sculptures at the time.

---

#### → [#15](#15--seated-scribe) — Seated Scribe (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2620–2500 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg/500px-Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg" width="180" alt="Seated Scribe">

**Theme:** Funerary, religious

> Both were carved to for religious purposes and to gain favor—with the scribe it was to help with the afterlife, with the figures it was to give more attention to the god.

## #16 — Standard of Ur

**Sumerian** · c. 2600–2400 B.C.E. · Wood inlaid with shell, lapis lazuli, and red limestone · Royal Tombs at Ur (modern Tell el-Muqayyar, Iraq) · 8.5 × 19.5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Denis_Bourez_-_British_Museum%2C_London_%288747049029%29_%282%29.jpg/330px-Denis_Bourez_-_British_Museum%2C_London_%288747049029%29_%282%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Standard of Ur">

### Summary

A small Sumerian box inlaid in **shell, lapis lazuli, and red limestone**, depicting matched scenes of **"War" and "Peace"** — an early register-based narrative of Mesopotamian royal ideology.

- **Historical Context.** Recovered from the **Royal Tombs at Ur** by Leonard Woolley. Ur was a major Sumerian city-state dating back to the Ubaid period (c. 3800 B.C.E.), once on the Persian Gulf coast (now far inland).
- **Function.** **Unknown**. Woolley proposed it was carried on a pole as a royal standard; others think it was the **soundbox of a musical instrument**.
- **Content.** Two long sides: the **Peace** panel shows the king feasting at top while subjects bring livestock and goods in procession; the **War** panel shows chariots trampling enemies and prisoners paraded before the king.
- **Form.** **Three registers** per side, organized with **hierarchical scale** (the king is enlarged). Flat, profile figures with emphasized eyes and eyebrows stand on clear ground lines — a mosaic of shell, lapis, and limestone set in bitumen.

**Vocabulary:** inlay · mosaic · register · hierarchical scale

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#203](#203--night-attack-on-the-sanjo-palace) — Night Attack on the Sanjō Palace (Japanese, Kamakura, late 13th century)

**Theme:** Japanese; battle narrative

> both portrays victories in battle

#### → [#50](#50--rebecca-and-eliezer-at-the-well-and-jacob-wrestling-the-angel-from-the-vienna-genesis) — Vienna Genesis (Jacob Wrestling the Angel) (Early Byzantine, early 6th century)

**Theme:** Christianity / narrative sequence

> both tell a story in a linear perspective

---

#### → [#24](#24--last-judgment-of-hu-nefer-from-his-tomb-page-from-the-book-of-the-dead) — Last Judgment of Hu-Nefer (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1275 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Egyptian; hierarchical scale

> the most important person is portrayed as larger and other people looking right to them

---

## #17 — Great Pyramids (Menkaura, Khafre, Khufu) and Great Sphinx

**Egyptian, Old Kingdom, Fourth Dynasty** · c. 2550–2490 B.C.E. · Cut limestone, granite · Giza, Egypt · Khufu: 455 ft tall; Khafre: 448 ft; Menkaura: 213 ft; Sphinx: 66 × 63 × 238 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Pyramids of Giza">

### Summary

Three monumental **royal tombs** for the pharaohs **Khufu, Khafre, and Menkaura**, guarded by the **Great Sphinx** — emblems of Old Kingdom power, geometry, and funerary belief.

- **Historical Context.** Built during the Old Kingdom (Fourth Dynasty), when a highly centralized theocratic state headed by a god-king enabled projects at this scale. Located beside the **Nile**, whose predictable flooding underwrote Egyptian agriculture; the dominant solar deity was **Ra**. The pyramids decrease in size in each generation, tracking the kingdom's declining resources.
- **Function.** **Funerary monuments** to protect and provision the pharaoh on his journey to the **afterlife**, and to project the state's power. Interior chambers hold the sarcophagus and grave goods; **false doors and shafts** deterred tomb robbers. The **Great Sphinx** (likely Khafre's face on a lion body) guarded Khafre's causeway.
- **Content.** Three nearly perfect **pyramidal** masses, corners aligned to the **cardinal directions**; each sits within a complex that once included a mortuary temple and causeway. The **Sphinx**, carved **in situ** from the bedrock, was Egypt's first colossal sculpture.
- **Form.** Stacked limestone cores with **Tura limestone casing** and red granite details. Massive, geometrically pure silhouettes; smooth, originally polished surfaces; hieroglyphic-lined interior chambers.

**Vocabulary:** in situ · hieratic (hierarchical) scale · necropolis · ka · hieroglyphics
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#193](#193--terracotta-warriors-from-the-mausoleum-of-the-first-qin-emperor-of-china) — Terracotta Warriors (Qin Dynasty, c. 221–209 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Glorification of Ruler and Country; Tomb Monument on Colossal scale

> The scale of the work indicates the strength of dynasty similar to the large scale of the pyramids. The work also was for protecting and glorifying the ruler in the afterlife.

---

#### → [#209](#209--taj-mahal) — Taj Mahal (Mughal India, 1632–1653 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/330px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg" width="180" alt="Taj Mahal">

**Theme:** Glorification of Country; Tomb Monument on Colossal scale

> The Taj mahal honors Shah Jahan's wife by building her a intricate and large monument that also serves to show the strength of the Mughal empire.

---

#### → [#30](#30--audience-hall-apadana-of-darius-and-xerxes) — Audience Hall (apadana) of Darius and Xerxes (Persian, c. 520–465 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Glorification of Country

> The scale of the audience hall indicates the strength of Persian Empire. There are also complex details to impress visitors. Similar to the Great Sphinx the audience hall features columns with capitals shaped like Bulls that protect the Persian Empire.

---

## #18 — King Menkaura and Queen

**Egyptian, Old Kingdom, Fourth Dynasty** · c. 2490–2472 B.C.E. · Greywacke · Giza, Egypt (Menkaura Valley Temple) · 56 × 22½ × 21¾ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="240" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

### Summary

A rigid, paired greywacke portrait of **pharaoh Menkaura and his queen** (probably Khamerernebty II) — a canonical Old Kingdom **dyad statue** designed to house the royal **ka**.

- **Historical Context.** Found in Menkaura's Valley Temple near his pyramid at Giza. Menkaura's complex was never finished — resources were waning — and he did not live to complete it. Ancient Egyptians believed a person's **ka** needed a preserved likeness to return to in the afterlife.
- **Function.** A **tomb dyad** to guarantee the king's rebirth in the afterlife. Idealized, ageless bodies present Menkaura as eternally youthful and divinely powerful.
- **Content.** Menkaura and a royal woman (likely his consort) stand side-by-side, both striding forward with the **left foot advanced** (Menkaura's more pronounced). She embraces him with one arm around his waist and the other across his upper arm.
- **Form.** High relief against a back slab; originally **brightly painted** (traces of red on the king's face, yellow on the queen's face, black on her hair). **Smooth, geometric bodies**, equal height conveying parity; frontal, symmetrical, with fists clenched (a standard royal gesture).

**Vocabulary:** ka · high relief · visual hierarchy / hieratic scale
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#17](#17--great-pyramids-menkaura-khafre-khufu-and-great-sphinx) — Great Pyramids of Giza (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2550–2490 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Pyramids">

**Theme:** Funerary

> Built for King Menkaura and with the same purpose of guiding him in the Afterlife

---

#### → [#15](#15--seated-scribe) — Seated Scribe (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2620–2500 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg/500px-Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg" width="180" alt="Seated Scribe">

> Also created in the Old Kingdom 4th Dynasty

## #19 — The Code of Hammurabi

**Susian / Babylonian (Hammurabi)** · c. 1792–1750 B.C.E. · Basalt · found at Susa (modern Iran); now Louvre, Paris · 7 ft 4 in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/Code_of_Hammurabi.jpg/330px-Code_of_Hammurabi.jpg" width="240" alt="The Code of Hammurabi">
### Summary

A basalt **stele** carved with the **earliest extensive codified law**, crowned by a relief of **Hammurabi receiving authority from the sun god Shamash**.

- **Historical Context.** Erected under **Hammurabi**, who ruled northern and western Mesopotamia from **Babylon**. The stele was later carried off to **Susa** as war booty. Its 300-plus laws, written in **cuneiform**, regulate commerce, family, property, and crime, and famously establish "**eye for an eye**" (lex talionis). The punishments scale by social class.
- **Function.** A public monument publishing the king's law and asserting its **divine origin**, so the code would outlast Hammurabi himself.
- **Content.** At the top, a high-relief scene: **Hammurabi stands before the enthroned Shamash**, god of justice, who hands him a rod and ring (symbols of authority). Below, densely inscribed laws cover the stone in cuneiform.
- **Form.** A tall, tapering **basalt stele** in high relief. Figures shown in the **Egyptian composite convention** (shoulders frontal, face in profile). King and god are nearly **equal in height** — Hammurabi is only slightly smaller — emphasizing their near-equivalent standing. Lightning bolts radiate from Shamash's shoulders.

**Vocabulary:** stele · cuneiform · hierarchical scale · relief
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#13](#13--palette-of-king-narmer) — Palette of King Narmer (Predynastic Egypt, c. 3000–2920 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Narmer_Palette.jpg/330px-Narmer_Palette.jpg" width="180" alt="Palette of King Narmer">

**Theme:** Religious

> Both have Egyptian pose/ Composite View

---

#### → [#155](#155--yaxchilán) — Yaxchilán (Maya, 725 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious

> Both tell a story of a god passing power

---

#### → [#208](#208--jahangir-preferring-a-sufi-shaikh-to-kings) — Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings (Mughal, c. 1615–1618 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious

> Both have Hierarchy of Scale and giving a book to the Sufi - sign of respect

---

## #20 — Temple of Amun-Re and Hypostyle Hall

**New Kingdom Egypt, 18th–19th Dynasties** · Temple c. 1550 B.C.E.; Hall c. 1250 B.C.E. · Cut sandstone and mud brick · Karnak, Egypt · Complex ~60 acres; hypostyle hall 340 × 170 ft with 66-ft columns

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Amun_Temple_Karnak.jpg/330px-Amun_Temple_Karnak.jpg" width="240" alt="Temple of Amun-Re and Hypostyle Hall">
### Summary

The colossal religious heart of **Karnak (Thebes)** — expanded over centuries by New Kingdom pharaohs including **Thutmose I and Rameses II** — centered on a **hypostyle hall** with 134 columns.

- **Historical Context.** Begun under **Senusret I** (Middle Kingdom) and continuously expanded during the New Kingdom, when Thebes was Egypt's capital. The composite god **Amun-Re** (fusing local Theban Amun with the sun god Re) rose to national prominence, and successive pharaohs built at Karnak to link themselves with the god and with past dynasties.
- **Function.** A **temple complex** considered Amun-Re's **earthly dwelling** — part religious center, part working estate for the priesthood, part royal burial site. Access grew more restricted toward the interior: only priests and the pharaoh entered the inner sanctuary.
- **Content.** Multiple pylons frame sequential courtyards leading to the **hypostyle hall** — a forest of columns whose taller central nave allows **clerestory lighting** — then the barque shrine and sanctuary housing the cult statue of Amun-Re. Obelisks, sphinxes, and painted relief narrate battles and divine myths.
- **Form.** **Post-and-lintel** sandstone construction. The hall's **lotus** and **papyrus** column capitals symbolize Upper and Lower Egypt; ground level rises and light dims as one moves inward — a physical metaphor for the approach to the divine. Two axes (N–S and E–W); an avenue of **sphinxes** once connected Karnak to Luxor.

**Vocabulary:** pylon · peristyle court · hypostyle hall · sanctuary · clerestory · hieroglyphs · colonnade
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#21](#21--mortuary-temple-of-hatshepsut) — Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1473–1458 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, Power

> Created during the New Kingdom period Temples used for worship of Amun-Re Commemorating rulers/achievements

---

#### → [#31](#31--temple-of-minerva-veii-and-sculpture-of-apollo) — Temple of Minerva (Etruscan, c. 510–500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both buildings used for worship, dedicated to a specific religious figure Statues representing gods are present in both

---

#### → [#30](#30--audience-hall-apadana-of-darius-and-xerxes) — Audience Hall of Darius and Xerxes (Persian, c. 520–465 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Power

> both dedicated to leaders/powerful figures large halls intended for large numbers of people (though the Temple of Amun-Re was more exclusive)

---

## #21 — Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut

**New Kingdom Egypt, 18th Dynasty** · c. 1473–1458 B.C.E. · Sandstone, partially carved into a rock cliff, and red granite · Deir el-Bahri, near Luxor, Egypt · ~97 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Hatshepsut_Temple.jpg/330px-Hatshepsut_Temple.jpg" width="240" alt="Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut">
### Summary

A terraced mortuary temple for **Hatshepsut**, one of the few female pharaohs, whose colonnaded levels are carved directly into a **limestone cliff** at Deir el-Bahri.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Hatshepsut**, who took the throne after her husband's death and ruled for twenty years — often depicting herself in male pharaonic regalia to legitimize her rule. A successful **Punt expedition** during her reign enriched Egypt and is commemorated on the temple's colonnades. Construction took about fifteen years.
- **Function.** A **memorial** to Hatshepsut (she was actually buried in the **Valley of the Kings**), with chapels for **Hathor**, **Anubis**, a royal cult chapel, a solar cult chapel, and a **sanctuary for Amun**. Family members were interred within the complex.
- **Content.** Three broad **terraces** linked by ramps, fronted by colonnades. The **Punt Colonnade** narrates Hatshepsut's trade expedition; the **Birth Colonnade** depicts her as divinely fathered by Amun through Thutmose I, legitimizing her rule. The image-set **Kneeling Statue of Hatshepsut** wears male regalia.
- **Form.** **Sandstone** partly carved into the cliff face behind it — an unusual fusion of freestanding architecture and in situ rock-cut architecture. Unadorned columns meet the entablature directly, producing a starkly rhythmic colonnade. Axial symmetry; large open courtyards.

**Vocabulary:** mortuary temple · pharaoh · courtyard · colonnade · chapel

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#20](#20--temple-of-amun-re-and-hypostyle-hall) — Temple of Amun-Re and Hypostyle Hall (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1550–1250 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, Memorializing the dead

> The Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut contains a sanctuary for Amun, also known as a place to worship the god. These two temples paid tribute to the Egyptian sun god Amun, and were not designed solely as tombs like the Great Pyramids at Giza.

---

#### → [#30](#30--audience-hall-apadana-of-darius-and-xerxes) — Audience Hall of Darius and Xerxes (Persian, c. 520–465 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Power, Memorializing a ruler's accomplishments

> Like the Audience Hall of Darius and Xerxes, the Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut was commissioned by a ruler to serve as a public display of their power, achievements, and glory. However, the two structures differ in that the Audience Hall was not constructed with the intention of serving funerary purposes, nor as a temple for the gods.

---

#### → [#38](#38--great-altar-of-zeus-and-athena-at-pergamon) — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon (Hellenistic Greek, c. 175 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> The structures of the Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut and the Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon are remarkably similar. Both include colonnades, and have open spaces in the front of the entrance. Hatshepsut's mortuary temple included a sanctuary for the god Amun, and the Great Altar contained places for worshipping the Greek gods Zeus and Athena. Both were constructed with religion in mind.

---

## #23 — Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin

**New Kingdom Egypt, 18th Dynasty** · c. 1323 B.C.E. · Gold with inlay of enamel and semiprecious stones · Egyptian Museum, Cairo · 6 ft 7 in long

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Tutankhamun_coffin.jpg/330px-Tutankhamun_coffin.jpg" width="240" alt="Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin">
### Summary

The innermost of three nested coffins holding the mummy of the boy-king **Tutankhamun** — **solid gold** inlaid with semiprecious stones, discovered intact by Howard Carter in 1922.

- **Historical Context.** Son of **Akhenaten**, Tutankhamun took the throne as a child and **reversed his father's monotheism**, restoring worship of Amun and the capital at **Thebes**. He died around eighteen. The tomb's unlooted state made its contents the defining image of Egyptian royal burial.
- **Function.** A **mummiform coffin** to house and protect the king's body on its journey to the **afterlife** — its expense and imagery also proclaim his divine status.
- **Content.** The king in idealized youth wearing the **nemes headcloth** and **false beard**, arms crossed over the chest holding the **crook and flail** (attributes of Osiris). Protective goddesses **Nekhbet** (vulture) and **Wadjet** (cobra) spread their wings across the torso; **Isis** and **Nephthys** are engraved at the feet. The surface is densely covered with hieroglyphic spells.
- **Form.** **Solid gold** with inlays of **colored glass and semiprecious stones** (lapis lazuli, turquoise, carnelian). A nearly vacuum-free **horror vacui** surface; vertically symmetrical; life-size and body-hugging. Original **scrap-gold value** alone is estimated at $1.7 million.

**Vocabulary:** pharaoh · sarcophagus · hieroglyphics · ankh · horror vacui
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#29](#29--sarcophagus-of-the-spouses) — Sarcophagus of the Spouses (Etruscan, c. 520 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Commemoration

> meant to house the body/ashes of the dead; both include symbol of life after death (crook & a flail // egg); full length portraits w/ broad shoulders

---

#### → [#209](#209--taj-mahal) — Taj Mahal (Mughal India, 1632–1653 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/330px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg" width="180" alt="Taj Mahal">

**Theme:** Commemoration

> meant to honor the death of someone significant in society; both used highly expensive materials to make (gold // precious stones); display characteristics/features from the belief system of that society (afterlife ritual & Egyptian gods // Islam)

---

#### → [#193](#193--terracotta-warriors-from-the-mausoleum-of-the-first-qin-emperor-of-china) — Terracotta Warriors (Qin Dynasty, c. 221–209 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Commemoration

> meant to protect the death of someone significant in society (so that they arrive safely to afterlife); both were also located in mausoleums; full length size; very intricate details (personalized); display characteristics/features from the belief system of that society (afterlife ritual & Egyptian gods // afterlife & Daoism)

## #24 — Last Judgment of Hu-Nefer, from his tomb (page from the Book of the Dead)

**New Kingdom Egypt, 19th Dynasty** · c. 1275 B.C.E. · Painted papyrus scroll · Thebes · 45.7 × 83.4 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Hunefer_Papyrus.jpg/330px-Hunefer_Papyrus.jpg" width="240" alt="Last Judgment of Hu-Nefer, from his tomb (page from the Book of the Dead)">
### Summary

A brightly painted **Book of the Dead** scroll that walks the royal scribe **Hu-Nefer** through the **weighing of the heart** ritual — a commissioned guide to safe passage into the afterlife.

- **Historical Context.** Hu-Nefer was a **royal scribe**, overseer, and steward of **King Sety I**, likely buried at Memphis. The quality of the papyrus signals his wealth. Egyptian religious belief held that the **ka** faces judgment after death: the heart is weighed against the feather of **Ma'at** (truth); heavy hearts are devoured.
- **Function.** A personalized, **spell-filled guide** buried with Hu-Nefer to ensure he passes the judgment. It is part of the broader Book of the Dead tradition lining New Kingdom tombs.
- **Content.** In **continuous narrative**: the **Opening of the Mouth** ritual before the tomb; the weighing of the heart overseen by **Anubis**; the ibis-headed **Thoth** recording the verdict; the monstrous **Devourer (Ammit)** waiting to consume unworthy souls; Hu-Nefer then led before enthroned **Osiris**, flanked by **Isis** and **Nephthys**.
- **Form.** Rigid, brightly colored figures arranged in **registers** and shown in the canonical **composite view**. **Hieratic scale** enlarges gods over mortals; hieroglyphic text runs in vertical columns. A snapshot aesthetic — no continuous motion, but discrete static tableaux.

**Vocabulary:** hieratic scale · continuous narrative · papyrus · ka
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#21](#21--mortuary-temple-of-hatshepsut) — Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1473–1458 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** New Kingdom funerary practice, ritualistic Egyptian behaviors

> Both the scroll and Hatshetsut's temple display the attention given to the funerary practices of the pharaohs and high officials. These rituals took a very prominent role in society.

---

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** New Kingdom funerary practice, elaborate details of death

> Both Books of the Dead and Tutankhamen's tomb give insight into the utmost detail and effort which was injected into Egyptian tombs

---

#### → [#17](#17--great-pyramids-menkaura-khafre-khufu-and-great-sphinx) — Great Pyramids (Menkaura, Khafre, Khufu) (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2550–2490 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Pyramids of Giza">

**Theme:** Assistance for the dead in reaching the afterlife

> The pyramids, along with functioning as massive tombs, were specially designed to assist the ka in reaching heavenly status. In the same vein, the scroll assists Hunefer in passing the trial.

---

## #25 — Lamassu from the citadel of Sargon II, Dur Sharrukin

**Neo-Assyrian** · c. 720–705 B.C.E. · Alabaster (gypsum) · Dur Sharrukin (modern Khorsabad, Iraq) · ~10 ft tall (each)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Lamassu_Sargon_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg/330px-Lamassu_Sargon_Dur_Sharrukin.jpg" width="240" alt="Lamassu from the citadel of Sargon II, Dur Sharrukin">
### Summary

Colossal **human-headed, winged-bull guardians** flanking the palace gates of **Sargon II** — composite protective spirits carved with **five legs** to read correctly from both front and side.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned for **Sargon II**'s new capital **Dur Sharrukin** (Khorsabad) during the Neo-Assyrian Empire's peak. The **Lamassu** derive from earlier Mesopotamian protective spirits (**lama**), demonized in scale to intimidate enemies.
- **Function.** **Apotropaic gate guardians** of the palace, asserting royal power and protecting the throne room. Cuneiform inscriptions on the body praise the king and **curse** anyone who criticizes him.
- **Content.** Hybrid body: **bull** (strength, royalty), **eagle wings** (heavenly reach), **bearded human head** (intelligence, wisdom), horned crown (divinity). Cuneiform text covers the lower body.
- **Form.** High relief in **gypsum alabaster**. **Five legs**: from the front the beast appears standing (two front legs), from the side it strides forward (four legs) — a clever registration of two views. The front is nearly in the round; the side is cut into the stone block.

**Vocabulary:** inscribe · lamassu · gypsum
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#65](#65--alhambra) — Alhambra (Nasrid Dynasty, 1354–1391 C.E.)

**Theme:** Defensive fortress, appealing to the eye, royal power

> The Alhambra was the Spanish Muslims last stand, so they made it beautiful & welcoming to those who would enter for diplomatic reasons. Both the Lamassu & the Alhambra were made to impress those who entered the kingdom.

---

#### → [#178](#178--aka-elephant-mask) — Aka Elephant Mask (Bamileke, 19th–20th c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Royal power

> This mask was for the high officials of the time. The elephant symbolized the power of the person who had it. This is another example of a strong power, using a creature of some kind to show their power to outsiders.

---

#### → [#17](#17--great-pyramids-menkaura-khafre-khufu-and-great-sphinx) — Great Pyramids of Giza / Great Sphinx (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2550–2490 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg/330px-Pyramids_of_the_Giza_Necropolis.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Pyramids of Giza and Great Sphinx">

**Theme:** Royal power, protective, mythological guardian

> The Sphinx is a mythical creature just like the Lamassu. Both beasts protected their creators/leaders, one in death the other in life.

---

## #26 — Athenian Agora

**Archaic through Hellenistic Greek** · 600 B.C.E.–150 C.E. · Marble (plan) · Athens, Greece · ~397,000 sq ft (excavated)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/Agora_Stoa_Athenians.jpg/330px-Agora_Stoa_Athenians.jpg" width="240" alt="Athenian Agora">
### Summary

The civic and commercial heart of ancient Athens — a multi-purpose **public square** that fused market, courthouse, temple, and philosophical arena, threaded by the **Panathenaic Way**.

- **Historical Context.** Classical Athens grew wealthy on maritime trade and Delian League tribute; the agora's scale and furnishings reflect that prosperity. The site was devastated by the Persian invasion and rebuilt in subsequent centuries.
- **Function.** All-purpose civic center: **market**, political meeting ground, **philosophical forum** (Socrates famously interrogated passers-by here), mint, and law court. Citizens gathered to **vote**; temples and altars were distributed throughout.
- **Content.** A large open area ringed by stoas, temples, government buildings, and workshops. The **Panathenaic Way** cuts diagonally through the agora toward the **Acropolis**. Structures on-site included the Tholos, the Bouleuterion, and various stoas (including the restored Stoa of Attalos).
- **Form.** Largely a **planned open square** rather than a single building — buildings in **marble** and stone, post-and-lintel construction, integrated around public circulation.

**Vocabulary:** colonnade · column · façade · peristyle
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#28](#28--peplos-kore-from-the-acropolis) — Peplos Kore from the Acropolis (Archaic Greek, c. 530 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Realism, religion

> The Peplos Kore was housed within the Acropolis, a structure near the agora. Worshippers likely travelled between the two locations frequently.

---

#### → [#19](#19--the-code-of-hammurabi) — The Code Stele of Hammurabi (Babylonian, c. 1792–1750 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Authority, law

> The agora was the center for political life in Athens and by extension a place from which the law emanated. Likewise, Hammurabi's basalt statues evoke the same power from its imposing structure and carved laws.

---

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Imperial Roman, rebuilt c. 62–79 C.E.)

**Theme:** Wealth, local prominence, residence

> Citizens of local importance made their home within or near the agora. It was not uncommon for politicians or merchants to live in private residences there.

---

## #27 — Anavysos Kouros

**Archaic Greek** · c. 530 B.C.E. · Marble with remnants of paint · National Archaeological Museum, Athens · 6 ft 4 in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Kouros_Anavysos.jpg/250px-Kouros_Anavysos.jpg" width="240" alt="Anavysos Kouros">
### Summary

A life-size Archaic **kouros** — a grave marker for a youth named **Kroisos** who died in battle — whose heavier musculature and bolder posture mark the move away from Egyptian-style rigidity.

- **Historical Context.** Made in 6th-century B.C.E. Greece, an Archaic culture wealthy from Mediterranean trade. An inscription found near the statue reads "Stay and mourn at the monument of dead Kroisos, whom raging Ares slew as he fought in the front ranks," identifying the subject as an aristocratic youth killed in war.
- **Function.** A **funerary marker** atop the grave of Kroisos; simultaneously an **idealized image of male youth**, not a portrait.
- **Content.** Standing frontal male nude — left foot advanced, fists clenched, long braided hair, subtle **Archaic smile**. Slim waist, broad shoulders, and strongly defined thighs and calves.
- **Form.** Freestanding **marble** once painted (traces remain on the lips). The rigid, symmetrical pose and frontality derive from Egyptian precedent, but the **rounder musculature** and more lifelike proportions (~1:7 head-to-body) signal a move toward naturalism.

**Vocabulary:** kouros · Archaic smile · mortuary · idealism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Idealism, statue of youth

> The Doryphoros Polykleitos

---

#### → [#18](#18--king-menkaura-and-queen) — King Menkaura and Queen (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2490–2472 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="180" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

**Theme:** Idealism, commemorative

> Resembles Ancient Egyptian sculpture in form, depicts an idealized version of a real figure for after they had died

---

#### → [#42](#42--head-of-a-roman-patrician) — Head of a Roman Patrician (Republican Roman, c. 75–50 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Idealism, height of career

> Both are idealized versions; one as youthful and strong, the other as wise and fair.

---

## #28 — Peplos Kore from the Acropolis

**Archaic Greek** · c. 530 B.C.E. · Marble with painted details · Acropolis (Athens); now Acropolis Museum · 118 cm tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Peplos_Kore.jpg/250px-Peplos_Kore.jpg" width="240" alt="Peplos Kore from the Acropolis">
### Summary

An Archaic **kore** — a young woman in a heavy woolen **peplos** — found on the **Athenian Acropolis**, once vividly painted and likely a votive image of a goddess.

- **Historical Context.** Excavated from the Athenian Acropolis in the late 19th century. Korai (female clothed) and kouroi (male nude) figures were a defining genre of Archaic Greek sculpture, often set up as votive dedications on the sanctuary. Her iconography may identify her as **Artemis** or **Athena** rather than a generic maiden — arm holes suggest she once held a bow.
- **Function.** A **votive offering** on the Acropolis, likely dedicated by a male worshipper to a goddess; possibly an image of the goddess herself. Korai could also serve as grave markers.
- **Content.** Frontal young woman in a heavy woolen **peplos** belted at the waist; missing left arm once extended forward. Distinctive **Archaic smile**, carefully styled wavy hair, remnants of bright paint (red, blue, yellow) once covered her garment with animal patterns.
- **Form.** **Parian marble** with **encaustic paint**. Rigid, frontal stance; squared shoulders; symmetrical. Bright original polychromy contradicts the common misconception of white Greek marble; smooth planes of fabric replace the earlier stylized drapery.

**Vocabulary:** kore · peplos · encaustic paint

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#37](#37--winged-victory-of-samothrace) — Winged Victory of Samothrace (Hellenistic Greek, c. 190 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Display of naturalistic human figures

> Using drapery, the female figure isn't necessarily hidden from view.

---

#### → [#41](#41--seated-boxer) — Seated Boxer (Hellenistic Greek, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Naturalism

> Both the Peplos Kore and the Seated Boxer represent naturalistic views of the subjects.

---

#### → Victory Adjusting Her Sandal (Athens, c. 410 B.C.E.) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Females in religion

> While Victory is shown pulling off a sandal before stepping onto sacred ground, the Peplos Kore herself is sacred in which she is an offering to the goddess.

---

## #29 — Sarcophagus of the Spouses

**Etruscan** · c. 520 B.C.E. · Terracotta · from Cerveteri (ancient Caere), Italy; now National Etruscan Museum, Rome · 3 ft 9½ in × 6 ft 7 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cf/Sarcophagus_of_the_Spouses_from_Villa_Giulia_in_Rome.jpg/330px-Sarcophagus_of_the_Spouses_from_Villa_Giulia_in_Rome.jpg" width="240" alt="Sarcophagus of the Spouses">
### Summary

A life-size Etruscan **terracotta cinerary urn** topped by a husband and wife reclining together at a banquet — a rare glimpse of Etruscan funerary ritual and of a society where women dined beside men.

- **Historical Context.** Excavated in the 19th century at the Banditaccia necropolis of **Caere** (modern Cerveteri). The Etruscans' own language is only partly deciphered, so their tombs provide the richest evidence for their culture. **Terracotta** dominated Etruscan art because Italian marble was not yet exploited.
- **Function.** A **cinerary container** to hold the cremated remains of an elite couple and assert their status in the afterlife. The banquet iconography reflects the Etruscan **celebratory** approach to funerary rites.
- **Content.** A smiling couple recline side-by-side on a wineskin-shaped cushion; the woman pours drops of perfume into her husband's hand while he embraces her shoulders. She may hold a **pomegranate**, symbol of immortality. Stylized almond eyes, beaded braids, and pointed shoes are characteristically Etruscan.
- **Form.** **Terracotta**, fired in sections and then joined — technically ambitious at this scale. Figures rendered in **high relief** above the waist, schematic below. Rounded Mediterranean faces but distinctly Etruscan angular joints and elongated fingers and toes.

**Vocabulary:** sarcophagus · anthropoid · sculpture
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="180" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

**Theme:** Funerary, Religion

> Both pieces of art depict a man and woman of high status, both have a funerary purpose for the spirits to prosper in the afterlife, both show woman supporting the man

**Theme:** Funerary, Religion

> Both pieces of art are Etruscan, both depictions have a funerary purpose for the afterlife

---

#### → [#21](#21--mortuary-temple-of-hatshepsut) — Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1473–1458 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary, Religion

> Both pieces of art have a funerary purpose, in both pieces of art there is a possible offering being showcased for the gods to grant the couple and Hatshepsut a positive afterlife

## #30 — Audience Hall (apadana) of Darius and Xerxes

**Persian (Achaemenid)** · c. 520–465 B.C.E. · Limestone · Persepolis, Iran · 72 columns, each ~25 m tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Apadana_Darius_Persepolis.jpg/330px-Apadana_Darius_Persepolis.jpg" width="240" alt="Audience Hall (apadana) of Darius and Xerxes">
### Summary

The immense **columned audience hall** of the Achaemenid kings at **Persepolis**, whose staircase reliefs depict 23 subject nations bringing **tribute** to the Persian king.

- **Historical Context.** The **Achaemenid Persian Empire**, at its height, reached from the Indus to Northern Greece and from Central Asia to Egypt. Begun by **Darius I** and completed by his son **Xerxes**, the apadana celebrates Persia's multi-ethnic rule and the empire's explicit policy of religious and cultural tolerance.
- **Function.** The hall in which the king received **tribute** from the empire's subject nations at the New Year festival. Reliefs on the north and east stairways showed each nation presenting its distinctive gifts, mirroring the delegations who saw themselves depicted as they ascended the stairs.
- **Content.** Two monumental stairways carved with procession reliefs: **23 delegations** in varied dress bring silver and gold vessels, weapons, textiles, jewelry, and animals. Stylized cypress trees separate groups; guards flank the king, who appears centrally.
- **Form.** A massive raised stone platform supporting **72 columns** with complex **bull- or lion-headed capitals** symbolizing royal authority; **verandas** open on three sides (unique among Persepolis palaces). The vanished roof was timber.

**Vocabulary:** apadana · hypostyle · column · relief · veranda · capital
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Parthenon / Acropolis (Iktinos and Kallikrates, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Dedication to a central figure; gathering to worship/honor a superior figure

> Both the Parthenon and Apadana are dedicated to a central figure to the respective nation (Athena, Persian king). They also display similar architectural elements, such as columned sides that are open to the outside and reliefs along the platform.

---

#### → [#44](#44--colosseum-flavian-amphitheater) — Colosseum (Imperial Roman, 70–80 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colosseo_2020.jpg/500px-Colosseo_2020.jpg" width="180" alt="Colosseum">

**Theme:** Reflecting the power of an emperor/ruler over its citizens/subjects

> In both the Roman and Persian empire, the emperor rarely had anything to do with the everyday lives of the people. These magnificent places provided an area where the emperor could display his magnificence and dominance in person to his subjects.

---

#### → [#20](#20--temple-of-amun-re-and-hypostyle-hall) — Temple of Amun-Re and Hypostyle Hall (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1550–1250 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Dedicated to honor a figure of great importance, such as a god or ruler

> The Temple and Amun-Re have similar architectural elements, the most notable being a hypostyle hall. They also both serve to express the superiority and power of the god/ruler through similar depictions- in the Temple, kings offer tribute to their gods, and in the Apadana, the king receives from his subjects.
