## #31 — Temple of Minerva (Veii) and sculpture of Apollo

**Etruscan; master sculptor Vulca** · c. 510–500 B.C.E. · Original temple of wood, mud brick, and tufa; terracotta sculpture · Veii, near Rome, Italy · Apollo of Veii ~5 ft 11 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2e/Apollo_di_Veio_-_Museo_di_Villa_Giulia_1.jpg/250px-Apollo_di_Veio_-_Museo_di_Villa_Giulia_1.jpg" width="240" alt="Temple of Minerva (Veii) and sculpture of Apollo">
### Summary

An Etruscan temple (known mainly from foundations and literary accounts) whose roof once bore a row of painted terracotta statues, including the striding **Apollo of Veii** attributed to the master **Vulca**.

- **Historical Context.** The Etruscans developed temple architecture under Greek influence but adapted it for local materials; because the temple used **wood, mud brick, and tufa**, only foundations survive. Etruscan art is typically anonymous, but ancient sources attribute the temple sculptures to **Vulca** of Veii. The **Apollo of Veii** is the only near-complete survivor.
- **Function.** A place of **worship** modeled distantly on Greek practice but distinctly Etruscan in orientation: accessible only from a frontal staircase and set on a high podium. The rooftop sculptures re-enacted a **mythological scene** (Apollo and Hercules disputing the Ceryneian hind).
- **Content.** An Etruscan-plan temple: deep front porch, three cellas at the rear. The acroterion sculptures depicted a dynamic mythic confrontation — Apollo striding forward in lively motion, muscles straining, drapery rippling.
- **Form.** **Terracotta** sculpture is brightly painted, heavier and more stylized than Greek marble equivalents. **Tuscan columns** (unfluted, simple base and capital); 5:6 floor-plan proportions; decorative color. The **Archaic smile** and patterned hair show Greek influence, while the taut striding pose and rooftop placement are Etruscan inventions.

**Vocabulary:** terracotta · column · Etruscan · Tuscan column
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 3.1, 3.2

---

**Theme:** Religious

> Both works have a religious function and they are both made out of tufa block.

> Both works are examples of Etruscan art. And it has a similar design to the Apollo of Veii.

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Advanced Architecture

> Both the temple and the Pantheon utilize columns in their architecture.

## #32 — Tomb of the Triclinium

**Etruscan** · c. 480–470 B.C.E. · Tufa and fresco · Monterozzi Necropolis, Tarquinia, Italy

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Pittore_forse_attico%2C_affreschi_della_tomba_del_triclinio%2C_500-475_ac_ca%2C_01.jpg/330px-Pittore_forse_attico%2C_affreschi_della_tomba_del_triclinio%2C_500-475_ac_ca%2C_01.jpg" width="240" alt="Tomb of the Triclinium">
### Summary

A small Etruscan **chamber tomb** at Tarquinia whose painted walls turn a burial chamber into a **celebratory dining room**, with frescoed banqueters, dancers, and musicians.

- **Historical Context.** The **Etruscans** (c. 700–300 B.C.E.) were a wealthy Iron Age culture on Italy's central-western coast, trading with Celts to the north and Greeks to the south. Because their written language is only partly understood, their **elaborately painted tombs** supply our best evidence of their culture. The Romans eventually absorbed them.
- **Function.** To ease the deceased's **transition to the afterlife** and to host a festive **funerary banquet** with family. The tomb also held grave goods and the remains of the deceased while commemorating their status.
- **Content.** A back wall fresco shows **three couches (triclinium)** with pairs of male and female diners attended by servants; cats, birds, and a rooster roam underfoot. The side walls show **male and female dancers and a musician** with a **barbiton** (lyre). Men are painted darker than women, per convention; the ceiling is checkered to mimic the tents of funerary celebrations.
- **Form.** A rock-cut chamber of modest size, painted in **buon fresco** with vibrant reds, blacks, and whites. The intimate scale and saturated color create an atmosphere of warmth rather than mourning.

**Vocabulary:** triclinium · fresco · chamber tomb

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#29](#29--sarcophagus-of-the-spouses) — Sarcophagus of the Spouses (Etruscan, c. 520 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary, commemorative

> Like the tomb of the triclinium, the sarcophagus of the spouses emphasizes a reverence for the dead along with a very personal and intimate commermation of them. Both are Etruscan.

---

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary, commemorative

> Tutankhamun's sarcophagus, like the tomb of the triclinium, holds the remains of the deceased while projecting a immense sense of reverence and wealth in the process. Both were meant to commemorate the deceased while emphasizing their status.

---

#### → [#31](#31--temple-of-minerva-veii-and-sculpture-of-apollo) — Temple of Minerva (Etruscan, c. 510–500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious

> The temple of Minerva is a very small space compared to many religious temples. This helps accomplish a sense of intimacy similar to what one would experience visiting the deceased in the tomb of the triclinium.

---

## #33 — Niobides Krater

**Classical Greek, Niobid Painter** · c. 460–450 B.C.E. · Clay, red-figure technique (white highlights) · Louvre, Paris · 21.3 × 22 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/Niobids_Krater.jpg/330px-Niobids_Krater.jpg" width="240" alt="Niobides Krater">
### Summary

An Early Classical **red-figure calyx-krater** showing **Artemis and Apollo slaughtering the Niobids**, painted with a figural arrangement that breaks the ground line — pointing toward the look of lost **wall painting**.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in the early decades of the Classical period by the anonymous artist called the **Niobid Painter**. The krater was a vessel for mixing wine with water. In myth, Niobe boasted her fourteen children outshone the two (Apollo and Artemis) of the goddess **Leto**; Leto's children killed them in revenge.
- **Function.** A **krater** for mixing wine and water at a symposium.
- **Content.** **Side A** depicts the massacre of the Niobids by Artemis and Apollo. **Side B** shows **Herakles and Athena** surrounded by warriors — ambiguously, possibly pre-battle honoring of Herakles as a protective hero. Figures scatter at multiple heights with no single ground line.
- **Form.** **Red-figure technique** — figures reserved in the clay color, background painted black. **Contrapposto** poses borrowed from Polykleitan sculpture; stoic faces; thick ornamental borders survive from the Geometric tradition. Distributing figures across multiple levels implies landscape depth, an early move away from the frieze arrangement of earlier Greek vases.

**Vocabulary:** krater · red-figure painting

---

**Theme:** Death, Judgement

> Twisted perspective, idea of gods judging mortals, functional piece, many figures, narrative

---

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Athleticism

> Contraposto, proportions, muscular, celebrates athleticism, stoic face

---

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Imperial Roman, rebuilt c. 62–79 C.E.)

**Theme:** Various scenes, daily life, historical events

> Framed, thick borders, narrative, multiple figure

## #34 — Doryphoros (Spear Bearer)

**Polykleitos** · Original c. 450–440 B.C.E. · Roman copy in marble of Greek original in bronze · Museo Archeologico Nazionale, Naples (from Pompeii) · 6 ft 11 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Doryphoros.jpg/250px-Doryphoros.jpg" width="240" alt="Doryphoros (Spear Bearer)">
### Summary

**Polykleitos's Canon** embodied — a **contrapposto** spear-bearer whose mathematical proportions (1:7 head to body) defined **High Classical** Greek ideal beauty and shaped Western art for millennia.

- **Historical Context.** Polykleitos, a master sculptor of the **High Classical** period, wrote a treatise (the "Canon") setting out mathematical ratios for the ideal human body; the Doryphoros was his paradigmatic illustration of it. When Rome conquered Greek cities after 211 B.C.E., Roman patrons commissioned **marble copies** of famous Greek bronzes — including this one, via **lost-wax** casting techniques.
- **Function.** Greek original: likely an embodiment of the Canon displayed as an exemplar. The surviving Roman marble was found in a **palestra** in Pompeii — appropriate to inspire athletes. Other copies decorated private homes.
- **Content.** A nude young warrior, probably **Achilles**, originally holding a spear over his left shoulder. **Heroic nudity** and **undistinguished facial features** mark him as an ideal type, not an individual.
- **Form.** **Contrapposto / chiastic stance**: weight on the right leg, left leg relaxed; the opposing arm tenses while the other relaxes — creating a **cross-balance** of tension and repose through the body. Strict **symmetria**, idealized musculature, impassive face. The Roman marble copy adds a tree-stump support that the bronze original did not need.

**Vocabulary:** Canon of proportions · contrapposto / chiastic · symmetria · kallos · idealism · 1:7 ratio

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#43](#43--augustus-of-prima-porta) — Augustus of Prima Porta (Imperial Roman, early 1st century C.E.)

**Theme:** Contrapposto, idealism, kallos, arête, warrior

> By modeling after the Doryphoros, the Augustus Prima Porta was used as propaganda to convey an energetic yet collected leader of the people.

---

#### → [#41](#41--seated-boxer) — Seated Boxer (Hellenistic Greek, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Realism, strength, heroic, nudity

> The seated boxer is a Hellenistic piece that has roots in the High Classical Period in which realism becomes increasingly dramatic. Both are images of strength and heroism.

---

#### → [#27](#27--anavysos-kouros) — Anavysos Kouros (Archaic Greek, c. 530 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Archaic smile, idealism, kouros, nudity

> The spear bearer has remnants of the archaic smile, which was still emphasized idealism. In addition, both kouros are nude which would become a Greek characteristic.

---

## #35 — Acropolis (Parthenon)

**Iktinos and Kallikrates, High Classical Greek** · c. 447–410 B.C.E. · Marble · Athens, Greece · Cella 29.8 × 19.2 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/330px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg" width="240" alt="Acropolis (Parthenon)">
### Summary

The **Parthenon**, crown of the **Athenian Acropolis**, built under **Pericles** as a Doric temple to **Athena Parthenos** — a high-water mark of Classical Greek architecture engineered to look perfectly regular through subtle curves.

- **Historical Context.** Designed by **Iktinos and Kallikrates**, with sculptural program by **Phidias**, during Athens's democratic Golden Age after the Persian invasion (480 B.C.E.) had destroyed the earlier Acropolis. Converted into a church and later a mosque; partly blown up in 1687 when Ottoman powder stored inside was hit by Venetian fire. Many of the sculptures are now in the British Museum.
- **Function.** A **temple to Athena Parthenos** (Athena the Virgin), housing a colossal chryselephantine cult statue by Phidias. Not a congregational space — ritual happened outside; every four years the **Panathenaic procession** culminated with the presentation of a peplos to Athena's old image.
- **Content.** Pediments depict Athena's birth (east) and her contest with Poseidon (west); metopes show battles (Centaurs vs. Lapiths, Gigantomachy, etc.); the continuous **Ionic frieze** depicts the **Panathenaic Procession**, including the **Plaque of the Ergastines** presenting the peplos.
- **Form.** **Doric peripteral** temple: 8 × 17 columns; marble post-and-lintel construction. Calculated **optical refinements** — columns with subtle **entasis**, stylobate curved upward, corner columns thicker — create the illusion of perfect straight geometry.

**Vocabulary:** Doric · Ionic · stylobate · pediment · triglyphs · metopes · cella / naos · peripteral · colonnade · entablature · peplos · frieze · marble
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#31](#31--temple-of-minerva-veii-and-sculpture-of-apollo) — Temple of Minerva (Etruscan, c. 510–500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, architecture

> Both housed statues of deities. Both were used in ceremonies over congregational worship

---

#### → [#38](#38--great-altar-of-zeus-and-athena-at-pergamon) — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon (Hellenistic Greek, c. 175 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, architecture, Greek gods

> Both were temples utilizing Greek art styles, one Hellenistic and one Classical. Both explore the mythology of the Gigantomachy in their reliefs

---

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, architecture

> Both were used as temples, both were converted to houses of worship for other religions at some point. Both reflect use of styles exclusive to their cultures (concrete, temple style)

---

## #36 — Grave Stele of Hegeso

**Classical Greek, attributed to Kallimachos** · c. 410 B.C.E. · Pentelic marble and paint · Kerameikos (Dipylon cemetery), Athens · 1.56 × 0.97 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Grave_Stele_Hegeso.jpg/250px-Grave_Stele_Hegeso.jpg" width="240" alt="Grave Stele of Hegeso">
### Summary

A refined Classical **grave relief** showing **Hegeso, daughter of Proxenos**, seated and examining a piece of jewelry held out by her maid — a Late Classical monument to Athenian female domestic life.

- **Historical Context.** Found in the Dipylon cemetery of Athens and attributed to **Kallimachos**. Classical Athenian women were legally defined by their fathers and husbands and spent most of their time in the household. The stele's inscription names Hegeso and Proxenos; the fine Pentelic marble and high-quality carving imply an aristocratic commission.
- **Function.** A **grave marker** commemorating Hegeso and asserting her family's wealth and status through both its material and its imagery.
- **Content.** Hegeso is seated on an elegant chair with her feet on a footstool. A slave girl (standing) holds out an open jewelry box from which Hegeso lifts an object — once painted, now lost — likely a necklace. An architectural frame with Ionic columns and a pediment supports the inscription.
- **Form.** Low **relief** in Pentelic marble. Naturalistic **drapery** reveals body contours; **hierarchy of scale** keeps the standing slave the same height as the seated Hegeso, so Hegeso's superior status is emphasized by her elevated footstool and central, framed placement rather than by size.

**Vocabulary:** domestic · idealization · grave stele
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#38](#38--great-altar-of-zeus-and-athena-at-pergamon) — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon (Hellenistic Greek, c. 175 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Mythological

> Both contain high reliefs and have express and expressionless face on each person. Both are also highly detailed and naturalistic.

---

#### → [#22](#22--akhenaton-nefertiti-and-three-daughters) — Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and three daughters (New Kingdom Amarna, c. 1353–1335 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary

> Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and three daughters depicts the family as an idealized version of themselves, similar to the grave stele of Hegeso. Both patrons are high status and honor the dead. However, Akhenaten's art is more religious whereas the stele of Hegeso is more funerary based.

---

#### → [#21](#21--mortuary-temple-of-hatshepsut) — Mortuary Temple of Hatshepsut (New Kingdom Egypt, c. 1473–1458 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary

> The mortuary temple of Hatshepsut depicts a woman of high status. Both are made with high quality, detail, and contains relief work in order to represent and honor the deceased subject. Both the grave stele of Hegeso and Mortuary temple were both created in memory of the deceased.

---

## #37 — Winged Victory of Samothrace

**Hellenistic Greek** · c. 190 B.C.E. · Parian marble · Louvre, Paris · ~9 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Nike_of_Samothrace%2C_Louvre.jpg/330px-Nike_of_Samothrace%2C_Louvre.jpg" width="240" alt="Winged Victory of Samothrace">
### Summary

**Nike** alighting on the prow of a stone ship — a dramatic **Hellenistic** masterpiece of wet drapery and spiral composition, commemorating a naval victory at the **Sanctuary of the Great Gods on Samothrace**.

- **Historical Context.** Made in the **Hellenistic** era, after Alexander the Great's death, when **Rhodes** and Pergamon competed as artistic centers. The statue shows Rhodian influence and probably commemorates a **Rhodian naval victory** — either at Myonnisos or Side (190 B.C.E.) over Antiochus III.
- **Function.** A **votive** monument to **Nike** (goddess of victory), dedicated at the Sanctuary of the Great Gods (Cabeiri) on Samothrace. The Nike stood atop a stone ship's prow, announcing the dedication with her now-lost trumpet or gesture.
- **Content.** **Nike** striding forward, wings spread, wet chiton pressed against her body by sea wind; no head, no arms. Originally cupped hands or trumpet to herald a victory. A fountain once fed by water animated the scene.
- **Form.** Subtractive **Parian marble**. The body coils into a **spiral composition**; tense forward movement against the wind. **Wet drapery** clings and billows, describing the body beneath. Pure white, fine-grained marble; designed for a **left-front viewing angle** — the right side is less detailed.

**Vocabulary:** subtractive · sanctuary · votive

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#38](#38--great-altar-of-zeus-and-athena-at-pergamon) — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon (Hellenistic Greek, c. 175 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, historical, mythological, military

> This was also built around the Hellenistic Greek period. The figures are similar to the Winged Victory with muscles strained and with a lot of tension and movement, which are common features the Hellenistic style. This piece was also constructed to commemorate military victories as well as serve as a tribute to the gods.

---

#### → [#40](#40--alexander-mosaic-from-the-house-of-the-faun-pompeii) — Alexander Mosaic from the House of the Faun (Republican Roman, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical, military

> Similar to the Winged Victory, this piece commemorates a military victory, the victory of Alexander the Great over the King of Persia. This also has a lot of motion within the piece, with bodies tense in a tide of battle.

---

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis: Temple of Athena Nike (Classical Greek, c. 427–424 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, historical, mythological, military

> This piece also commemorates military victory: the Greek victory over the Persians in the Battle of Marathon. It also serves as a tribute to the gods, considered as a home for the deity to reside in.

---

## #38 — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon

**Hellenistic Greek** · c. 175 B.C.E. · Marble (architecture and sculpture) · Asia Minor (present-day Turkey) · ~115 × 110 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Pergamon_Altar.jpg/330px-Pergamon_Altar.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon">
### Summary

An enormous **Hellenistic altar** whose wrap-around **Gigantomachy frieze** — the Olympian gods battling the Giants — turns the high-baroque drama and pathos of late Greek sculpture into a metaphor for the **Attalid** dynasty's victory.

- **Historical Context.** Built under **Eumenes II** of the Attalid dynasty in **Pergamon**, a former Alexandrian successor kingdom that became a major center of Hellenistic art. The altar commemorates the Attalid victory over Gallic (Galatian) invaders and Seleucid rivals.
- **Function.** An **altar** (not a temple), approached up a grand flight of stairs. A tribute to Zeus and Athena and a triumphal statement of Attalid political supremacy.
- **Content.** The continuous frieze depicts the **Gigantomachy** — the Olympians defeating the earth-born **Giants**. In the frieze shown in the image set, **Athena** drags **Alcyoneus** (a Giant) from the earth while **Gaea** (earth mother) looks up in anguish; a winged **Nike** crowns Athena.
- **Form.** Ionic colonnade enclosing a square altar platform on a high podium. The nearly sculpted-in-the-round **high relief** frieze shows **straining muscles, twisting bodies, wind-whipped drapery, and vivid pathos** — the full Hellenistic baroque idiom.

**Vocabulary:** frieze · colonnade · peristyle · Ionic columns
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis (Parthenon) (Classical Greek, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Mythological and religious themes

> The temple probably influenced the form of the Great Altar at Pergamon, as evidence by the extensive use of columns and a peristyle-like form.

---

#### → [#41](#41--seated-boxer) — Seated Boxer (Hellenistic Greek, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Realism and drama

> The realist portrayal of an athlete after a fight is emblematic of the changes Hellenism brought to Greek art, changes that are also present in the Great Altar.

---

#### → [#47](#47--ludovisi-battle-sarcophagus) — Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus (Late Imperial Roman, c. 250 C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical

> The use of increased drama is evident in the expression of the figures on the sarcophagus; it demonstrates an extension of the Hellenistic awareness of empty/negative space, although taken to the extreme.

---

## #39 — House of the Vettii

**Imperial Roman** · c. second century B.C.E.; rebuilt c. 62–79 C.E. · Cut stone and fresco · Pompeii, Italy · Ground floor ~3,609 sq ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/House_of_the_Vettii%2C_Pompeii%2C_20230623_133502_37.jpg/330px-House_of_the_Vettii%2C_Pompeii%2C_20230623_133502_37.jpg" width="240" alt="House of the Vettii">
### Summary

A richly frescoed **Pompeiian domus** owned by two **freedman brothers** — the Vettii — whose grand atrium, peristyle garden, and ostentatious wall paintings survive because of the **79 C.E. eruption of Vesuvius**.

- **Historical Context.** The brothers **Aulus Vettius Restitutus** and **Aulus Vettius Conviva** were wealthy freedmen merchants who bought and redecorated the house after the 62 C.E. earthquake displaced many old elite families. The house is a paradigm of the Roman **Fourth-Style** wall painting and exemplifies new freedman wealth in late-1st-century Pompeii.
- **Function.** Private residence *and* stage for the Roman **patron-client ritual**. Patrons received clients in the **atrium** each morning; the house's scale and decoration broadcast status and cemented the patron's authority.
- **Content.** Standard domus plan: **fauces** (narrow entry), **atrium** with **impluvium**, **tablinum**, and a columned **peristyle garden**. Prominent **strongboxes** in the atrium displayed wealth; frescoes in the Fourth Style — including mythological scenes and the famous image of **Priapus** — line the walls.
- **Form.** Stone masonry carrying an inward-facing plan organized around open courtyards; colonnaded peristyle around a formal garden. Fourth-Style frescoes combine illusionistic architectural vistas with framed mythological panels in saturated reds, yellows, and blacks.

**Vocabulary:** atrium · impluvium · peristyle garden

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious site

> Much like the House of the Vettii the Pantheon utilizes Roman architecture in accordance to religious views and display of power of wealthy. The Vettii brothers contributed to public works similar to the Pantheon and both the House and the Pantheon reinforce the subjugation of common people.

---

#### → [#32](#32--tomb-of-the-triclinium) — Tomb of the Triclinium (Etruscan, c. 480–470 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funeral setting

> Similar to the House of the Vettii frescoes decorate walls and there are depictions of a dining area. Both works convey the views of everyday life in regards to ceremonial functions.

---

#### → [#58](#58--church-of-sainte-foy) — Church of Sainte-Foy (Romanesque Europe, c. 1050–1130 C.E.)

**Theme:** Church, religious

> Like the House, the Church of Sainte-Foy utilizes wide open architecture and columns. Repetition in classical Greco-Roman styles is present in each work.

---

## #40 — Alexander Mosaic from the House of the Faun, Pompeii

**Republican Roman, copy after Greek (Hellenistic) original** · c. 100 B.C.E. (Roman); Greek original c. 310 B.C.E. · Mosaic (tesserae) · National Archaeological Museum, Naples · 8 ft 11 in × 16 ft 9 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Alexander_Mosaic.jpg/330px-Alexander_Mosaic.jpg" width="240" alt="Alexander Mosaic from the House of the Faun, Pompeii">
### Summary

A floor **mosaic** from the grandest house in Pompeii that copies a lost Greek painting of the **Battle of Issus** — **Alexander the Great** driving his cavalry through Darius III's lines.

- **Historical Context.** Found in the floor of the **House of the Faun** in Pompeii, preserved by the **Vesuvius eruption of 79 C.E.** The Greek original — possibly by **Philoxenos of Eretria** or **Helen of Egypt** — commemorated **Alexander's victory over Darius III** at Issus (333 B.C.E.), a decisive moment in the collapse of the Persian Empire.
- **Function.** A spectacularly elaborate **floor mosaic** for an elite domus — unusually detailed for something meant to be walked on, clearly made to be admired by guests.
- **Content.** Left: **Alexander** in a cuirass bears down on horseback, young, focused, spear forward. Right: **Darius III**, arms extended, looks back in alarm as his charioteer whips the horses into retreat. Fallen Persian soldiers and a forest of sarissas fill the space.
- **Form.** Dense **opus vermiculatum** ("worm work") in tiny tesserae, dominated by **warm reds, yellows, and browns**. Sophisticated **foreshortening** (notably the horse seen from behind), strong **diagonals**, dramatic chiaroscuro — techniques preserved from a Greek painting.

**Vocabulary:** mosaic · mural · foreshortening · tesserae
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#13](#13--palette-of-king-narmer) — Palette of King Narmer (Predynastic Egypt, c. 3000–2920 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Narmer_Palette.jpg/330px-Narmer_Palette.jpg" width="180" alt="Palette of King Narmer">

**Theme:** Victory in battle; belittling of enemies

> This demonstrates a portrayal of history through artwork and the romanticizing of victors of battle.

---

#### → [#203](#203--night-attack-on-the-sanjo-palace) — Night Attack on the Sanjō Palace (Japanese, Kamakura, late 13th c.)

**Theme:** Horror of battle

> This demonstrates a portrayal of history through artwork and the intricacies of war.

---

#### → [#47](#47--ludovisi-battle-sarcophagus) — Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus (Late Imperial Roman, c. 250 C.E.)

**Theme:** Horror of battle; belittling of enemies

> This demonstrates a portrayal of history through artwork and the humiliation of enemies. In addition, there is a crowded nature, creating a sense of nervous energy.

---

## #41 — Seated Boxer

**Hellenistic Greek** · c. 100 B.C.E. · Bronze · Palazzo Massimo alle Terme, Rome · 4 ft 2½ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Seated_Boxer_%2836466552736%29.jpg/250px-Seated_Boxer_%2836466552736%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Seated Boxer">
### Summary

An unflinchingly **realist** Hellenistic bronze of a **battered older athlete** — cut lips, broken nose, cauliflower ears, blood-filled scars — designed to elicit **pathos** rather than idealized awe.

- **Historical Context.** One of two bronzes unearthed on the Quirinal Hill of Rome in 1885, carefully buried near the Baths of Constantine. It dates to the **Hellenistic** period (323–31 B.C.E.), when Greek sculptors turned to heightened emotion, wider social range, and unidealized realism.
- **Function.** A **commemorative image of a boxer** — possibly a champion, possibly a generic type. Unlike earlier athletic statues that glorified the ideal body, this one invites **empathy** for the wear of a life in the ring.
- **Content.** A mature, **bearded** boxer sits between bouts, caestus-wrapped hands on his knees. Facial **wounds**, a broken nose, cauliflower ears, and a bruised mouth bespeak rounds of violence. His head turns upward as if hearing someone call his name.
- **Form.** **Lost-wax cast bronze**, assembled from eight segments with nearly invisible joins. **Copper inlays** redden the lips, scars, and blood drops on shoulder, forearm, and thigh. The tense torso still reads as strong — Hellenistic realism does not abandon musculature — but the **pathos** is in the face.

**Vocabulary:** pathos · bronze · sculpture · lost-wax process
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#42](#42--head-of-a-roman-patrician) — Head of a Roman Patrician (Republican Roman, c. 75–50 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Ancient Mediterranean, Realism

> Both mark a departure from earlier works of art that depicted the idealized human male form, with the *Head of a Roman Patrician* going as far as to emphasize imperfections, making the patrician look older and more wrinkled to emphasize his wisdom.

---

#### → [#37](#37--winged-victory-of-samothrace) — Winged Victory of Samothrace (Hellenistic Greek, c. 190 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Ancient Mediterranean, Hellenistic, Drama

> Both sculptures, being of the same Hellenistic period, included a lot more emotion and pathos in comparison to past pieces, which largely did not depict emotion.

---

#### → [#29](#29--sarcophagus-of-the-spouses) — Sarcophagus of the Spouses (Etruscan, c. 520 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Ancient Mediterranean, Daily Life

> While of different cultures, both depict daily lives of people living in the respective societies of the artworks.

---

## #42 — Head of a Roman Patrician

**Republican Roman** · c. 75–50 B.C.E. · Marble · Palazzo Torlonia, Rome (from Otricoli) · Bust-sized

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Roman_Patrician_Head.jpg/250px-Roman_Patrician_Head.jpg" width="240" alt="Head of a Roman Patrician">
### Summary

A **veristic** Republican portrait of an unknown, elderly Roman **patrician** — wrinkles, sunken cheeks, and hooked nose rendered to advertise **gravitas** and accumulated service, the opposite of Greek idealization.

- **Historical Context.** Carved in late Republican Rome, when senate-level **patricians** ran the state; aristocratic lineage and political accomplishment were signaled by images of **age, not youth**. The style is **verism**, an exaggerated naturalism likely influenced by Etruscan ancestor portraits and Greek Hellenistic realism.
- **Function.** A **portrait bust** celebrating a specific patrician's career — probably displayed in his atrium (alongside wax death masks, *imagines*, of ancestors) and shown in public processions to advertise the family's political pedigree.
- **Content.** A portrait of an unnamed elderly man with a **hooked nose**, deeply **furrowed brow**, sagging jowls, and thin-lipped mouth — an image of **experience, authority, and civic virtue**. No dynamism; direct, emotionless gaze.
- **Form.** Marble bust carved in **verism** — exaggerated wrinkles and sags without adornment. Hair is minimal; attention is concentrated in the expressive topography of the face.

**Vocabulary:** verism · gravitas · (lack of) dynamism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 2.1, 3.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#41](#41--seated-boxer) — Seated Boxer (Hellenistic Greek, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Realism, Historical

> Both these pieces depict highly realistic individuals, showcasing rather than glossing over their physical flaws.

---

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Classical, Greek

> Both types of art were highly sought out by Romans, Dryphoros for its Grecian roots and the head of a Roman Patrician for its depiction of a roman's valor

---

#### → [#43](#43--augustus-of-prima-porta) — Augustus of Prima Porta (Imperial Roman, early 1st c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical, Portraiture

> Both these pieces of art were meant to depict the grandeur and power of a leader/ruler.

---

## #43 — Augustus of Prima Porta

**Imperial Roman** · Early 1st century C.E. · Marble · Villa of Livia, near Prima Porta, Rome · 2.03 m tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Augustus_Prima_Porta.jpg/250px-Augustus_Prima_Porta.jpg" width="240" alt="Augustus of Prima Porta">
### Summary

A marble portrait of **Augustus** as perpetually youthful commander, blending Polykleitan **contrapposto** with an iconography-packed **cuirass** — imperial image-making as propaganda.

- **Historical Context.** Likely commissioned c. 20 B.C.E. to celebrate the diplomatic return of Roman standards from the Parthians — a signature event of the early Augustan **Pax Romana**. Found at the Villa of Augustus's wife **Livia**.
- **Function.** **Imperial propaganda.** The statue projects an idealized, youthful, and godlike Augustus, dressed as a military commander addressing his troops (*adlocutio* gesture).
- **Content.** Augustus stands barefoot (a divine attribute) in military regalia; the **breastplate** is dense with symbolism — the return of the Parthian standards, personifications of conquered peoples, Sol and Caelus, Apollo and Diana. A tiny **Cupid on a dolphin** at his ankle asserts his descent from **Venus** (the Julian family myth).
- **Form.** **Contrapposto** after Polykleitos's Doryphoros; idealized musculature; serene, youthful, generic face (Augustus is always depicted this way despite aging). High relief across the cuirass; originally brightly painted.

**Vocabulary:** contrapposto · symbolism · militaristic · directional
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.1, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#19](#19--the-code-of-hammurabi) — Code Stele of Hammurabi (Babylonian, c. 1792–1750 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Power

> The Code Stele of Hammurabi, like the Augustus of Prima Porta, was created as propaganda to enhance the image of the ruler of an empire.

---

#### → [#22](#22--akhenaton-nefertiti-and-three-daughters) — Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and Three Daughters (Amarna Egypt, c. 1353–1335 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Leadership

> The carving of the pharaoh Akhenaton, his wife Nefertiti, and their daughters connects to this piece because it also highlights a proper image of the royal family, which was considered to be the ideal for the time.

---

#### → [#18](#18--king-menkaura-and-queen) — King Menkaura and Queen (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2490–2472 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="180" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

**Theme:** Strength

> The statue of King Menkaura and his queen found in Egypt is very similar in purpose and design to the Augustus of Prima Porta because it also uses strong physical forms to communicate the ideal that the ruler himself is a strong and capable leader.

---

## #44 — Colosseum (Flavian Amphitheater)

**Imperial Roman, Flavian dynasty** · 70–80 C.E. · Stone and concrete · Rome, Italy · 615 × 510 ft; ~50,000–80,000 spectators

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colosseo_2020.jpg/500px-Colosseo_2020.jpg" width="240" alt="Colosseum">

### Summary

The largest amphitheater ever built — begun by **Vespasian**, completed by **Titus**, named for the Flavian dynasty — funded by spoils from the **Great Jewish Revolt** and fitted with sophisticated concrete engineering.

- **Historical Context.** Built to return to the Roman people land Nero had seized for his Domus Aurea. Financed by the sack of Jerusalem (70 C.E.); constructed in part by some **100,000 Jewish prisoners**. Opened with **100 days of games** under Titus.
- **Function.** Mass entertainment: **gladiatorial combat, wild-animal hunts,** and even flooded **mock naval battles** (naumachiae). 80 entrances managed crowd flow; the **hypogeum** (below-stage) raised animals and combatants into the arena via a pulley system; the **velarium** (awning) shaded spectators above.
- **Content.** An ellipse of stacked stone arcades. Sand (*arena*) covered the floor to absorb blood; statues once filled the upper arches; bronze shields decorated the attic.
- **Form.** **Concrete** construction with **travertine limestone** facing — the engineering breakthrough that allowed free-standing mass entertainment venues. Four stories with stacked column orders: **Tuscan, Ionic, Corinthian, Composite** engaged pilasters frame rhythmic arcades. Barrel-vaulted radial corridors distribute spectators.

**Vocabulary:** amphitheatre · velarium · hypogeum
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#42](#42--head-of-a-roman-patrician) — Head of a Roman Patrician (Republican Roman, c. 75–50 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Roman power, wealth

> This artwork demonstrates the ideals Patrician, whose power, age and corresponding wisdom allows him to lead Rome effectively. The Colosseum models the power and wealth of Rome.

---

#### → [#45](#45--forum-of-trajan) — Forum of Trajan (Imperial Roman, 106–113 C.E.)

**Theme:** Roman Power, Architecture

> Built in 106-112 AD, the Forum of Trajan was built from the spoils of war from conquering Dacia, similar to the Colosseums funding. It was also a massive monumental complex, similar to the Colosseum for some purposes

---

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis (Classical Greek, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Architecture, Fortress

> Serving as both a fortress and place of worship for gods such as Athena, it served similar purposes to the Colosseum in later times. It was also built as a complex with monuments, similar to the Colosseum.

---

## #45 — Forum of Trajan

**Imperial Roman; Apollodorus of Damascus** · Forum and markets 106–112 C.E.; column completed 113 C.E. · Brick and concrete (architecture); marble (column) · Rome, Italy · 1000 × 600 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Forum_of_Trajan_September_2015-1.jpg/330px-Forum_of_Trajan_September_2015-1.jpg" width="240" alt="Forum of Trajan">
### Summary

The **grandest imperial forum** in Rome — a complex of plaza, basilica, libraries, multilevel markets, and a narrative **column** — commemorating Trajan's conquest of **Dacia**.

- **Historical Context.** Designed by **Apollodorus of Damascus** (architect of the Pantheon) for **Trajan** ("Optimus Princeps"), whose conquests pushed the Empire to its greatest extent. Construction required cutting away a 40-ft hill.
- **Function.** Civic and commemorative. The forum hosted public life (courts, legal proceedings, political gatherings); the Basilica Ulpia served legal and administrative needs; twin Greek and Latin **libraries** flanked the **Column of Trajan**; the multilevel **Markets of Trajan** housed 150+ shops — a progenitor of the shopping mall.
- **Content.** Sequential axial spaces: entry arch → vast colonnaded forum (with equestrian bronze of Trajan) → **Basilica Ulpia** → libraries → **Column of Trajan** rising between them, its continuous helical relief narrating the Dacian Wars. The **semicircular exedra** of Trajan's Markets wraps one side.
- **Form.** Concrete-and-brick construction faced in marble. Monumental axial plan with symmetry, colonnades, triumphal arches. The **125-ft marble column** carries a spiraling band of figural relief roughly 600 ft long — a pioneering continuous narrative.

**Vocabulary:** column · arch · portico · exedra · basilica · relief · arcade
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#44](#44--colosseum-flavian-amphitheater) — Colosseum (Imperial Roman, 70–80 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Colosseo_2020.jpg/500px-Colosseo_2020.jpg" width="180" alt="Colosseum">

**Theme:** Public, large scale, first of its kind, architecture

> The Colosseum was another Roman public amenity that was built for the well-being of citizens.

---

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Public, temple, large scale architecture

> The Pantheon was designed by the same architect and shares many architectural similarities. Although its overall purpose is different, it does function as a public amenity to Roman citizens.

---

#### → Column of Trajan (part of the Forum complex; self-reference in image set)

**Theme:** Commemorative, artistic relief

> The forum is centered around this work of art commemorating Trajan.

---

## #46 — Pantheon

**Imperial Roman** · 118–125 C.E. · Concrete with stone facing · Rome, Italy · 142 ft high dome, 142 ft interior diameter, 150 ft overall height

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Pantheon_Rome.jpg/330px-Pantheon_Rome.jpg" width="240" alt="Pantheon">
### Summary

**Hadrian's Pantheon** — a temple "to all the gods" whose **coffered concrete dome** and **oculus** remain the largest unreinforced concrete dome ever built, and define the possibilities of Roman architecture.

- **Historical Context.** Completed under **Hadrian** on the site of an earlier temple built by Marcus Agrippa (hence the surviving inscription). Designed by **Apollodorus of Damascus**. Converted into a **Christian church** (Santa Maria ad Martyres) in 609 C.E. — which is why it survives.
- **Function.** A temple **to all the gods** — a political gesture that folded traditional Roman religion into the imperial order. Later a church and pilgrimage site; today also a museum.
- **Content.** A rectangular **portico** with 16 granite Corinthian columns fronts a vast circular **rotunda** topped by a **hemispherical coffered dome**. At the apex, a 27-ft **oculus** admits sun and rain.
- **Form.** The building is essentially a sphere inscribed in a cylinder: the interior width equals the dome's height. Poured **concrete** graded from heavy aggregate at the base to light volcanic pumice near the oculus; five rings of **coffers** reduce weight and embellish the inner dome. Marble and porphyry **floor** patterns continue the geometry below.

**Vocabulary:** Corinthian columns · oculus · coffers · coffered dome · portico · barrel vaults · rotunda · vestibule · pediment / tympanum · dome

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis / Parthenon (Classical Greek, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** (not specified in snapshot)

> (Snapshot connection table left Themes/Connections blank)

---

#### → [#31](#31--temple-of-minerva-veii-and-sculpture-of-apollo) — Temple of Minerva (Etruscan, c. 510–500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** (not specified in snapshot)

> (Snapshot connection table left Themes/Connections blank)

#### → [#38](#38--great-altar-of-zeus-and-athena-at-pergamon) — Great Altar of Zeus and Athena at Pergamon (Hellenistic Greek, c. 175 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** (not specified in snapshot)

> (Snapshot connection table left Themes/Connections blank)

## #47 — Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus

**Late Imperial Roman** · c. 250–260 C.E. · Proconnesian marble · Rome, Italy · ~5 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Ludovisi_Battle_Sarcophagus.jpg/330px-Ludovisi_Battle_Sarcophagus.jpg" width="240" alt="Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus">
### Summary

A high-relief Roman **battle sarcophagus** showing Romans overwhelming Goths in a writhing, claustrophobic tangle — late Empire funerary art shaped by the **Crisis of the Third Century**.

- **Historical Context.** Made during the **Crisis of the Third Century** (235–284 C.E.), when plague, civil war, and barbarian invasion nearly unraveled the Roman Empire. Depictions of military triumph in this period are partly propaganda against very real battlefield losses.
- **Function.** A **funerary monument** for a wealthy (unidentified) Roman military commander, probably the young horseman at the center. An **X** on his forehead signals devotion to **Mithraism**, a soldiers' mystery cult.
- **Content.** A mass of Roman soldiers tramples and impales **Goth** warriors, whose curly hair, helmetless heads, and anguished faces mark them as barbarians. At the center, the commander rides triumphantly, his victory asserted by the contrast of his composure against the chaos.
- **Form.** **High relief** in white marble. **Horror vacui** — the surface has almost no negative space. **Anti-classical**: expressive, pain-filled faces replace serene idealization; scale differences are smaller than typical hieratic scale but the overall composition still privileges the central horseman.

**Vocabulary:** anti-classical · sarcophagus · high relief · Crisis of the Third Century
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#40](#40--alexander-mosaic-from-the-house-of-the-faun-pompeii) — Alexander Mosaic (Republican Roman, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Victory in battle, portrayal of enemies as weak

> Both works show a battle in which the winner is clear: in the case of the sarcophagus, the Romans, and in the case of the mosaic, the Macedonians. They are both commemorative, although the sarcophagus reflects upon an entire life and the mosaic just remembers a battle.

---

#### → [#13](#13--palette-of-king-narmer) — Palette of King Narmer (Predynastic Egypt, c. 3000–2920 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Narmer_Palette.jpg/330px-Narmer_Palette.jpg" width="180" alt="Palette of King Narmer">

**Theme:** Victory in battle, portrayal of enemies as weak

> On the middle-right of one side of the palette, enemies are shown laying on the floor, decapitated and castrated. The Egyptians show their enemies as unable to defeat them, similar to the Goths being crushed under the Romans in the battle sarcophagus. Both are relief sculpture.

---

#### → [#16](#16--standard-of-ur) — Standard of Ur (Sumerian, c. 2600–2400 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/38/Denis_Bourez_-_British_Museum%2C_London_%288747049029%29_%282%29.jpg/330px-Denis_Bourez_-_British_Museum%2C_London_%288747049029%29_%282%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Standard of Ur">

**Theme:** Portrayal of enemies as weak

> The Standard of Ur uses hieratic scale, similar to the battle sarcophagus, as a way of indicating the enemies being fought are lesser than the winning side. Also, there are figures being trampled by soldiers on horseback in both works.

---

## #50 — Rebecca and Eliezer at the Well and Jacob Wrestling the Angel, from the Vienna Genesis

**Early Byzantine Europe** · Early 6th century C.E. · Illuminated manuscript (tempera, gold, and silver on purple vellum) · Österreichische Nationalbibliothek, Vienna · 12.5 × 9.25 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Vienna_Genesis_Rebecca.jpg/330px-Vienna_Genesis_Rebecca.jpg" width="240" alt="Rebecca and Eliezer at the Well and Jacob Wrestling the Angel, from the Vienna Genesis">
### Summary

The **oldest well-preserved illustrated biblical manuscript** — a Greek book of **Genesis** painted on **purple-dyed vellum** with silver ink, made in a Byzantine workshop for an elite patron.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in the **early 6th century** C.E., likely in **Constantinople** or Syria. Christianity dominated Byzantine art; literacy remained an elite privilege, so illustration was a vital way to communicate scripture to the broader lay audience.
- **Function.** An **illuminated codex** owned by an aristocrat or high church official, where rich pictures carried the narrative for less-literate viewers and declared the book's sanctity through costly materials.
- **Content.** Two scenes from Genesis: **Rebecca meets Eliezer at the well**, where she is chosen as Isaac's wife after offering water to his camels; and **Jacob wrestling the angel** at the ford of the Jabbok, where he is renamed Israel. Both use **continuous narrative** — the same figures appear several times in one frame as the story unfolds.
- **Form.** **Tempera, gold, and silver** on **purple-dyed vellum** (a color associated with imperial authority). Classical painterly techniques survive — **cast shadows, foreshortening, perspective** — alongside a beginning of the flatter Byzantine hieratic style. A silver line marks the bridge/pathway guiding the eye.

**Vocabulary:** vellum · Genesis · codex
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

## #51 — San Vitale

**Early Byzantine Europe** · c. 526–547 C.E. · Brick, marble, and stone veneer; mosaic · Ravenna, Italy · ~60 × 18 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/San_Vitale_Ravenna.jpg/330px-San_Vitale_Ravenna.jpg" width="240" alt="San Vitale">
### Summary

A **centrally planned** Byzantine church in Ravenna whose sanctuary walls are lined with the most vivid Byzantine mosaics in the West — the imperial panels of **Justinian and Theodora**.

- **Historical Context.** Sponsored by the banker **Julianus Argentarius** and completed under **Justinian** after the Byzantine reconquest of Ravenna from the Ostrogoths. Celebrates Justinian's authority in both church and state and asserts orthodoxy after Arian rule.
- **Function.** Originally a **martyrium of Saint Vitalis**, and a congregational church. Used to proclaim the unity of imperial and religious power: Justinian and Theodora are shown making liturgical offerings.
- **Content.** Biblical and imperial **mosaics**: Christ as cosmic ruler in the apse; Old Testament scenes (Abraham, Melchizedek) on the sanctuary walls; and the famous twin panels of **Justinian with his attendants** (left) and **Theodora with hers** (right), both **haloed**, presenting the **paten and chalice** for the Eucharist.
- **Form.** **Octagonal centrally-planned** church with an **ambulatory** wrapping a domed core. Layered arches and connecting columns create weightless interior; **tesserae** in gold and rich color flatten figures and float them against gold backgrounds. Justinian in royal **purple**; frontal, symmetrical, hieratic.

**Vocabulary:** centrally-planned · ambulatory · spolia · mosaic · martyrium · paten · Eucharist · tesserae
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Grand architecture

> Buildings with Circular Plans

---

#### → [#40](#40--alexander-mosaic-from-the-house-of-the-faun-pompeii) — Alexander Mosaic (Republican Roman, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Technique of tesserae

> Mosaics with small piece of stone that show shading and movement

---

#### → [#185](#185--the-dome-of-the-rock) — Dome of the Rock (Umayyad, 691–692 C.E.)

**Theme:** Light within the building

> Both Byzantine architecture with windows along inner perimeter of the dome

## #52 — Hagia Sophia

**Anthemius of Tralles and Isidorus of Miletus, Byzantine** · 532–537 C.E. · Brick and ceramic elements with stone and mosaic veneer · Istanbul (Constantinople), Turkey · 269 × 240 × 180 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Agia_Sofia_seen_from_Southwest_47-3.jpg/330px-Agia_Sofia_seen_from_Southwest_47-3.jpg" width="240" alt="Hagia Sophia">
### Summary

**Justinian's** "Holy Wisdom" — a colossal Byzantine church whose **pendentive-supported central dome** seemed, to its 6th-century beholders, "suspended from heaven." Later a mosque, a museum, and again a mosque.

- **Historical Context.** The current building is the **third** Hagia Sophia on the site. The first two, begun under Constantine I and Constans I, were burned (the second during the **Nika riots** of 532). **Justinian** rebuilt immediately, commissioning the mathematicians **Anthemius of Tralles and Isidorus of Miletus**. Served as the Cathedral of Constantinople for over a millennium; after **Mehmed II**'s conquest in 1453 it became a **mosque**; secularized as a museum in 1934.
- **Function.** Great Byzantine **cathedral**, then **imperial mosque**, then museum. In all incarnations a theater for sacred light and imperial ceremony.
- **Content.** Originally a gold-and-mosaic interior with a giant cross at the dome's crown; iconic mosaics of Christ, the Virgin, and emperors (some recovered after the Iconoclasm that ended in 843). After 1453, Christian images were whitewashed, **minarets, a mihrab, a minbar, and medallions of Arabic calligraphy** were added.
- **Form.** A fusion of **longitudinal basilica and centralized building**. The **32-m main dome** rides on **pendentives**, anchored by two half-domes, supported by massive piers; windows at its base produce an effect of levitation. Derivative **Ionic** capitals cut with lace-like acanthus; four slim pencil **minarets** added under Ottoman rule.

**Vocabulary:** cathedral · apse · basilica · squinch · pendentive · dome · cornice · nave · capital · spandrel · entablature · minaret · Mecca · mihrab · calligraphy
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Parthenon / Acropolis (Classical Greek, c. 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, large-scale monument, repurposed building

> change in purpose, religious imagery/content on exterior and interior

---

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Large-scale monument, repurposed building

> architectural elements (ex. columns, dome)

---

#### → [#56](#56--great-mosque-of-córdoba) — Great Mosque of Córdoba (Umayyad, c. 785–786 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, large-scale monument, repurposed building

> change in design that accompanied change in purpose, religious imagery/content

---

## #54 — Virgin (Theotokos) and Child between Saints Theodore and George

**Early Byzantine Europe** · Sixth or early seventh century C.E. · Encaustic on wood · Monastery of Saint Catherine, Mount Sinai, Egypt · 69 × 48 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Hagia_Sophia_Constantinople_Theotokos_02.jpg/250px-Hagia_Sophia_Constantinople_Theotokos_02.jpg" width="240" alt="Virgin (Theotokos) and Child between Saints Theodore and George">
### Summary

One of the **oldest surviving Christian icons**, preserved at St. Catherine's Monastery on **Mount Sinai** — the Virgin flanked by warrior saints under the descending hand of God.

- **Historical Context.** Made in the early Byzantine Empire, whose official religion was **Eastern Orthodox Christianity**. Survived the **Iconoclasm** controversy (c. 726–843) because Sinai lay outside Byzantine political control. The image helped fuel the debate about whether sacred images were legitimate intermediaries or forbidden idols.
- **Function.** An **icon**, hung on the **iconostasis** or handled during ritual. Icons were repainted from frequent touching; they were not worshipped but venerated as conduits for prayer to the holy figures they depicted.
- **Content.** **Theotokos** ("God-bearer," the Virgin Mary) enthroned, holding the infant **Christ**; flanked by military saints **Theodore** (left) and **George** (right); behind, two **angels** look up toward heaven as the **hand of God** descends between them.
- **Form.** **Encaustic** (pigments suspended in hot wax) on wood. Large **gold halos** flatten and sanctify the figures; Mary's blue drapery models her volume while the saints stare frontally at the viewer. Classical Hellenistic shading survives alongside the flatter, more frontal conventions that would become Byzantine orthodoxy.

**Vocabulary:** encaustic · icons / iconoclasm · iconostasis
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#51](#51--san-vitale) — San Vitale (Justinian and Theodora panels) (Early Byzantine, c. 526–547 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, historical and political themes

> The panels of Justinian and Theodora display use of similar styles as the Virgin and Child between Saints despite using different media; the halos in the panels act as both religious and political messages and the composition of the frontal figures and lack of realistic scenery as background remove the panels from reality, conveying a message of permanence.

---

#### → [#62](#62--röttgen-pietà) — Röttgen Pietà (Late medieval Europe, c. 1300–1325 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious and Gothic heightened emotion

> The raw emotion conveyed on the Virgin Mary's face as she holds the dead body of Christ shows a marked transition in religious art from the detached, almost regal depiction of religious figures from Early Byzantine art to Gothic art.

---

#### → [#71](#71--madonna-and-child-with-two-angels) — Madonna and Child with Two Angels (Fra Filippo Lippi, c. 1465 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious and increasing realism

> Also depicting a Virgin Mary and infant Jesus, this early Renaissance piece is emblematic of the changes that would occur in the representation of religious figures by grounding them in reality and minimizing the halos, as compared with the Virgin and Child between Saints.

---

## #55 — Lindisfarne Gospels: St. Matthew, cross-carpet page; St. Luke portrait page; St. Luke incipit page

**Early medieval (Hiberno-Saxon / Insular) Europe; attributed to Bishop Eadfrith** · c. 700 C.E. · Illuminated manuscript (ink, pigments, and gold on vellum) · Lindisfarne Priory, Northumbria, England · 365 × 275 mm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/St._Matthew_-_Lindisfarne_Gospels_%28710-721%29%2C_f.25v_-_BL_Cotton_MS_Nero_D_IV.jpg/250px-St._Matthew_-_Lindisfarne_Gospels_%28710-721%29%2C_f.25v_-_BL_Cotton_MS_Nero_D_IV.jpg" width="240" alt="Lindisfarne Gospels: St. Matthew, cross-carpet page; St. Luke portrait page; St. Luke incipit page">
### Summary

A lavishly illuminated gospel book made at the island monastery of **Lindisfarne** — a synthesis of **Irish (Celtic), Anglo-Saxon, and Mediterranean** artistic traditions in the **Hiberno-Saxon** (Insular) style.

- **Historical Context.** Produced at **Lindisfarne Priory** in Northumbria, traditionally attributed to **Bishop Eadfrith**. Honors **Saint Cuthbert**, whose body was buried there and whose cult drew pilgrims. The book fuses **Italian** (Mediterranean) classical painting traditions with **Celtic interlace** and **Anglo-Saxon animal-style** metalwork ornament.
- **Function.** A **deluxe gospel book** for the altar, used in Christian liturgy and as an object of veneration. Originally had a jeweled **cumdach** (book-shrine) cover added in the 8th century; both outer covers are now lost.
- **Content.** Contains the gospels of **Matthew, Mark, Luke, and John** with canon tables, evangelist portraits, "cross-carpet" pages (full-page crosses), and elaborate **incipit** (opening-letter) pages.
- **Form.** **Illuminated manuscript**: ink, pigments, and gold on **vellum**. Dense **interlace**, zoomorphic knots, and spiral ornament derived from metalwork; Evangelist portraits in a flatter Byzantine-influenced figure style. Integration of figure, pattern, and script unique to Insular art.

**Vocabulary:** illuminated manuscript · insular · Hiberno-Saxon
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#64](#64--golden-haggadah-the-plagues-of-egypt-scenes-of-liberation-and-preparation-for-passover) — Golden Haggadah (Late medieval Spain, c. 1320 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both represent Religious manuscripts

#### → [#50](#50--rebecca-and-eliezer-at-the-well-and-jacob-wrestling-the-angel-from-the-vienna-genesis) — Vienna Genesis (Early Byzantine, early 6th c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both contained stories of the bible including the biographies of the life of Jesus

---

#### → [#53](#53--merovingian-looped-fibulae) — Merovingian looped fibulae (Early medieval Europe, mid-6th c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical, Highly decorative

> Both were highly decorative pieces, with the Lindisfarne Gospel being covered in jewels and diamonds originally

## #56 — Great Mosque of Córdoba

**Umayyad (Islamic Iberia)** · c. 785–786 C.E. · Stone masonry · Córdoba, Spain · 590 × 425 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Great_Mosque_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_%28Spain%29.jpg/330px-Great_Mosque_of_C%C3%B3rdoba_%28Spain%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Mosque of Córdoba">
### Summary

A **hypostyle** mosque of seemingly endless **red-and-white double arches** — built by the exiled Umayyad prince **Abd al-Rahman I** in Iberia, later partly converted into a Christian cathedral.

- **Historical Context.** After the **Abbasids** overthrew the Umayyad dynasty in Damascus (750 C.E.), **Abd al-Rahman I** fled to Spain and established a new Umayyad capital at **Córdoba**, then one of Europe's wealthiest and most learned cities. The mosque's original core was expanded several times; in the 13th century the **Reconquista** reclaimed Córdoba and a Christian cathedral was later built *inside* the mosque.
- **Function.** A **Friday mosque** (jami) and political-symbolic heart of Umayyad al-Andalus. After 1236, repurposed as a Catholic cathedral without demolishing the Islamic fabric.
- **Content.** A vast **hypostyle prayer hall** with **856 reused Roman and Visigothic columns** supporting distinctive **horseshoe arches** in red-brick and white-stone voussoirs. An ornate **mihrab** niche faces Mecca (oriented south — unusual, a local adaptation). Outside: orange-tree courtyard, minaret.
- **Form.** Stone masonry. **Two-tiered horseshoe arches** (lower and upper) multiply the visual height of the reused Roman columns and produce a forest-like interior with no fixed focal point. The mihrab dome is decorated with intricate **ribbed vaulting** and gold mosaic calligraphy.

**Vocabulary:** hypostyle hall · mosque · mihrab

---

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg/330px-Taj_Mahal_%28Edited%29.jpeg" width="180" alt="Taj Mahal">

**Theme:** Islamic Architecture

> Both incorporate examples of Islamic architecture

## #58 — Church of Sainte-Foy

**Romanesque Europe** · Church c. 1050–1130 C.E.; Reliquary of Saint Foy 9th century C.E. with later additions · Stone (architecture); stone and paint (tympanum); gold, silver, gemstones, and enamel over wood (reliquary) · Conques, France · Church ~1550 m; reliquary 33.5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Church_Sainte-Foy_Conques.jpg/330px-Church_Sainte-Foy_Conques.jpg" width="240" alt="Church of Sainte-Foy">
### Summary

A **Romanesque pilgrimage church** at Conques on the route to **Santiago de Compostela** — its **Last Judgment tympanum** warns pilgrims of damnation, and its reliquary of the child-martyr **Sainte-Foy** is encrusted with centuries of accumulated jewels.

- **Historical Context.** **Saint Foy** was a 12-year-old Christian girl martyred in 2nd-century Gaul for refusing to abjure her faith. The abbey at Conques was a stop on the **pilgrimage route to Santiago de Compostela**; pilgrim devotion funded the church. The bejeweled reliquary drew debate — **Bernard of Angers** worried that it blurred the line between veneration and idolatry.
- **Function.** A **pilgrimage church** and monastic abbey. Its cruciform plan with wide aisles and an ambulatory allowed pilgrim circulation around the relics without disturbing monastic worship.
- **Content.** The **west tympanum** depicts the **Last Judgment**: enthroned Christ points one hand to the saved (heaven, ordered) and the other to the damned (hell, chaotic), with the weighing of souls below. The reliquary statue of Sainte-Foy holds martyrdom attributes and is covered in **jewels and ancient cameos** donated by pilgrims over centuries.
- **Form.** **Romanesque** barrel-vaulted nave with arcades and stone columns; **cruciform plan** (symbol of Christ's cross). The tympanum is painted stone **relief**. The reliquary is carved wood sheathed in gold, silver, and gems — a reuse and aggregation across eras.

**Vocabulary:** pilgrim · Romanesque · abbey · tympanum · reliquary
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#49](#49--santa-sabina) — Santa Sabina (Late Antique Europe, c. 422–432 C.E.)

> decorated door/ tympanum

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Standing_male_worshiper_MET_DT850.jpg/250px-Standing_male_worshiper_MET_DT850.jpg" width="180" alt="Votive Figures from Eshnunna">

**Theme:** Religious

> praying, looking upwards

## #59 — Bayeux Tapestry

**Romanesque Europe (English or Norman)** · c. 1066–1080 C.E. · Embroidery on linen · Bayeux, France · ~20 in × 230 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/43/Bayeux_Tapestry_-_Scene_1_Restorers.jpg/330px-Bayeux_Tapestry_-_Scene_1_Restorers.jpg" width="240" alt="Bayeux Tapestry">
### Summary

A 230-ft embroidered **continuous narrative** of the **1066 Norman Conquest of England** — ending with **William's victory over Harold** at the Battle of Hastings — probably made in Canterbury and likely commissioned by William's half-brother, **Bishop Odo**.

- **Historical Context.** Commemorates the **Battle of Hastings (1066)**, where **William of Normandy** defeated **King Harold, Earl of Wessex** for the English throne. High-quality Anglo-Saxon needlework points to a **Canterbury** workshop. Patron is likely **Odo, Bishop of Bayeux** (William's half-brother), who appears prominently.
- **Function.** A **political monument in textile form**. Probably displayed at first in Odo's hall (its scale and secular content suggest a grand secular hanging), later in Bayeux Cathedral. Tells the Norman version of events, justifying William's claim.
- **Content.** **75 scenes** with Latin **tituli** narrating events from King Edward the Confessor's death to Harold's accession, William's invasion fleet and feast, the battle itself, Harold's death (famously, "hit in the eye" by an arrow), and Norman victory. The end (likely William's coronation) is now lost. Upper and lower borders carry animals, **Aesop's Fables**, and scenes of hunting and husbandry.
- **Form.** **Embroidered wool** on linen — a true tapestry is woven, so this is technically an embroidery. **Ten shades** of wool yarn dyed with madder (reds), dyer's rocket (yellow), and woad (blues/greens). Figures outlined in black, flat colors, blank backgrounds; the middle zone carries the main story with upper/lower borders as commentary.
- **Vocabulary:** tapestry · tituli · textile · continuous narrative

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#45](#45--forum-of-trajan) — Column of Trajan (Imperial Roman, 113 C.E.)

**Theme:** Monumental decoration

> Both Trajan's Column and the Bayeux Tapestry honor a ruler's military victory.

---

#### → [#40](#40--alexander-mosaic-from-the-house-of-the-faun-pompeii) — Alexander Mosaic (Republican Roman, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Military victory

> The mosaic also depicts a battle between two rulers, and one being defeated.

---

#### → [#47](#47--ludovisi-battle-sarcophagus) — Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus (Late Imperial Roman, c. 250 C.E.)

**Theme:** War, military

> The Bayeux Tapestry and the Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus both show soldiers fighting and the armor they wore at the time.

---

## #60 — Chartres Cathedral

**Gothic Europe** · Original c. 1145–1155 C.E.; reconstructed c. 1194–1220 C.E. · Limestone, stained glass · Chartres, France · 430 × 105 ft; nave 121 ft high

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Chartres_Cathedral.jpg/330px-Chartres_Cathedral.jpg" width="240" alt="Chartres Cathedral">
### Summary

A High **Gothic** cathedral whose **flying buttresses** liberated the walls for **vast stained-glass** windows, made the Virgin Mary its patron, and drew pilgrims to the relic of her **Sancta Camisa**.

- **Historical Context.** Located ~95 km SW of Paris. Begun in the Romanesque style (1145–1155), largely destroyed by fire in 1194, and rebuilt in the **High Gothic** style (1194–1220). The asymmetric west towers preserve this history — Romanesque spire on the right, Gothic on the left. The relic of Mary's **Sancta Camisa** (tunic she allegedly wore at Christ's birth) made Chartres a major pilgrimage site.
- **Function.** Bishop's seat and **pilgrimage cathedral**, a house for relics, and a classroom of stone and glass for the illiterate faithful.
- **Content.** Three major sculpted **portal tympana** on the west, south, and north façades with accompanying jamb statues (Royal Portal on the west is an icon of High Romanesque-into-Gothic carving). Three large **rose windows** depict the Last Judgment (west), Christ (south), and the Virgin and Child (north); the interior holds ~150 medieval **stained glass** windows including the famous "Chartres blue."
- **Form.** **Gothic limestone** with **ribbed groin vaults**, **pointed arches**, and a **seven-bay nave** fronted by a **narthex**. External **flying buttresses** transfer the vault's thrust outward, freeing the walls for vast window openings. Stained glass floods the interior with jewel-toned light.

**Vocabulary:** Romanesque · Gothic · stained glass · rose window · clerestory · flying buttress · narthex · nave

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#49](#49--santa-sabina) — Santa Sabina (Late Antique Europe, c. 422–432 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Function, cruciform floor plan

#### → [#65](#65--alhambra) — Alhambra (Nasrid Dynasty, 1354–1391 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Function

## #61 — Dedication Page with Blanche of Castile and King Louis IX of France, Scenes from the Apocalypse from Bibles moralisées

**Gothic Europe** · c. 1225–1245 C.E. · Illuminated manuscript (ink, tempera, and gold leaf on vellum) · Morgan Library, New York · 14¾ × 10¼ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Blanche_of_Castile_and_King_Louis_IX_of_France.jpg/330px-Blanche_of_Castile_and_King_Louis_IX_of_France.jpg" width="240" alt="Dedication Page with Blanche of Castile and King Louis IX of France, Scenes from the Apocalypse from Bibles moralisées">
### Summary

A **Moralized Bible** (Bible moralisée) dedication page depicting the French **queen regent Blanche of Castile** beside her son **King Louis IX** on gold-backed thrones, with a cleric instructing a scribe below — a luxury personal Bible for a Christian king.

- **Historical Context.** When the French king died in 1226, his widow **Blanche of Castile** ruled as regent until her son **Louis IX** came of age. She defeated rebellious barons and handed her son a peaceful kingdom. Made c. 1225–1245 C.E. (likely taking eight years), the book was a lavish royal commission.
- **Function.** **Personal devotional book** for Louis IX — a volume of scriptural morals and lessons to guide his reign. The Apocalypse scenes offered meditative material on John's Revelation, reinforcing Christian kingship.
- **Content.** Dedication page: Blanche and Louis IX enthroned under trilobed arches on a gold background — a visual parallel to **Virgin and Christ enthroned in heaven**, sanctioning the king's divine authority. Below, a cleric instructs a scribe in the production of the book. The Apocalypse scenes narrate John's Vision in eight medallions with Latin text.
- **Form.** **Ink, tempera, and gold leaf on vellum**. Gold backgrounds emphasize royalty; **royal blue** connects the page to French monarchy. Gothic proportions — small heads, attenuated figures; little perspective. Arches overhead evoke cathedral architecture.

**Vocabulary:** Moralized Bible · Apocalypse · Andachtsbild

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#170](#170--sika-dwa-kofi-golden-stool) — Sika dwa kofi (Golden Stool) (Ashanti, c. 1700 C.E.)

**Theme:** Ceremonial

> The Golden Stool is used for ceremonial purposes and the Blanche of Castile and Louis XI depict the ceremony, the crowning of Louis XI as king.

---

#### → [#210](#210--red-and-white-plum-blossoms) — Red and White Plum Blossoms (Ogata Kōrin, c. 1710 C.E.)

**Theme:** Colors

> The White and Red Plum uses a variety of colors: green, red, yellow in order to bring the idea of spring to life. The Scenes of the Apocalypse also uses very bold colors in order differentiate people of different races and to add charisma in the characters.

---

#### → [#196](#196--gold-and-jade-crown) — Gold and Jade Crown (Silla, 5th–6th century C.E.)

**Theme:** Gold as a medium

> The Gold and Jade Crown uses an extensive amount of gold creatively and so does the manuscript of the crowning of Louis XI

## #62 — Röttgen Pietà

**Late medieval Europe (German Gothic)** · c. 1300–1325 C.E. · Painted wood · Rheinisches Landesmuseum, Bonn, Germany · 34½ in high

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/Piet%C3%A0_Roettgen.jpg/250px-Piet%C3%A0_Roettgen.jpg" width="240" alt="Röttgen Pietà">
### Summary

A **German Gothic Andachtsbild** — a painted wooden **Pietà** deliberately disturbing in its grief, showing an emaciated, wound-torn Christ in a sorrowing Mary's lap — a devotional image for private prayer.

- **Historical Context.** 13th- and 14th-century Christianity, shaped by **Saint Francis of Assisi**'s emphasis on Christ's human suffering, embraced **mysticism** — direct personal encounter with divine pain. Made during an age of plague and hardship, the Pietà images the Late-Gothic **Christus patiens** ("suffering Christ") type, replacing the earlier **Christus triumphans** (triumphant Christ).
- **Function.** An **Andachtsbild** — devotional image for personal meditation. Its deliberately grotesque pathos invited the worshipper to identify with both **Mary's grief** and **Christ's suffering**, drawing closer to God through shared human pain.
- **Content.** Mary holds the lifeless body of Christ across her lap. His body is shockingly **emaciated**; wounds pour thick clots of blood; his head lolls on a spindly neck, disproportionately large. Mary's face is a mask of anguish, not serenity.
- **Form.** **Painted wood** at about 35 in — portable enough for private prayer. Rustic carved surface (not polished like marble), making the horror feel more immediate. The usual triangular Pietà composition is broken by Christ's dangling head. Red blood clots form a disturbing rhythmic **pattern**.

**Vocabulary:** Christus triumphans · Christus patiens · mysticism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

**Theme:** Religious, Depiction of Christ

> Made to emphasize the suffering of Christ and his mother's angst.

#### → [#95](#95--the-virgin-of-guadalupe-virgen-de-guadalupe) — The Virgin of Guadalupe (Miguel González, c. 1698 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, Symbol of Identity, Mysticism

> Encourages a personal connection with a deity.

#### → [#119](#119--the-burghers-of-calais) — The Burghers of Calais (Auguste Rodin, 1884–1895 C.E.)

**Theme:** Sadness, Tragedy, Sculpture conveying emotion

> This piece encourages the viewer to experience the pain of the tragedy with the realism of the sculptures.
