## #63 — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel, including Lamentation

**Giotto di Bondone (artist); unknown architect** · Chapel c. 1303 C.E.; fresco c. 1305 · Brick (architecture) and fresco · Padua, Italy · *Lamentation* fresco 7 ft 7 in × 7 ft 9 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3a/Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-36-_-_Lamentation_%28The_Mourning_of_Christ%29_adj.jpg/330px-Giotto_-_Scrovegni_-_-36-_-_Lamentation_%28The_Mourning_of_Christ%29_adj.jpg" width="240" alt="Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel, including Lamentation">
### Summary

A small **Proto-Renaissance** chapel in Padua covered in **Giotto's** fresco cycle — a turning point in Western painting where **weight, space, and human emotion** enter medieval religious art.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Enrico Scrovegni**, a wealthy Paduan banker, partly as atonement for his father's (and his own) sin of **usury** — charging interest was considered sinful. Built on the site of an ancient Roman arena. Marks the **Late Gothic / Proto-Renaissance** moment around 1300, when humanist thought and naturalistic depiction began to reshape religious art.
- **Function.** A private **family chapel** for the Scrovegni, dedicated to the **Virgin of Charity**. The fresco cycle served both as personal atonement and as instruction for illiterate worshippers ("picture-Bible" function).
- **Content.** A complete cycle of **38 scenes** from the lives of Mary and Christ arranged in three tiers; the **Last Judgment** covers the west wall and the **Annunciation** frames the chancel. In the *Lamentation* scene (image set focus), mourners grieve over Christ's body — expressions range from stoic to convulsed; angels wheel in the sky; a barren rocky diagonal leads the eye to the heads of Christ and Mary.
- **Form.** **Buon fresco** on plaster over brick. **Modeled volumes** (bodies feel heavy; drapery falls over real limbs); sparse but believable architectural space; attention to **individualized emotion**. Halos preserve divine status in otherwise humanized figures.

**Vocabulary:** fresco · Late Gothic · Proto-Renaissance
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#49](#49--santa-sabina) — Santa Sabina (Late Antique Europe, c. 422–432 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, worship

> Similar simple style, place of worship, church

---

#### → [#75](#75--sistine-chapel-ceiling-and-altar-wall-frescoes) — Sistine Chapel Ceiling and altar wall frescoes (Michelangelo, c. 1508–1541 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, fresco wall paintings

> Fresco like paintings on the walls, edging toward realism

---

#### → [#78](#78--entombment-of-christ) — Entombment of Christ (Jacopo Pontormo, 1525–1528 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, story of Jesus's life

> Image set focus on Lamentation, realism or borderline realism

---

## #65 — Alhambra

**Nasrid Dynasty** · 1354–1391 C.E. · Whitewashed adobe stucco, wood, tile, paint, and gilding · Granada, Spain · 26 acres

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Alhambra_Granada.jpg/330px-Alhambra_Granada.jpg" width="240" alt="Alhambra">
### Summary

The fortified palace-city of the **Nasrid** sultans — the **last Muslim rulers** of Iberia — where carved stucco, tilework, poetry, fountains, and gardens create a paradisiacal retreat inside austere outer walls.

- **Historical Context.** Built by the **Nasrid dynasty** (1232–1492), Islam's last toehold in Spain, after the Christian **Reconquista** had taken most of the peninsula. Founded by **Muhammad I**; the most celebrated palace interiors are the work of **Yusuf I** (Comares Palace) and **Muhammad V** (Palace of the Lions). Conquered by the Catholic Monarchs (Ferdinand and Isabella) in 1492; served as a Christian royal residence and later fell into neglect until 19th-century restoration.
- **Function.** A **fortified palace-city** — seat of Nasrid government and royal residence. Contained within the walls: three palaces, an alcazaba (citadel), mosque, baths, workshops, and a town.
- **Content.** The **Court of the Lions** (12 marble lions around a fountain), the **Hall of the Two Sisters** (muqarnas vault of some 5,000 cells), the **Comares Palace** (Throne room), the **Generalife** garden villa, and the **Partal Palace**. Arabic **poetry by Ibn Zamrak** carved at eye level "voices" the palace's parts.
- **Form.** **Whitewashed adobe stucco** over brick; **tile** dadoes in intricate geometric patterns; **muqarnas** (stalactite) vaulting; courtyard fountains and reflecting pools. **Horseshoe and lobed arches**; a plain exterior concealing elaborate interiors — a legacy of Umayyad taste. Water throughout reflects the Qur'anic vision of paradise as a "garden watered by delightful streams."

**Vocabulary:** medieval palaces · Palacio de los Leones · Generalife · Salón de Comares · Islamic architecture · Moorish citadel · Cuarto Dorado · Hall of Two Sisters (muqarnas)

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Imperial Roman, c. 62–79 C.E.)

**Theme:** Luxury mansions

> Cross-cultural; representative of the luxury arts of antiquity and the Islamic Middle Ages

---

#### → [#190](#190--the-court-of-gayumars-folio-from-shah-tahmasp's-*shahnama*) — The Court of Gayumars (Sultan Muhammad, c. 1522–1525 C.E.)

**Theme:** Illustrative of the luxury arts of the Islamic world

> illuminated manuscript of the Shahnama (Book of Kings)—an epic poem describing the history of kingship in Persia; representative of the luxury arts of the Islamic Middle Ages

---

#### → [#57](#57--pyxis-of-al-mughira) — Pyxis of al-Mughira (Umayyad, c. 968 C.E.)

**Theme:** Gift / princely object

> use of calligraphy as a symbolic and decorative element in both works

## #67 — Pazzi Chapel, Basilica di Santa Croce

**Filippo Brunelleschi (architect)** · c. 1429–1461 C.E. · Masonry · Florence, Italy · 36 × 60 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Pazzi_Chapel_Florence.jpg/330px-Pazzi_Chapel_Florence.jpg" width="240" alt="Pazzi Chapel, Basilica di Santa Croce">
### Summary

A small Early Renaissance **chapter house** attached to **Santa Croce** — **Brunelleschi's** geometric essay in white stucco and **pietra serena**, a calm rebuttal to Gothic ornament.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by the **Pazzi family** (rival of the **Medici**) as a chapter house for the Santa Croce friars, with a family **tomb** below. Brunelleschi planned it but did not live to see it completed; construction continued through the 1460s. Part of the same early-Renaissance moment as Brunelleschi's dome on Florence Cathedral.
- **Function.** A **chapter house** (meeting place for monks) and Pazzi family burial chapel.
- **Content.** Entrance portico with barrel-vaulted ceiling; interior square centered under a small ribbed dome on pendentives; **glazed terracotta tondi** by **Luca della Robbia** depicting the Apostles in the blue-and-white vault and the Evangelists in the pendentives. **Pazzi coat of arms** and heraldic elements decorate the interior.
- **Form.** **Pietra serena** (greenish-grey stone) pilasters and architraves articulate white stucco walls — a linear grammar of **geometry**, symmetry, and clear proportion. The plan (short rectangle subdividing into three bays with a central domed square) echoes ancient central-plan architecture. Intended harmony over ornament.

**Vocabulary:** cloister · barrel vault · portico · pendentives
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1

---

**Theme:** Religious architecture

> Renaissance chapels

#### → [#84](#84--mosque-of-selim-ii) — Mosque of Selim II (Sinan, 1568–1575 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious architecture

> Place for religious community

#### → [#186](#186--great-mosque-masjid-e-jameh-isfahan) — Great Mosque of Isfahan (Seljuk / Safavid, c. 700–1700 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious architecture

> Place of communal meeting as well serving a religious purpose

## #68 — The Arnolfini Portrait

**Jan van Eyck** · 1434 C.E. · Oil on oak panel · National Gallery, London · 32.4 × 23.6 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/The_Arnolfini_Portrait.jpg/330px-The_Arnolfini_Portrait.jpg" width="240" alt="The Arnolfini Portrait">
### Summary

A small oil-on-panel double portrait by **Jan van Eyck** — probably the Italian merchant **Giovanni di Nicolao Arnolfini** and his wife in their Bruges bedroom — dense with symbolism, hyper-detail, and debated narrative (marriage? document? memorial?).

- **Historical Context.** Made in 1434 by van Eyck, working in **Bruges** at the height of **Early Netherlandish / Northern Renaissance** painting. The north pioneered **oil painting**, which enabled unmatched detail, glazing, and luminosity. Unlike Italian Renaissance emphasis on perspective and anatomy, the Northern Renaissance excels at minute description and loaded symbolism.
- **Function.** Debated. A common theory: van Eyck's signature, "Johannes de eyck fuit hic 1434" ("Jan van Eyck was here, 1434"), above a convex mirror reflecting two figures (witnesses), casts him as witness to a **wedding or betrothal contract**. Alternative readings treat it as a memorial portrait or ostentation of wealth.
- **Content.** An affluent couple holds hands in a richly appointed bedroom: he in dark purple robe, she in green gown gathered over her abdomen (likely fashion, not pregnancy). A single lit **candle** (God's presence), a **dog** (fidelity), **shoes removed** (sacred ground), **oranges** (wealth/fertility), a **mirror** ringed with **Passion of Christ** scenes, a **Saint Margaret** figurine on the bedpost (patron of childbirth).
- **Form.** **Oil on oak panel** built up in glazes, allowing extraordinary **photographic detail** — hairs on the dog, beads of the chandelier, woven carpet patterns. Lighting enters from the left window, grounding the space; the **convex mirror** expands the scene into the viewer's space.

**Vocabulary:** tempera · symbolism · double portrait · chiaroscuro
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.4, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#180](#180--veranda-post-of-enthroned-king-and-senior-wife-*opo-ogoga*) — Veranda Post (Olowe of Ise, Yoruba, c. 1910–1914 C.E.)

**Theme:** Depiction of couple, genre art, royal members

> Both works depict couples in a secular setting, with the Arnolfini Portrait depicting the lower class. However the purposes of the works differ greatly, with the Veranda Post justifying a king's power and the portrait serves as a marriage contract.

---

#### → [#18](#18--king-menkaura-and-queen) — King Menkaura and Queen (Old Kingdom Egypt, c. 2490–2472 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a8/Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Menkaura_and_Queen_Khamerernebty_II._in_Boston_1.jpg" width="180" alt="King Menkaura and Queen">

**Theme:** Depiction of couple, belief in afterlife, royal members

> Though Menkaura and his Queen also depict a couple, it serves as an afterlife spiritual refuge for their souls, in accordance to the Egyptian beliefs.

---

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565 C.E.)

**Theme:** Northern Renaissance

> The same focus on details on be seen in both works. In the Hunters in the Snow, each of the figures in the distant ice field is depicted to have different poses and the moods of the larger figures in the foreground are subtle yet obvious if one is to observe closely.

## #69 — David

**Donatello** · c. 1440–1460 C.E. · Bronze · Museo Nazionale del Bargello, Florence · 5.5 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/David_Michelangelo.jpg/250px-David_Michelangelo.jpg" width="240" alt="David">
### Summary

**Donatello's** bronze **David** — the first free-standing **nude** since antiquity — a strikingly sensual, almost feminine youth who plants his foot on the severed head of Goliath.

- **Historical Context.** Made in the early Italian Renaissance by **Donatello** (Donato di Niccolò, 1386–1466), a Florentine master of both marble and bronze and the protégé of **Cosimo de' Medici**, who probably commissioned the work. The return of **heroic nudity** was a conscious revival of Greek and Roman traditions; medieval nudity had been confined to moral subjects (Adam and Eve; the damned).
- **Function.** Most likely commissioned by **Cosimo de' Medici** for the central courtyard of the Palazzo Medici. Read variously as: civic symbol (**Florence as David** triumphing over great powers), humanist celebration of antique nudity, Medici family allegory, or a homoerotic personal statement.
- **Content.** The biblical David after his victory over **Goliath** — his slingshot in one hand, Goliath's sword in the other, Goliath's severed helmet-crowned head beneath his feet. He wears only a **shepherd's hat** crowned with laurel (victory) and ornate **leather boots**. A feather from Goliath's helmet trails up David's inner thigh.
- **Form.** **Lost-wax cast bronze**, free-standing in the round (radical at the time). Soft **contrapposto**, polished skin set against rougher boots and curled locks. The **graceful, almost feminine** body contrasts with the brutality of the severed head — a studied ambiguity that contributes to the work's lasting fascination.

**Vocabulary:** bronze · shepherd · nudity · victorious · youthful · femininity · frailty · sensuality
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.1, 3.2, 3.3, 3.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

**Theme:** Medium/balance

> There is an original version of Doryphoros made out of bronze just like how there are bronze and marble statue of David by Donatello.

#### → [#48](#48--catacomb-of-priscilla) — Catacomb of Priscilla (Late Antique Europe, c. 200–400 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Good shepherd – both David and Jesus were shepherds

**Theme:** Religion

> Bible characters (old testament)

**Theme:** Religion

> Biblical scenes (old testament)

## #71 — Madonna and Child with Two Angels

**Fra Filippo Lippi** · c. 1465 C.E. · Tempera on wood · Uffizi, Florence · 635 × 950 mm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f2/Madonna_and_Child_and_Two_Angels_%28Botticelli%29.jpg/330px-Madonna_and_Child_and_Two_Angels_%28Botticelli%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Madonna and Child with Two Angels">
### Summary

A landmark of early Italian Renaissance **humanization** of sacred figures.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Florence by the Carmelite friar Fra Filippo Lippi, a leading figure of the early Italian Renaissance influenced by **Masaccio**. By the mid-15th century artists were recognized as intellectuals, and Madonna-and-Child scenes were a standard commission; the Medici family patronized Lippi extensively.
- **Function.** A private devotional panel, likely a Medici-circle commission; the specific patron is not documented. The intimate scale suits domestic veneration rather than altar display.
- **Content.** The youthful Mary is shown in profile at prayer; the Christ Child is held forward by two smiling, child-like angels. One of Lippi's own lovers reportedly modeled for Mary. The halos are thin, almost transparent; pearls signal the Immaculate Conception; a distant seashore and city frame a symbolic "Heavenly Jerusalem."
- **Form.** A Florentine interior frames an atmospheric landscape with rocks, sea, and a distant city. Trompe-l'oeil architectural frame, naturalized anatomy, asymmetrical composition, and diminished halos emphasize the **humanity** of holy figures — a hallmark of the Italian Renaissance going back to Giotto.

**Vocabulary:** tempera · Italian Renaissance · Madonna and Child · naturalism · humanization · atmospheric perspective · trompe l'oeil · Florentine
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.3, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#72](#72--birth-of-venus) — Birth of Venus (Sandro Botticelli, c. 1484–1486)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/500px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" width="180" alt="Birth of Venus">

**Theme:** Renaissance, religious figures

> Tempera as medium, commissioned works

---

#### → [#85](#85--calling-of-saint-matthew) — Calling of Saint Matthew (Caravaggio, c. 1597–1601)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg/500px-The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Calling of Saint Matthew">

**Theme:** Religious figures

> Humanization of religious figures – Biblical theme

#### → [#66](#66--annunciation-triptych-merode-altarpiece) — Annunciation Triptych (Merode Altarpiece) (Workshop of Robert Campin, c. 1427–1432)

**Theme:** Mary, Renaissance

> Florentine setting, symbolism, humanization

## #72 — Birth of Venus

**Sandro Botticelli** · c. 1484–1486 C.E. · Tempera on canvas · Uffizi, Florence · 172.5 × 278.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/500px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" width="240" alt="Birth of Venus">

### Summary

The **earliest surviving large-scale painting of a nude mythological female** since antiquity.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in **Florence** during the early Renaissance, almost certainly commissioned by a member of the **Medici** family (likely Lorenzo di Pierfrancesco). Botticelli drew on Homer's Homeric Hymn describing Venus born of sea foam, on Poliziano's poetry, and on the ancient **Aphrodite of Knidos** (modest pose). Canvas was increasingly favored over panel in the humid conditions of Medici country villas.
- **Function.** Likely a decorative piece for the Medici's **Villa di Castello**, perhaps a wedding gift; Venus signaled love, fertility, and civic virtue.
- **Content.** Venus stands nude on a shell, drifting to shore; at left, the wind gods Zephyr and Chloris/Aura blow her in; at right, a nymph (Hora of Spring) waits with a flower-strewn cloak. Her pose echoes the *Venus Pudica* gesture.
- **Form.** Tempera gives the flesh a translucent glow. The composition is strikingly **flat**: gold-outlined hair, stylized V-shaped waves, figures on a single plane, and heavy linear patterning take precedence over depth. Contrapposto stance; idealized, elongated body.

**Vocabulary:** tempera · Renaissance · nymph · contrapposto · Venus Pudica
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#80](#80--venus-of-urbino) — Venus of Urbino (Titian, c. 1538)

**Theme:** Marital duties, including motherhood

> Both paintings depict Venus at its center as a symbol of love, and in the case of the Venus of Urbino, sensuality. The beauty of Venus is evident in both paintings, and is used to convey messages, albeit of differing themes. The paintings also fulfilled more private uses, rather than for public display and grandeur, and were likely hung in the homes of the patrons.

---

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Spear Bearer) (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** To display a canon of proportions of idealized form, beauty, and athleticism.

> Both pieces of art are full-length nudes, although the spear-bearer is a male. Additionally, Doryphoros represents an idealized male and human form of the time, just as Venus in the Birth of Venus is idealized, her face free of any blemishes and her golden hair long and flowing. The Doryphoros also displays a contrapposto stance, one that Venus also adopts.

---

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis (Iktinos and Kallikrates, 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** East Pediment depicts the birth of Athena

> Both the pediment and the painting depict the birth of goddesses and their origins, and each goddess is welcomed by those surrounding them. The statues of the East Pediment are also in one plane and display an essentially flat scene, although the statue itself is three dimensional, much like the Birth of Venus.

---

## #74 — Adam and Eve

**Albrecht Dürer** · 1504 C.E. · Engraving · Metropolitan Museum of Art, New York · 9.75 × 7.56 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29.jpg/330px-Albrecht_D%C3%BCrer_-_Adam_and_Eve_%28Prado%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Adam and Eve">
### Summary

Dürer's **printed manifesto** on ideal human proportion, fusing Northern engraving with Italian classicism.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in Nuremberg by the leading Northern Renaissance printmaker. Dürer was obsessed with **classical canon** and bodily proportion, and had traveled to Italy to study Greco-Roman sculpture. The work predates the Fall of Man; the balance of the Four Humors (melancholic/phlegmatic/sanguine/choleric) is still intact.
- **Function.** An engraving meant for wide distribution, doubling as a theological meditation and as a display of Dürer's learning. It was the only work on which he inscribed his **full name and date** on a placard.
- **Content.** Adam holds a branch of the Tree of Life; Eve holds a branch of the Tree of Knowledge, with the **snake** offering an apple. Four animals — **elk (melancholic), ox (phlegmatic), rabbit (sanguine), cat (choleric)** — embody the humors; the cat-and-mouse pairing foreshadows the predatory tension introduced by the Fall. A parrot may represent Ave/Eve wordplay.
- **Form.** Deeply incised copper-plate lines yield rich tonal range and crisp detail. Both figures stand in **contrapposto** based on classical sculpture (Apollo Belvedere / Medici Venus); Dürer prioritizes geometric ideal over Northern naturalism.

**Vocabulary:** melancholic · phlegmatic · sanguine · choleric · engraving · contrapposto · Four Humors
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#72](#72--birth-of-venus) — Birth of Venus (Sandro Botticelli, c. 1484–1486)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/500px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" width="180" alt="Birth of Venus">

**Theme:** Birth, beginning

> Both do not contain expected naturalistic human figures from the Renaissance, and refer to Greek art techniques and styles.

---

#### → [#73](#73--last-supper) — Last Supper (Leonardo da Vinci, c. 1494–1498)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg/500px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg" width="180" alt="Last Supper">

**Theme:** Christian belief

> Both are stories from the bible. Both portray high attention to the details of the human figures. There is also high emphasis on the symbols of good and evil.

---

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Spear Bearer) (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Ideal proportions

> Adam and Eve utilizes the ideal body proportions and contrapposto from Doryphoros.

## #75 — Sistine Chapel ceiling and altar wall frescoes

**Michelangelo** · Ceiling: c. 1508–1512 C.E.; Altar: c. 1536–1541 C.E. · Fresco · Vatican City · Ceiling 133′ × 46′; Altar wall 45′ × 40′

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Sistine_Chapel_ceiling.jpg/330px-Sistine_Chapel_ceiling.jpg" width="240" alt="Sistine Chapel ceiling and altar wall frescoes">
### Summary

The definitive **High Renaissance** monument, followed decades later by a darker, **proto-Mannerist** Last Judgment.

- **Historical Context.** **Pope Julius II** commissioned Michelangelo in 1508 to replace the ceiling's star-spangled decoration after cracking from adjacent excavations. Michelangelo, already a sculptor by training, persuaded the Pope to paint Old Testament scenes instead of the twelve apostles originally requested. The altar wall's *Last Judgment* was painted a generation later under Paul III, amid the **Protestant Reformation** and Counter-Reformation anxieties.
- **Function.** The Sistine Chapel hosts **papal conclaves**; the frescoes amplify the sacred authority of the space through biblical narrative and divine imagery.
- **Content.** The ceiling runs nine Genesis scenes (Separation of Light from Darkness → Drunkenness of Noah) flanked by prophets, sibyls, and *ignudi*. The altar wall's *Last Judgment* shows Christ at center making his verdict, the damned hauled to hell by **Charon** (after Dante) toward **Minos**.
- **Form.** Pared-down "**essential figures**" grow larger in later panels (e.g., Creation of Adam); the *ignudi* display **sprezzatura** — contorted yet at ease. Vivid color; muscular, sculptural anatomy. The altar wall shifts to a denser, more agitated, darker palette anticipating Mannerism.

**Vocabulary:** essential figures · fresco · sprezzatura · ignudi
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#63](#63--arena-scrovegni-chapel-including-lamentation) — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel, including Last Judgment (Giotto di Bondone, c. 1305)

**Theme:** religious

> The art pieces use foreshortening, where objects that are closer are larger in size. The chapel also contains the scene of the Last Judgement, where Christ is shown in the center. There is a clear division between heaven and hell.

---

#### → [#71](#71--madonna-and-child-with-two-angels) — Madonna and Child with Two Angels (Fra Filippo Lippi, c. 1465)

**Theme:** religious, personal

> The piece is realistic and shows and idealistic portrayal of figures. There is more expressiveness of Mary cradling the baby compared to earlier works of the Theotokos position. There are painted frames around the edge, similar to the realistic columns painted by Michelangelo.

---

#### → [#58](#58--church-of-sainte-foy) — Church of Sainte-Foy and Reliquary (Conques, France, c. 1050–1130)

**Theme:** religious

> The Sainte-Foy is inspired by ancient Roman culture, similar to the inspirations of the Renaissance. The tympanum also depicts the Last Judgement with Christ once again in the center.

---

## #76 — School of Athens

**Raphael** · 1509–1511 C.E. · Fresco · Stanza della Segnatura, Vatican · 500 × 770 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/The_School_of_Athens_by_Raphael_%28Vatican%29.jpg/330px-The_School_of_Athens_by_Raphael_%28Vatican%29.jpg" width="240" alt="School of Athens">
### Summary

The paradigmatic **High Renaissance synthesis** of classical philosophy and Christian humanism.

- **Historical Context.** Raphael (b. Urbino, 1483) studied in Perugia under Perugino, then moved to Florence c. 1504 absorbing Leonardo's sfumato and Michelangelo's monumentality. In 1508, Pope **Julius II** summoned him to Rome to fresco the **Stanza della Segnatura**, the pope's private library. The room's four walls pair Philosophy (*School of Athens*), Theology (*Disputa*), Poetry (*Parnassus*), and Justice.
- **Function.** Decoration of the papal library / signing chamber of the Apostolic Signatura. The scheme harmonizes secular reason with Christian theology, aligning pagan philosophers with Christian truth.
- **Content.** **Plato** (with *Timaeus*, pointing up to ideal forms) and **Aristotle** (with *Ethics*, gesturing to the material world) stand at center. Pythagoras, Heraclitus (a portrait of Michelangelo), Euclid (portrait of Bramante), Ptolemy, Zoroaster, Diogenes, and a self-portrait of Raphael populate the hall; **Apollo** and **Minerva** flank in niches.
- **Form.** Rigorous **one-point linear perspective** with vanishing point between Plato and Aristotle, set inside a coffered barrel-vaulted basilica echoing Bramante's new St. Peter's. Figures distributed in balanced left/right groupings; bright, open illumination; Renaissance color palette.

**Vocabulary:** fresco · chiaroscuro · linear perspective · foreshortening
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#75](#75--sistine-chapel-ceiling-and-altar-wall-frescoes) — Sistine Chapel ceiling and altar wall frescoes (Michelangelo, 1508–1541)

**Theme:** time period; medium; same building; commissioned by same person

> The Sistine Chapel ceiling and altar wall frescoes were painted at almost the exact same time as the School of Athens. They are both frescoes, although the Sistine Chapel ones are on the ceiling while the School of Athens is on a wall. Both pieces were commissioned by Pope Julius II to decorate the Vatican. The artists, Michelangelo and Raphael, are seen as two artists who epitomized the ideals of the High Renaissance.

---

#### → [#79](#79--allegory-of-law-and-grace) — Allegory of Law and Grace (Lucas Cranach the Elder, c. 1530)

**Theme:** conflicts between two different ideas; similar time period

> In Allegory of Law and Grace, Protestant and Catholic ideas towards God are contrasted; the left side features a scene of judgement and represents traditional ideas from the Old Testament while the right side shows God's mercy and represents the New Testament. The same contrast between ideas occurs in the School of Athens: thinkers aligning more with Plato's idealism are on the left while those aligning with Aristotle's realism are on the right. In addition, both art pieces were made during the Renaissance, in the early 1500s.

---

#### → [#73](#73--last-supper) — Last Supper (Leonardo da Vinci, c. 1494–1498)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg/500px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg" width="180" alt="Last Supper">

**Theme:** grouping; linear perspective; similar time period

> In the Last Supper, figures are painted in groups of three to represent the holy trinity. In the School of Athens, individuals also stand in groups, yet they are more fluid and less organized than in the Last Supper. Both art pieces use linear perspective in order to bring depth to the painting. In addition, both pieces were painted during the Renaissance, but the Last Supper was painted slightly earlier than the School of Athens.

---

## #77 — Isenheim altarpiece

**Matthias Grünewald** · c. 1512–1516 C.E. · Oil on wood · Unterlinden Museum, Colmar (originally Isenheim, Germany) · 9′ 9½″ × 10′ 9″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ad/Isenheim_altarpiece_in_Colmar.jpg/330px-Isenheim_altarpiece_in_Colmar.jpg" width="240" alt="Isenheim altarpiece">
### Summary

A **Northern Renaissance** altarpiece whose brutal Crucifixion confronts the suffering of the hospital's dying patients.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by Grünewald for the monastic hospital at **Isenheim** run by the **Antonite order**, which treated victims of **ergotism** ("St. Anthony's Fire") — a fungal poisoning from rye causing gangrene, hallucinations, and death. Northern Renaissance style coincides with the dawn of the **Protestant Reformation**.
- **Function.** Hospital altarpiece for worship and for comforting the sick. Images of suffering saints and Christ's wounds offered a mirror to — and meaning for — the patients' own afflictions. Altarpieces also framed the **Eucharist**.
- **Content.** A folding altarpiece with **three positions**. First (closed): the Crucifixion with John the Baptist, Mary, John the Evangelist, Mary Magdalene, flanked by Sts. Sebastian and Anthony; predella shows the Lamentation. Second: Annunciation, Angel Concert with Madonna and Child, Resurrection. Third: St. Paul and St. Anthony in the Desert; Temptation of St. Anthony; wooden sculptures of Sts. Jerome, Anthony, and Augustine.
- **Form.** Complex structure with **two sets of movable wings** over a sculpted shrine. Christ's body is distorted, pocked, and ulcerated — visually matched to ergotism lesions. Unsettling bright/black color contrasts; hybrid demons in the Temptation; bodies not idealized.

**Vocabulary:** altarpiece · movable wings · panel · predella · ergotism · St. Anthony · Northern Renaissance
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#62](#62--röttgen-pietà) — Röttgen Pietà (Unknown, c. 1300–1325)

**Theme:** Christian, depicts Christ's dead body as a sacrifice to humanity, Sculpture

> Distorted figure, Christian theme of Christ's death as a sacrifice

---

#### → [#66](#66--annunciation-triptych-merode-altarpiece) — Annunciation Triptych (Merode Altarpiece) (Workshop of Robert Campin, c. 1427–1432)

**Theme:** Christian, triptych (three-part panel), Annunciation

> Three-part panel (also an altarpiece), object of devotion, depicts the Annunciation

---

#### → [#78](#78--entombment-of-christ) — Entombment of Christ (Jacopo da Pontormo, 1525–1528)

**Theme:** Christian, depicts Lamentation

> Depicts Lamentation, unusually bright and vivid colors

---

## #78 — Entombment of Christ

**Jacopo da Pontormo** · 1525–1528 C.E. · Oil on wood · Capponi Chapel, Santa Felicita, Florence · 300 × 203 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/The_Entombment_of_Christ-Caravaggio_%28c.1602-3%29.jpg/330px-The_Entombment_of_Christ-Caravaggio_%28c.1602-3%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Entombment of Christ">
### Summary

A foundational **Mannerist** altarpiece — anti-classical, weightless, and swirling with emotion.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Florence as the High Renaissance gave way to Mannerism, amid the **Protestant Reformation**, the Sack of Rome (1527), Copernican cosmology, and the Medici's re-abolition of the Florentine republic. Pontormo was a student of Andrea del Sarto; his style was shaped by Michelangelo's late works.
- **Function.** Altarpiece for the **Capponi Chapel** of Santa Felicita in Florence. It sits beside Pontormo's own Annunciation frescoes, juxtaposing Christ's death with his incarnation.
- **Content.** Christ's pale, limp body is lifted by two youthful figures; the Virgin swoons backward; Mary Magdalene and other mourners press in. Notably, **no tomb and no cross appear** — only bodies and grief. Pontormo inserts a self-portrait at the far right.
- **Form.** Classic **Mannerist** features: elongated limbs, small heads, pastel/high-key color (acid pink, seafoam, lilac), no stable ground line, anticlassical **circular composition** centering on a knot of hands. Figures seem to float; palette chosen to cut through the chapel's dim interior.

**Vocabulary:** anticlassical composition · circular composition · Mannerism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 3.1, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#63](#63--arena-scrovegni-chapel-including-lamentation) — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel, including Lamentation (Giotto di Bondone, c. 1305)

**Theme:** grief and loss, emotion

> Both The Entombment of Christ and the fresco, Lamentation, from the Arena Chapel, depict scenes of grief and loss, where the figures mourn and yearn for the return of Christ from death.

---

#### → [#66](#66--annunciation-triptych-merode-altarpiece) — Annunciation Triptych (Merode Altarpiece) (Workshop of Robert Campin, c. 1427–1432)

**Theme:** inspiring faith

> The Annunciation Triptych focuses on the Annunciation, where it is announced to the Virgin Mary that she's going to give birth to Christ. Here, the emotion is joyful. On the opposite end of the spectrum, the Entombment of Christ depicts the death of Christ, filled with grief and negative emotions. However, both artworks serve the purpose of inspiring faith in onlookers.

---

#### → [#134](#134--memorial-sheet-for-karl-liebknecht-*in-memoriam-karl-liebknecht*) — Memorial Sheet for Karl Liebknecht (Käthe Kollwitz, 1919–1920)

**Theme:** grief and loss, emotion

> The Memorial Sheet for Karl Liebknecht depict the topic of human grief over the death of a person, using the stark contrast of black and white in woodcut to magnify the grief. Similarly, in The Entombment of Christ, while there is no weeping, the yearning on the faces depicts the grief of the figures over the death of Christ.

---

## #79 — Allegory of Law and Grace

**Lucas Cranach the Elder** · c. 1530 C.E. · Woodcut and letterpress · Schlossmuseum, Gotha (original painting) · 32.4 × 46.5 in (original painting)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Law_Grace_Cranach.jpg/330px-Law_Grace_Cranach.jpg" width="240" alt="Allegory of Law and Grace">
### Summary

A visual catechism of **Martin Luther's theology** — justification by faith, not works — disseminated through the printing press.

- **Historical Context.** Made in Germany during the height of the **Protestant Reformation**. Cranach was Luther's close friend and portraitist in **Wittenberg**. Reformers rejected the sale of indulgences and emphasized that salvation comes by **grace**, not good works. The printing press made the image cheap to reproduce.
- **Function.** Didactic Protestant propaganda illustrating the core Lutheran distinction between Law (condemnation) and Gospel (salvation).
- **Content.** A central **tree** splits the composition. **Left (Law):** a nude "Everyman" is chased by Death and the Devil toward Hell; Moses holds the Tablets; Old Testament scenes (Adam and Eve's Fall, the brazen serpent) loom. **Right (Grace/Gospel):** John the Baptist points the same figure toward the crucified Christ; behind, the risen Christ triumphs over Death. Lutheran Bible verses caption each half.
- **Form.** Woodcut reproducible at scale. Figures are naturalistic and in contrapposto; distinctive **Protestant bodily type** with small heads, round bellies. The dead tree (left) sprouts leaves (right) — a visual unity/contrast device. No deep perspectival space; didactic clarity prioritized.

**Vocabulary:** Protestant Reformation · good works · Gospel/Grace · printing press · woodblock printing
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#58](#58--church-of-sainte-foy) — Church of Sainte-Foy (Conques, France, c. 1050–1130)

**Theme:** Religion, Christianity, Last Judgement on Christ

> Both pieces have people who were condemned to hell and people who received Jesus' salvation, and they are divided in two lines.

---

#### → [#63](#63--arena-scrovegni-chapel-including-lamentation) — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel: Last Judgement (Giotto di Bondone, c. 1305)

**Theme:** Religion, Christianity, Last Judgement of Christ

> Again, the righteous and the condemned are separated: in this piece by Jesus on his throne.

---

#### → [#74](#74--adam-and-eve) — Adam and Eve (Albrecht Dürer, 1504)

**Theme:** Religious, fall from Grace

> Adam and Eve are the subjects of this piece and they are featured in Allegory of Law and Grace as well. They are both prints and are both religious.

---

## #82 — Il Gesù, including Triumph of the Name of Jesus ceiling fresco

**Giacomo della Porta (façade); Giovanni Battista Gaulli (fresco)** · Façade 1568–1584; fresco 1676–1679 · Brick, marble, fresco, stucco · Rome · 75 × 35 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Il_Gesu_Rome.jpg/330px-Il_Gesu_Rome.jpg" width="240" alt="Il Gesù, including Triumph of the Name of Jesus ceiling fresco">
### Summary

The **mother church of the Jesuits**, whose façade template and illusionistic ceiling define the Italian **Baroque**.

- **Historical Context.** Conceived by **St. Ignatius of Loyola**, founder of the Society of Jesus, and bankrolled by Cardinal **Alessandro Farnese**. Church built during the Counter-Reformation to model a new preaching-focused architecture; Gaulli's ceiling fresco came a century later at the height of Roman high Baroque.
- **Function.** Principal Jesuit church; its plan and façade became the prototype for Jesuit churches worldwide (St. Michael, Munich; St. Ignatius, Buenos Aires). Designed to draw large audiences to hear sermons and participate in ornate liturgy — Counter-Reformation theatrics.
- **Content.** The **ceiling fresco** shows the sacred monogram **IHS** glorified in a burst of golden light as saints and angels ascend, while the damned (painted on stucco that spills over the frame) plummet into darkness below.
- **Form.** Della Porta's two-story façade has **six pairs of Corinthian pilasters** below, **four pairs** above, joined by scrolling volutes — a proto-Baroque departure from Renaissance classicism. Gaulli's ceiling exploits **trompe l'oeil** and sculpted stucco bursting through painted sky (*di sotto in sù*), abolishing the boundary between architecture and image.

**Vocabulary:** façade · Jesuits · trompe l'oeil · Baroque
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#89](#89--ecstasy-of-saint-teresa) — Ecstasy of Saint Teresa (Gian Lorenzo Bernini, c. 1647–1652)

**Theme:** Italian Baroque

> Both works are highly characteristic of the Baroque period, with rich decoration, and a very high degree of theatricality. The lighting in the church and the Ecstasy of St. Theresa both emphasize the drama of the situation, and relay great emotion and passion. Both also intend to connect emotionally with the audience, and the tension and feeling in both works is almost tangible. Both are also of religious subjects, and are intended to inspire greater devotion in the audience, so to counter the Reformation.

---

#### → [#85](#85--calling-of-saint-matthew) — Calling of Saint Matthew (Caravaggio, c. 1597–1601)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg/500px-The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Calling of Saint Matthew">

**Theme:** Mannerism

> Both works have a high emotion, and tension, though evoke different emotions, as Il Gesù inspires awe at the glory of God, the Calling of St. Mathew is infused with the tension of the scene as the tax collector, Mathew, as sinner, is questioning whether he has been chosen to follow Jesus. The Calling of St. Mathew thus acts to challenge the audience to see themselves as St. Mathew, while the Il Gesù ceiling fresco urges the viewers to triumph in the Sacred Name of Jesus. Both in style are similar due to the theatricality, and the emphasis of light in unusual ways (in the Calling of St. Mathew, the light shines not through not through the window, but rather mysteriously from the direction of the hand of Jesus) and are intended to evoke piety in the observers.

---

#### → [#88](#88--san-carlo-alle-quattro-fontane) — San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane (Francesco Borromini, 1638–1646)

**Theme:** Baroque

> Both the façade of the Il Gesù and the façade of the San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane exhibit deviations from the classical style and the styles prevalent in the earlier Renaissance. The San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane does so in its undulating exterior, and its two levels of columns is similar to the façade of the Il Gesù. The San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane also has sculptures in the niches of the façade. The deviations from the Renaissance norm set both works in the Baroque period.

---

## #83 — Hunters in the Snow

**Pieter Bruegel the Elder** · 1565 C.E. · Oil on wood · Kunsthistorisches Museum, Vienna · 46″ × 63¾″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/240px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="Hunters in the Snow">
### Summary

A **secular Northern Renaissance landscape** — one of the first in Western art to give nature the lead role.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Antwerp/Brussels during the 16th-century **Dutch/Flemish Northern Renaissance**, amid the Reformation's break from overtly religious art. Bruegel was a seminal influence on the Dutch Golden Age. The work comes from an unusually cold stretch of the so-called "Little Ice Age."
- **Function.** One of a cycle of **six Labors of the Months** panels (five survive; others include *Gloomy Day*, *Haymaking*, *Harvesters*, *Return of the Herd*) commissioned by Antwerp merchant Niclaes Jongelinck for his country house. The cycle updates a medieval calendar tradition in a secular, peasant-genre mode.
- **Content.** Three weary hunters trudge home with a single fox through barren snow, bony dogs in tow, toward a village and frozen pond where figures skate and play proto-ice-hockey. Jagged mountains, not native to the Low Countries, loom behind.
- **Form.** "Balcony" / high-angle perspective drops the viewer above the scene; cool muted **grey-white-blue** palette; remarkable atmospheric depth achieved via diminishing scale and aerial perspective. Bare tree trunks and black birds form strong diagonal lines leading the eye into the distance.

**Vocabulary:** oil paint · landscape · Northern European tradition · genre painting · Labor of the Months
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#111](#111--slave-ship-slavers-throwing-overboard-the-dead-and-dying-typhoon-coming-on) — Slave Ship (J.M.W. Turner, 1840)

**Theme:** Hardship

> Reveals the suffering of slaves that were ill and thrown overboard due to the captain figuring he could not collect money for a slave that is about to die. Similar to the suffering that is captured in Hunters in the Snow

---

#### → [#235](#235--rebellious-silence-from-the-women-of-allah-series) — Rebellious Silence, from the Women of Allah series (Shirin Neshat, photo by Cynthia Preston, 1994)

**Theme:** Hardship

> Photographic portrait of an Iranian woman who has a poem on her face that expresses piety. There is clear tension and hardship imbued in the photograph, which can be related to the hardship in Hunters in the Snow.

---

#### → [#127](#127--the-steerage) — The Steerage (Alfred Stieglitz, 1907)

**Theme:** Hardship

> Depiction of people who are disadvantaged due to reasons external to their will. This resembles the source of hardship for the hunters in Bruegel's piece.

---

## #84 — Mosque of Selim II

**Sinan (architect)** · 1568–1575 C.E. · Brick and stone · Edirne, Turkey · 190 × 130 m (complex)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/Blue_Mosque_Istanbul.jpg/330px-Blue_Mosque_Istanbul.jpg" width="240" alt="Mosque of Selim II">
### Summary

Mimar **Sinan's** self-proclaimed masterpiece — the structural climax of **Ottoman imperial mosque** design.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by Sultan **Selim II**, son of Suleiman the Magnificent, for **Edirne**, the Ottomans' former Balkan capital and the first major city European travelers encountered. Finished the year after Selim's death, 1575. Sinan set out to surpass the dome of the Byzantine **Hagia Sophia** — and claimed to have done so.
- **Function.** Congregational mosque with a full *külliye* complex: two **madrasas**, library, hospital, and shops (the shops and a Qur'an school were added under Murad III). UNESCO World Heritage Site since 2011.
- **Content.** The layout aligns **square → octagon → circle** (muezzin's platform / eight piers / dome) — a Muslim meditation on earthly vs. celestial geometry. **Iznik tiles** decorate the interior with saz leaves, Chinese-cloud motifs, and Hadith inscriptions emphasizing Islamic identity after the 1571 naval defeat at Lepanto.
- **Form.** A vast central dome (~31.25 m diameter) rests on **eight piers** and eight squinches decorated with **muqarnas**. Four slender **minarets** at the corners of the prayer hall frame the dome; 18 subsidiary domes surround it but are visually dominated by the central dome.

**Vocabulary:** minaret · madrasa · squinch · muqarna · Iznik tile
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#52](#52--hagia-sophia) — Hagia Sophia (Anthemius of Tralles and Isidorus of Miletus, 532–537)

**Theme:** Religious

> Although originally Christian, the Hagia Sophia was used as a mosque while the Ottoman Empire ruled over Istanbul. There are four minarets on the corners of the Hagia Sophia and a large central dome, similar to those of the Mosque of Selim II.

---

#### → [#56](#56--great-mosque-of-córdoba) — Great Mosque of Córdoba (Umayyad, 785–786 and later)

**Theme:** Religious

> The Great Mosque of Córdoba is also an Islamic mosque, although the architectural layout is quite different, as it is much more rectangular and there is currently a cathedral placed in the middle of it.

---

#### → [#168](#168--great-mosque-of-djenné) — Great Mosque of Djenné (Mali, founded c. 1200; rebuilt 1906–1907)

**Theme:** Religious

> The Great Mosque of Djenné is similar in function to the Mosque of Selim II, although it is created of adobe and also has a more rectangular structure, like the mosque at Córdoba.

---

## #85 — Calling of Saint Matthew

**Caravaggio** · c. 1597–1601 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Contarelli Chapel, San Luigi dei Francesi, Rome · 322 × 340 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg/500px-The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg" width="240" alt="The Calling of Saint Matthew">

### Summary

A defining **Italian Baroque** painting whose diagonal shaft of light bears the weight of the miracle.

- **Historical Context.** Caravaggio's first major public commission, secured by his patron **Cardinal Francesco del Monte** for the Contarelli Chapel in the French national church in Rome. Painted at the height of the Counter-Reformation, which pushed artists to create emotionally direct, accessible images.
- **Function.** One of three paintings on the life of Matthew for the chapel. Meant to evangelize ordinary viewers by placing a gospel moment in a recognizable tavern setting.
- **Content.** Four finely dressed figures count money at a table; Levi the tax collector (probably the bearded man at center) points at himself — "who, me?" — as Christ and Peter, in biblical dress, enter from the right. A diagonal **shaft of light** mirrors Christ's outstretched hand, whose pose echoes the finger of God in Michelangelo's Sistine Chapel.
- **Form.** Hallmark Caravaggesque **tenebrism** / chiaroscuro: a raking light from upper right cuts across a dark interior. Contemporary 16th-century clothing vs. Christ's timeless robes collapses sacred and everyday. Figures are life-size, anatomically truthful, and psychologically specific.

**Vocabulary:** Baroque · chiaroscuro · tenebrism · religious work
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#71](#71--madonna-and-child-with-two-angels) — Madonna and Child with Two Angels (Fra Filippo Lippi, c. 1465)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both paintings depict a scene with biblical figures (Madonna, Jesus, Peter, and Matthew). Halos are relatively invisible. Subjects are depicted in three dimensions and proportionally true.

---

#### → [#82](#82--il-gesù-including-triumph-of-the-name-of-jesus-ceiling-fresco) — Il Gesù, including Triumph of the Name of Jesus (Della Porta / Gaulli, 16th–17th c.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both are Baroque in nature and depict religious scenes. Il Gesù, in fact, is a church, and The Calling of St. Matthew has been displayed in another church since its creation. Human anatomy is precise, figures are three-dimensional, and great detail is attended to.

---

#### → [#89](#89--ecstasy-of-saint-teresa) — Ecstasy of Saint Teresa (Gian Lorenzo Bernini, c. 1647–1652)

**Theme:** Religion

> Both artworks depict a saint in significant moments of their respective lives. The human anatomy is neither exaggerated nor simplified, and although both are detailed, the emphasis is on the overall work.

---

## #87 — Self-Portrait with Saskia

**Rembrandt van Rijn** · 1636 C.E. · Etching · Fitzwilliam Museum, Cambridge · 10.5 × 9.5 cm (plate)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/70/Self-portrait_with_Saskia.jpg" width="240" alt="Self-Portrait with Saskia">
### Summary

An intimate **Dutch Baroque etching** — a marriage portrait doubling as a self-advertisement for the artist.

- **Historical Context.** Rembrandt (b. Leiden, 1606) was the leading Dutch Baroque painter and a pioneer who elevated **etching** from reproductive technique to independent art form. Made two years after his marriage to **Saskia van Uylenburgh**, the wealthy niece of his dealer. The Dutch Golden Age valued secular genre, portraiture, and printmaking for a middle-class market.
- **Function.** A personal **marriage commemoration** and a display piece for prospective patrons, advertising Rembrandt's virtuosity in etching. Self-portraits let him experiment with costumes and lighting.
- **Content.** Rembrandt at his drawing table in fanciful historical dress (plumed beret, cloak), **porte-crayon** in hand, meets the viewer's eye directly; young Saskia sits behind him, looking off into middle distance. The setup suggests he is drawing her, and the viewer has interrupted a quiet studio moment.
- **Form.** Loose, sketch-like **crosshatching** and expressive line. Deep bite creates rich dark shadows that pull Rembrandt forward; Saskia is rendered in lighter lines receding back. High-contrast Baroque chiaroscuro in black ink alone.

**Vocabulary:** etching · printmaking · crosshatching · bite (acid)
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.1, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#74](#74--adam-and-eve) — Adam and Eve (Albrecht Dürer, 1504)

**Theme:** Original Sin, Biblical reference, perfection before chaos

> Both works use the same technique and materials, and both manage to achieve a great range of values despite the difficulty of engraving. Both attempt at life-drawing, but Rembrandt's is more realistic versus Durer's idealistic.

---

#### → [#68](#68--the-arnolfini-portrait) — The Arnolfini Portrait (Jan van Eyck, 1434)

**Theme:** Marriage, social status, prosperity, commemoration

> The two pieces both commemorate a marriage. Both serve as glimpses into the couple's lives and possess a feeling of intimacy.

---

#### → [#76](#76--school-of-athens) — School of Athens (Raphael, 1509–1511)

**Theme:** Artistic ideal, perfection of technique, social class, intellectuals

> In the School of Athens, Raphael portrays himself among the great intellectuals he painted. Similarly, Rembrandt focuses on a self-portrait in this piece. He also often inserted a self-portrait into his religious paintings much like Raphael.

---

## #88 — San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane

**Francesco Borromini (architect)** · 1638–1646 C.E. · Stone and stucco · Rome, Italy · ~20 × 12 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/San_Carlo_alle_Quattro_Fontane_September_2015-2a.jpg/330px-San_Carlo_alle_Quattro_Fontane_September_2015-2a.jpg" width="240" alt="San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane">
### Summary

A tiny, **undulating Italian Baroque church** — Borromini's first solo commission and a prototype for dynamic wall architecture.

- **Historical Context.** Designed for the Spanish **Discalced Trinitarian** order on a cramped corner of the Quirinal Hill in Rome named for its **four fountains**. Borromini, trained as a stonemason and nephew/rival of Bernini, had to wrestle with a non-perpendicular, narrow site. His first independent project (façade completed after his 1667 suicide by his nephew Bernardo).
- **Function.** Conventual church for the Trinitarians, a small community of Spanish monks, plus their monastery and cloister; the order was founded to ransom Christians held captive by Muslim corsairs.
- **Content.** An unprecedented **oval plan** derived from a Greek cross stretched on the long axis, fitted ingeniously onto an impossible site. Exterior **façade undulates** in a concave-convex-concave rhythm, broken by niched sculptures. Interior dome is ornamented with deep **coffering** in geometric hexagons, octagons, and Greek crosses that shrink upward to exaggerate height.
- **Form.** Stone and stucco. Serpentine walls, minimal color, dramatic upward compression of the dome, hidden windows washing it with light. The two-story façade shifts in style between Borromini's lower half and his nephew's upper half.

**Vocabulary:** façade · dome · cherubs · coffering
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#58](#58--church-of-sainte-foy) — Church of Sainte-Foy and Reliquary (Conques, c. 1050–1130)

**Theme:** Religious, architecture, pilgrimage church

> Prior to the Protestant reformation this is a romanesque church and connects to San Carlino by its religious symbolism and construction

---

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Rome, 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, ancient roman architecture, now a church

> The Pantheon is similar to San Carlino because it had multiple building periods and multiple people commissioned it due to deaths.

---

#### → [#51](#51--san-vitale) — San Vitale (Ravenna, 526–547 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious, Byzantine basilica church

> San Vitale is similar to San Carlino because it is a centrally planned church, even though San Carlino is not a basilica circumstance made it centrally planned.

---

## #89 — Ecstasy of Saint Teresa

**Gian Lorenzo Bernini** · c. 1647–1652 C.E. · Marble (sculpture); stucco and gilt bronze (chapel) · Cornaro Chapel, Santa Maria della Vittoria, Rome · life size

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6b/Ecstasy_of_Saint_Teresa_September_2015-2a.jpg/250px-Ecstasy_of_Saint_Teresa_September_2015-2a.jpg" width="240" alt="Ecstasy of Saint Teresa">
### Summary

The **archetypal Italian Baroque *Gesamtkunstwerk*** — sculpture, architecture, painting, and light staged as theater.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by Cardinal **Federico Cornaro** for his family chapel during the **Counter-Reformation**. The subject is **St. Teresa of Ávila** (1515–1582), co-founder of the Discalced Carmelites, recently canonized (1622); her memoir described an angel repeatedly piercing her heart with a golden arrow, leaving her in mystical "sweet pain."
- **Function.** An altar shrine meant to induce piety in viewers by dramatizing a saint's private vision. The Cornaro donors appear in relief in the "theater boxes" flanking the stage.
- **Content.** St. Teresa swoons on a cloud, eyes closed, mouth open, foot dangling; a smiling **angel** prepares to plunge his golden arrow into her heart. Above, a **hidden window** pours natural light through **gilt-bronze rays**, concentrating divine grace on the figures.
- **Form.** Virtuoso **marble** rendered as weightless cloud and fluttering drapery. Dynamic diagonal composition, extreme emotion, airy textures contrasted against architectural niche. Combines sculpture, architecture, stucco, fresco, and concealed lighting into a unified multi-media illusion.

**Vocabulary:** gesamtkunstwerk · Baroque · bridal mysticism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#37](#37--winged-victory-of-samothrace) — Winged Victory of Samothrace (Hellenistic Greek, c. 190 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** The naturalistic representation of divine (the angel of the Ecstasy St. Teresa); the action

> The action is very realistic and imbued with energy. The Winged Nike is about to fly off, and the angel in St. Teresa is full of energy as he is about to spear St. Teresa. In both the energy is very evident. Furthermore, the clothing is similar, and has a sense of lightness and airiness, even though the medium is dense marble.

---

#### → [#82](#82--il-gesù-including-triumph-of-the-name-of-jesus-ceiling-fresco) — Il Gesù, including Triumph of the Name of Jesus (Della Porta / Gaulli)

**Theme:** Both evoke the greatness of God, and show divine light, and highly realistic, dramatic figures

> Both are highly dramatic, and give the illusion of weightlessness and use light as a symbol for the glory of God. Both have much action, and use drama to draw in the audience to the wonder of the scene.

---

#### → [#75](#75--sistine-chapel-ceiling-and-altar-wall-frescoes) — Sistine Chapel ceiling and altar wall frescoes (Michelangelo, 1508–1541)

**Theme:** Both have religious subjects, and evoke awe and wonder in the reader

> Both have religious subjects, but St. Theresa in Ecstasy is more personal, and draws in the reader more. The Sistine Chapel however is very awe inspiring with the great number of frescos.

---

## #90 — Angel with Arquebus, Asiel Timor Dei

**Master of Calamarca (La Paz School)** · c. 17th century C.E. · Oil on canvas · La Paz, Bolivia · 160 × 110 cm

<img src="https://smarthistory.org/wp-content/uploads/2021/01/asiel-timor-dei-angel.jpg" width="240" alt="Angel with Arquebus, Asiel Timor Dei">

### Summary

A colonial Andean **evangelization tool** dressing a Spanish-fashioned, gun-toting angel in the vocabulary of the Counter-Reformation.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in the **Viceroyalty of Peru** at a workshop in Calamarca (Bolivia) under José López de los Ríos. Created during a concerted **extirpation of idolatry** campaign to replace surviving Andean winged deities with Christian angels. Counter-Reformation theology cast angels as a militia of the Church Militant.
- **Function.** Hung in a church to impress indigenous Andean viewers with the **triple authority** of the Spanish crown — aristocratic (court dress), military (firearm), and spiritual (angelic). Native viewers could map the image onto their own sky/weather deities.
- **Content.** A young androgynous **arquebusier angel** — named Asiel Timor Dei ("Fear of God") in the scroll — stands in elegant Spanish court dress holding a loaded **arquebus** (early matchlock musket), rendered in a military drill pose from a European manual.
- **Form.** Flat, frontal Andean-style composition with careful attention to lace collar, sash, and shoes; wings cropped by frame; luxurious fabrics. Oil on canvas in a provincial Baroque idiom.

**Vocabulary:** history painting · devotional painting · arquebus · Viceroyalty of Peru
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#69](#69--david) — David (Donatello, c. 1440–1460)

**Theme:** Triumph of Youth, Martial Display

> Both depict androgynous figures triumphant

---

#### → [#71](#71--madonna-and-child-with-two-angels) — Madonna and Child with Two Angels (Fra Filippo Lippi, c. 1465)

**Theme:** Matronly love, Christianity

> It has Angels

---

#### → [#89](#89--ecstasy-of-saint-teresa) — Ecstasy of Saint Teresa (Gian Lorenzo Bernini, c. 1647–1652)

**Theme:** Celibates having sex

> It also has Angels

---

## #91 — Las Meninas

**Diego Velázquez** · c. 1656 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museo del Prado, Madrid · 10′ 5″ × 9′ 1″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg/400px-Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg" width="240" alt="Las Meninas">

### Summary

A **Spanish Baroque** group portrait that folds the painter, the royal family, and the viewer into a single enigmatic space.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by Velázquez at the court of **Philip IV** of Spain, where he served as chamberlain and court painter in the Alcázar Palace, Madrid. Velázquez's last great work; the red cross of **Santiago** on his chest was reportedly added after his knighthood was awarded (1659) — perhaps by the king himself.
- **Function.** Meant to hang in Philip IV's private office. Part group portrait, part self-portrait, part meditation on painting as a liberal art — Velázquez asserts the dignity of his profession at a time when painters were considered craftsmen.
- **Content.** **Infanta Margarita**, her *meninas* (ladies-in-waiting), a dwarf, a dog, and a chaperone occupy the foreground; Velázquez stands at a huge canvas to the left; king and queen appear reflected in a mirror on the back wall, suggesting they occupy the viewer's position.
- **Form.** Loose, atmospheric brushwork. Complex **spatial ambiguity**: we cannot tell whether we are the king's subjects, the painter's sitters, or both. Tenebrist side-lighting pulls figures from shadow; a doorway at the back opens into deeper space and frames a courtier silhouetted against light.

**Vocabulary:** fourth wall · chiaroscuro · self-portrait
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#85](#85--calling-of-saint-matthew) — Calling of Saint Matthew (Caravaggio, c. 1597–1601)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg/500px-The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew-Caravaggo_%281599-1600%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Calling of Saint Matthew">

**Theme:** Baroque chiaroscuro

> Both use dramatic light on key figures in a dark interior to guide the eye and heighten psychological intensity, though Velázquez replaces Caravaggio's theatrical narrative with courtly ambiguity.

---

#### → [#126](#126--les-demoiselles-d'avignon) — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon (Pablo Picasso, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="180" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

**Theme:** A group of figures depicted

> Both works consist of a depiction of multiple (more than three) individuals, in varying poses and appearance. However, the figures in Las Meninas are recognizable people from real life, while those in Les Demoiselles are heavily abstracted.

---

#### → [#98](#98--the-tête-à-tête-from-marriage-à-la-mode) — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode (William Hogarth, c. 1743)

**Theme:** Paintings within paintings

> Both works embed pictures inside the picture — Velázquez shows Rubens copies on the rear wall and his own canvas in progress, while Hogarth uses paintings of saints as a moralizing contrast to the couple's decadence.

---

## #92 — Woman Holding a Balance

**Johannes Vermeer** (Dutch Baroque) · c. 1664 C.E. · Oil on canvas · National Gallery of Art, Washington D.C. · 39.7 × 35.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="Woman Holding a Balance">

### Summary

A Dutch Baroque meditation on moral balance, juxtaposing earthly wealth with the Last Judgment.

- **Historical Context.** Vermeer (b. Delft, 1632 – d. 1675) spent his life in Delft; the seated woman is likely his wife Catharina. The painting-within-a-painting of the **Last Judgment** hangs directly behind her, connecting her "mundane act" to the Weighing of Souls. Her blue robe and downcast gaze recall images of the **Virgin Mary**; her costume reflects mid-1660s Dutch fashion.
- **Function.** Painted for a private home. An allegory urging temperance — weighing unborn souls and material possessions against the religious themes of the painting above.
- **Content.** A young woman, possibly pregnant, holds an **empty** balance before a table of jewelry boxes overflowing with pearls and gold. A mirror and draped window are at left; the *Last Judgment* painting fills the wall behind her, with Christ's figure directly above her head.
- **Form.** Vermeer likely used a **camera obscura**; orthogonals converge precisely at her fingertip, the picture's vanishing point. Trademark blue/grey/yellow palette; pearls rendered in two layers (gray glaze + thick specular highlight); light seeps in through a gap in the curtains.

**Vocabulary:** Intimate interior scene · Last Judgment · painting within a painting · balance · spiritual vs. materialistic · camera obscura · opulent furnishings · pearls · chiaroscuro
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#91](#91--las-meninas) — Las Meninas (Diego Velázquez, 1656)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg/330px-Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg" width="180" alt="Las Meninas">

**Theme:** Baroque, daily life

> Painting within a painting, Details on dress, expression.

---

#### → [#68](#68--the-arnolfini-portrait) — The Arnolfini Portrait (Jan van Eyck, 1434)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/The_Arnolfini_portrait_%281434%29.jpg/330px-The_Arnolfini_portrait_%281434%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Arnolfini Portrait">

**Theme:** Portrait of couple

> Woman may not be pregnant, wearing clothes showing her fuller in stomach area, Use of light, well-appointed interior scene.

---

#### → [#85](#85--calling-of-saint-matthew) — Calling of St. Matthew (Caravaggio, c. 1597–1601)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Caravaggio_%E2%80%94_The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew.jpg/500px-Caravaggio_%E2%80%94_The_Calling_of_Saint_Matthew.jpg" width="180" alt="Calling of St. Matthew">
