## #94 — Screen with the Siege of Belgrade and hunting scene

**Circle of the González Family** (New Spain) · c. 1697–1701 C.E. · Oil on wood, inlaid with mother-of-pearl · Mexico · 90½ × 108⅝ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f9/Screen_Siege_Belgrade_hunting.jpg/330px-Screen_Siege_Belgrade_hunting.jpg" width="240" alt="Screen with the Siege of Belgrade and hunting scene">
### Summary

A two-sided **biombo enconchado** whose materials and form embody the syncretism of early 18th-century New Spain.

- **Historical Context.** Created in early 18th-century **New Spain**, a viceroyalty spanning Mexico, much of the modern U.S., Florida, and the Philippines. The culture was highly syncretic — shaped by Spaniards, indigenous Americans, and the Japanese (the screen form itself). Commissioned for viceroy **José Sarmiento de Valladares**.
- **Function.** The battle side served as **political propaganda**, shown to officials the viceroy met with — depicting a very contemporary Hapsburg victory. The hunting side faced the room where the viceroy's wife entertained guests, functioning decoratively while still displaying the family's wealth.
- **Content.** Battle side depicts the **1698 Siege of Belgrade** (Hapsburgs vs. Ottomans) with cramped streets of fighting figures, linear and atmospheric perspective, and emotional soldier faces. The reverse shows a serene hunting landscape emphasizing scenery over figures. Both sides feature floral borders showcasing the shell inlay.
- **Form.** Oil on wood (a Spanish medium), Mexican-invented **enconchado** (shell inlay) technique, and Japanese-derived folding screen format — literally layered continents of influence. The lacquerware base was inspired by imported Japanese lacquer boxes.

**Vocabulary:** screen · biombo · Battle of Belgrade · biombo enconchado
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#210](#210--red-and-white-plum-blossoms) — White and Red Plum Blossoms (Ogata Kōrin, c. 1710–1716)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/77/Ogata_Korin_-_RED_AND_WHITE_PLUM_BLOSSOMS_%28National_Treasure%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Ogata_Korin_-_RED_AND_WHITE_PLUM_BLOSSOMS_%28National_Treasure%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="White and Red Plum Blossoms">

**Theme:** Nature used as room décor, screen

> (compared to the piece as a whole) Ogata Korin's Red and White Plum Blossoms is a screen used to divide a room, the same purpose as the Valladares screen. Screens such as Korin's were the source of inspiration for the artist of the Valladares screen.

---

#### → [#47](#47--ludovisi-battle-sarcophagus) — Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus (Late Imperial Roman, c. 250 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8a/Grande_Ludovisi_Altemps_Inv8574.jpg" width="180" alt="Ludovisi Battle Sarcophagus">

**Theme:** Victory in battle

> (compared to the battle side) The figures in the sarcophagus are equally as close together and cramped as in the screen; they both show the trappings of war and battle while also illustrating a clear victory for one side. Both works showed victory in order to strengthen outsiders' view of a person; in the case of the sarcophagus, it was a dead warrior, in the case of the screen, it was the viceroy with a reputation and public image to upkeep.

---

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Hunters in the Snow">

**Theme:** Emphasis on nature

> (compared to the hunting side) In both works, the emphasis is on the composition rather than the people in it, although there are people present. The viewer is meant to observe the whole scene as a whole rather than focus in on certain parts, and because of this the figures are not individualized nor very emotive. In both scenes there is a use of atmospheric perspective to give the viewer a sense of depth and an increased appreciation for nature.

---

## #95 — The Virgin of Guadalupe (Virgen de Guadalupe)

**Miguel González** · c. 1698 C.E. · Oil on canvas on wood, inlaid with mother-of-pearl · LACMA (originally Basilica of Guadalupe, Mexico City) · 39 × 27½ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a6/Virgen_Guadalupe_colonial.jpg/330px-Virgen_Guadalupe_colonial.jpg" width="240" alt="The Virgin of Guadalupe (Virgen de Guadalupe)">
### Summary

A luxury **enconchado** version of Mexico's most venerated miraculous image, fusing Catholic devotion, indigenous identity, and Asian-derived craft.

- **Historical Context.** Refers to the 1531 apparition of the Virgin to **Juan Diego Cuauhtlatoatzin**, an early indigenous convert, on the hill of **Tepeyac**. The Virgin appeared with **mestiza features** and spoke Nahuatl, asking for a shrine to replace the pagan Aztec site. Her image miraculously imprinted itself on his **tilma** after he carried Castilian roses to Bishop Zumárraga. The miracle accelerated mass indigenous conversion and became a core symbol of Mexican identity. **Miguel González** — likely mestizo or Amerindian — worked in late 17th-century Mexico and was a leading practitioner of *enconchado* alongside his father and brother.
- **Function.** A devotional icon for prayer and veneration; the expensive materials suggest a wealthy patron's home altar or a church.
- **Content.** The Virgin stands in contemplative prayer with **ashen mestiza skin**, crowned, surrounded by sun rays, standing on a crescent moon — iconography drawn from the **Woman of the Apocalypse** (Revelation). Four corner **roundels** show the apparition narrative; a medallion below carries the **seal of Mexico City**. White lilies (purity) and four-petaled roses (Juan Diego's roses) line the borders.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas laid on wood, with **mother-of-pearl inlay** (*enconchado*) across frame, crown, and mantle — a technique influenced by Japanese *maki-e* lacquer brought by Iberian traders.

**Vocabulary:** enconchado · marianismo · cherub · mestizo/a · tilma · roundel
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 2.1, 2.2, 2.3, 3.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#97](#97--spaniard-and-indian-produce-a-mestizo) — Spaniard and Indian Produce a Mestizo (Juan Rodríguez Juárez, c. 1715)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Castas_01mestiza_max.jpg/250px-Castas_01mestiza_max.jpg" width="180" alt="Spaniard and Indian Produce a Mestizo">

**Theme:** Racial/ethnic divides in New Spain, the casta system

> They both reflect the mixed heritage of Mexico and Latin America as a whole, depicting mestizos, or people who are of mixed descent.

---

#### → [#94](#94--screen-with-the-siege-of-belgrade-and-hunting-scene) — Screen with the Siege of Belgrade and hunting scene (Circle of the González Family, c. 1697–1701)

**Theme:** Military conflict, religious tensions between Christians and Muslims

> Both works make use of the enconchado technique, were produced by members/followers of the González family, and reflect Japanese influence.

---

#### → [#81](#81--frontispiece-of-the-codex-mendoza) — Frontispiece of the Codex Mendoza (Viceroyalty of New Spain, c. 1541–1542)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/CodexMendoza01.jpg/500px-CodexMendoza01.jpg" width="180" alt="Codex Mendoza frontispiece">

**Theme:** The history of the Aztec empire and its people

> They represent the influence of both indigenous and Spanish culture on the developing cultures and societies in Latin America

---

## #96 — Fruit and Insects

**Rachel Ruysch** · 1711 C.E. · Oil on wood · Uffizi, Florence · 44 × 60 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/59/Fruit_Insects_still_life.jpg/330px-Fruit_Insects_still_life.jpg" width="240" alt="Fruit and Insects">
### Summary

A **Dutch Golden Age still life** by the most celebrated female flower painter of her era, marrying botanical science with Protestant moral allegory.

- **Historical Context.** **Rachel Ruysch** (1664–1750) began exhibiting at 15 and became the Dutch Republic's most documented woman painter. Her father Frederik Ruysch was a noted professor of **botany and anatomy**; she trained under flower painter Willem van Aelst. The Protestant Dutch Republic's market-driven art scene encouraged specialization in genres like flowers, still life, and landscape — the era of "tulip mania."
- **Function.** Domestic decoration for the Dutch middle and upper-middle class. Functioned as both a showpiece of scientific observation and a **vanitas** — reminding the Protestant viewer that earthly abundance fades and faith endures.
- **Content.** An impossible mixed-season bouquet: **squash and corn** (autumn) beside **peaches** (summer); grapes (wine/Christ's blood) beside wheat (bread/Christ's body), an allusion to the Eucharist. Insects alight on rotting fruit — signs of decay and mortality.
- **Form.** Oil on wood. Complementary **red–green** balance; solid forms in front, airy stems behind; photograph-precise botanical rendering drawn from Ruysch's scientific sketchbooks. The composition looks spontaneously natural but is meticulously composed.

**Vocabulary:** still life · Dutch Golden Age · botany · vanitas · tulip mania
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#92](#92--woman-holding-a-balance) — Woman Holding a Balance (Johannes Vermeer, c. 1664)

**Theme:** Centers around Christianity in everyday life; reference to the Virgin Mary

> Both images come from the Dutch Golden Era and would have functioned similarly. This painting would have been hung in a house as a reminder to maintain a strong Christian faith.

---

#### → [#79](#79--allegory-of-law-and-grace) — Allegory of Law and Grace (Lucas Cranach the Elder, c. 1530)

**Theme:** Protestant faith; greed, vanity, salvation

> Both works are products of the protestant faith. They serve as reminders to live a holy life.

---

#### → [#121](#121--the-coiffure) — The Coiffure (Mary Cassatt, 1890–1891)

**Theme:** Everyday life; upper-middle class patronage

> Shows an independent woman who is happy being by herself. Deals with Everyday life, a painting that resonates the most among the middle class. Would have most likely be patronized by an upper-middle class man.

---

## #97 — Spaniard and Indian Produce a Mestizo

**Attributed to Juan Rodríguez Juárez** · c. 1715 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Breamore House, Hampshire, UK · 31.8 × 41.5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Casta_Painting_Mestizo.jpg/330px-Casta_Painting_Mestizo.jpg" width="240" alt="Spaniard and Indian Produce a Mestizo">
### Summary

An early **casta painting** classifying the racial mixtures of colonial New Spain within a single idealized family portrait.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in early-18th-century **New Spain (Mexico)** amid intensifying Spanish anxiety about **mestizaje** — inter-ethnic mixing — and a desire to impose legible hierarchy on a fluid colonial society. *Casta* series typically ran 16 panels; earlier works idealize the first mixtures, later ones denigrate more complex ones.
- **Function.** Likely commissioned by viceregal patrons as a gift shipped to Spain — an ethnographic display meant to reassure European audiences that Spanish bloodlines, though mixing, remained ordered and noble. Also served Enlightenment-era classifying impulses.
- **Content.** An **elite Spanish husband** in a powdered wig and French court dress stands beside his **indigenous wife** in a traditional *huipil*; their child (a "**mestizo**") is carried by a young servant boy. All three family members interlock gazes in a circular composition.
- **Form.** European oil-on-canvas portraiture conventions — soft brushwork, realism — with symbolic costume choices coded for race and class. Red accents link the family members; the husband receives the brightest white highlights, emphasizing his "purity."

**Vocabulary:** Caste painting (pinturas de castas) · mestizo · huipil
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#22](#22--akhenaton-nefertiti-and-three-daughters) — Akhenaten, Nefertiti, and Three Daughters (Amarna, c. 1353–1335 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Family, everyday life

> Both depict elite figures in a happy and successful marriage with their children.

---

#### → [#87](#87--self-portrait-with-saskia) — Self-Portrait with Saskia (Rembrandt van Rijn, 1636)

**Theme:** Portraiture, marriage

> Both works depict their married subjects as content and share a harmonious atmosphere. In both piece, the husband is the most emphasized.

---

#### → [#98](#98--the-tête-à-tête-from-marriage-à-la-mode) — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode (William Hogarth, c. 1743)

**Theme:** Everyday life, portraiture, marriage

> Both works depict the result of a marriage between two people; the difference being that this result is a negative and unsuccessful marriage.

---

## #98 — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode

**William Hogarth** · c. 1743 C.E. · Oil on canvas · National Gallery, London · 27.5 × 35.7 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Hogarth_Marriage_Mode_Scene2.jpg/330px-Hogarth_Marriage_Mode_Scene2.jpg" width="240" alt="The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode">
### Summary

A biting **satire of aristocratic marriage** produced for the expanding English middle class as engraved prints.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in mid-18th-century London during the **Industrial Revolution**, when the merchant bourgeoisie was rising and the aristocracy declining. Hogarth pioneered the **"modern moral subject**," modeling paintings for affordable engraved prints he sold at one shilling. *Marriage à la Mode* is a six-painting series about arranged marriages.
- **Function.** Painted as a **model for prints** so ordinary buyers could own the series. A satirical narrative skewering greed, arranged marriages, venereal disease, and aristocratic decadence.
- **Content.** The second scene of the cycle. **Viscount Squanderfield** slouches exhausted after a night out; his wife stretches coyly after her own entertainment; a steward throws up his hands at unpaid bills. A dog sniffs a **lady's bonnet** in the Viscount's pocket. Saints stare from paintings in the adjoining room, contrasting with the erotic canvas shrouded by a green curtain.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas, packed with **symbolic detail** (broken Greek head for impotence; mismatched Rococo-Chinoiserie-Palladian clock ridiculing fashionable taste). Three figures distributed across the canvas; receding rooms create perspectival depth that fits the satirist's narrative richness.

**Vocabulary:** satire · realism · "à la Mode"
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#91](#91--las-meninas) — Las Meninas (Diego Velázquez, c. 1656)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg/400px-Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg" width="180" alt="Las Meninas">

**Theme:** Paintings within paintings

> Both Las Meninas and The Tete a Tete are paintings depicting paintings. Their purposes are different, however. In The Tete a Tete, the paintings serve to contrast a past morality with the current debauchery of the couple. In Las Meninas, the angle of the figures and the easel are enigmatic, confusing the viewer about who is painting what, and yet there are even more paintings by Rubens on the walls.

---

#### → [#68](#68--the-arnolfini-portrait) — The Arnolfini Portrait (Jan van Eyck, 1434)

**Theme:** Small symbols within details, marriage

> Both the Arnolfini Portrait and The Tete a Tete are noticeable for their use of symbolism in each detail. In the Arnolfini Portrait, even such things as the oranges on the windowsill demonstrate wealth. In The Tete a Tete, the broken nose on the Classical Greek head can represent impotence. Both works also depict the union of a man and woman. However, The Tete a Tete is satirical and their relationship is a complete mess in comparison to the gentle joining of hands in The Arnolfini Portrait.

---

#### → [#101](#101--the-swing) — The Swing (Jean-Honoré Fragonard, 1767)

**Theme:** Infidelity

> The Swing is quite a naughty painting. The lady is being watched by her lover on the left, while being pushed by an older man who is unaware of the lover. Both The Tete a Tete and The Swing depict an unfaithful woman. However, The Swing seems more romantic in the idea as the angel sculpture holds its finger as if to keep the lover a secret. The Swing values pleasure and base human aspirations.

---

## #99 — Portrait of Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz

**Miguel Cabrera** · c. 1750 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museo Nacional de Historia, Castillo de Chapultepec, Mexico · 41½ × 32½ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Sor_Juana_Ines_de_la_Cruz_portrait.jpg/250px-Sor_Juana_Ines_de_la_Cruz_portrait.jpg" width="240" alt="Portrait of Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz">
### Summary

A **posthumous devotional portrait** of colonial Mexico's greatest intellectual — nun, poet, scholar, and proto-feminist.

- **Historical Context.** Painted ~55 years after Sor Juana's death (1651–1695). She was a criolla child prodigy who chose the **Jeronymite convent** in Mexico City to continue her studies, since marriage was the only alternative. She wrote plays and poetry and defended women's right to education. Cabrera, New Spain's leading 18th-century painter, worked from earlier portraits since he never met her.
- **Function.** Commemorative portrait commissioned by admirers. Asserts the compatibility of **theology** and **intellectual life** for women — Sor Juana as a model of sanctity and scholarship.
- **Content.** Sor Juana stands in her **library** reading a **work of St. Jerome** (her order's patron), a **rosary** in hand. She wears the Jeronymite habit with a large **escudo de monja** (nun's badge) at her chest depicting the **Annunciation**. Behind her, shelves of books — philosophy, theology, literature, science — testify to her learning.
- **Form.** Realistic oil portrait in a restricted palette (black, white, brown, red). Crisp geometric rendering of books and scholarly instruments; soft, slightly hazy modeling on face and habit.

**Vocabulary:** Jeronymites · escudo de monja · Annunciation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#174](#174--portrait-mask-mblo-of-moya-yanso) — Portrait Mask (Mblo) (Baule peoples, late 19th – early 20th c.)

**Theme:** Portraits

> These masks were a realistic but also abstract representation of someone's inner state of beauty. Since the portrait of Sor Juana Ines de la Cruz is a posthumous work, and the artist never actually met her, it could mean the portrait was also idealized.

---

#### → [#205](#205--portrait-of-sin-sukju) — Portrait of Sin Sukju (Korean, c. 15th century)

**Theme:** Portraits

> This portrait is similar to the one of Sor Juana Ines de la Cruz because it depicts a notable person in their traditional robes and clothing, similar to Sor Juana's habit of her nunery.

---

#### → [#242](#242--lying-with-the-wolf) — Lying with the Wolf (Kiki Smith, 2001) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Portraits

> Smith's work has been interpreted as a symbol of feminism. The wolf, an animal women and men are afraid of, is her companion in this piece. They are equal companions which has brought up the question if it is a critique on the social hierarchy today, where men and women don't feel equal. Similar to the portrait of Sor Juana, this piece challenges social norms and triumphs feminism.

---

## #100 — A Philosopher Giving a Lecture on the Orrery

**Joseph Wright of Derby** · c. 1763–1765 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Derby Museum and Art Gallery · 4′ 10″ × 6′ 8″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Orrery_Joseph_Wright.jpg/330px-Orrery_Joseph_Wright.jpg" width="240" alt="A Philosopher Giving a Lecture on the Orrery">
### Summary

The unofficial painter of the **British Enlightenment** turns a science demonstration into a secular altarpiece.

- **Historical Context.** Painted during the **Age of Enlightenment** in Derby, a Midlands hub of the Industrial Revolution where Wright was connected to the **Lunar Society** (Erasmus Darwin, Josiah Wedgwood, James Watt). As science displaced religion at the center of intellectual life, Wright translated Baroque devotional compositions into images of laboratory wonder.
- **Function.** A scene of **public scientific demonstration**. An *orrery* is a mechanical model of the solar system. The lamp at the center plays the sun; audience members — adults and children — react to the demonstration.
- **Content.** A **gray-haired philosopher** gestures over the orrery; a man takes notes; children lean in with wonder; a young man shields his eyes from the glow; a seated man listens rapt. The figures encircle the instrument like saints around a holy relic.
- **Form.** Dramatic **chiaroscuro** — hidden light source at the orrery's center throws faces in and out of shadow, straight from Caravaggio. Oil on canvas; warm amber light against deep blacks; composition echoes religious Nativities or *sacra conversazione*, secularized for the age of reason.

**Vocabulary:** Age of Enlightenment · "scientific thinking" · chiaroscuro · orrery
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#99](#99--portrait-of-sor-juana-inés-de-la-cruz) — Portrait of Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz (Miguel Cabrera, c. 1750)

**Theme:** Religion, intellectual freedom

> Depicts a woman who chose to become a nun to pursue her intellectual opportunities. Shows shift to Enlightenment ideals

---

#### → [#107](#107--la-grande-odalisque) — La Grande Odalisque (Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres, 1814)

**Theme:** Romanticism / Expressionism

> Shows new ideals being broken, just like the Enlightenment, except with emotion and expressionism.

---

#### → [#113](#113--the-stone-breakers) — The Stone Breakers (Gustave Courbet, 1849)

**Theme:** Realism / reality

> Shows the physical breaking of barriers, paralleling a similar theme but with a fundamental approach.

---

## #101 — The Swing

**Jean-Honoré Fragonard** · 1767 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Wallace Collection, London · 81 × 64.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/The_Swing_Fragonard.jpg/330px-The_Swing_Fragonard.jpg" width="240" alt="The Swing">
### Summary

The definitive **French Rococo** painting — playful, erotic, and wealth-drenched.

- **Historical Context.** Fragonard worked during the late reign of Louis XV, when aristocratic France indulged in lighthearted, ornate **Rococo** painting in retreat from Baroque grandeur. His patrons included Madame de Pompadour and Jean Chardin; this commission came from the **Baron de Saint-Julien**, Receiver General of the French Clergy, who specified the scene's erotic conceit himself.
- **Function.** A **private erotic cabinet picture** commissioned to illustrate a love triangle: Saint-Julien, his mistress, and an oblivious cuckolded husband or bishop.
- **Content.** A young woman soars on a swing in a wooded garden, kicking her **shoe** into the air; her lover lies hidden in the bushes at left, staring up her billowing skirt and pointing at her with his outstretched hat. An older, cuckolded man at right pushes the swing, unaware. Two **putti** witness the scene; one puts a finger to its lips (silence).
- **Form.** Asymmetrical Rococo composition. Pastel pink-and-green palette, feathery brushwork, atmospheric garden setting, abundance of ornate detail (ruffles, foliage, statuary). Strong diagonal lines sweep from lower left to upper right.

**Vocabulary:** oil on canvas · Rococo · putto
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#86](#86--henri-iv-receives-the-portrait-of-marie-de'-medici-from-the-marie-de'-medici-cycle) — Henri IV Receives the Portrait of Marie de' Medici (Peter Paul Rubens, 1621–1625)

**Theme:** Shows peace and prosperity Marie de' Medici brought to the Kingdom

> Like the Swing, it tells a story and has great detail on the bodies.

---

#### → [#99](#99--portrait-of-sor-juana-inés-de-la-cruz) — Portrait of Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz (Miguel Cabrera, c. 1750)

**Theme:** Considered the first feminist of the Americas, Sor Juana lived as a nun of the Jeronymite order in 17th-century Mexico.

> Both of the art work's focus are on a woman. Juana Ines de la Cruz is the main figure of the artwork and the lady swinging through the air is the center of that piece.

---

#### → [#100](#100--a-philosopher-giving-a-lecture-on-the-orrery) — A Philosopher Giving a Lecture on the Orrery (Joseph Wright of Derby, c. 1763–1765)

**Theme:** Strong internal light source

> Both works show a great amount of detail, and represent the time periods very well.

## #102 — Monticello

**Thomas Jefferson (architect)** · 1768–1809 C.E. · Brick, glass, stone, and wood · Charlottesville, Virginia · 110 × 87′9″ × 44′7″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Monticello_%28cropped%29.JPG/330px-Thomas_Jefferson%27s_Monticello_%28cropped%29.JPG" width="240" alt="Monticello">

### Summary

Jefferson's self-designed **Neoclassical** Virginia estate — his 40-year "essay in architecture."

- **Historical Context.** Built on a hilltop in Albemarle County, Virginia ("little mountain" in Italian). Jefferson inherited the land at 21 and began construction in 1768. Largely self-taught, he drew on extensive reading about **ancient Rome** and the **Italian Renaissance**. After his wife Martha's death, a trip to **France** exposed him to European architecture and inspired a near-total redesign; the second Monticello was roughly twice the size of the first and incorporated his inventions (revolving bookshelves, copying machines).
- **Function.** A **plantation house** for Jefferson and the enslaved people who labored there; he lived in the finished home only between 1808 and his death in 1826. Now restored by the Thomas Jefferson Foundation and operated as a museum.
- **Content.** 43 rooms total across the estate; 33 in the main house, with twelve cellars. Features a **dome** with an **oculus** skylight, 12 additional skylights, 8 fireplaces, north and south **pavilions**, and orchards, a vineyard, and an 18-acre ornamental grove.
- **Form.** **Neoclassical** design using Italian Renaissance principles; **Doric columns**, porticoes, pediment above the doorway, and a Palladian-inspired plan. Built from red bricks dug from the construction site itself.

**Vocabulary:** Neoclassical · columns · Enlightenment · dome · oculus · pavilion · porticoes · Palladian · pediment
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 2.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Parthenon (Iktinos and Kallikrates, 447–432 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/da/The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg/330px-The_Parthenon_in_Athens.jpg" width="180" alt="Parthenon">

**Theme:** Monumental buildings, Classical Architecture

> Both structures have Doric columns on the outside and a pediment supported by columns. Although the Parthenon was destroyed the original building had a pediment. Both are very similar in overall dimensions. The Monticello is 44 feet and 7 inches and the Parthenon was 45 feet high.

---

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Pompeii, c. 62–79 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Antigua_ciudad_de_Pompeya%2C_Italia%2C_2023-03-27%2C_DD_210.jpg/330px-Antigua_ciudad_de_Pompeya%2C_Italia%2C_2023-03-27%2C_DD_210.jpg" width="180" alt="House of the Vettii">

**Theme:** Residential Buildings, Classical Roman Architecture

> Both the House of Vettii and The Monticello were built and used for residential purposes. The Monticello and the House of Vettii consist of columns and have very extensive floor plans. Although the House of Vettii consists of a peristyle and the Monticello has a dome with an oculus open skylight, both structures have open skylights.

---

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Imperial Roman, 118–125 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Pantheon_%28Rome%29_-_Right_side_and_front.jpg/250px-Pantheon_%28Rome%29_-_Right_side_and_front.jpg" width="180" alt="Pantheon">

**Theme:** Monumental buildings, Classical Roman Architecture

> Both buildings consist of columns on the outside. Both have domes and pediments in the structure. The Pantheon was dedicated to the 7 gods and the Monticello was dedicated and designed by Thomas Jefferson. Both buildings have very intricate interiors. Both buildings were rebuilt at least twice.

---

## #103 — The Oath of the Horatii

**Jacques-Louis David** (Revolutionary and Napoleonic France) · 1784 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Musée du Louvre, Paris · 129.8 × 167.2 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Le_Serment_des_Horaces_-_Jacques-Louis_David_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_INV_3692_%3B_MR_1432.jpg/500px-Le_Serment_des_Horaces_-_Jacques-Louis_David_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_INV_3692_%3B_MR_1432.jpg" width="240" alt="The Oath of the Horatii">

### Summary

The founding manifesto of **French Neoclassicism** — a call to civic virtue on the eve of the French Revolution.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in late 18th-century France as artists were turning away from **Rococo**. David trained first under the Rococo painter **Boucher**, then under **Joseph-Marie Vien**. He went to Italy in 1775, filling 12 sketchbooks and studying Raphael; saw the ruins of **Pompeii** in 1779. Commissioned by **Louis XVI** in 1784; unveiled in David's Rome studio in 1785, then at the **Paris Salon**. David drew on **Poussin**'s *Rape of the Sabine Women* for the lictor figure.
- **Function.** An **exemplum virtutis** celebrating stoic patriotism — the brothers' restrained emotion, order, and patriotic duty supported the rising republican call to sacrifice for the state. Interpreted in the context of the approaching French Revolution.
- **Content.** The story from **Livy**: the three Horatii brothers pledge to their father to fight the Curiatii of Alba Longa to the death, representing Rome. The women — intermarried with the Curiatii — slump in grief at right, knowing they will lose either way. Three swords in the father's raised left hand; the sons' arms salute rigidly.
- **Form.** Life-size figures arranged as a frieze in the foreground; three sculptural groups placed before three arches in a Roman atrium. Men built from straight lines, vivid colors, geometric tension; women rendered in **sinuous** curves and muted tones. Stark, clinical light; space cut off by the arches in the manner of Roman relief sculpture.

**Vocabulary:** Atrium · exemplum virtutis · lictor · Salon · sinuous
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#108](#108--liberty-leading-the-people) — Liberty Leading the People (Eugène Delacroix, 1830)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg/500px-La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg" width="180" alt="Liberty Leading the People">

**Theme:** Liberty, patriotism, duty

> revolutionary France, pro-Republic

---

#### → [#76](#76--school-of-athens) — The School of Athens (Raphael, 1509–1511)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/%22The_School_of_Athens%22_by_Raffaello_Sanzio_da_Urbino.jpg/330px-%22The_School_of_Athens%22_by_Raffaello_Sanzio_da_Urbino.jpg" width="180" alt="The School of Athens">

**Theme:** Knowledge, wisdom

> use of ancient figures, mathematical

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Pompeii, c. 62–79 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Antigua_ciudad_de_Pompeya%2C_Italia%2C_2023-03-27%2C_DD_210.jpg/330px-Antigua_ciudad_de_Pompeya%2C_Italia%2C_2023-03-27%2C_DD_210.jpg" width="180" alt="House of the Vettii">

**Theme:** mythology, daily life

> Roman, represents transition in artistic style

## #104 — George Washington

**Jean-Antoine Houdon** · 1788–1792 C.E. · Marble · Richmond, Virginia · 6′ 2″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4a/Jean-Antoine_Houdon%27s_George_Washington.jpg/250px-Jean-Antoine_Houdon%27s_George_Washington.jpg" width="240" alt="George Washington">

### Summary

A life-size marble portrait of Washington as citizen-soldier, carved by a French master with unprecedented realism.

- **Historical Context.** Carved after the **American Revolutionary War** in honor of Washington's role. The **Virginia General Assembly** commissioned **Benjamin Harrison V** to hire a French sculptor; 20-year-old Houdon (trained at the Académie Royale, winner of the 1761 Prix de Rome) insisted on marble, not bronze. Unwilling to work from a 2D drawing, he traveled to **America in 1785** to take his own measurements and a bust of Washington. He then completed the piece over four years in Paris. One of the only works of a U.S. hero made by a foreigner — Americans deliberately sought a foreign artist.
- **Function.** Commemorates the Revolutionary War as a "monument of affection and gratitude." Presents Washington as the ideal American — a soldier and government figure who is still one of the people, seen in his casual attire and accompanying cane. A conscious rejection of the robes/regalia conventions of older civilizations.
- **Content.** Washington stands accurately portrayed, his sword not in his hand (non-aggression). At his hip are **thirteen rods (fasces)** symbolizing the unity and strength of the United States. Behind him is a **plow** — referencing Washington returning home to his farm after the war rather than to another position of power.
- **Form.** Reduction-carved marble, exactly 6′ 2″ (Washington's true height), in **contrapposto** looking toward the viewer's left. Common period military uniform; leans casually against a pillar with cane in hand. Considered one of the most accurate depictions of Washington ever made.

**Vocabulary:** fasces · equestrian · duality
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 1.4, 2.2, 3.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros / Spear Bearer (Polykleitos, c. 450–440 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/02_10_2024_Photo_Paolo_Villa-Napoli-mann-Museo_Archeologico-FO232526tris-Arte_Greca_copia_romana-Policleto_Doriforo_Polykleitos_Doryphoros_from_Pompeii-marmo_marble_carrara.jpg/250px-thumbnail.jpg" width="180" alt="Doryphoros">

**Theme:** Form, sculpture

> contrapposto idealized man carved statue

#### → [#108](#108--liberty-leading-the-people) — Liberty Leading the People (Eugène Delacroix, 1830)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg/500px-La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg" width="180" alt="Liberty Leading the People">

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg/500px-Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg" width="180" alt="Self-Portrait as a Soldier">

## #106 — Y no hai remedio (And There's Nothing to Be Done), from Los Desastres de la Guerra, plate 15

**Francisco de Goya** · 1810–1823 C.E. (published 1863) · Etching, drypoint, burin, and burnishing · Metropolitan Museum of Art · 14 × 16.7 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Goya_Disasters_War_15.jpg/330px-Goya_Disasters_War_15.jpg" width="240" alt="Y no hai remedio (And There's Nothing to Be Done), from Los Desastres de la Guerra, plate 15">
### Summary

A **print protest** against Napoleonic occupation — one of 82 bitter etchings chronicling the Peninsular War.

- **Historical Context.** Made during the **Peninsular War** (1808–1814), after Napoleon's brother Joseph was installed as king of Spain. **Francisco de Goya** — former court painter to Charles IV — secretly produced the *Disasters of War* series (82 plates) between 1810 and 1820; they were too inflammatory to publish in his lifetime and only appeared posthumously in **1863**.
- **Function.** Protest art. Indicts the **atrocities committed on civilians** during the French occupation and Spanish reprisals, and the pointless cruelty of war generally. "**And there is nothing to be done**" — the caption — underscores helpless inevitability.
- **Content.** A man in ragged white, **blindfolded and tied to a pole**, awaits execution by firing squad; a mangled corpse lies at his feet; more bound victims line up behind. A row of **rifles** projects into the scene from the right, aimed at the central figure.
- **Form.** Black-and-white etching with dense cross-hatching (burin, drypoint, burnishing) producing rich shadow and tenebrous atmosphere. The tied figure in near-Christ-like white contrasts starkly with encompassing darkness; extreme depth suggests the slaughter continues indefinitely.

**Vocabulary:** drypoint · burnisher · etching · The Disasters of War
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#203](#203--night-attack-on-the-sanjo-palace) — Night Attack on the Sanjo Palace (Kamakura Japan, c. 1250–1300)

**Theme:** mass of bodies, war scene, expressions of horror

> Both pieces are depictions of war and death, especially focusing on the permanence of the lives lost in these attacks. These works both center around the invasion of an enemy that disturbs the peace of the individuals that originally reside there.

---

#### → [#119](#119--the-burghers-of-calais) — The Burghers of Calais (Auguste Rodin, 1884–1895)

**Theme:** consequences of war, weak, tortured bodies, invasions by foreigners

> Both pieces illustrate how war and invasions affects the people of a country. They both demonstrate the vulnerability exhibited by individuals that are close to their death and know that is coming. Figures in both works are wearing ragged clothing and look malnourished.

---

#### → [#108](#108--liberty-leading-the-people) — Liberty Leading the People (Eugène Delacroix, 1830)

**Theme:** war, dead bodies, freedom, sacrifice, revolution

> Both works focus on an illuminated central figure that represents a hero. The paintings greatly showcase the losses that war comes with and the weapons that are used to do that (rifles).

---

## #107 — La Grande Odalisque

**Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres** · 1814 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Louvre, Paris · 35″ × 64″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/La_Grande_Odalisque.jpg/330px-La_Grande_Odalisque.jpg" width="240" alt="La Grande Odalisque">
### Summary

A **Neoclassical-to-Romantic** hybrid — a deliberately anatomically distorted nude steeped in **Orientalist** fantasy.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Caroline Murat**, Napoleon's sister and Queen of Naples. Ingres was trained by **Jacques-Louis David** and considered himself a Neoclassicist, but his elongated figures and fascination with "Oriental" subjects anticipate **Romanticism**. The painting scandalized the 1819 Salon; critics complained the odalisque had several extra vertebrae.
- **Function.** Private **erotic commission** for a royal patron. The subject capitalized on European fascination with Ottoman harem culture while providing cover for an otherwise transgressive nude.
- **Content.** A **nude concubine** reclines on a bed of satin sheets, her back to the viewer, head turned to gaze calmly over her shoulder. She holds a **peacock-feather fan**; a turban, pearl jewelry, and a **hookah** anchor her in a fantasy harem setting.
- **Form.** Smooth, porcelain flesh; cool jewel-tone silks; **dark tenebrist background**. Ingres deliberately lengthens the spine and limbs to heighten the **serpentine line** and eroticism — privileging beauty over anatomy. Deep shadow half-veils her face.

**Vocabulary:** Romanticism · Neoclassicism · oil painting · Oriental · tenebrism · portraiture
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

**Theme:** Portraiture, Greek mythology, nude female figure

> Focuses on the nude female form, portraiture

**Theme:** Nude female figures

## #108 — Liberty Leading the People

**Eugène Delacroix** · 1830 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Louvre, Paris · 260 × 325 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a7/Liberty_Leading_the_People.jpg/330px-Liberty_Leading_the_People.jpg" width="240" alt="Liberty Leading the People">
### Summary

A **French Romantic** allegory that became the iconic image of popular revolution.

- **Historical Context.** Painted to celebrate the **July Revolution of 1830** that overthrew King Charles X (brother of the guillotined Louis XVI) and installed the "Citizen King" **Louis-Philippe I**. Delacroix — a **Rubéniste** (color-first) as opposed to David's **Poussiniste** (line-first) heirs — was a leader of the French Romantic movement. The government purchased the painting but hid it from public view for fear of its revolutionary power.
- **Function.** Political painting: memorial, **propaganda**, and national-pride piece. Depicts the French people of all classes uniting against tyranny.
- **Content.** A bare-breasted allegorical **Liberty** in a **Phrygian cap** strides over the dead, holding the **tricolor flag** in one hand and a musket with bayonet in the other. Behind her: a bourgeois man in a top hat (possibly Delacroix himself), a street urchin with dueling pistols (inspiration for *Les Misérables*' Gavroche), and a worker in laborer's garb — the revolution as cross-class alliance. Dead soldiers and civilians foreground the cost.
- **Form.** Pyramidal composition anchoring on the flag's red and Liberty's white dress. Bright highlights against a **smoky, atmospheric background**; vigorous brushwork; contrast and color (not Neoclassical line) carry emotional weight. Scale heightens heroic drama.

**Vocabulary:** Neoclassicism · French Romanticism · July Revolution of 1830 · Phrygian cap
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#109](#109--the-oxbow-view-from-mount-holyoke-northampton-massachusetts-after-a-thunderstorm) — The Oxbow (Thomas Cole, 1836)

**Theme:** American nationalism

> Oxbow points to the future expansion of the United States, Liberty Leading the People points to the future liberty of the French people

---

#### → [#111](#111--slave-ship-slavers-throwing-overboard-the-dead-and-dying-typhoon-coming-on) — Slave Ship (J.M.W. Turner, 1840)

**Theme:** Nature, Landscape

> Uses the contrast of colors to create a strong sense of emotion and turbulence

---

#### → [#118](#118--the-valley-of-mexico-from-the-hillside-of-santa-isabel-el-valle-de-méxico-desde-el-cerro-de-santa-isabel) — The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel (José María Velasco, 1882)

**Theme:** Nature, Landscape, Nationalism

> Both paintings reflect an important period in the development of national identity

## #109 — The Oxbow (View from Mount Holyoke, Northampton, Massachusetts, after a Thunderstorm)

**Thomas Cole** · 1836 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Metropolitan Museum of Art, New York · 51.5 × 76 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/The_Oxbow.jpg/330px-The_Oxbow.jpg" width="240" alt="The Oxbow (View from Mount Holyoke, Northampton, Massachusetts, after a Thunderstorm)">
### Summary

A foundational **Hudson River School** landscape dramatizing America's choice between **wilderness and civilization**.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by **Thomas Cole**, founder of the **Hudson River School** of American landscape painting, during a pause from his allegorical *Course of Empire* series (commissioned by patron **Luman Reed**). Painted at the height of **Manifest Destiny**, the belief in America's divinely ordained westward expansion.
- **Function.** A **Romantic landscape** meant for display at the National Academy of Design's annual exhibition. Reed's commission let Cole return to what he did best while working out national-identity themes in miniature.
- **Content.** A diagonal composition splits the canvas in two: the **left** shows storm-swept **wilderness** — dark clouds, a lightning-struck tree — while the **right** reveals a sunlit, cultivated **Connecticut River valley** wrapped around the iconic oxbow bend. A tiny **self-portrait** of Cole, painting at his easel, sits in the foreground of the wilderness side.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; dramatic **tonal contrast** between storm and sun; atmospheric perspective pulls the eye deep into the valley. The curving river, the diagonal cloud line, and the lone tree split anchor a careful moral geometry.

**Vocabulary:** Hudson River School · Manifest Destiny · Romanticism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#111](#111--slave-ship-slavers-throwing-overboard-the-dead-and-dying-typhoon-coming-on) — Slave Ship (J.M.W. Turner, 1840)

**Theme:** Landscape, Sublime

> The Slave Ship is also a landscape scene that displays the grandeur of nature, similar to the power of nature shown by the split tree

---

#### → [#120](#120--the-starry-night) — The Starry Night (Vincent van Gogh, 1889)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Starry Night">

**Theme:** Landscape

> Starry Night by Vincent Van Gogh is also a landscape scene, showing the city of Saint-Remy.

---

#### → [#125](#125--mont-sainte-victoire) — Mont Sainte-Victoire (Paul Cézanne, 1902–1904)

**Theme:** Landscape

> Mont Sainte-Victoire by Paul Cezanne is also a landscape painting, depicting the Mont Sainte-Victoire in Paul Cezanne's hometown.

## #110 — Still Life in Studio

**Louis-Jacques-Mandé Daguerre** · 1837 C.E. · Daguerreotype · French Photographic Society, Paris · ~6.5 × 8.5 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/5/52/Daguerreotype_Daguerre_Atelier_1837.jpg" width="240" alt="Still Life in Studio">

### Summary

Daguerre's earliest surviving daguerreotype — a test image that announced photography's potential as both science and art form.

- **Historical Context.** Created in the infancy of photography, ten years after Niépce's **View from the Window at Le Gras** (c. 1827), which required an 8-hour exposure. Acquainted with Niépce, Daguerre sought to solve the issues of long exposures and light sensitivity. Using a **copper plate with silver coating** and halogens like iodine and bromine, Daguerre captured crisp images in **15–30 minutes**, later preserved with mercury and a sodium compound. The Daguerreian Process was publicly released in **1839**; exposures dropped below a minute by 1842.
- **Function.** As Daguerre's earliest surviving daguerreotype, most likely a **test** of his photographic process. The diverse textures (fabric, stucco, plaster, painting, metal, wood, wicker) let him analyze his process's ability to capture tonal range. The two stucco heads substituted for real people, since portraiture was not yet possible at those exposure times. Some interpret the inclusion of sculpture and a framed painting as an argument for photography as a new **art form**.
- **Content.** An arrangement of inanimate studio objects with no living figures (though two stucco human heads appear). A test composition of textures and tones.
- **Form.** Overlapping arrangement creates negative space and perspective depth — the curtain is blocked by a wicker pot holder, placed behind the stucco heads. Intense **chiaroscuro** (approaching tenebrism): light from the window throws sharp shadows on the heads. Diagonal lines on the left panel direct attention to the stucco heads as focal point.

**Vocabulary:** Daguerreotype (Daguerreian Process) · camera obscura
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 2.3, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#114](#114--nadar-raising-photography-to-the-height-of-art) — Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art (Honoré Daumier, 1862)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg/500px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg" width="180" alt="Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art">

**Theme:** The popularization of photography in the mid-1800s, whether or not photography could be considered an art

> The Daguerreian Process, invented by Daguerre and used to make Still Life in Studio, helped to popularize photography as shown in Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art, as daguerreotypes produced clear images and later improvements reduced exposure time significantly.

---

#### → [#117](#117--the-horse-in-motion) — The Horse in Motion (Eadweard Muybridge, 1878)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington%2C%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford%3B_driven_by_C._Marvin%2C_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track%2C_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg/500px-The_Horse_in_motion._%22Abe_Edgington%2C%22_owned_by_Leland_Stanford%3B_driven_by_C._Marvin%2C_trotting_at_a_2-24_gait_over_the_Palo_Alto_track%2C_15th_June_1878_LOC_13624627695.jpg" width="180" alt="The Horse in Motion">

**Theme:** Rapid advancements in photography (especially shutter speed), using photography to capture moments instantaneously.

> Daguerre contributed greatly to the advancement of photography with his daguerreotypes and the reduction of exposure time, which eventually reached a point as in The Horse in Motion where photography could capture actual movement with how fast shutter speeds had gotten.

---

#### → [#96](#96--fruit-and-insects) — Fruit and Insects (Rachel Ruysch, 1711)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Rachel_Ruysch_-_Still-Life_with_Fruit_and_Insects_-_WGA20557.jpg" width="180" alt="Fruit and Insects">

**Theme:** Vanitas painting, the transience of life

> Daguerre chose to depict a scene of still life with inanimate objects rather than a landscape or portrait, and the still life had been introduced as a subject by Dutch and Flemish painters such as Ruysch.

## #111 — Slave Ship (Slavers Throwing Overboard the Dead and Dying, Typhoon Coming On)

**Joseph Mallord William Turner** (British) · 1840 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museum of Fine Arts, Boston · 2.98 × 4.02 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Slave-ship.jpg/330px-Slave-ship.jpg" width="240" alt="Slave Ship">

### Summary

A **Romantic** seascape turning the 1781 **Zong massacre** into an abolitionist plea through the sublime wrath of nature.

- **Historical Context.** **Romanticism** reacted against the Enlightenment's logical, emotionally-removed mindset — instead chasing strong emotions like disgust, horror, awe, and transcendence. Turner was part of the British **Royal Academy**, known for landscapes that manipulated light to create otherworldliness. He was prompted to create this work after reading about the slave ship **The Zong** in Thomas Clarkson's book: in 1781, The Zong's captain threw sick and dying enslaved people overboard to collect insurance money. Slaves had been freed in England since 1833, but exceptions still permitted it.
- **Function.** Painted to speak in favor of the **abolitionist movement**. Turner inscribed his poem "The Fallacies of Hope," expressing the wrath of nature/the universe against those engaging in the slave trade — echoed in the vivid blood-red horizon and chaos of sea and sky.
- **Content.** Depicts the historical event aboard **The Zong**, whose captain discarded sick and dying enslaved people because insurance paid only for accidental drownings, not sickness from poor conditions.
- **Form.** Dramatic color creates awe and otherworldly nature. Light represents God; the brewing storm sky shows the wrath incurred. Rapid brushwork evokes the chaos of the storm. The ship and figures in the ocean are small and hard to make out — the viewer is overcome with the power of the sea and sky.

**Vocabulary:** Romanticism · abolition · The Zong
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#109](#109--the-oxbow-view-from-mount-holyoke-northampton-massachusetts-after-a-thunderstorm) — The Oxbow (Thomas Cole, 1836)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Cole_Thomas_The_Oxbow_%28The_Connecticut_River_near_Northampton_1836%29.jpg/330px-Cole_Thomas_The_Oxbow_%28The_Connecticut_River_near_Northampton_1836%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Oxbow">

**Theme:** Strength of nature

> Both paintings show the strength of nature, based on real things (Turner's historical event and Cole's on an actual place), romantic.

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, c. 1000 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Fan_Kuan_-_Travelers_Among_Mountains_and_Streams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Fan_Kuan_-_Travelers_Among_Mountains_and_Streams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Travelers Among Mountains and Streams">

**Theme:** Shows how man is vulnerable to nature

> Humbling portrait of mankind. Both paintings show how man can be small and unimportant because nature has the upper hand.

---

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, 1830–1833)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg/500px-Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg" width="180" alt="Under the Wave off Kanagawa">

**Theme:** Shows nature as a personified force.

> Presents nature as personified capable of extracting revenger or having volition.

## #112 — Palace of Westminster (Houses of Parliament)

**Charles Barry and Augustus W. N. Pugin (architects)** · late middle ages, rebuilt 1840–1870 C.E. · Limestone masonry and glass · London, England (along the Thames) · 3.24 hectares (8 acres)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Houses_of_Parliament_in_2022_%28cropped%29.jpg/330px-Houses_of_Parliament_in_2022_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Palace of Westminster">

### Summary

A **Gothic Revival** seat of British parliamentary democracy, rebuilt on a site used for government since the 13th century.

- **Historical Context.** The first royal palace on the site (Thorney Island) was built in the late 11th century and served as a royal residence until a 1512 fire. The palace has housed **Parliament** since the 13th century and was also home to the Royal Courts of Justice. On **October 16, 1834**, most of the buildings burned down (though Westminster Hall, the Cloisters of St Stephen's, and the Jewel Tower survived). A public competition in 1836 required **Gothic or Elizabethan** style — both representing conservative values over the era's popular Neoclassicism. **Charles Barry** (experienced in classical architecture) won with **Pugin** (a Gothic expert) assisting. Mostly completed by 1870.
- **Function.** Meeting place of the **House of Commons and House of Lords**. The Palace — and especially the Elizabethan tower (Big Ben) — is a symbol of parliamentary democracy.
- **Content.** Over 1,100 rooms, 100 staircases, and 3 miles of passageways across four floors. Three main towers: **Victoria Tower** (tallest, above the Sovereign's entrance), the octagonal Central Tower (for ventilation), and **Elizabeth Tower** (four-faced clock bell tower). Three separate entrances for the Monarch, Parliament members, and the public. Extended into the Thames by reclaiming ~8 acres of land.
- **Form.** Built in the **Perpendicular Gothic** style, popular in the 15th century and during the 19th-century Gothic Revival. Sand-colored **limestone from Anston quarry** was cheap and ideal for elaborate carvings. Numerous windows and towers give the structure a light, airy appearance.

**Vocabulary:** tower · pointed arch · ribbed vault · arcade
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#60](#60--chartres-cathedral) — Chartres Cathedral (High Gothic, begun c. 1145; rebuilt c. 1194–1220)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Chartres_-_Cath%C3%A9drale_%282012.01%29_08.jpg/330px-Chartres_-_Cath%C3%A9drale_%282012.01%29_08.jpg" width="180" alt="Chartres Cathedral">

> Gothic architecture, windows/light, ornate exterior, towers

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Versailles-Chateau-Jardins02.jpg/330px-Versailles-Chateau-Jardins02.jpg" width="180" alt="Palace of Versailles">

**Theme:** Monarchy, Power

> Created for the government's use, built upon previous historic sites, incorporates numerous gardens, luxurious and intended to be impressive

#### → [#65](#65--alhambra) — Alhambra (Nasrid dynasty, 1354–1391)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Dawn_Charles_V_Palace_Alhambra_Granada_Andalusia_Spain.jpg/330px-Dawn_Charles_V_Palace_Alhambra_Granada_Andalusia_Spain.jpg" width="180" alt="Alhambra">

**Theme:** Power

## #113 — The Stone Breakers

**Gustave Courbet** · 1849 C.E. (destroyed in 1945) · Oil on canvas · Gemäldegalerie, Dresden · 5′ 5″ × 8′ 5″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg/330px-Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg" width="240" alt="The Stone Breakers">

### Summary

A foundational **Realist** painting dignifying rural laborers on a monumental scale.

- **Historical Context.** Part of the **Realist** movement. Courbet was inspired after driving on the highway and noticing two men breaking stones; struck by this true expression of poverty, he later arranged for them to sit in his studio. The painting was made only one year after **Marx and Engels** wrote *The Communist Manifesto* (1848). Poverty was extremely prevalent during this time.
- **Function.** Bringing awareness to the brutality behind severe poverty — an extremely realistic depiction of poor people's lives in the 1850s. Fine detail and color scheme allowed wider audiences to understand rural hardship, and emphasize the isolation of being physically and economically trapped.
- **Content.** A daily-life piece recording the abuse and deprivation that was common in mid-century French rural life. The figures break stones down to rubble to be used for paving. "The figures were born poor and will continue being poor for the rest of their existence." The realism allows them to be treated with basic honesty and sincerity.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas. The hill reaches the top of the canvas everywhere except the upper-right corner, where a tiny patch of bright blue sky appears. Brushwork is rough, uncommon for the time. Courbet refused to focus only on the parts of the image that would usually receive the most attention — attending to faces and rock equally. Dominant hues of brown and ochre.

**Vocabulary:** stone · poverty · paving · Realism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#92](#92--woman-holding-a-balance) — Woman Holding a Balance (Johannes Vermeer, c. 1664)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Woman Holding a Balance">

**Theme:** Realism

> Real life depictions and realistic attention to detail is seen in both the Women Holding a Balance and The Stonebreakers.

---

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Pieter_Bruegel_the_Elder_-_Hunters_in_the_Snow_%28Winter%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Hunters in the Snow">

**Theme:** Daily Life Scenes

> Both pieces emphasize the daily lives of people at work and the difficulties behind harsh labor.

---

#### → [#121](#121--the-coiffure) — The Coiffure (Mary Cassatt, 1890–1891)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg/250px-Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg" width="180" alt="The Coiffure">

**Theme:** Realism

> Both artworks shine on a topic that is often brushed over by the common public. For instance, The Coiffure is shining on the importance of women and The Stonebreakers shines the light on the brutality of poverty

## #114 — Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art

**Honoré Daumier** · 1862 C.E. · Lithograph · Museum of Fine Arts, Boston · 27.2 × 22.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg/250px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg" width="240" alt="Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art">

### Summary

A satirical lithograph mocking the claim that **photography could be considered fine art**.

- **Historical Context.** Daumier (b. 1808) was a French printmaker and caricaturist who made his career skewering political and social figures — he first worked for the comic journal *La Caricature* — in a 19th-century France defined by political instability and tension between rulers and public. The print targets **Nadar** (Gaspard-Félix Tournachon), the real photographer famous for taking the **first aerial photographs from a hot air balloon**.
- **Function.** Published as social critique: photography was still a new, contested medium, and Daumier uses humor to argue that it does not belong alongside traditional art.
- **Content.** Nadar in top hat and coat, nearly tumbling out of his balloon's basket as he works a camera; his hat flies off and hair goes wild. Every rectangular building below is labeled "Photographie," and the balloon is labeled "Photographie Nadar"; the buildings repeat and blur toward the horizon.
- **Form.** Small black-and-white lithograph with a soft, sketchy quality and slight shading. The figure and buildings are exaggerated and cartoonish rather than naturalistic — the distortion drives the joke.

**Vocabulary:** Photography · Nadar · camera obscura · daguerreotype · satire · lithograph

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#144](#144--fountain-second-version) — Fountain (Marcel Duchamp, 1950/1917)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Marcel_Duchamp%2C_1917%2C_Fountain%2C_photograph_by_Alfred_Stieglitz.jpg/250px-Marcel_Duchamp%2C_1917%2C_Fountain%2C_photograph_by_Alfred_Stieglitz.jpg" width="180" alt="Fountain">

**Theme:** Dada, satire, critique, playful

> Both pieces use a rather everyday form of art (black and white print, storebought urinal) to convey some critique of the state of art. They both question the boundaries of art and whether new forms fit into the category. Both also use playful humor to get their concepts across.

---

#### → [#231](#231--untitled-#228-from-the-history-portraits-series) — Untitled #228 (Cindy Sherman, 1990)

**Theme:** Photography, traditions, the definition of art, change over time

> Nadar is critiquing the use of photography as art, and Cindy Sherman's photography attempts to pass it off as so. Cindy Sherman also stretches/plays with the boundaries of what can be considered art, often mimicking historical/classical pieces like she did in Untitled (#228).

---

#### → [#147](#147--marilyn-diptych) — Marilyn Diptych (Andy Warhol, 1962)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/87/Marilyndiptych.jpg" width="180" alt="Marilyn Diptych">

**Theme:** Cartoon, commercialization, industrialization

> The Marilyn Diptych is similar to Nadar in that both make use of simplistic cartoon-like techniques in order to make a clear point. Both also deal with themes of commercialization and industrialization and their effects on society.

---

## #115 — Olympia

**Édouard Manet** · 1863 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Musée d'Orsay, Paris · 51 in × 75 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/500px-Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg" width="240" alt="Olympia">

### Summary

A direct, confrontational nude that challenged academic taste by swapping the goddess for a **Parisian prostitute**.

- **Historical Context.** Manet painted within an artistic era dominated by the **French Royal Academy** and its standards of taste, and designed the work as a response to the Academy's lofty ideals for the female nude. He was also living through rapid industrialization and population growth, which brought cultural changes he wanted to represent rather than ignore.
- **Function.** Intended to **challenge artistic conventions** in style, subject matter, and theme. The broad, quick, visible brushstrokes stand against the academy's preference for invisible handling, and the choice to show a Parisian prostitute with her maid rather than a goddess or odalisque was considered too vulgar for exhibition.
- **Content.** A prostitute and her maid, surrounded by signs of prostitution and the seedy underbelly of modern Paris: the **cat at Olympia's feet**, the name *Olympia* itself, and the ornaments she wears all carry that association. The **maid** — rendered through the racial stereotypes of the time — stands in the background presenting flowers.
- **Form.** The nude figure is very pale and positioned atop pale sheets, contrasting starkly with the **dark background** and the maid's complexion. The effect is amplified by the painting's **shallow depth**, which seems to cover only a few feet.

**Vocabulary:** Realism · Salon · Impressionism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#80](#80--venus-of-urbino) — Venus of Urbino (Titian, 1534)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tiziano_-_Venere_di_Urbino_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Tiziano_-_Venere_di_Urbino_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Venus of Urbino">

**Theme:** Nude women

> Pose of Olympia took inspiration from the Venus of Urbino

**Theme:** Nude women

#### → [#113](#113--the-stone-breakers) — The Stone Breakers (Gustave Courbet, 1849)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg/330px-Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg" width="180" alt="The Stone Breakers">

## #116 — The Saint-Lazare Station

**Claude Monet** · 1877 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Musée d'Orsay, Paris · 21.4 × 28.9 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg/330px-La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg" width="240" alt="The Saint-Lazare Station">

### Summary

An Impressionist study of a bustling Parisian train station as a site of **modern life, light, and atmosphere**.

- **Historical Context.** Monet was a founding artist of **Impressionism**, which emphasized small brushstrokes, accurate depictions of lighting, and the passage of time — and was at the time dismissed as frivolous compared to the chiaroscuro of traditional European painting. The Gare Saint-Lazare was a symbol of modern Paris, a site favored by forward-looking artists (**Manet** also depicted it). The series was made the year after Monet's wife **Camille** was diagnosed with tuberculosis. Critics coined the name "Impressionists" partly from the paintings' unfinished look.
- **Function.** The set of paintings of the Gare Saint-Lazare were painted as a **study of light throughout the day** on a single subject, the train station.
- **Content.** Trains, workers, and everyday passengers at the Gare Saint-Lazare, with **billowing smoke** from the trains covering most of the scene. Different views of the station can be identified precisely by where Monet stood. Smoke, skies, and lighting take on blue hues ranging to yellow depending on time of day and weather — the smoke, light, and color become the real focus.
- **Form.** Small, quick brushstrokes and a wide variance in color to portray lighting with depth. Oil on canvas. Objects and figures are not drawn in great detail — Monet focuses on general shape and lighting, yielding near-abstract human figures.

**Vocabulary:** Impressionism · plein air
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#118](#118--the-valley-of-mexico-from-the-hillside-of-santa-isabel-el-valle-de-méxico-desde-el-cerro-de-santa-isabel) — The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel (José María Velasco, 1882)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_-_The_Valley_of_Mexico_from_the_Santa_Isabel_Mountain_Range_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel">

**Theme:** Land + history of the land (of Mexico)

> Both paintings are studies of natural features. Velasco and Monet's paintings both studied cloud formations, but Velasco focused more on botanical studies while Monet focused on the light and shading.

---

#### → [#113](#113--the-stone-breakers) — The Stone Breakers (Gustave Courbet, 1849)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg/330px-Gustave_Courbet_-_The_Stonebreakers_-_WGA05457.jpg" width="180" alt="The Stone Breakers">

**Theme:** Real life

> Both Monet and Courbet attempted to paint what people would see and work at in their daily lives, albeit perhaps of different social classes. Both used human figures and the landscape to show a sense of motion and business.

---

#### → [#121](#121--the-coiffure) — The Coiffure (Mary Cassatt, 1890–1891)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg/250px-Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg" width="180" alt="The Coiffure">

**Theme:** Domestic scene

> Both Monet and Cassatt were part of the Impressionist movement. The figures portrayed in both paintings are naturalistic, but Monet's is more stylized and abstract, painting only the bare movements of a person. Both are part of a series of artwork, and utilize a pastel color scheme.
