## #117 — The Horse in Motion

**Eadweard Muybridge** · 1878 C.E. · Albumen print · San Francisco · 24 photographs

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Eadweard_Muybridge-Sallie_Gardner_1878.jpg/250px-Eadweard_Muybridge-Sallie_Gardner_1878.jpg" width="240" alt="The Horse in Motion">

### Summary

A motion-capture photographic series that settled whether a galloping horse ever goes **fully airborne** — and helped lay the groundwork for cinema.

- **Historical Context.** Muybridge moved to the United States from a small English town in 1850, worked briefly as a bookseller, then took up photography. He rose to fame in 1872 helping **Leland Stanford** — politician and co-founder of Stanford University — settle the popular debate over whether a horse goes completely airborne during a gallop. Using new motion-capture technology, Muybridge produced a never-before-seen moving-image series; up until then artists had always depicted galloping horses with all four feet outstretched. He later copied the silhouettes onto a disc viewed through his **zoopraxiscope**, an ancestor of the movie projector, and his work is foundational to modern cinema.
- **Function.** Created to capture the running motion of a horse frame by frame and resolve Stanford's $25,000 wager. Muybridge set up a line of glass-plate cameras along the racetrack at Stanford's **Palo Alto Stock Farm** (now Stanford University), each shutter triggered by a **tripwire** as the horse passed. The resulting photographs confirmed Stanford's bet.
- **Content.** Shows **Sallie Gardner**, one of Stanford's Standardbred trotting horses, airborne at the trot. Due to low camera quality, the horse and rider appear only as dark shapes against a white background. In total, 24 images capture the horse in various stages of its running cycle.
- **Form.** A sequential documentation of a moving subject. The horse stays centered in frame even at top speed thanks to the tripwire system. Each image shows a distinct position; the gait resolves into a 1-2-3-4 beat — left hind, left front, right hind, right front.

**Vocabulary:** zoopraxiscope · gallop
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#110](#110--still-life-in-studio) — Still Life in Studio (Louis-Jacques-Mandé Daguerre, 1837)

**Theme:** Photography, composition

> Photography as an art. Showcase technological advancements in photography.

---

#### → [#114](#114--nadar-raising-photography-to-the-height-of-art) — Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art (Honoré Daumier, 1862)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg/250px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg" width="180" alt="Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art">

**Theme:** Collodion method (glass plate must be kept wet), satire

> Centered on the use of new photographic methods. Influenced by what was going on in that time period.

---

#### → [#127](#127--the-steerage) — The Steerage (Alfred Stieglitz, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Alfred_Stieglitz_-_The_Steerage_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Alfred_Stieglitz_-_The_Steerage_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="The Steerage">

**Theme:** Photography, evolution of photography

> Capturing moving subjects. Photograph with impact on society. Milestone in photography.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

**Prompt:** Based on the form, function, and/or context of Muybridge's Horse in Motion, infer or explain the possible intentions for creating a specific work of art.

> The horse is in the center of every photograph. The image set depicts the progression of a horse galloping. Because each image captures a still moment in time, it allows the viewer to more closely observe the gait of a horse in motion. During the time period of creation, how or even if a horse went completely airborne while running was a highly debated question. So, the artist's intention was to precisely document a horse's gallop for scientific observation and to settle a longstanding inquiry.

---

## #119 — The Burghers of Calais

**Auguste Rodin** · 1884–1895 C.E. · Bronze · Place du Soldat Inconnu, Calais, France · 82½ × 94 × 95 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/Statue_bourgeois_calais_rodin.jpg/250px-Statue_bourgeois_calais_rodin.jpg" width="240" alt="The Burghers of Calais">

### Summary

A commemorative civic bronze that rejects heroic convention to honor six citizens' willingness to die for their city.

- **Historical Context.** During the **Hundred Years' War**, Calais was besieged by **Edward III of England**; after eleven months, the city decided to surrender. Terms required six of its most prominent leaders to come out barefoot in sackcloth, holding the keys to the city, to be executed. When they appeared, Edward III's wife **Philippa** implored him to spare them, and he did.
- **Function.** Commemorative: acknowledging the sacrifice the six were willing to make for their city. The work is meant to be seen up close and personally — Rodin intentionally set the figures low to the ground so they feel human and real.
- **Content.** Rodin was commissioned to commemorate the six citizens (or **burghers**) who offered to sacrifice their lives for Calais. The commissioners had wanted a single heroic figure to represent the group, but Rodin deemed it more appropriate to represent all six, depicting them **as they were leaving the city** heading toward execution, not as heroic figures after their reprieve.
- **Form.** The six burghers are arranged in a rough circle, allowing viewing from multiple angles. They are about life size and set as a group low to the ground, approachable and personal. Each face conveys a different emotion, exhibiting the feelings the men must have felt walking toward what they believed was their death.

**Vocabulary:** burgher · lost wax casting · Hundred Years' War
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#8](#8--stonehenge) — Stonehenge (Neolithic Europe, c. 2500–1600 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg/250px-Stonehenge2007_07_30.jpg" width="180" alt="Stonehenge">

**Theme:** Sacrifice, ritual

> Both are connected to sacrifice and ritual, and both are arranged in rough circles that can be viewed from multiple angles.

---

#### → [#180](#180--veranda-post-of-enthroned-king-and-senior-wife-*opo-ogoga*) — Veranda Post of enthroned king and senior wife (Olowe of Ise, c. 1910–1914)

**Theme:** Commemoration, rulers

> Both showcase multiple important figures grouped together in order to honor them. Both are near life-size.

---

#### → [#89](#89--ecstasy-of-saint-teresa) — Ecstasy of Saint Teresa (Gian Lorenzo Bernini, 1647–1652)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Ecstasy_of_St._Teresa_HDR.jpg/250px-Ecstasy_of_St._Teresa_HDR.jpg" width="180" alt="Ecstasy of Saint Teresa">

**Theme:** Religion, emotions

> Both make use of large figures showing an excess of emotion, though they do show rather opposite ones. Also, the Ecstasy makes Teresa into a godlike figure, while the Burghers make the men into human figures.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

**Prompt:** How did context affect artistic decisions the artist made?

> The statues were meant to commemorate the sacrifice the Burghers were willing to make, so Rodin made the sculpture depict the strong emotions the men must have been feeling at the time. The six men stand together as a group, ready to go to their fate. Rodin captured the moment and gave it new life, allowing people to understand what happened in Calais during the Hundred Years' War and relate to it as well as realize the gravity of it.

---

## #120 — The Starry Night

**Vincent van Gogh** · 1889 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 2′5″ × 3′0″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="The Starry Night">

### Summary

A Post-Impressionist night landscape painted largely from imagination and memory while Van Gogh was committed in an asylum.

- **Historical Context.** Van Gogh was a Dutch painter and major figure of **Post-Impressionism**. After moving to Paris in 1886 he adopted brighter palettes and looser brushwork under the influence of the impressionists and neo-impressionists. *The Starry Night* was painted while he was at the **Saint-Paul-de-Mausole asylum**, to which he had been admitted after cutting off his own ear. He was deeply religious, expressed in letters his wish to paint the starry night, and produced the work largely in the studio from imagination, memory, and earlier works. Only one painting sold in his lifetime; he was also a socialist who believed modern life separated people from one another.
- **Function.** To express his emotional and psychological responses to the world. While battling mental illness, Van Gogh sought respite through painting; dissatisfied with modern life, he emphasized nature to reconnect viewers with themselves. He felt he should paint a scene as he experienced, imagined, and interpreted it rather than exactly as seen.
- **Content.** The **cypress tree** reaches to the sky from the ground like a flame — sky representing death, earth representing life, the tree a bridge linking the earthly realm to the afterlife (a period belief held people journeyed to the stars after death). The brightest star is **Venus**, whose namesake goddess evokes Van Gogh's longing for a companion. The pulsing sky shows the power of nature; the swirling waves match astronomical observations of nebulae.
- **Form.** Post-Impressionist **impasto** — thick paint, clearly visible brushstrokes rather than blended surfaces — over a composite landscape. Dark nocturnal colors and short strokes give motion and energy. **Prussian blue** (developed in the 19th century to enrich blue) saturates the sky. The composition is ordered by the cypress, the steeple, and the nebulae.

**Vocabulary:** impasto · Post-Impressionism · composite landscape

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, c. 1000)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Fan_Kuan_-_Travelers_Among_Mountains_and_Streams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/250px-Fan_Kuan_-_Travelers_Among_Mountains_and_Streams_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Travelers Among Mountains and Streams">

**Theme:** Landscape, religious

> Like the Starry Night, this piece emphasizes nature and its power in comparison to humans. The symbols of nature are used to convey the artist's message and are arranged in order for the viewer's eyes.

---

#### → [#109](#109--the-oxbow-view-from-mount-holyoke-northampton-massachusetts-after-a-thunderstorm) — The Oxbow (Thomas Cole, 1836)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b1/Cole_Thomas_The_Oxbow_%28The_Connecticut_River_near_Northampton_1836%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Oxbow">

**Theme:** Landscape

> Like Van Gogh, Thomas Cole painted the piece from memory and imagination to express his emotions. Both pieces are landscapes that emphasize the power of nature and the human will to conquer it.

---

#### → [#111](#111--slave-ship-slavers-throwing-overboard-the-dead-and-dying-typhoon-coming-on) — Slave Ship (Joseph Mallord William Turner, 1840)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Slave-ship.jpg/330px-Slave-ship.jpg" width="180" alt="Slave Ship">

**Theme:** Landscape, historical

> Both pieces use dramatic colors and rough brush strokes to express emotions. There are themes of man versus nature present, showing the power of nature.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

**Prompt:** How does context influence artistic decisions about creating a work of art?

> Context of the artist's beliefs and environments can influence artistic decisions in a work of art. For example, Van Gogh was a highly religious man that believed in the afterlife and heaven. The Starry Night contains symbols representing the afterlife, connecting his beliefs to his art. He was also a man who emphasized the beauty of nature. The subject of many of Gogh's paintings focused on nature instead of the modern world. The environment around Gogh during his time in Paris was filled with influential artists that inspired his painting style. The work of impressionists and neo-impressionists led Gogh to develop his own style of post-impressionism in reaction.

---

## #121 — The Coiffure

**Mary Cassatt** · 1890–1891 C.E. · Drypoint and aquatint · National Gallery of Art, Washington, D.C. · 14⅜ × 10½ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e2/Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg/250px-Mary_Cassatt%2C_The_Coiffure%2C_1890-1891%2C_NGA_5933.jpg" width="240" alt="The Coiffure">

### Summary

An intimate print that fuses **Japanese ukiyo-e** aesthetics with French Impressionism to portray a moment of bourgeois domestic life.

- **Historical Context.** *The Coiffure* was directly influenced by the **Japonisme** movement of the 19th century. Cassatt was American by heritage but spent most of her life in France; in April 1890 she visited an exhibition of Japanese woodblock prints (**ukiyo-e**) at the École des Beaux-Arts in Paris. European interest in Japanese art had grown since Commodore Perry's 1853 opening of Japan, and Cassatt — like Degas, Pissarro, and Monet — began working in that style. She drew directly on **Kitagawa Utamaro's *Takashima Ohisa Using Two Mirrors to Observe Her Coiffure***, and possibly on Ingres's *La Grande Odalisque*, which she had studied in the 1860s. The result is a cultural blend of Japanese and Impressionist styles.
- **Function.** The work has no explicit traditional function — it shows the impact of foreign influence on art, a glimpse of bourgeois daily life, and the nude female figure presented **without sensuality**.
- **Content.** A half-nude young woman does her hair in a bedroom to get ready for the day. Her back is turned to the viewer, her front reflected in a mirror facing the viewer. There is no pose — she is depicted naturally, unaware of being observed (the central subject turned away from the viewer is an influence from ukiyo-e).
- **Form.** Primarily **pastel colors**, with contrasts between the smooth curves of the figure and the sharp, straight lines of mirror, wall, and furniture. The curvy wallpaper designs contrast with the harder edges elsewhere. Cassatt used **drypoint and aquatint** — an intaglio technique carved into copper plates with acid producing the tones. The work is more intimate and tender than many typical Impressionist works.

**Vocabulary:** Impressionism · aquatint · drypoint · avant-garde · japonisme · coiffure
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#92](#92--woman-holding-a-balance) — Woman Holding a Balance (Johannes Vermeer, c. 1664)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Johannes_Vermeer_-_Woman_Holding_a_Balance_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Woman Holding a Balance">

**Theme:** Domestic life, genre scenes, religious themes, allegories, vanity

> Both depict a woman ignorant of the viewer. The items of 'luxury' are prevalent in both pieces. In The Coiffure, it is the coiffure itself—the luxury to have done-up hair, and in Vermeer's piece, it is the jewelry and other garments laid out in front of the central figure on the table. Both pieces also clearly are genre scenes, showing daily life.

---

#### → [#107](#107--la-grande-odalisque) — La Grande Odalisque (Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres, 1814)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/La_grande_odalisque_-_Jean-Auguste_Dominique_Ingres_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_1158.jpg/500px-La_grande_odalisque_-_Jean-Auguste_Dominique_Ingres_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_1158.jpg" width="180" alt="La Grande Odalisque">

**Theme:** Domestic life, femininity, cultural social tensions or classes, sensuality

> Both central figures are nude or part-nude, and have their backs to the viewer. However, while The Coiffure depicts the figure nude but not sensual, the subject of La Grande Odalisque is a concubine, and in turn depicted sensually. Both are again, still portraying domestic life and femininity.

---

#### → [#36](#36--grave-stele-of-hegeso) — Grave stele of Hegeso (attributed to Kallimachos, c. 410 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/NAMA_St%C3%A8le_d%27H%C3%A8g%C3%A8s%C3%B4.jpg/250px-NAMA_St%C3%A8le_d%27H%C3%A8g%C3%A8s%C3%B4.jpg" width="180" alt="Grave stele of Hegeso">

**Theme:** Domestic life, genre scenes, funerary themes

> Both depict the domestic life, though the Grave stele of Hegeso is a funerary work depicted with domestic life, while The Coiffure is obviously not funerary, and much more intimate.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

#### Takashima Ohisa Using Two Mirrors to Observe Her Coiffure (Kitagawa Utamaro, c. 1795) — *not in image set*

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7a/Kitagawa_Utamaro_-_Takashima_Ohisa_Using_Two_Mirrors_to_Observe_Her_Coiffure_Night_of_the_Asakusa_Marketing_Festival_-_MFA_Boston_21.6410FXD.jpg/250px-Kitagawa_Utamaro_-_Takashima_Ohisa_Using_Two_Mirrors_to_Observe_Her_Coiffure_Night_of_the_Asakusa_Marketing_Festival_-_MFA_Boston_21.6410FXD.jpg" width="180" alt="Takashima Ohisa Using Two Mirrors">

**Prompt:** How was a singular piece of artwork, such as Mary Cassatt's *The Coiffure*, or a group of related works, influenced by specific traditions and/or changes?

> The Coiffure was directly influenced by Japanese art; specifically, Takashima Ohisa Using Two Mirrors to Observe Her Coiffure, by Kitagawa Utamaro. Takashima Ohisa Using Two Mirrors to Observe Her Coiffure depicts two women doing their hair and looking in a mirror, much alike The Coiffure. The Japanese style of depicting 'everyday life' is prevalent in the influences on The Coiffure. Additionally, both works have a lack of shadow, stronger outlines, and the central figure(s) are oblivious to the viewer.

---

## #122 — The Scream

**Edvard Munch** · 1893 C.E. · Tempera and pastels on cardboard · Oslo · 66 × 83 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg/330px-Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg" width="240" alt="The Scream">

### Summary

An iconic **Symbolist** image of existential dread that helped spark the **Expressionist** movement.

- **Historical Context.** The work is iconic of the Symbolist movement and is said to have sparked the Expressionist movement of the early 1900s. Munch made **four versions**, all included in his series **"The Frieze of Life."** A Norwegian, he studied at the Oslo Academy in the 1800s and was going through a very dark period when he painted *The Scream*: his sister had been committed to an asylum, and Munch felt he too was drifting into insanity. The setting is a hill in **Ekeberg, Norway**, near both a slaughterhouse and the madhouse his sister was in. Munch wrote in his diary that the work was more symbolic — on a walk at sunset he felt "a scream from nature" and a sudden sadness, describing the almost violent redness of the sky.
- **Function.** An abstract representation of Munch's central themes — **sickness, isolation, fear, and death** — and of his own mindset at a time when he felt he might be succumbing to madness. Munch had lost his mother to tuberculosis at age 5 and had almost no family, saying he was married to his paintings.
- **Content.** A genderless, simplified figure stands on a walkway beside a railing, overlooking a river and a hill. The figure is highly unrealistic, as is the background. The **extreme reds of the sky** over swirling water and sky contrast with the straight lines of the bridge.
- **Form.** Made in four versions — two paintings, two pastels. The first combined oil, tempera, and pastel; the second was pure tempera; the pastels were drawn on cardboard. Munch's decision to produce four versions reflects his dedication to experimentation, a spark of innovation characteristic of the Symbolist movement and later extended by Expressionism.

**Vocabulary:** Symbolist movement · Expressionist movement · tempera
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#120](#120--the-starry-night) — The Starry Night (Vincent van Gogh, 1889)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/500px-Van_Gogh_-_Starry_Night_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="The Starry Night">

**Theme:** Dark colors, Expressionist, Symbolist

> Both Van Gogh and Munch were in negative mental states and thus expressed their mindsets through their paintings. They both employ dark colors and swirls and are very symbolic of the abstract.

---

#### → [#136](#136--composition-with-red-blue-and-yellow) — Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow (Piet Mondrian, 1930)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg/330px-Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg" width="180" alt="Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow">

**Theme:** Abstract, contemporary

> Both pieces of art show the new contemporary movement which puts focus sharply away from realism and toward more of what viewers interpret the art as rather than what the art itself shows.

#### → [#143](#143--dream-of-a-sunday-afternoon-in-the-alameda-park-*sueño-de-una-tarde-dominical-en-la-alameda-central*) — Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in Alameda Central Park (Diego Rivera, 1947)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Sue%C3%B1o_de_una_tarde_dominical_en_la_Alameda_Central_%28Dream_of_a_Sunday_Afternoon_in_the_Alameda_Central%29.jpg/500px-Sue%C3%B1o_de_una_tarde_dominical_en_la_Alameda_Central_%28Dream_of_a_Sunday_Afternoon_in_the_Alameda_Central%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in Alameda Central Park">

**Theme:** Abstract, Symbolism

> Both paintings show seemingly naturalistic scenes describing abstracted positions. The Scream shows Munch's mental state while Rivera shows a dreamlike state juxtaposed with historical figures.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

**Prompt:** Students analyze how contextual variables lead to different interpretations of a work of art.

> Most of the interpretations of this artwork are based off of what is known of Munch's personal life at the time of the painting's creation. Context is extremely important both at the micro and macro level of the painting—on the micro level, the artist Munch had been going through very dark times throughout his life and thus expressed these emotions on the canvas. At the macro level, in the early 1900s, the Symbolist movement was blossoming, in which artists did often express themselves, an era in which the artist's personal history did matter in contrast with earlier art in which most artists were unknown or simply created commissioned art.

---

## #123 — Where Do We Come From? What Are We? Where Are We Going?

**Paul Gauguin** · 1897–1898 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museum of Fine Arts, Boston · 139 × 375 cm (55 × 148 in)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paul_Gauguin_-_D%27ou_venons-nous.jpg/500px-Paul_Gauguin_-_D%27ou_venons-nous.jpg" width="240" alt="Where Do We Come From? What Are We? Where Are We Going?">

### Summary

A large symbolic canvas meditating on the **cycle of human life**, painted in Tahiti during a period of personal crisis.

- **Historical Context.** Paul Gauguin was a French artist initially trained in Impressionism in Paris, where he befriended **Pissarro** and **Van Gogh** and was heavily influenced by **Cézanne**, whose parallel, constructive brushstrokes he adopted. After several visits to Tahiti and a desire to escape what he saw as artificial European culture, he settled in French Polynesia. The work was produced shortly after the death of his beloved daughter **Aline** — he was in grief, had attempted suicide by self-poisoning, and was already suffering the degenerative effects of syphilis. He died at 54 and was buried in the Marquesas Islands.
- **Function.** To depict Gauguin's fascination with Tahitian life and culture — a place he sought out in isolation from modern society after becoming disillusioned with his career as a stockbroker in Europe — and to express his emotional suffering after his daughter's death and his interpretation of the life cycle.
- **Content.** A symbolism of the cycle of life from birth to old age and death. On the **far right**, three nude women — two meeting the viewer's gaze — and beside them a **newborn child on a rock**, the beginning of life. In the **center**, a figure **picks fruit from a tree**, a reference to **Adam and Eve** and the Tree of Knowledge — the loss of innocence. On the **left**, an old and weak figure **covers her ears**, unable to find peace; this pose recalls Munch's *The Scream* and may parallel Gauguin himself, in despair from the memories of his earlier life.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; Gauguin is renowned for his masterful, emotive use of color. Though the palette is largely warm, the tone gives a deep and dark feeling. There is a detectable contrast of light and dark: the **background is mainly blue and green**, depicting an Eden-like garden, while the **foreground figures are yellow and red**, highlighting the artist's focus.

**Vocabulary:** Post-Impressionism · Tahiti · catechism · Adam and Eve
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#122](#122--the-scream) — The Scream (Edvard Munch, 1893)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg/330px-Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg" width="180" alt="The Scream">

**Theme:** Allegory & Human nature

> Reference back to The Scream

---

#### → [#79](#79--allegory-of-law-and-grace) — Allegory of Law and Grace (Lucas Cranach the Elder, c. 1529)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/GOTHA-cranach-veljo.jpg/500px-GOTHA-cranach-veljo.jpg" width="180" alt="Allegory of Law and Grace">

**Theme:** Allegory & Faith

> Similar style

#### → [#125](#125--mont-sainte-victoire) — Mont Sainte-Victoire (Paul Cézanne, 1902–1904)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c3/Paul_cezanne%2C_mount_sainte-vistoire%2C_1902-04.JPG" width="180" alt="Mont Sainte-Victoire">

**Theme:** Landscape

> Similar brushstrokes and use of color

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

**Prompt:** How did Paul Gauguin's personal experience influence this piece of art? Where did he get inspirations from when naming the artwork?

> Gauguin's gone through a lot throughout his life: economic problems, lost of daughter, lost of hope. These experiences gave him inspirations when creating this artwork, and through his own struggles, he acknowledged the darkness within human nature and the shortness of time.

## #124 — Carson, Pirie, Scott and Company Building

**Louis Sullivan (architect), Daniel Burnham** · 1899–1903 C.E. · Iron, steel, glass, and terra cotta · Chicago, Illinois · 30,490 ft² area, 207 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/92/Carson%2C_Pirie%2C_Scott_and_Company_Building_%28Sullivan_Center%29%2C_Chicago%2C_Illinois_%289179422705%29.jpg/330px-Carson%2C_Pirie%2C_Scott_and_Company_Building_%28Sullivan_Center%29%2C_Chicago%2C_Illinois_%289179422705%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Carson, Pirie, Scott and Company Building">

### Summary

An early Chicago skyscraper, originally the **Sullivan Center**, that embodies Sullivan's dictum "form ever follows function."

- **Historical Context.** The building was created after the **Chicago Great Fire of 1871**. In 1902, Bavarian partners David Mayer and Leopold Schlesinger settled on a twelve-story structure at State and Madison. It stood out for its **steel frame**, large windows, and terra cotta and bronze cladding — materials that made it effectively fire resistant. A multi-year renovation completed in 2006 at $68.9 million, and parent company Bon-Ton Stores closed the department store after the 2006 Christmas season; the building has since been leased in portions while remaining a landmark piece of architecture.
- **Function.** Multi-functional: described as the "ideal **tripartite skyscraper**." The first floor — bright, open, with easy public access — serves commerce; the upper floors are office space with uniform, repetitive windows. The ground floor is a **department store** with large windows and broad entrances intended to draw shoppers in. Height is heavily emphasized, and a distinctive **cornice** marks the top to set the building apart.
- **Content.** An innovative modern commercial building blending work and commerce. Sullivan based the design on his principle that a building's exterior must reflect the social purpose of the space: a light, accessible base level for retail; uniform upper floors for offices; and a cornice to give the structure individuality.
- **Form.** Composed of iron, steel, glass, bronze, and terra cotta. Glass is used throughout, emphasized on the ground floor. Iron gives the structure its rigidity, steel frames the whole, and bronze and terra cotta — sturdy, lightweight, fire-resistant — handle the decorative skin.

**Vocabulary:** early Chicago skyscraper · height · window-shopping · versatile · conformity of structure · Chicago landmark · U.S. historic landmark
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.1, 3.2

---

> Innovation, architecture, unique

**Theme:** Modernist, unique levels

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/9b/MAXXI_%2827483747665%29.jpg/330px-MAXXI_%2827483747665%29.jpg" width="180" alt="MAXXI National Museum">

This earlier skyscraper was extremely unique and paved the way for later architects to break the mold with never buildings.

## #125 — Mont Sainte-Victoire

**Paul Cézanne** · 1902–1904 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Aix-en-Provence, France · 73 × 91.9 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Mont_Sainte-Victoire.jpg/330px-Mont_Sainte-Victoire.jpg" width="240" alt="Mont Sainte-Victoire">
### Summary

One of Cézanne's eleven **series** paintings of the mountain near his hometown — a structural study that became the key bridge from Impressionism to **Cubism**.

- **Historical Context.** Cézanne was born in **Aix-en-Provence** (1839) and felt closely tied to its landscape. He worked mostly outside the Paris art scene and was not widely recognized until late in life. The mountain, known as the "Mountain of Holy Victory," commemorates Provence's ancient Roman inhabitants defeating invaders. Later classified under **Post-Impressionism** — artists who responded to Impressionism with simplified forms, abstraction, and emotional/symbolic content.
- **Function.** Cézanne aimed to "**make Impressionism something solid and durable, like the art of the museums**" — analyzing the underlying structure of his subject rather than recording fleeting appearance. None of his mountain paintings look the same; each tests a different structural solution.
- **Content.** The imposing **Mont Sainte-Victoire** dominates the distance. The composition divides into three bands: blue mountain, yellow plain, foreground trees. A few houses are vaguely visible below; no figures, no narrative.
- **Form.** Patches of **undiluted color** and geometric construction build the forms. Warmer/darker foreground colors against cooler background colors create depth even as the paint remains emphatically surface-bound. Rough, visible brushstrokes; the mountain is grounded and firmly built from color planes.

**Vocabulary:** Post-Impressionism · series · structural analysis
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.4, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg/330px-La_Gare_Saint-Lazare_-_Claude_Monet.jpg" width="180" alt="The Saint-Lazare Station">

**Theme:** landscape style · industrialization · Impressionism · rough brushstrokes

> Impressionism was the basis of the post-impressionist style

#### → [#120](#120--the-starry-night) — The Starry Night (Vincent van Gogh, 1889)

**Theme:** life and death · emotion · wind patterns · landscape

> post-impressionism · manipulation of color · rough brushstrokes

---

#### → [#123](#123--where-do-we-come-from?-what-are-we?-where-are-we-going?) — Where Do We Come From? What Are We? Where Are We Going? (Paul Gauguin, 1897–1898)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Paul_Gauguin_-_D%27ou_venons-nous.jpg/500px-Paul_Gauguin_-_D%27ou_venons-nous.jpg" width="180" alt="Where Do We Come From? What Are We? Where Are We Going?">

## #126 — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon

**Pablo Picasso** · 1907 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 8′ × 7′8″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="240" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

### Summary

Widely cited as the **starting point of Cubism**.

- **Historical Context.** Picasso (b. Málaga, 1881; trained in Barcelona) moved to Paris, where **Gertrude Stein** patronized him and **Cézanne**'s flat, anti-illusionistic painting reshaped his approach. A recent exhibit of **African and Iberian masks** directly inspired the right-hand faces. The figures are prostitutes from a brothel on **Carrer d'Avinyó** (Avignon Street) in Barcelona.
- **Function.** Meant to **upstage Matisse's *Bonheur de Vivre*** (1906) — deliberately provocative, radical, and anti-decorative.
- **Content.** Five nude prostitutes in confrontational poses; fruit on a table at bottom-center; three stare directly at the viewer. (An earlier version included a male client on the left, later painted out.)
- **Form.** Limited red/blue/pink/warm-grey palette; no real background, just sharp planes. Left three figures are abstracted but human; right two wear **mask-like**, geometric faces. Multiple vantage points, no linear perspective, no depth.

**Vocabulary:** Cubism · avant-garde · primitivism · salon
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.3, 3.2, 3.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#91](#91--las-meninas) — Las Meninas (Diego Velázquez, 1656)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg/330px-Las_Meninas%2C_by_Diego_Vel%C3%A1zquez%2C_from_Prado_in_Google_Earth.jpg" width="180" alt="Las Meninas">

**Theme:** A group of figures depicted

> Both works consist of a depiction of multiple (more than three) individuals, in varying poses and appearance. In *Las Meninas*, however, the figures were all recognizable people from real life, while the figures in *Les Demoiselles d'Avignon* are much too abstracted (as well as less well known).

---

#### → [#115](#115--olympia) — Olympia (Édouard Manet, 1863)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/500px-Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg" width="180" alt="Olympia">

**Theme:** Female figure(s) with gaze at viewer

> In both works, the female figure(s) appear to look out of the canvas, at whoever is viewing the work. Such direct eye contact from women is uncommon, especially in pieces depicting unclothed women both implied to be prostitutes. The so-called male gaze is reversed by this breaking of the fourth wall.

---

#### → [#72](#72--birth-of-venus) — The Birth of Venus (Sandro Botticelli, c. 1484–1486)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg/500px-Sandro_Botticelli_-_La_nascita_di_Venere_-_Google_Art_Project_-_edited.jpg" width="180" alt="The Birth of Venus">

**Theme:** Ideals of female beauty; standing female figure

> Picasso intended to abstract the female body to create an alternative perspective on their beauty; their attractiveness can still be actively conveyed through less representational art. In *The Birth of Venus*, Botticelli painted the goddess in the ideal body and appearance of women according to the Renaissance society. Perfect proportions and ratios, adopted from classical times, are evident, also creating a standard of beauty. More conspicuous is the presence of standing female figures.

---

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

#### Madame Cézanne in a Red Dress (Paul Cézanne, 1888–1890) — *not in image set*

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/07/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne%2C_1888-90%2C_Madame_C%C3%A9zanne_%28Hortense_Fiquet%2C_1850%E2%80%931922%29_in_a_Red_Dress%2C_oil_on_canvas%2C_116.5_x_89.5_cm%2C_The_Metropolitan_Museum_of_Art%2C_New_York.jpg" width="180" alt="Madame Cézanne in a Red Dress">

**Prompt:** Using specific formal and contextual evidence, compare the two works and analyze the possible influences, if any, that one of the works might have had on the other.

> *Madame Cézanne in a Red Dress* was created decades before *Les Demoiselles d'Avignon*, and Cézanne was known to be one of Picasso's early influences after his arrival in Paris in the early 20th century. While it is not solely this piece, the work on the left was consequential for the work on the right, through their formal similarities.
>
> *Madame Cézanne in a Red Dress* is painted with a more representational style, with actual attention given to details in the human figure, like hands, or eyes. On the other hand, the five figures are almost completely devoid of detail, left as only angular planes of flat colors (shades of reds and pinks). However, both works depict females, and the artists' ideal or interpretation of beauty. Cézanne painted his wife, who is most likely a subject of affection for him, and the five prostitutes were painted in way to push the boundaries of what could be seen as a female figure; even simple shapes could convey the female body. Additionally, there is a lack of realistic detail in both works' backgrounds; there is just enough line to convey the shadow of the creases in fabric and drapery, but the pattern is muddled in *Madame Cézanne*, and the drapery takes on a geometric form in *Les Demoiselles d'Avignon*. Furthermore, *Madame Cézanne* is sitting stiffly in a chair with a stoic expression and gaze towards the viewer, as are three of the five figures in the other piece. Both works do not convey any real depth, with subjects placed in a claustrophobic position that eliminates the illusion of three dimensions on the two-dimensional works. For example, *Madame Cézanne* is placed seemingly right against the wall, with other objects, like the painting, on the wall as well. Picasso achieves the same flatness by reducing the objects in the painting to abstractions, like the table on which the fruit is placed, as well as using several different vantage points.

---

## #127 — The Steerage

**Alfred Stieglitz** · 1907 C.E. · Photogravure · J. Paul Getty Museum (one of five known versions) · 13 1/8 × 10 3/8 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Alfred_Stieglitz_-_The_Steerage_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Alfred_Stieglitz_-_The_Steerage_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="The Steerage">

### Summary

Stieglitz's landmark photograph arguing that **photography belongs on the wall with fine art**.

- **Historical Context.** Taken on a 1907 transatlantic voyage during the peak of European immigration to the U.S., before the **Emergency Quota Act of 1921** cut that flow. Stieglitz, the son of a German immigrant, moved in avant-garde circles and published the work in his magazine *291* alongside Cubist drawings by Picasso — pitching photography into the wider **modernist** turn toward abstract form.
- **Function.** Primarily to elevate photography to the status of fine art; secondarily a quiet commentary on **class stratification**. Picasso reportedly admired its "collagelike dispersal of forms and shifting depths."
- **Content.** Two tiers on a ship bound New York → Europe: well-dressed first-class passengers above, shabbier steerage passengers (returnees from Ellis Island) below, a white **drawbridge** slashing a dividing line between them. A man in a round straw hat looks down from above.
- **Form.** Silver photogravure print built from geometric shapes — the round hat, the triangular mast, the chained drawbridge, the crossed suspenders on a man's back. Stieglitz said he saw "shapes related to each other… a picture of shapes and underlying that the feeling I had about life."

**Vocabulary:** photogravure · intaglio · modernism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#114](#114--nadar-raising-photography-to-the-height-of-art) — Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art (Honoré Daumier, 1862)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg/250px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg" width="180" alt="Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art">

**Theme:** Photography not truly high art; attempting and failing to capture the true essence of life

> Debate about whether photography can be considered equal to art-- 114 is satirical and argues that it cannot, while 127 attempts to prove it can

---

#### → [#126](#126--les-demoiselles-d'avignon) — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon (Pablo Picasso, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="180" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

**Theme:** Sexuality; renewal of European art; repurposing art of other cultures to revitalize European art

> Strong focus on unique form and composition; claustrophobic conditions; portrayal of frowned-upon sectors of society, though not as critical as should perhaps be

#### → [#132](#132--improvisation-28-second-version) — Improvisation 28 (second version) (Vassily Kandinsky, 1912)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Vasily_Kandinsky_Improvisation_28_%28second_version%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Improvisation 28">

**Theme:** Cataclysm and salvation; transformation of European art

> Strong focus on form over content, departure from preceding Realism

## #128 — The Kiss

**Gustav Klimt** (Art Nouveau / Vienna Secession) · 1907–1908 C.E. · Oil and gold leaf on canvas · Österreichische Galerie Belvedere, Vienna · 5′11″ × 5′11″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/The_Kiss_-_Gustav_Klimt_-_Google_Cultural_Institute.jpg/330px-The_Kiss_-_Gustav_Klimt_-_Google_Cultural_Institute.jpg" width="240" alt="The Kiss by Klimt">

### Summary

The signature painting of Klimt's **Golden Phase** and of Viennese **Art Nouveau**.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Vienna during Klimt's 1898–1908 **Golden Phase**, heavily influenced by the **Byzantine mosaics** he had seen in Ravenna and by the Vienna Secession / Arts and Crafts push against academic art. The sensual theme continues Klimt's earlier *Beethoven Frieze* and *Stoclet Frieze*; unlike his "pornographic" Vienna Ceiling paintings, *The Kiss* was embraced by critics.
- **Function.** A universal, sensual image of love made in a period of urban modernization and anxiety — meant to freeze an ideal, all-consuming embrace.
- **Content.** A kneeling couple lock in a kiss on the edge of a flowering meadow. The man wears a gold robe patterned with **black-and-white rectangles** and a crown of vines; the woman wears a tight dress with floral **circles and ovals**, her hair scattered with flowers that read as a halo. The woman is the emotional protagonist — she receives the kiss actively, not as passive object.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas with layered **gold and silver leaf** that produces a flat, non-spatial ground. Linear/rectangular male ornament set against curvilinear/floral female ornament; heads pushed toward the top of the canvas in the manner of Japanese woodblock composition.

**Vocabulary:** Art Nouveau · Arts and Crafts · Vienna Secession · gold leaf
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 2.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#129](#129--the-kiss) — The Kiss (Constantin Brancusi, 1907–1908)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/The_Kiss_LCCN93511527.jpg/250px-The_Kiss_LCCN93511527.jpg" width="180" alt="The Kiss by Brancusi">

**Theme:** Genre, passion

> Both pieces depict similar scenes of a kiss. Both depict the eternity of the kiss, as both couples are firmly embracing each other and lost in the moment.

---

#### → [#95](#95--the-virgin-of-guadalupe-virgen-de-guadalupe) — Virgin of Guadalupe (Miguel González, 1698)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg/250px-Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg" width="180" alt="Virgin of Guadalupe">

**Theme:** Religious, devotion

> Both pieces have a female protagonist. Both females have similar facial forms, and the sensuousness of the females is emphasized in both paintings. Both deviate from sexualizing the females and instead portraying their emotion and passion.

---

#### → [#87](#87--self-portrait-with-saskia) — Self-Portrait with Saskia (Rembrandt van Rijn, c. 1636)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Rembrandt_-_Rembrandt_and_Saskia_in_the_Scene_of_the_Prodigal_Son_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Self-Portrait with Saskia">

**Theme:** compassion, marriage

> Both paintings depict a couple embracing each other. The female figures are in the foreground in both pieces, with Saski standing out due to her contrast in shadowing and lighting from Rembrandt. Compassion is displayed in both pieces, as both couples are supporting each other.

---

## #129 — The Kiss

**Constantin Brâncuși** · 1907–1908 C.E. (this version 1916) · Limestone · Philadelphia Museum of Art · 11 × 10¼ × 8½ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d6/The_Kiss_LCCN93511527.jpg/250px-The_Kiss_LCCN93511527.jpg" width="240" alt="The Kiss by Brancusi">

### Summary

A radical **proto-Cubist** reduction of the embrace to a single, nearly monolithic block.

- **Historical Context.** Brâncuși, a Romanian sculptor working in Paris, made the first version in 1907–1908 and returned to the subject repeatedly. This limestone version was carved in 1916 on commission from Brâncuși's New York patron **John Quinn** after the original had already been given away. It sits within the larger **proto-Cubist** turn toward extreme geometrization that was reshaping European sculpture alongside Picasso's and Braque's painting.
- **Function.** A deliberate counter-statement to the elaborate sentimental versions of *The Kiss* that preceded it (notably **Rodin**'s 1882 bronze and Klimt's 1908 painting), recasting the subject as stone simplicity.
- **Content.** Two figures locked face-to-face, carved from a single block. A vertical line separates them; a small curve marks the woman's breast; longer hair distinguishes her; the two eyes merge into one almost cyclopic form; pairs of arms wrap around the sides to form the embrace.
- **Form.** A cubic limestone mass, almost symmetrical, with only minimal incised lines for arms, hair, eyes, and mouth. Brâncuși refused any base — he said "any kind of arrangement will have the look of an amputation."

**Vocabulary:** geometrization · avant-garde · proto-Cubism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 2.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#128](#128--the-kiss) — The Kiss (Gustav Klimt, 1907–1908)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/40/The_Kiss_-_Gustav_Klimt_-_Google_Cultural_Institute.jpg/330px-The_Kiss_-_Gustav_Klimt_-_Google_Cultural_Institute.jpg" width="180" alt="The Kiss by Klimt">

**Theme:** Other, Religious (sort of)

> It is similar to The Kiss by Brancusi due to the subject matter. Both paintings depict The Kiss, although Klimt's version is a painting done almost to look like a Byzantine icon.

---

#### → [#126](#126--les-demoiselles-d'avignon) — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon (Pablo Picasso, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="180" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

**Theme:** Portraiture

> Brancusi drew a lot of inspiration from Picasso's Cubist artworks. The use of geometric shapes and figures are similar between The Kiss and Les Demoiselles d'Avignon.

---

#### → [#142](#142--the-jungle) — The Jungle (Wifredo Lam, 1943)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/21/%27The_Jungle%27%2C_gouache_on_paper_painting_by_Wifredo_Lam%2C_1943%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art.jpg/250px-%27The_Jungle%27%2C_gouache_on_paper_painting_by_Wifredo_Lam%2C_1943%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art.jpg" width="180" alt="The Jungle">

**Theme:** Historical

> The Jungle by Wilfredo Lam is very similar to The Kiss due to its geometrization of figures. Just like Les Demoiselles d'Avignon, its use of geometric shapes to depict people is characteristic of Cubism.

## #130 — The Portuguese

**Georges Braque** · 1911 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Kunstmuseum Basel, Switzerland · 116.7 × 81.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/The_Portuguese_Braque.jpg/250px-The_Portuguese_Braque.jpg" width="240" alt="The Portuguese">
### Summary

A keystone work of **Analytic Cubism**, made at the height of Braque's collaboration with Picasso.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in the summer of 1911 in **Céret**, Catalonia, during the intense Braque–Picasso partnership that produced **Analytic Cubism**. Braque built on **Cézanne**'s geometric reduction of form and pushed it further, deconstructing the object into faceted planes. The style paralleled the upheaval in physics from Newton to Einstein and Heisenberg.
- **Function.** A radical departure from Renaissance pictorial illusionism: instead of describing reality, the painting analyzes it — dissolving a single subject into multiple viewpoints, introducing stenciled letters and numbers as shards of reality inside the abstracted form.
- **Content.** A Portuguese musician Braque had seen in a Marseilles bar, rendered almost unrecognizably. Shapes replace forms; stenciled words and numbers float across the surface; the only clearly legible subject is the vague outline of a figure.
- **Form.** Reduced, near-**monochrome** palette of warm browns. Light and shadow are detached from any consistent source. Multiple viewpoints coexist on one plane — cylinders, cubes, and triangles dominate — and the canvas hovers between flatness and three-dimensional structure.

**Vocabulary:** Cubism · Analytical Cubism · multiple viewpoints · monochrome
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#125](#125--mont-sainte-victoire) — Mont Sainte-Victoire (Paul Cézanne, 1902–1904)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/00/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne_-_Montagne_Saint-victoire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Paul_C%C3%A9zanne_-_Montagne_Saint-victoire_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="180" alt="Mont Sainte-Victoire">

**Theme:** Manipulating form and space, treating nature as basic shapes

> Cezanne began representing surfaces in multiple planes at the same time, an idea taken a step further by the cubists.

---

#### → [#126](#126--les-demoiselles-d'avignon) — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon (Pablo Picasso, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="180" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

**Theme:** Representing form and space in new ways, representation and abstraction

> Picasso continued Cezanne's work by representing figures in an inconsistent way, painting forms at multiple times in one space

---

#### → [#145](#145--woman-i) — Woman, I (Willem de Kooning, 1950–1952)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2a/Woman_I-Willem_de_Kooning.jpg" width="180" alt="Woman, I">

**Theme:** Using abstraction to present emotion, borrowing of Cubist ideas

> The Cubists paved the way for Abstract Expressionism by tearing down old conventions of form and space

### Compare-and-contrast exercise (LO 3.5)

#### Goldfish (Henri Matisse, 1912) — *in image set as #131*

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Goldfish_Matisse.jpg/250px-Goldfish_Matisse.jpg" width="180" alt="Goldfish">

**Prompt:** How was Braque's *The Portuguese* and the Cubist movement influenced by previous art movements?

> The primary influences on this work of art are Fauvism and the works of Paul Cezanne. Fauvism started the break from Impressionism and post-Impressionism by emphasizing painterly qualities and color over the last traces of representation and realism. For example, the use of wild brush strokes and striking colors would not have been employed inside *The Portuguese* if Matisse had not employed the style in works like *Goldfish*. Cezanne introduced the idea of using geometry to represent the form of the object. In his *Mont Sainte-Victoire*, he depicted the form of the mountain over an actual, realistic depiction of a mountain. The obvious geometrical intent gives way to abstraction, an idea completed by the works of Braque and Picasso.

---

## #131 — Goldfish

**Henri Matisse** · 1912 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Pushkin State Museum, Moscow · 140 × 98 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Goldfish_Matisse.jpg/250px-Goldfish_Matisse.jpg" width="240" alt="Goldfish">

### Summary

One of Matisse's signature **Fauvist** compositions, painted just after his trips to **Morocco**.

- **Historical Context.** Matisse (French, 1869–1954) painted this in 1912 outside Paris, newly returned from **Morocco**, where he had been struck by the relaxed, contemplative daily life he saw around goldfish bowls. The work sits firmly inside **Fauvism** ("wild beasts"), the early-20th-century movement Matisse helped lead, which prized bold, unnaturalistic color and loose brushwork over realistic description.
- **Function.** To evoke contemplation. Goldfish became, for Matisse, a symbol of the tranquil, meditative state he had observed in Morocco — a subject for quiet looking rather than narrative.
- **Content.** A cylindrical glass bowl of goldfish sits on a small garden table, surrounded by lush pink flowers and green foliage. The fish's reflections in the water glass are nearly abstract, only loosely echoing the bodies above.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; saturated pink, green, red, and orange; flat patterned backgrounds that compress depth. The fish are the only sharply rendered subject — everything else is simplified into broad color planes typical of Fauvism.

**Vocabulary:** Fauvism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → Earth's Creation (Emily Kame Kngwarreye, 1994) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** acrylic, landscape

> bold strokes, painting

#### → [#61](#61--dedication-page-with-blanche-of-castile-and-king-louis-ix-of-france-scenes-from-the-apocalypse-from-bibles-moralisées) — Dedication Page of the Blanche of Castille and Louis IX Moralized Bible (Gothic, c. 1225–1235)

**Theme:** illuminated manuscript, gothic style, Biblical themes, historical

> simplified subjects, contrasting subject and background

---

#### → Pure Land (Mariko Mori, 1998) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** color photograph on class; surrealism

> use of water and hazy background contrasting with vibrant subject.

## #132 — Improvisation 28 (second version)

**Wassily Kandinsky** (Der Blaue Reiter / Expressionism) · 1912 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Solomon R. Guggenheim Museum, New York · 43⅞ × 63⅞ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/1/18/Vasily_Kandinsky_Improvisation_28_%28second_version%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Improvisation 28 (second version)">

### Summary

An early landmark of **pure abstraction**, translating music into paint.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in **Munich** during Kandinsky's **Der Blaue Reiter** ("Blue Rider") period — one of the two great German **Expressionist** groups. Russian-born Kandinsky developed his abstract theory in Munich, exchanging ideas with Franz Marc and other avant-garde artists and drawing on Russian folk art's stylized floral patterns.
- **Function.** To make **atonal music** visible. The title *Improvisation* refers to the act of making, not the subject; Kandinsky wanted the painting to evoke the discordant, unresolved feeling of music without a tonic note.
- **Content.** Nominally a landscape — faint mountains and hill forms emerge on close inspection — but deliberately left so abstract that each viewer reads their own forms. A rough divide two-thirds across the canvas echoes passages in a musical composition.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; sweeping overlapping lines in saturated color set against a grey-navy ground. Straight lines clash with curves; smaller half-circles near the bottom suggest staccato notes. Intentionally disunified to mirror atonal music.

**Vocabulary:** Expressionism · Der Blaue Reiter · Improvisation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.2, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → Stadia II (Julie Mehretu, 2004) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Complete abstraction, Abstract representation, Use of abstract forms to convey ideas

> Both Kandinsky's Improvisation and Mehretu's Stadia make great use of color, shape, and line to convey an idea or suggest a form; in Kandinsky's case, the discordant nature of music without a tonic note, represented in a landscape, and Stadia II as the bustling nature of the globalized world shown in a busy stadium.

---

#### → [#130](#130--the-portuguese) — The Portuguese (Georges Braque, 1911)

**Theme:** Complete abstraction

> While Kandinsky's work shows music by expressing several seemingly related forms, Braque explores representing an object so many times, from so many viewpoints, that it becomes unrecognizable. Both use abstraction to convey an idea that would normally be too obvious to the reader.

---

#### → [#109](#109--the-oxbow-view-from-mount-holyoke-northampton-massachusetts-after-a-thunderstorm) — The Oxbow (Thomas Cole, 1836)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Cole_Thomas_The_Oxbow_%28The_Connecticut_River_near_Northampton_1836%29.jpg/330px-Cole_Thomas_The_Oxbow_%28The_Connecticut_River_near_Northampton_1836%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Oxbow">

**Theme:** Landscape, thoughtful and unrealistic composition

> Both Kandinsky and Cole manipulate a landscape to represent what ideas they get across. Cole's idealized landscape appears more beautiful and breathtaking than it probably did in real life, conveying the emotion he felt while seeing the view rather than the view itself; similarly, Kandinsky's composition shows the music as he heard it, rather than showing exactly where each instrument came in.

---

## #133 — Self-Portrait as a Soldier

**Ernst Ludwig Kirchner** (Die Brücke / German Expressionism) · 1915 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Allen Memorial Art Museum, Oberlin College, Ohio · 24 × 27 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg/500px-Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg" width="240" alt="Self-Portrait as a Soldier">

### Summary

An anti-war **Expressionist** self-portrait painted during Kirchner's breakdown in WWI.

- **Historical Context.** Kirchner served briefly as a driver in **WWI** before being discharged within months for partial paralysis, drug and alcohol addiction, weak lungs, and collapsing mental health. Co-founder of **Die Brücke** ("The Bridge"), the 1905–1913 German Expressionist group that merged Primitivism with modernist avant-garde. The painting was later seized and displayed in the Nazi **Degenerate Art Exhibition (1937)** as "Soldier with Whore"; Kirchner committed suicide in Switzerland in 1938.
- **Function.** A public expression of Kirchner's disillusionment, psychological damage, and fear that the war had ended him as an artist.
- **Content.** Kirchner stands in uniform at the foreground, eyes blank blue, **right hand severed** — though he never lost his hand, the amputation symbolizes fear he could no longer paint. Behind him, a gaunt, androgynous naked model (possibly a symbolic self-reflection) sits with empty black eyes. The model also seems to blur into a painting on the wall.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; chaotic composition; saturated red background, black canvas behind the model, green wall matching his severed stump; figures rendered with elongated, jaundiced-yellow bodies. All colors are vivid and disorganized.

**Vocabulary:** Primitivism · Expressionism · Die Brücke
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#150](#150--lipstick-ascending-on-caterpillar-tracks) — Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks (Claes Oldenburg, 1969–1974)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/79/Lipstick-catepillar.jpg/250px-Lipstick-catepillar.jpg" width="180" alt="Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks">

**Theme:** Anti-War

> Both works are explicitly against war, and were created primarily to express their creator's views on war.

---

#### → [#122](#122--the-scream) — The Scream (Edvard Munch, 1893)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg/330px-Edvard_Munch%2C_1893%2C_The_Scream%2C_oil%2C_tempera_and_pastel_on_cardboard%2C_91_x_73_cm%2C_National_Gallery_of_Norway.jpg" width="180" alt="The Scream">

**Theme:** Anguish, Expressionism

> Both pieces feature highly stylized figures depicted in scenes of anguish, which is intended to depict the emotional state of the subject rather than the objective reality, as is custom with expressionist art.

---

#### → [#126](#126--les-demoiselles-d'avignon) — Les Demoiselles d'Avignon (Pablo Picasso, 1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/4c/Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg/330px-Les_Demoiselles_d%27Avignon.jpg" width="180" alt="Les Demoiselles d'Avignon">

**Theme:** Heavy embrace of primitivism, bridging between old and new styles.

> Both pieces are highly characteristic of primitivism in terms of their designs, which is very clearly seen in how both pieces draw upon very primitive styles while simultaneously experimenting with the avant-garde.

## #134 — Memorial Sheet for Karl Liebknecht (*In Memoriam Karl Liebknecht*)

**Käthe Kollwitz** (German Expressionism) · 1919–1920 C.E. · Woodcut · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 1 ft 1¾ in × 1 ft 7⅝ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/K%C3%A4the_Kollwitz_Gedenkblatt_f%C3%BCr_Karl_Liebknecht_1920.jpg/330px-K%C3%A4the_Kollwitz_Gedenkblatt_f%C3%BCr_Karl_Liebknecht_1920.jpg" width="240" alt="Memorial Sheet for Karl Liebknecht">

### Summary

A post-WWI woodcut that recasts a **political assassination** as a modern **Lamentation**.

- **Historical Context.** Made in the **disillusionment** after WWI, when German artists turned to print because it was cheap and mass-reproducible — ideal for political statements. Communist leader **Karl Liebknecht** was arrested and killed in custody in 1919 during the leftist uprising against the Socialist-led Weimar government. Kollwitz herself was closer to the Socialists, but had been moved by Liebknecht's charisma at a public speech; his family commissioned her to memorialize him.
- **Function.** A memorial, not an endorsement. Kollwitz mourns the man while withholding approval of his Communist politics, and casts his unjust death as evidence of the corruption of power — adopting the **Lamentation** format to compare his sacrifice to Christ's.
- **Content.** Liebknecht's body lies along the bottom of the sheet, draped in pure white. Men, women, and children crowd above him; one mourner bends over the body with hand to mouth. Downcast faces, slightly individualized, echo the grief of followers at Christ's Lamentation.
- **Form.** Two-dimensional **woodcut** print in black, white, and tan. Curvilinear lines carry most of the modeling; no naturalistic shading. Kollwitz had switched from etching to woodcut after seeing Ernst Barlach's work, finding the coarser medium truer to the monumental subject.

**Vocabulary:** printmaking · woodblock · Lamentation · German Expressionism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, 1830–1833)

**Theme:** Woodblock printing, Solid colors, Japanese Art, Curving lines, Non-Individualistic humans, Personify waves (tension is very present)

> Both are examples of wood block paintings that were produced. They use different types of lines but lines give depth and detail, there is no shading in either, due to the form of art. While Under the Wave has color, the colors are mostly solid and do not mix or vary. Lastly they are both show humans that are very similar looking, though the people in the Memoriam piece are a little more individualistic. Both pieces are able to produce great emotion in the viewer, sadness and grief and excitement and suspense even fear, while the emotions different the woodblock prints are both extremely expressive art pieces.

---

#### → [#209](#209--taj-mahal) — Taj Mahal (Mughal, 1632–1653)

**Theme:** Memorial, India Art, Architecture, Massive, Mausoleum, Influences of Muslim Art

> Both are examples of political gestures of power and importance. While the Taj Mahal portrays the significance of the dead through the monumental architecture and the grandness of the building itself, but the In Memoriam Karl Liebknecht the piece itself is not very imposing, but the connection to Jesus signifies the importance of the piece and significance of the death of Liebknecht. The two images both represent the importance of the deaths and act as memorials for the figures themselves.

---

#### → [#63](#63--arena-scrovegni-chapel-including-lamentation) — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel: Lamentation (Giotto, c. 1305)

**Theme:** Church, Lamentation, Fresco, Renaissance Art

> Both are scenes of Lamentation. The church has a classic Lamentation scene in which Jesus is being mourned by his mother, Mary Magdalene, and Saint John, as well as other followers. But this piece is a modern adaptation of the classic Lamentation scene. They differ greatly in their presentation of the Lamentation but they both create a pure image of the martyr that is being mourned.

## #135 — Villa Savoye

**Le Corbusier** (International Style / Modernism) · 1929 C.E. · Steel and reinforced concrete · Poissy-sur-Seine, France

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3c/VillaSavoye.jpg/250px-VillaSavoye.jpg" width="240" alt="Villa Savoye">

### Summary

The textbook demonstration of Le Corbusier's **Five Points of a New Architecture**.

- **Historical Context.** Designed by Swiss-French architect **Le Corbusier** (Charles-Édouard Jeanneret-Gris) in 1929 in the village of **Poissy**, just outside Paris. Sits at the center of the **International Style** of modernism, which stripped architecture of ornament and demanded that form follow function — a machine-age aesthetic abstracted from its landscape.
- **Function.** A private weekend country house for the Savoye family; simultaneously a built manifesto for Le Corbusier's **Five Points**: pilotis, roof garden, free plan, horizontal ribbon windows, and free façade.
- **Content.** Two floors on stilts. Ground floor holds the entrance hall, garage, and servants' rooms, laid out on the turning radius of a car. The main floor above has the bedrooms and a long ramp rising to the **roof garden**. Continuous **ribbon windows** run around the exterior.
- **Form.** Reinforced concrete and steel; a white cube lifted off the earth on slender **pilotis**. Walls are non-load-bearing, freeing the façade for aesthetics alone. Geometric, machine-like precision with no decorative relief.

**Vocabulary:** International Style · Modernist · Five Points · pilotis
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#146](#146--seagram-building) — Seagram Building (Mies van der Rohe and Philip Johnson, 1954–1958)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Seagram_Building_%2835098307116%29.jpg/330px-Seagram_Building_%2835098307116%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Seagram Building">

**Theme:** Modernism and functionalist aesthetic, International Style

> Both the Villa Savoye and the Seagram Buildings are exemplars of the modernist style, which emphasized functional utilitarianism, and also detachment from the environment to a more abstract form. Though also share the medium employed of steel. They are also both in the international style, which can be seen by the uniformity in both, which was emphasized in the Seagram Building with the windows only being able to be in the open, closed, or half open position, so to minimize the disorder from idiosyncratic windown opening tendencies.

---

#### → [#124](#124--carson-pirie-scott-and-company-building) — Carson, Pirie, Scott, and Company Building (Louis Sullivan, 1899–1903)

**Theme:** Modernism, commercial/residential architecture

> Both emphasize the windows: in the Villa Savoye the large horizontal windows, to let in natural light and to expound the 5 Points of the new Architectural Aesthetic with the horizontal windows requirement; the Carson, Pirie, Scott, and Company has the innovative steel framework to allow for large bay windows which made "window-shopping" possible. Thus, both buildings are similar in their media, and in their windows.

---

#### → [#139](#139--fallingwater) — Fallingwater (Frank Lloyd Wright, 1936–1939)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fallingwater3.jpg/330px-Fallingwater3.jpg" width="180" alt="Fallingwater">

**Theme:** Prairie Style, Modern Architecture

> The Prairie Style emphasizes the horizontal flat lines with overhanging eaves, which is extended in Fallingwater to the overhanging, pouring concrete cantilever terraces that mirror the pouring waters of the water fall.

---

## #136 — Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow

**Piet Mondrian** (De Stijl / Neoplasticism) · 1930 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Paris · 18.11 × 18.11 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg/330px-Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg" width="240" alt="Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow">

### Summary

The pure **Neoplastic** grid — primary colors, black lines, and nothing else.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Paris by **Piet Mondrian**, the Dutch co-founder with **Theo van Doesburg** of **De Stijl** ("The Style"), a utopian art movement born out of WWI disillusionment that sought spiritual harmony through pure abstraction. Mondrian had moved from the Netherlands to Paris in 1918 and remained there until 1938. By 1930 De Stijl was already declining.
- **Function.** Non-representational. The painting has no subject; it aims only to embody a universal cosmic order — spiritual harmony expressed in the simplest possible visual language.
- **Content.** Variations of squares and rectangles filled with the primary colors (red, blue, yellow) against white, bounded by thick black horizontal and vertical lines. A large red square dominates the upper right.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas. Looks flat when reproduced but the actual surface has texture — the whites are layered with visible directional brushwork while the blacks and primary colors are flatter. The composition is asymmetric yet balanced.

**Vocabulary:** Neoplasticism · De Stijl · abstraction · primary colors
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#149](#149--the-bay) — The Bay (Helen Frankenthaler, 1963)

**Theme:** Non-Object / Abstract

> Simplicity and harmony

---

#### → Stadia II (Julie Mehretu, 2004) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Abstract Landscape

> Geometric shapes with color blocks

---

#### → [#133](#133--self-portrait-as-a-soldier) — Self-Portrait as a Soldier (Ernst Ludwig Kirchner, 1915)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg/500px-Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg" width="180" alt="Self-Portrait as a Soldier">

**Theme:** Abstract Portrait

> Heavy use of primary colors

## #137 — Illustration from *The Results of the First Five-Year Plan*

**Varvara Stepanova** (Soviet Constructivism / Russian avant-garde) · 1932 C.E. · Photomontage (illustrated book) · Moscow · 11⅞ × 8⅞ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Soviet_Five_Year_Plan_Poster.jpg/330px-Soviet_Five_Year_Plan_Poster.jpg" width="240" alt="Illustration from *The Results of the First Five-Year Plan*">
### Summary

A **Constructivist photomontage** turning Stalin's first Five-Year Plan into visual propaganda.

- **Historical Context.** Made in **Moscow** in 1932, just as Stalin's first Five-Year Plan (1928–1932) was declared complete a year ahead of schedule. The Russian avant-garde had, by this point, redirected its experimental formal language toward state-sponsored graphic design and propaganda — art for the masses rather than the bourgeoisie.
- **Function.** Propaganda. The image teaches the Soviet public that the Plan worked, and that gratitude is owed to Lenin and Stalin — visual evidence calibrated to shape opinion and loyalty.
- **Content.** A Lenin portrait at right looks off into the "future"; below him a large crowd celebrates. At left, orators, white **CCCP** banners, and a prominent red numeral **5**. A red electrical tower and red-saturated foreground echo the Soviet flag.
- **Form.** **Photomontage** — multiple photographs sliced and layered at mismatched scale to create dynamism. Reduced palette: black-and-white and sepia photographs against geometric planes of red. Sharp contrasts and scale jumps push the images toward the viewer.

**Vocabulary:** USSR · photomontage · propaganda · Five-Year Plan · avant-garde · Constructivism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#108](#108--liberty-leading-the-people) — Liberty Leading the People (Eugène Delacroix, 1830)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg/500px-La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg" width="180" alt="Liberty Leading the People">

**Theme:** Social Commentary

> Both act as a sort of propaganda to encourage the common person to believe in the desired point of view

---

#### → [#231](#231--untitled-#228-from-the-history-portraits-series) — Untitled (#228), from the History Portraits series (Cindy Sherman, 1990)

**Theme:** Historical

> Both depict historical or well-known events that were to of likely occurred

#### → [#243](#243--darkytown-rebellion) — Darkytown Rebellion (Kara Walker, 2001)

**Theme:** Social Commentary

> Both works serve to try to educate the public on their content and spread the word of their thought provoking meanings
