## #138 — Object (*Le Déjeuner en fourrure*)

**Meret Oppenheim** (Surrealism) · 1936 C.E. · Fur-covered cup, saucer, and spoon (assemblage sculpture) · Museum of Modern Art, New York · cup Ø 10.9 cm; saucer Ø 23.7 cm; spoon L 20.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/Meret_Oppenheim._Object._Paris%2C_1936_-MoMA.png/330px-Meret_Oppenheim._Object._Paris%2C_1936_-MoMA.png" width="240" alt="Object (Le Déjeuner en fourrure)">

### Summary

The single most-cited object of **Surrealist sculpture**.

- **Historical Context.** Made in 1936 in Paris when Oppenheim was 23, at the peak of **Surrealism**. The work was born in a café conversation with **Picasso** and **Dora Maar**: Oppenheim was wearing a fur-covered brass-tube bracelet; Picasso joked that anything could be covered in fur, and Oppenheim shot back, "Even this cup and saucer." Shown soon after at MoMA's 1936 *Fantastic Art, Dada, Surrealism* exhibition, where visitors voted it "the quintessential Surrealist object."
- **Function.** Non-utilitarian. A provocation — pure Surrealist dépaysement, making the familiar disturbing — and a symbol of domesticity and femininity in the café-culture of 1930s Paris.
- **Content.** A café teacup, saucer, and spoon, all completely wrapped in **Chinese gazelle fur** in shades of bistre and camel yellow.
- **Form.** An **assemblage** of found objects — a Surrealist technique of juxtaposition. Realistic proportions, smooth geometry (circle of cup, oval of saucer), but the entire surface replaced with fur texture. The contradiction between fur (luxury, sensuality) and the mouth-to-cup action drives the effect.

**Vocabulary:** Surrealism · automatism · assemblage sculpture
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → Lying with the Wolf (Kiki Smith, 2001) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Relationships between humans and animals

> Both show instances of societal values towards animals: In the object, the piece shows how society takes advantage of animals to create fur pieces while Lying with the Wolf shows the potential bond between humans and animals.

---

#### → [#237](#237--pisupo-lua-afe-corned-beef-2000) — Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000) (Michel Tuffery, 1994)

**Theme:** Animal Consumption

> There are two connections shown: 1) both pieces focus on how humans interact and use animals and 2) Corned beef shows an animal made out of unconventional material that humans use to contain foods and drinks while The Object shows a material that humans use to contain foods and drinks (the café cup) made out of parts of an animal.

---

#### → Pink Panther (Jeff Koons, 1988) — *not in image set*

**Theme:** Hollywood fantasy

> Both pieces address certain aspects of the bourgeoisie at their respective time periods. The pink panther is shown as worn and tired which may refer to, again, how humans treated animals at that time. The pink panther, however, has been criticized with anti-feminist sentiments while Meret Oppenheim was known for leading her way through a male dominated era and portraying potentially feminist ideas in her works of art.

---

## #139 — Fallingwater

**Frank Lloyd Wright** (Prairie Style / American modernism) · 1936–1939 C.E. · Reinforced concrete, sandstone, steel, and glass · Bear Run, Pennsylvania · 5,330 sq ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fallingwater3.jpg/330px-Fallingwater3.jpg" width="240" alt="Fallingwater">

### Summary

Wright's career-defining **cantilevered house** built directly over a waterfall.

- **Historical Context.** Designed mid-Depression when architects like Wright were struggling; commissioned by Pittsburgh department-store owner Edgar J. Kaufmann after his son discovered Wright's work. The house extends Wright's earlier **Prairie School** (1900–1917) into the 1930s, embracing harmony with site, overhanging eaves, horizontality, and organic materials. Became a National Historic Landmark; given to the Western Pennsylvania Conservancy in 1963 and opened to the public.
- **Function.** A family weekend retreat for the Kaufmanns over Bear Run. Now a public museum and tourable landmark.
- **Content.** A three-level residence cantilevered directly over the stream and its waterfall. Ochre-colored concrete terraces extend outward; interior rooms open to the landscape; native **sandstone** walls anchor the vertical core.
- **Form.** Three-dimensional architecture built from **reinforced-concrete cantilevers**, sandstone walls, steel, and glass. Strong horizontal lines intersect the waterfall so that the house seems to generate the water itself. Ochre concrete matches local sandstone, binding the structure to its site; smooth concrete contrasts with rough stone.

**Vocabulary:** cantilever · terrace · mullion · Prairie Style
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#102](#102--monticello) — Monticello (Thomas Jefferson, 1768–1809)

**Theme:** Home, Distinctive Style of Architect

> These homes reflect the distinct style of each architect. Monticello reflects Jefferson's neoclassic style as well as his desire to save space in the home. Fallingwater depicts Wright's Prairie School of architecture design and the desired harmony with nature.

---

#### → [#135](#135--villa-savoye) — Villa Savoye (Le Corbusier, 1929)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/3/3c/VillaSavoye.jpg/250px-VillaSavoye.jpg" width="180" alt="Villa Savoye">

**Theme:** Home, Concrete, Rectangular, Early Twentieth Century Architecture

> These two homes possess similar building materials of concrete and steel, as well as distinct rectangular shapes in both. These living spaces were made in the early twentieth century by two very prominent architects, Wright and Le Corbusier.

---

#### → [#39](#39--house-of-the-vettii) — House of the Vettii (Roman, Pompeii, pre-79 C.E.)

**Theme:** Home, Open Space, Natural Lighting

> Both of these homes are from very different cultural periods, but both seek to incorporate vast amounts of natural light in the home. Interior design was an important aspect of both of these works as well.

---

## #140 — The Two Fridas

**Frida Kahlo** (Mexican modernism) · 1939 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museo de Arte Moderno, Mexico City · 67-11/16 × 67-11/16 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f9/The_Two_Fridas.jpg" width="240" alt="The Two Fridas">

### Summary

A double self-portrait made in the year Kahlo divorced **Diego Rivera**.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in 1939, the same year Kahlo divorced the Mexican muralist **Diego Rivera** (they remarried in 1940). Before marrying Rivera in 1929 Kahlo dressed in modern European clothing; Rivera encouraged her embrace of **Tehuana** indigenous dress, which became central to her identity. Kahlo painted roughly 200 works, most self-portraits; this double portrait marks an emotional counterweight to her 1931 *Frida and Diego Rivera* wedding double-portrait.
- **Function.** A personal expression of the divorce — resilience paired with grief. Kahlo noted it also drew on a childhood memory of an imaginary friend.
- **Content.** Two Fridas hold hands on a bench before a stormy sky. Left: "European" Frida in a white Victorian dress, her heart torn open, bleeding onto her lap where she holds surgical pincers that have cut the artery running between the two figures. Right: "Mexican" Frida in a traditional **Tehuana** dress, her heart intact, holding a small portrait of Rivera as a child. The blood trails across her white dress; she is at risk of bleeding out.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; nearly symmetrical composition fills the frame; the connecting artery is the literal and formal link between the two halves. Agitated clouds externalize Kahlo's inner turmoil; blood is used as a metaphor for union.

**Vocabulary:** self-portrait · indigenous · Tehuana · Mexican modernism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#68](#68--the-arnolfini-portrait) — The Arnolfini Portrait (Jan van Eyck, 1434)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/The_Arnolfini_portrait_%281434%29.jpg/330px-The_Arnolfini_portrait_%281434%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Arnolfini Portrait">

**Theme:** portrait, introspective

> depicts two lovers holding hands in a similar manner w/ a deep sense of connection between the two figures

---

#### → [#205](#205--portrait-of-sin-sukju) — Portrait of Sin Sukju (Joseon Korea, 15th century)

**Theme:** portrait

> full-body portrait with emphasis on clothing details and rather simple background and facial features

---

#### → [#105](#105--self-portrait) — Self-Portrait (Louise Élisabeth Vigée Le Brun, 1790)

**Theme:** self-portrait, self-reflection

> self-portrait of a female artist, oil on canvas

---

## #141 — The Migration of the Negro, Panel no. 49

**Jacob Lawrence** (Harlem Renaissance / modernist) · 1940–1941 C.E. · Casein tempera on hardboard · The Phillips Collection, Washington, D.C. · 18 × 12 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Migration_of_Negro_Lawrence_49.jpg/330px-Migration_of_Negro_Lawrence_49.jpg" width="240" alt="The Migration of the Negro, Panel no. 49">
### Summary

Panel 49 of Lawrence's 60-panel **Migration Series**, captioned *"They found discrimination in the North. It was a different kind."*

- **Historical Context.** Lawrence (b. 1917) moved to Harlem in 1924 and studied at the Harlem Art Workshop during the **Harlem Renaissance**. He looked to African American history for subject matter and painted this as part of his 60-panel series documenting the **Great Migration** — the movement of African Americans from the rural South to northern cities in search of work, housing, and freedom from Jim Crow. The series was split evenly between MoMA and The Phillips Collection in 1942.
- **Function.** To force a white public to see that Northern segregation still existed. Each panel is paired with a caption; the captions form an objective narrative voice that contrasts with the emotional images, juxtaposing an outside view with an inside one.
- **Content.** A dining room split by a taut **gold rope** that literally segregates the space: white diners on one side, Black diners on the other. One white figure looks almost haughty; another is absorbed in his newspaper. The Black figures are silhouetted, given form by the contours of their bodies rather than by faces — only the white figures get individual facial features. A small upper-right figure emphasizes isolation.
- **Form.** **Casein tempera** on hardboard; a neutral palette of browns and greys, flattened background, strong diagonals, silhouetted figures. Simplified angular forms rooted in **Cubism** but stripped down to essentials for narrative immediacy. The gold rope moves the eye across the composition.

**Vocabulary:** narrative · genre painting · Cubism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#140](#140--the-two-fridas) — The Two Fridas (Frida Kahlo, 1939)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/f/f9/The_Two_Fridas.jpg" width="180" alt="The Two Fridas">

**Theme:** Similar intent to explore topics like mixed heritage, medical conditions, oppression, and discrimination.

> Both artists express their identity and heritage in their artwork. As minority artists, their work often focuses on issues that minorities deal with.

---

#### → [#243](#243--darkytown-rebellion) — Darkytown Rebellion (Kara Walker, 2001)

**Theme:** Reflects on black history and condemns discrimination faced by blacks throughout history.

> Both convey immense emotion through simple depictions of human bodies, through a geometric simplification or silhouettes. Both focus on human experience and racial supremacy.

---

#### → [#235](#235--rebellious-silence-from-the-women-of-allah-series) — Rebellious Silence (Shirin Neshat, 1994)

**Theme:** Reflects on discrimination and oppression of a particular group. Examines multiple perspectives of an issue.

> Both works explore various perspectives of an issue or group of people. They combine views from the outside world, and the views of the subject group of people.

## #142 — The Jungle

**Wifredo Lam** (Cuban Surrealism / Cubism) · 1943 C.E. · Gouache on paper mounted on canvas · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 94¼ × 90½ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/21/%27The_Jungle%27%2C_gouache_on_paper_painting_by_Wifredo_Lam%2C_1943%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art.jpg/250px-%27The_Jungle%27%2C_gouache_on_paper_painting_by_Wifredo_Lam%2C_1943%2C_Museum_of_Modern_Art.jpg" width="240" alt="The Jungle">

### Summary

A **Cuban Surrealist–Cubist** indictment of colonialism and the afterlife of slavery.

- **Historical Context.** **Wifredo Lam** (Cuban, b. 1902) was of Afro-Cuban, Chinese, and European descent. Studying in Spain and Paris, he met the **Cubists** and **Surrealists** (including Picasso and Breton). After returning to Cuba he saw the poverty and oppression of Afro-Cubans firsthand, the legacy of sugar-plantation slavery and of the US-backed Batista regime. The painting was made in 1943, with WWII still ongoing.
- **Function.** A protest and reclamation. Lam wanted to expose the ongoing suffering of Black Cubans and the destructive legacy of colonialism and cash-crop economy — to push back against exoticized tropical imagery.
- **Content.** A dense jungle of sugarcane is occupied by four tall figures with elongated bodies, African-mask-like faces, bird- or animal-like hands and feet, and round buttocks. The figures are part human, part vegetal, part spirit; they appear to be harvesting their own crops, merging with the cane.
- **Form.** Gouache on paper mounted on canvas. Composition is denser at top, more open below; warm tones on the left contrast with cooler tones on the right. Geometric, Cubist breakdown of limbs combines with Surrealist hybrid forms; African mask vocabulary (partly via Picasso) drives the faces.

**Vocabulary:** gouache · Cubism · Surrealism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#181](#181--petra-jordan:-treasury-and-great-temple) — Petra, Jordan: Treasury and Great Temple (Nabataean, c. 400 B.C.E.–100 C.E.)

**Theme:** Ptolemaic and Roman Art: Use of figural art in religious contexts varies among traditions, whereas figural art is common in secular art forms across West and Central Asia.

> Art inspired from Diverse Cultural Traditions. Petra shows influence of Hellenistic as well as Near Eastern architectural influences while The Jungle draws on Lam's black heritage, Cuban culture, and Cubic and Surrealist artistic movements.

---

#### → [#64](#64--golden-haggadah-the-plagues-of-egypt-scenes-of-liberation-and-preparation-for-passover) — Golden Haggadah (Late medieval Spain, c. 1320)

**Theme:** Medieval artistic traditions include late antique, early Christian, Byzantine, Islamic, migratory, Carolingian, Romanesque, and Gothic, named for their principal culture, religion, government, and/or artistic style.

> Art influenced by various cultural traditions. The Golden Haggadah seems to draw upon Christian Gothic influences, despite being commissioned by Jewish patrons. Meanwhile, Petra features rosettes in its tombs, which is indicative of Near Eastern architecture, despite being a Hellenistic building.

---

#### → [#237](#237--pisupo-lua-afe-corned-beef-2000) — Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000) (Michel Tuffery, 1994)

**Theme:** Global Contemporary Art

> Art that critiques society. THe Pisupo Lua Afe criticizes Pacific Islanders' overreliance on canned food, having created a health epidemic in Pacific Island countries. The Jungle by Wifredo Lam critically examines Cuban society, which has discriminated against its black citizens.

---

## #143 — Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in the Alameda Park (*Sueño de una tarde dominical en la Alameda Central*)

**Diego Rivera** (Mexican Muralism) · 1947–1948 C.E. · Fresco · Museo Mural Diego Rivera, Mexico City · 4.8 × 15 m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rivera_Dream_Sunday.jpg/330px-Rivera_Dream_Sunday.jpg" width="240" alt="Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in the Alameda Park (*Sueño de una tarde dominical en la Alameda Central*)">
### Summary

A **history of Mexico** told as a dream in the Alameda Central park.

- **Historical Context.** Painted 1947–1948 originally for the **Hotel del Prado** in Mexico City. After the 1985 earthquake damaged the hotel, the fresco was moved to a purpose-built museum beside the **Alameda Central**, Mexico City's first public park and former Aztec marketplace. Spans three major eras: **Spanish conquest**, **Porfirio Díaz dictatorship**, and **Mexican Revolution**.
- **Function.** A public celebration and critique of Mexican identity — Rivera honors indigenous and popular culture while attacking the colonial Church, the dictatorship, and class oppression. Also a personal autobiography: Rivera paints himself as a child at center, holding hands with **La Catrina**, with **Frida Kahlo** behind him holding a yin-yang.
- **Content.** More than 400 historical figures: **Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz** in nun's habit; **Benito Juárez**, five-term indigenous-origin president; **Ignacio Ramírez** holding a paper reading "God does not exist"; Santa Anna handing keys of Mexico to General Winfield Scott; Emperor Maximilian and Empress Carlota; José Martí tipping his hat; Porfirio Díaz dozing with his medals; a police officer evicting a poor family from the park.
- **Form.** Enormous horror-vacui fresco — packed with figures edge to edge — vibrant saturated color, heroic scale.

**Vocabulary:** La Catrina · Porfirio Díaz · Sor Juana · fresco
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#76](#76--school-of-athens) — The School of Athens (Raphael, 1509–1511)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/%22The_School_of_Athens%22_by_Raffaello_Sanzio_da_Urbino.jpg/330px-%22The_School_of_Athens%22_by_Raffaello_Sanzio_da_Urbino.jpg" width="180" alt="The School of Athens">

**Theme:** scholarly, historical, progress, classic origins

> both frescoes depicting many famous historical figures that weren't alive at the same time colorful and crowded location is culturally significant

#### → [#108](#108--liberty-leading-the-people) — Liberty Leading the People (Eugène Delacroix, 1830)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg/500px-La_Libert%C3%A9_guidant_le_peuple_-_Eug%C3%A8ne_Delacroix_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Peintures_RF_129_-_apr%C3%A8s_restauration_2024.jpg" width="180" alt="Liberty Leading the People">

## #144 — Fountain (second version)

**Marcel Duchamp** (Dada) · 1950 C.E. (original 1917) · Readymade glazed sanitary china with black paint · Philadelphia Museum of Art · ~360 × 480 × 610 mm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Marcel_Duchamp%2C_1917%2C_Fountain%2C_photograph_by_Alfred_Stieglitz.jpg/250px-Marcel_Duchamp%2C_1917%2C_Fountain%2C_photograph_by_Alfred_Stieglitz.jpg" width="240" alt="Fountain by Duchamp">

### Summary

The founding work of **readymade** art and an icon of **Dada**.

- **Historical Context.** **Dada** emerged in Europe during WWI as a reaction against the violence, nationalism, and rationalism of the war — artists of the **Lost Generation** rejecting the whole apparatus of high art. Duchamp, in New York, helped found the **Society of Independent Artists** whose 1917 salon was advertised as unjuried; he submitted *Fountain* under the false name "R. Mutt" to test that promise. The board rejected it for indecency. The original was lost; only Alfred Stieglitz's photo and 17 authorized 1960s replicas survive.
- **Function.** Not a urinal — a provocation. A challenge to the institution of art: can a mass-produced object become art by the artist's choice and signature alone?
- **Content.** A porcelain urinal from **J. L. Mott Iron Works**, rotated 90°, signed **"R. Mutt 1917"** — a pun on the Mutt and Jeff comic strip, the manufacturer, or German "Armut" (poverty).
- **Form.** A **readymade** — an ordinary industrial object recontextualized as sculpture. Its reorientation frustrates the object's function and forces the viewer to consider form, shadow, and shape instead. Duchamp had a long-running interest in representing a fourth spatial dimension.

**Vocabulary:** Dadaism · readymade
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#138](#138--object-*le-déjeuner-en-fourrure*) — Object (Meret Oppenheim, 1936)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/5/5d/Meret_Oppenheim._Object._Paris%2C_1936_-MoMA.png/330px-Meret_Oppenheim._Object._Paris%2C_1936_-MoMA.png" width="180" alt="Object">

**Theme:** erotic overtones, reality-based objects, abstract, challenge against conventionalism

> Both are objects, rebranded as art. Their abstract quality and erotic overtones is suggestive of rejection/challenge to society.

---

#### → [#133](#133--self-portrait-as-a-soldier) — Self-Portrait as a Soldier (Ernst Ludwig Kirchner, 1915)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg/500px-Kirchner_-_Selbstbildnis_als_Soldat.jpg" width="180" alt="Self-Portrait as a Soldier">

**Theme:** masculinity, artist's discontent, abstract, challenge against conventionalism

> Both deal with the theme of masculinity at its most intimate, likely as a reaction against the displacement of the manhood by society.

---

#### → [#130](#130--the-portuguese) — The Portuguese (Georges Braque, 1911)

**Theme:** abstract, word media, spatial arrangement

> Both are abstract that is meant to challenge the audience's perception of depth and dimension. In addition, both include words as part of the art, typical of their respective genres.

## #145 — Woman, I

**Willem de Kooning** (Abstract Expressionism) · 1950–1952 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 192.7 × 147.3 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/2/2a/Woman_I-Willem_de_Kooning.jpg" width="240" alt="Woman, I">

### Summary

De Kooning's signature **Abstract Expressionist** assault on the commercial female image.

- **Historical Context.** De Kooning was a **Dutch-American** painter and one of the rare abstract expressionists with a traditional academic training, close to **Jackson Pollock** and **Mark Rothko**. He worked on *Woman, I* for almost two years — layering and scraping away paint repeatedly — because he valued the process over the finished product.
- **Function.** A critical response to the flood of commercialized, sexualized imagery of women in postwar advertising and photography. The result is deliberately monstrous — what those images actually look like when their components are combined.
- **Content.** A large, confrontational female figure, part Madonna and part pin-up, reduced to exaggerated eyes, teeth, and breasts — the parts commercial imagery fixates on. Arms and legs dissolve in the surrounding chaos.
- **Form.** Thick layers of oil in quick slashing brushstrokes (despite the two-year gestation). **Garish** hot pinks, oranges, and yellows set against dull neutrals, with a silver band down the right side. No stable spatial depth.

**Vocabulary:** expressionism · Abstract Expressionism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#107](#107--la-grande-odalisque) — La Grande Odalisque (Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres, 1814)

**Theme:** femininity

> Both have a lack of anatomical realism

---

#### → [#231](#231--untitled-#228-from-the-history-portraits-series) — Untitled #228 (Cindy Sherman, 1990)

**Theme:** Renaissance ideals

> Both refer to Renaissance depictions of women and integrate those elements into the works

---

#### → [#147](#147--marilyn-diptych) — Marilyn Diptych (Andy Warhol, 1962)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/87/Marilyndiptych.jpg" width="180" alt="Marilyn Diptych">

**Theme:** Commercialization of women

> Both works focus on the American public's new exposure to celebrities, advertisement, and commercialization and how it affects their perception of women

## #146 — Seagram Building

**Ludwig Mies van der Rohe and Philip Johnson** (International Style / Modernism) · 1954–1958 C.E. · Steel frame with glass curtain wall and bronze · New York City · 516 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/99/Seagram_Building_%2835098307116%29.jpg/330px-Seagram_Building_%2835098307116%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Seagram Building">

### Summary

The landmark **International-Style skyscraper** of postwar corporate America.

- **Historical Context.** Built during the postwar **1950s boom** in New York; at the time the most expensive skyscraper ever erected. **Mies van der Rohe** (German-born, emigrated to the U.S. before WWII) carried the European modernist creed into American corporate architecture — "structure is spiritual," "less is more." Co-designed with Philip Johnson.
- **Function.** Headquarters for the Canadian distiller **Joseph E. Seagram & Sons**; now owned by RFR Holdings. A monument to multinational corporate prestige.
- **Content.** A sheer bronze-and-glass tower set back on a granite plaza with twin reflecting pools on Park Avenue. The set-back created a new urban typology: the **plaza skyscraper**.
- **Form.** Steel frame encased in concrete for the building code. On the exterior, non-structural **bronze-toned I-beams** are welded to the curtain wall to suggest the building's skeleton — a visual honesty about structure. No decorative ornament. Interior lobby and restaurants ("The Four Seasons") lavishly finished in bronze, travertine, and marble.

**Vocabulary:** functionalist · corporate modernism · International Style · curtain wall
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 3.5

---

## #147 — Marilyn Diptych

**Andy Warhol** (Pop Art) · 1962 C.E. · Oil, acrylic, and silkscreen enamel on canvas · Tate Modern, London · 81 × 114 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/8/87/Marilyndiptych.jpg" width="240" alt="Marilyn Diptych">

### Summary

Warhol's Pop-art memorial for **Marilyn Monroe**, turning celebrity into a printed icon.

- **Historical Context.** Created in August 1962 immediately after Marilyn Monroe's death; first shown at the Stable Gallery in New York. Warhol was steeped in American pop culture and was a **Ruthenian Catholic** — the diptych format echoes religious altarpieces. Marilyn was the most famous **sex symbol** of the 1950s.
- **Function.** A commemoration of Monroe and a meditation on fame, mass-reproduction, life, and death. The contrast between color and greyscale halves stages Monroe's vitality on the left and her fade into death on the right.
- **Content.** The same 1953 publicity photograph for the film *Niagara*, repeated 25 times in color (left) and 25 times in greyscale (right) — the right side gradually degrading to near-nothing.
- **Form.** **Silkscreen** on canvas — mesh and stencils transfer the photograph onto the surface, making the painting a mechanical reproduction like a newspaper page. Faces differ in ink density; the flat, commercial palette (yellow hair, pink face, red lips) intensifies the celebrity-image effect.

**Vocabulary:** iconic · abstract expressionism · repetition · pop culture · emotionally flat
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 3.1

---

> Repetition with slight variation

**Theme:** Portraiture

## #148 — Narcissus Garden

**Yayoi Kusama** (Japanese / New York avant-garde) · Original installation and performance 1966 · ~1,500 mirrored plastic balls · First exhibited at Venice Biennale (1966)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Narcissus_Garden_Kusama.jpg/330px-Narcissus_Garden_Kusama.jpg" width="240" alt="Narcissus Garden">
### Summary

An **immersive mirror installation** that implicates the viewer in their own narcissism.

- **Historical Context.** Kusama (b. Nagano, Japan, 1929) moved to **New York** in 1958 and joined the avant-garde scene, drawing on abstract expressionism. She first exhibited *Narcissus Garden* at the **33rd Venice Biennale in 1966**, where — in a gold kimono — she acted as a street peddler selling the mirror spheres individually for $2 until organizers shut her down. That performance doubled as self-promotion and as a protest against art's commodification.
- **Function.** To confront viewers with their own vanity. The 1,500 reflective spheres distort and multiply the viewer's image; in the age of smartphones, the piece now generates selfies and social-media posts — which has made it a commentary on the attention economy as well as on classical narcissism.
- **Content.** References the Greek myth of **Narcissus**, cursed by **Nemesis** to fall in love with his own reflection; the title and mirrored ball-field recreate his pond. The arrangement varies by site: Venice, Le Consortium, Central Park, the Jardin des Tuileries, the Rockaways.
- **Form.** Identical, uniform silver plastic spheres placed in organic groupings that respond to the host landscape — lake, field, ruined building — with walkable channels between groups. The spheres are simultaneously a single work, a performance, and a crowd of reflective selves.

**Vocabulary:** narcissism · Narcissus · installation · performance
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 3.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#114](#114--nadar-raising-photography-to-the-height-of-art) — Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art (Honoré Daumier, 1862)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg/250px-Honor%C3%A9_Daumier%2C_Nadar_%C3%A9levant_la_Photographie_%C3%A0_la_hauteur_de_l%27Art%2C_1862%2C_NGA_42966.jpg" width="180" alt="Nadar Raising Photography to the Height of Art">

**Theme:** prevalence of photography as harmful

> Both Elevating Photography to the Level of Art and Narcissus Garden offer commentary on the prevalence of photography in society. Elevating Photography to the Level of Art directly criticizes photography. Its message is that photography should not be considered art as it requires no skill to take a photo in contrast to actually painting or drawing. The selfie-encouraging nature of Narcissus Garden also reflects how photography has become commonplace and easy to do in today's society.

---

#### → [#93](#93--the-palace-at-versailles) — Palace of Versailles (Louis Le Vau and Jules Hardouin-Mansart, begun 1669)

**Theme:** reflections, grandeur

> The Hall of Mirrors in the Palace of Versailles is similar to Narcissus Garden in that both involve the viewer in the artwork through his/her reflection. However, the Hall of Mirrors is elaborately decorated, while Narcissus Garden is more minimalist and spartan. In addition, the Hall of Mirrors had functional use: it was used as a passageway for Louis XVI and for family and court functions. In contrast, Narcissus Garden is an artwork which contemplates the issues of self-absorption and society's obsession with looks.

---

#### → [#139](#139--fallingwater) — Fallingwater (Frank Lloyd Wright, 1936–1939)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bc/Fallingwater3.jpg/330px-Fallingwater3.jpg" width="180" alt="Fallingwater">

**Theme:** connection with nature

> Fallingwater and Narcissus Garden are similar in that both connect with and integrate into their natural environment. Fallingwater is completely enveloped within the forest, and the ribbon windows allow the forest to almost integrate with the house. Narcissus Garden's silver spheres are placed directly inside its environment in order to create a kind of harmony with it, like a real garden would.

---

## #149 — The Bay

**Helen Frankenthaler** (Color Field / Abstract Expressionism) · 1963 C.E. · Acrylic on canvas · Detroit Institute of Arts · 8′9″ × 6′9″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Frankenthaler_The_Bay.jpg/330px-Frankenthaler_The_Bay.jpg" width="240" alt="The Bay">
### Summary

A defining **Color Field** painting made with Frankenthaler's signature **soak-stain** technique.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in New York during the postwar era when abstract expressionists sought a universal visual language in response to WWII trauma. Frankenthaler developed the **soak-stain method** after a 1952 trip to Nova Scotia, thinning oil (later acrylic) paint so heavily that it soaked into **unprimed canvas** — generating translucent fields meant to evoke hills, rocks, and water. She was introduced to Pollock, Rothko, and David Smith through her partner, critic **Clement Greenberg**.
- **Function.** Non-narrative and expressive. Frankenthaler rarely named her sources, leaving each viewer's interpretation open; she argued that emotional impact mattered more than subject.
- **Content.** An amorphous blue **promontory** against a cream-and-moss-green border — read as a bay viewed from above. The blue shifts from near-indigo at top to lighter violets and blues below.
- **Form.** **Color Field** painting: simplicity of line, color as subject. The soak-stain technique embeds pigment into the canvas rather than sitting on top; by tilting the canvas Frankenthaler lets the paint find its own edges. 1963 is also when she shifted from oil to **acrylic**.

**Vocabulary:** Abstract Expressionism · Color Field · soak-stain method
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#136](#136--composition-with-red-blue-and-yellow) — Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow (Piet Mondrian, 1930)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg/330px-Piet_Mondriaan%2C_1930_-_Mondrian_Composition_II_in_Red%2C_Blue%2C_and_Yellow.jpg" width="180" alt="Composition with Red, Blue and Yellow">

**Theme:** non-representational abstraction, emphasis on color

> Both pieces do not really represent anything, although the title *The Bay* does make it seem as if there is a bay represented. Both emphasize color rather than content or shape.

---

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, 1830–1833)

**Theme:** landscape, power of nature

> Both pieces depict a landscape (both that have to do with bodies of water) with nature dominating the piece.

---

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565)

**Theme:** landscape, perspective

> Both pieces are landscapes that accentuate their respective qualities of perspective; Hunters in the Snow emphasizes its 3-dimensional qualities while The Bay does the same with its 2-dimensionality

## #150 — Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks

**Claes Oldenburg** (Pop Art) · 1969–1974 C.E. · Cor-Ten steel, steel, aluminum, and cast resin; painted polyurethane enamel · Morse College, Yale University, New Haven · 292 × 299 × 131 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/79/Lipstick-catepillar.jpg/250px-Lipstick-catepillar.jpg" width="240" alt="Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks">

### Summary

A **Pop art anti-war monument** that turns cosmetics into a tank.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by a group of Yale architecture students in 1969 to protest the **Vietnam War** — what they called "the second American Revolution." Oldenburg, born in Sweden and educated at Yale, was a central figure in **Pop Art**. He rejected art that "sits on its ass in a museum," instead placing his monument in Yale's Beinecke Plaza where it served as a protest **speaking platform**. It deteriorated, was rebuilt, and was reinstalled at **Morse College**.
- **Function.** Political protest. Juxtaposes the feminine, consumerist product of lipstick with the masculine, violent apparatus of a tank to mock the U.S. obsession with beauty and consumption amid the casualties of Vietnam.
- **Content.** A giant cylindrical tube of lipstick mounted on the treads of a tank — reads simultaneously as a missile firing and a lipstick rising. Originally the lipstick was an inflatable that could be raised or lowered.
- **Form.** **Cor-Ten steel** tank body painted black; cylindrical pink-and-gold metallic lipstick above. Pop Art scale and color; inexpensive industrial materials; deliberately durable public monument aesthetics.

**Vocabulary:** Pop art · monument · Cor-Ten steel
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

## #153 — Chavín de Huántar

**Chavín culture** · 900–200 B.C.E. · Stone and granite (architectural complex and Lanzón); hammered gold alloy (jewelry) · Northern highlands, Peru · 3,150 ft elevation

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg/250px-Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg" width="240" alt="Chavín de Huántar">

### Summary

The **religious capital** of the pre-Incan **Chavín** culture in highland Peru.

- **Historical Context.** Located at high altitude in the Andes between the **Huachesca and Mosna rivers** — a location considered spiritually charged and a major site of **pilgrimage**. Construction began around 900 B.C.E. and continued in phases; the site is dominated by its **Old Temple** around which the later complex grew. Chavín art is famous for its **anthropomorphic** animal-human fusions.
- **Function.** A pilgrimage and religious center. The Old Temple's interior maze of tunnels (**galleries**) channels sound and air, likely to produce disorienting effects on visitors approaching the god.
- **Content.** Archaeological complex including the Old Temple, relief sculpture on a ruined stairway, and a gold nose ornament with serpent motifs. At the center of the Old Temple's maze stands the **Lanzón** — a 15-foot blade-shaped granite monolith carved in low relief with a composite jaguar-serpent-human deity.
- **Form.** Roughly dressed stone walls and floors, with smoother stones for interior decoration. Low-relief carvings are symmetrical and geometric. Materials (granite, gold alloy) were relatively high-quality for their region.

**Vocabulary:** Lanzón stone · galleries · anthropomorphism · pilgrimage
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#155](#155--yaxchilán) — Yaxchilán (Maya, 725 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Yaxchilan_lintel_25.jpg/250px-Yaxchilan_lintel_25.jpg" width="180" alt="Yaxchilán">

**Theme:** Carved panels, Native American civilizations, worship, religion

> Both structures have spiritual connections to certain deities. Lintel 25 in Yaxchílan shows Lady Xoc invoking the Vision Serpent and practicing bloodletting, leading to hallucinations. These both have semi-animal, semi-human deities.

---

#### → [#154](#154--mesa-verde-cliff-dwellings) — Mesa Verde cliff dwellings (Ancestral Puebloan, 450–1300 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg/330px-Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg" width="180" alt="Mesa Verde cliff dwellings">

**Theme:** Ancient civilizations, Native American, Architectural sites

> Both are architectural sites of Native American civilizations built mainly from materials that could be gathered from the narby regions.

#### → [#156](#156--great-serpent-mound) — Great Serpent Mound (Mississippian, c. 1070 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_Great_Serpent_Mound.jpg/330px-The_Great_Serpent_Mound.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Serpent Mound">

**Theme:** Native American, Serpentine motifs, Spiritual structures

> Chavín de Huántar has many serpent motifs, including the golden nose ring and the deity with snake heads. It shows that snakes may be a common animal of worship for Native American tribes, even across the continents.

## #154 — Mesa Verde cliff dwellings

**Ancestral Puebloan (Anasazi)** · 450–1300 C.E. · Sandstone · Montezuma County, Colorado · up to ~150 rooms per dwelling (Cliff Palace)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg/330px-Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg" width="240" alt="Mesa Verde cliff dwellings">

### Summary

Stone-and-mortar **cliff dwellings** of the Ancestral Puebloan people.

- **Historical Context.** Built by the **Ancestral Puebloans** (formerly called Anasazi, a term now rejected by modern Puebloans) — farmers of corn, beans, and squash in the Four Corners region. They likely descended from the Oshara tradition and Picosa culture. The cliff dwellings were constructed from roughly 1000–1100 C.E. and abandoned during a later migration for still-debated reasons (drought, resource depletion, conflict).
- **Function.** Residential and communal. Each dwelling clustered rooms around a **kiva** — a circular, subterranean chamber used for ceremonies, rituals, and daily life.
- **Content.** More than 600 dwellings survive at Mesa Verde; the largest, **Cliff Palace**, has ~150 rooms and over 20 kivas. Multi-story structures reached only by retractable ladders; interior walls painted with landscape murals.
- **Form.** **Sandstone** with mud mortar, wooden-beam supports, adobe. Rectangular rooms for stability; cylindrical kivas for ritual. Buildings stack one to four stories and fit the contours of the natural cliff alcoves.

**Vocabulary:** kiva · Ancestral Puebloan · cliff dwelling
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4

---

**Theme:** Religious, Rituals

> Comparing the two similar architectural styles can be seen. The lines of the structures are the same, both built in block looking structures.

## #155 — Yaxchilán

**Maya** (Late Classic) · 725 C.E. · Limestone (architectural complex and relief lintels) · Chiapas, Mexico · Lintel 25 ~51 × 34 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Yaxchilan_lintel_25.jpg/250px-Yaxchilan_lintel_25.jpg" width="240" alt="Yaxchilán Lintel 25">

### Summary

A Late Classic **Maya** city famous for its carved limestone **lintels** documenting royal blood-ritual.

- **Historical Context.** Yaxchilán is a Maya city on the Usumacinta River in Chiapas, Mexico. The most famous images are the lintels from **Structure 23**, commissioned by king **Shield Jaguar II** and dedicated to his wife **Lady K'abal Xook (Lady Xook)** to commemorate his accession to the throne.
- **Function.** The lintels, hung over doorways, publicly proclaimed royal legitimacy. **Bloodletting** was a central Maya royal rite — a portal through which ancestors and gods could be called — and tied Shield Jaguar's reign to the creation of humans from the gods' blood.
- **Content.** **Lintel 25** shows Lady Xook kneeling below a rising **Vision Serpent** that emerges from a basin of bloodied paper. A warrior-ancestor emerges from the serpent's mouth; the Mesoamerican rain-and-war god **Tlaloc** appears from its other head. **Lintel 24** shows her pulling a thorned cord through her tongue in the act of bloodletting itself. The stairs carry hieroglyphic writing and stelae.
- **Form.** High-quality **limestone low relief**. Dense, geometric compositions with intricate detail in headdresses, jewelry, and text; symmetrical and balanced; carefully positioned negative space.

**Vocabulary:** lintel · Lady Xook · Shield Jaguar · bloodletting · Vision Serpent
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4

---

**Theme:** Royalty, Religion

> This is another royal couple. The difference is the focus is more on the King in this one and both are actually pictured. But like the Lintel the King and the Queen seem to be of equal status.

#### → [#180](#180--veranda-post-of-enthroned-king-and-senior-wife-*opo-ogoga*) — Veranda Post of Enthroned King and Senior Wife (Opo Ogoga) (Olowe of Ise, Yoruba)

**Theme:** Royalty

> The queen standing beside the king who is on a throne. The Throne is like he is getting crowded like how the Lintel is to commemorate a thronening. Queen also seem equally important

---

#### → [#157](#157--templo-mayor-main-temple) — Templo Mayor (Aztec, 1375–1520)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Templo_Mayor_50.jpg/250px-Templo_Mayor_50.jpg" width="180" alt="Templo Mayor">

**Theme:** Religion

> Both things are from Old american groups. Share some of the same gods, ideals and ways. This temple could have something like a lintel inside. Would also have gods and blood rituals also very important and would happen here.

## #157 — Templo Mayor (Main Temple)

**Mexica (Aztec)** · 1375–1520 C.E. · Stone (temple); volcanic stone (Coyolxauhqui Stone); jadeite (Olmec-style mask); basalt (Calendar Stone) · Tenochtitlan (modern Mexico City) · base 328 × 262 ft; h. ~90 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Templo_Mayor_50.jpg/250px-Templo_Mayor_50.jpg" width="240" alt="Templo Mayor">

### Summary

The ritual heart of the **Aztec** capital — twin pyramids to **Tlaloc** and **Huitzilopochtli** at the navel of the universe.

- **Historical Context.** Construction began c. 1390 C.E. and continued through seven successive enlargements by Aztec rulers (notably **Motecuhzoma I**, 1454) until Cortés's forces razed the city in 1521. The design reflects the **Mexica cosmos**: four parts arranged around the **axis mundi**. Built on the spot where, according to the Mexica, Huitzilopochtli showed them the sign of the **eagle on the cactus eating a snake** — still on the Mexican flag.
- **Function.** Ritual and political center of the Aztec empire. The paired deities embodied **atl-tlachinolli** ("burnt water") — warfare, which produced the empire's wealth. Required **human sacrifice**, bloodletting, copal incense, and music.
- **Content.** A single stone platform carrying **twin pyramids**: left shrine (painted blue and white) to **Tlaloc**, god of rain, on a sacred mountain called Tonacatepetl; right shrine (painted red and white) to **Huitzilopochtli**, god of war and sun, on the Hill of Coatepec. Twin staircases lead up; serpent-head balustrades flank the bases. A **Wall of Skulls** (*tzompantli*) at the foot holds the plastered skulls of sacrificial victims.
- **Form.** Stone covered in **stucco**; broad pyramid steps and symmetrical twin temples. Integrated with the **Coyolxauhqui Stone** at the base of the Huitzilopochtli stair — the dismembered moon goddess — and with the **Calendar Stone** and Olmec-style jadeite mask in the surrounding precinct.

**Vocabulary:** axis mundi · Tlaloc · Huitzilopochtli · atl-tlachinolli · tzompantli
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#159](#159--city-of-cusco-including-qorikancha-santo-domingo-and-walls-at-saqsa-waman) — City of Cusco, including Qorikancha and Sacsayhuaman (Inka, c. 1440)

**Theme:** Social order, keep rulers powerful

> Ceremonial and political center used to consolidate imperial power over a diverse population.

---

#### → [#161](#161--city-of-machu-picchu) — City of Machu Picchu (Inka, c. 1450–1540)

**Theme:** Religious site for large city

> Religious complex serving the ritual life of a large city.

#### → [#153](#153--chavín-de-huántar) — Chavín de Huántar (Chavín, 900–200 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg/250px-Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg" width="180" alt="Chavín de Huántar">

**Theme:** Ceremonial center and center of power

> Both are Andean / Mesoamerican ceremonial centers that combined religious authority with political power.

---

#### → [#154](#154--mesa-verde-cliff-dwellings) — Mesa Verde Cliff Dwellings (Ancestral Puebloan, 450–1300 C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg/330px-Cliff_Palace-Colorado-Mesa_Verde_NP.jpg" width="180" alt="Mesa Verde cliff dwellings">

**Theme:** Continuous rebuilding due to elemental challenges

> Both sites were rebuilt in successive phases responding to environmental and political pressures.

## #158 — Ruler's feather headdress (probably of Motecuhzoma II)

**Mexica (Aztec)** · 1428–1520 C.E. · Feathers (quetzal and cotinga) and gold · Museum of Ethnology, Vienna · 46 × 69 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Feather_headdress_Moctezuma_II.JPG" width="240" alt="Ruler's feather headdress">

### Summary

The only surviving Aztec **feather headdress** and reputedly a gift from **Motecuhzoma II** to Cortés.

- **Historical Context.** Traditionally identified as **Motecuhzoma II's** *quetzalapanecayotl*, probably given to **Hernán Cortés** during the conquest and shipped to **Charles V**, Holy Roman Emperor. Now in Vienna's Weltmuseum. A replica using traditional techniques sits in the National Museum of Anthropology in Mexico City.
- **Function.** A regalia / display object signaling royal and divine authority; possibly worn on the back of a shield-device rather than atop the head. As a diplomatic gift it performed Aztec political importance for a foreign conqueror.
- **Content.** 400 long green feathers from the tail of the **resplendent quetzal** (each male bird produces only two per year, making the feathers extraordinarily rare and sacred) plus blue **cotinga** feathers, arranged in a semicircular fan with gold disks and bead-work at the base. The number 400 symbolizes eternity in Mexica numerology.
- **Form.** Semicircular fan radiating from a gold-decorated band. The neon-green quetzal feathers dominate; gold accents form a halo-like disc above the wearer. Symmetrical and balanced, meant to extend and magnify the wearer's head.

**Vocabulary:** Aztec · Moctezuma II · Cortés · Charles V · quetzal bird · Tenochtitlan
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#215](#215--'ahu-'ula-feather-cape) — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather Cape) (Hawaiian, late 18th century)

**Theme:** Feathers, colors, signified power

> Bird feathers, ruler's regalia — both use rare, sacred feathers to mark royal power.

---

#### → [#94](#94--screen-with-the-siege-of-belgrade-and-hunting-scene) — Screen with the Siege of Belgrade and Hunting Scene (Circle of the González family, c. 1697–1701)

**Theme:** One of a kind (like the headdress)

> Royal Mexican family object — unique surviving luxury piece from colonial Mexico, hybridizing indigenous and European motifs.

#### → [#95](#95--the-virgin-of-guadalupe-virgen-de-guadalupe) — Virgin of Guadalupe (Miguel González, 1698)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg/250px-Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg" width="180" alt="Virgin of Guadalupe">

**Theme:** Her image is found throughout Mexico, Mexico city (past Aztec sight)

> Influenced by Spanish conquest

## #159 — City of Cusco, including Qorikancha, Santo Domingo, and Walls at Saqsa Waman

**Inka** (with Spanish colonial additions) · c. 1440 C.E. (convent added 1550–1650) · Andesite, granite ashlar masonry; later Baroque stone · Central highlands, Peru · historic center 142 ha

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0b/Iglesia_de_la_Compa%C3%B1%C3%ADa_de_Jes%C3%BAs%2C_Plaza_de_Armas%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_74.JPG/330px-Iglesia_de_la_Compa%C3%B1%C3%ADa_de_Jes%C3%BAs%2C_Plaza_de_Armas%2C_Cusco%2C_Per%C3%BA%2C_2015-07-31%2C_DD_74.JPG" width="240" alt="Cusco">

### Summary

The **Inka imperial capital** — "navel" of the empire — and the Spanish colonial city layered over it.

- **Historical Context.** The name *Cusco* derives from a Quechua word meaning "navel" or "center." A minor city-state before **Pachacuti** expanded the Inka empire in the 1400s; at Spanish contact it held up to 200,000 people. The city was divided into four quarters for immigrants from the four imperial provinces and into upper (**hanan**) and lower (**hurin**) halves along ancestral lines. Said to be laid out in plan as a **puma**, with **Sacsayhuaman** as the head. After the 1533 Spanish conquest, colonial Baroque churches — notably Santo Domingo — were built directly on top of Inka foundations.
- **Function.** Axis mundi — political, religious, economic, and military capital. **Qorikancha** ("Golden Enclosure") was the temple of the sun god **Inti**; Sacsayhuaman was its fortress and ceremonial plaza.
- **Content.** City of **kanchas** — enclosed building-groups around courtyards — of which Qorikancha was the grandest. The fortress walls of Sacsayhuaman include the famous **Stone of Twelve Angles**. After 1533 the Spanish built the Church of Santo Domingo on Qorikancha's plinth, keeping the Inka foundation walls intact.
- **Form.** **Ashlar masonry** — polygonal blocks cut so precisely that no mortar is needed — for elite walls, with thatch and mud brick for lower-status construction. The fit of stones (including one with 12 angles) resists earthquakes. The overall plan uses hierarchy of scale, with Sacsayhuaman dominating the north.

**Vocabulary:** ashlar masonry · kancha · hanan / hurin · hierarchy of scale
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.2, 3.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#206](#206--forbidden-city) — Forbidden City (Ming dynasty, begun 1406)

**Theme:** Power, History/Memory, Urban Experience, Sacred Spaces

> Commissioned by rulers Seats of imperial power Had secluded areas that served as refuges of an imperial subculture that shut out an underclass of "commoners"

---

#### → [#213](#213--nan-madol) — Nan Madol (Saudeleur Dynasty, c. 700–1600)

**Theme:** Power, Natural World, Shared Spaces, History/Memory

> Sites of experiments in centralization Contained peoples from all across a domain which the rulers of the city had set up Unique example of masonry style that developed independently

---

#### → [#167](#167--conical-tower-and-circular-wall-of-great-zimbabwe) — Conical Tower and Circular Wall of Great Zimbabwe (Shona, c. 1000–1400)

**Theme:** Natural World, Power, Sacred Spaces, Performance, History/Memory

> Stunning examples of independently invented masonry style Linked with monarchical power

## #161 — City of Machu Picchu

**Inka** · c. 1450–1540 C.E. · Granite (architectural complex) · Central highlands, Peru · ~80,000 acres

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Machu_Picchu%2C_2023_%28012%29.jpg/250px-Machu_Picchu%2C_2023_%28012%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Machu Picchu">

### Summary

An isolated **Inka royal estate** built 8,000 feet up in the Andes.

- **Historical Context.** Built by **Pachacuti Inca Yupanqui**, the Inka emperor whose 15th-century expansion created the largest pre-Columbian empire in the Americas. Located at the top of a steep Andean ridge; unknown to the Spanish conquistadors and so never looted. "Rediscovered" in 1911 by Hiram Bingham.
- **Function.** Primarily a **royal retreat** for Pachacuti and the Inka elite — for banquets, ceremonies, and religious observance. Too remote to serve as an administrative center. Includes an **observatory** for tracking the sun and the **Intihuatana Stone**, aligned so that it casts no shadow at the equinoxes.
- **Content.** Terraced agricultural fields cut into the slope; a dry moat for collecting rainwater; ashlar-masonry temples, houses, the observatory, and the Intihuatana; characteristic **trapezoidal doorways**. Separate zones for royalty (precisely cut stone) and servants (rougher masonry).
- **Form.** Granite **ashlar masonry** — stones shaped to fit together without mortar, quarried locally and moved without the wheel or draft animals. Structures follow the terrain; all the same pale granite gives unity to the complex.

**Vocabulary:** terraced fields · dry moat · ashlar · Intihuatana
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#206](#206--forbidden-city) — Forbidden City (Ming dynasty, begun 1406)

**Theme:** Exclusive

> Both are large and exclusive estates that were meant for royals and elite members of society.

---

#### → [#45](#45--forum-of-trajan) — Forum of Trajan (Roman, dedicated 112 C.E.)

**Theme:** Stratification of Society

> Both were built to be used by the elites. Also near the within the Forum of Trajan there is the marketplace which is not as extravagant as the forum because it is mean to be used by the masses. Similarly some of the buildings at Machu Picchu do not have as precisely cut stones, showing that these buildings were for servants.

---

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Tutankhamun's Tomb (Egyptian New Kingdom, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Seclusion

> Both King Tut's Tomb and the City of Machu Picchu were both isolated. Machu Picchu was never found by the Spanish invaders and survived undisturbed until 1911 when an American found it. Similarly King Tut's Tomb remained extremely intact and unlooted because it was not in a common place for grave robbers to look in.

---

## #162 — All-T'oqapu tunic

**Inka** · 1450–1540 C.E. · Camelid fiber and cotton · Museo Chileno de Arte Precolombino, Santiago, Chile · 77.2 × 90.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/All-toqapu_tunic_Inca.jpg/330px-All-toqapu_tunic_Inca.jpg" width="240" alt="All-T'oqapu tunic">
### Summary

A luxury Inka **tunic** densely covered in **t'oqapu** geometric blocks — wearable iconography of the empire.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in the Inka empire at its height, made by the **acllas** (chosen women) who specialized in textile weaving and ritual duties. Inka textiles were more valuable than gold; fine cloth (**qompi**) was reserved for royalty and religious offering. A tunic covered entirely in t'oqapu — likely worn only by the emperor himself.
- **Function.** A status symbol and possibly a ritual offering. Cloth could be burned as tribute to the sun god **Inti**. Once cut from the loom, the textile was believed to lose its spiritual essence (**camac**).
- **Content.** Over 100 squares filled with distinct **t'oqapu** geometric patterns, each symbol representing a family, ethnic group, clan, or unit of the empire. The denser the t'oqapu, the higher the status of the wearer.
- **Form.** Fine rectangular **qompi** cloth with neck and arm openings. Warm reds, ochres, blacks, and yellows — the red cochineal dye came from insects and was expensive. Weave is extraordinarily fine and the ink embedded throughout the fiber.

**Vocabulary:** t'oqapu · qompi · acllas · iconography
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#163](#163--bandolier-bag) — Bandolier Bag (Lenape, c. 1850)

**Theme:** Indigenous People / Mesoamerica

> Similar cultures

## #163 — Bandolier bag

**Lenape (Delaware tribe, Eastern Woodlands)** · c. 1850 C.E. · Beadwork on leather · 68 × 47 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Pete_Moos-Mille_Lacs_Band-c1913-Bandolier_Bag.jpg/330px-Pete_Moos-Mille_Lacs_Band-c1913-Bandolier_Bag.jpg" width="240" alt="Bandolier bag">
### Summary

An elaborately **beaded ceremonial pouch** that took the form of a European soldier's cartridge bag.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Lenape** (Delaware) people of the Eastern Woodlands, a confederation of three clans who traced descent through the female line. The shoulder-pouch form originated as the European soldier's **bandolier** for rifle cartridges; after contact, Native tribes adapted both form and material. Glass beads and silk ribbons reached tribal hands through the **fur trade**.
- **Function.** Ceremonial rather than utilitarian. Worn as part of men's regalia at dances and ritual gatherings. Only elite or elder men wore them; a display of prestige.
- **Content.** Symmetrical, abstracted beadwork designs. The contrasting colors (often blue and red against white) may correspond to the **four cardinal directions** and the sky-and-underworld cosmology. Plains-region bandolier bags tend toward floral patterns; Eastern Woodlands ones lean more geometric.
- **Form.** Glass beads stitched to leather or trade cloth, with silk ribbons and fringed tassels hanging from the bottom edges. Small beads create tight geometric patterns; ribbons and fringe produce motion as the wearer moves.

**Vocabulary:** beadwork · embroidery · bandolier · fringe · indigenous adaptation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#164](#164--transformation-mask) — Transformation mask (Kwakwaka'wakw, late 19th c.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg/250px-Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg" width="180" alt="Transformation mask">

**Theme:** Ceremonial purpose, contact with Europeans, vivid coloring

> Both the Transformation Mask and the Bandolier Bag were influenced by contact with the Europeans. The Transformation Mask's contact is evident through the pigmentation used to create the vivid coloring. This is similar to the fact that the beads used in the bandolier bags were attained through trade with Europeans, but the Bandolier Bag is even more intrinsically related to the Europeans in that these bags served a purpose for the Europeans in their original construction.

---

#### → [#215](#215--'ahu-'ula-feather-cape) — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather Cape) (Hawaiian, late 18th c.)

**Theme:** ceremonial purpose, intricate design

> The 'Ahu 'ula feather cape was worn by Hawaiian royalty, and took years to create due to the rarity of the birds whose feathers make up the material. Like the bandolier bag, these objects were desired by Europeans. Both the 'Ahu 'ula and the Bandolier Bag serve traditions in their indigenous people.

---

#### → [#95](#95--the-virgin-of-guadalupe-virgen-de-guadalupe) — The Virgin of Guadalupe (Miguel González, 1698)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg/250px-Virgen_de_guadalupe1.jpg" width="180" alt="Virgin of Guadalupe">

**Theme:** indigenous traditions, materials gained through trade

> Similar to the acquiring of beads from the Europeans for the bandolier bags, Miguel Gonzalez acquired mother of pearl from Polynesian and European traders. Both pieces interpret a European or Christian tradition in a way unique to the indigenous culture.

---

## #164 — Transformation mask

**Kwakwaka'wakw** (Northwest Coast of Canada) · Late 19th century C.E. · Red cedar wood, paint, and string · open 31 × 45 × 47 in.; closed 20.5 × 17 × 29.5 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg/250px-Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg" width="240" alt="Transformation mask">

### Summary

A hinged ceremonial mask that **opens from animal to human face** during performance.

- **Historical Context.** The **Kwakwaka'wakw** live on the western coast of British Columbia. Their cosmologies include ancestors who transform between animal and human forms, shedding skins or masks to reveal human selves within. Clans are tied to sacred animals (raven, thunderbird, eagle, wolf, bear); sub-clans (**numayn**) hold specific privileges, including the right to wear certain transformation masks.
- **Function.** Worn over the head with a costume of shredded cedar bark for ritual performance during **Potlatch** and winter initiation ceremonies. Strings let the dancer open the outer animal face to reveal the human ancestor inside — the dancer literally performs the transformation of a supernatural being.
- **Content.** Here, an eagle (one of the four Kwakwaka'wakw clans) that opens to reveal the face of a human ancestor at its center. During the transformation moment the performer turns his back to the audience to heighten the mystery.
- **Form.** Red **cedar** wood, hinged mechanism, painted in the **formline** style of the Northwest Coast — bilateral symmetry, calligraphic wavy lines, ovoid eyes, U- and S-shaped elements. Vivid reds, blacks, greens, and oranges.

**Vocabulary:** transformation mask · Potlatch · formline · numayn
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#4](#4--running-horned-woman) — Running Horned Woman (Tassili n'Ajjer, c. 6000–4000 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** human and animal hybrids

> Both the Running Horned Woman and the transformation mask have zoomorphic features. The Running Horned Woman shows a woman with horns (an animalistic feature), while the transformation mask is a combination of a human face with that of an eagle on two different fronts.

---

#### → [#178](#178--aka-elephant-mask) — Aka elephant mask (Bamileke, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** high societal status

> The Aka elephant mask is similar to the transformation mask in that both are used to convey high societal status, along with the other ceremonial and ritual functions the masks serve.

---

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu mask (Sande Society, Mende, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** ceremonies: initiation rites

> The Bundu mask and the transformation mask both have ceremonial functions, specifically in initiation rites. The Bundu mask was used for initiation rites into adulthood while the transformation mask was used during winter initiation rites.

---

## #165 — Painted elk hide

Attributed to **Cotsiogo (Cadzi Cody)**, Eastern Shoshone · c. 1890–1900 C.E. · Natural pigments on elk hide · Wind River Reservation, Wyoming · 81 × 78 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Story_robe_Shoshone_Amerind.png/330px-Story_robe_Shoshone_Amerind.png" width="240" alt="Painted elk hide">
### Summary

An Eastern Shoshone **hide painting** made for tourist sale, depicting banned and permitted rituals together.

- **Historical Context.** **Cotsiogo** (Cadzi Cody, c. 1866–1912), Eastern Shoshone, was relocated with his tribe to the **Wind River Reservation** in Wyoming. There he produced hide paintings for sale to Euro-American tourists as a way to earn a living under the reservation system. Hide painting on the Plains was traditionally narrative — recording history — and men typically painted on tipis, shields, and robes.
- **Function.** Commercial art that doubled as cultural preservation. Cotsiogo depicted scenes tourists wanted: buffalo hunts, the **Sun Dance**, and the secular **Wolf Dance**. Because the U.S. had outlawed the Sun Dance (1935), Cotsiogo often blended the two dances in a single image so the religious ritual could still be shown.
- **Content.** Feathered and bustle-clad men circle a buffalo head between two poles — the Sun Dance to the creator. Around this central ritual, women gather by a fire, and men on horses hunt and butcher buffalo. The buffalo was sacred to Shoshone life.
- **Form.** Local red, yellow, brown, green, and blue pigments painted freehand on a dried, outstretched elk hide. Two-dimensional figures with **composite view** (torso frontal, head and legs in profile). Movement conveyed by spatial composition and the gallop of horses.

**Vocabulary:** figural motif · pigment · Sun Dance · Wolf Dance · composite view
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565)

**Theme:** Human Interaction

> Both pieces portray the effects of human contact with other cultures and nature.

---

#### → [#81](#81--frontispiece-of-the-codex-mendoza) — Codex Mendoza (Aztec / Spanish commission, c. 1541–1542)

**Theme:** Aztec History and Military Might

> Both pieces portray the respective society's history and culture with the intention of being viewed by a foreign audience.

---

#### → [#97](#97--spaniard-and-indian-produce-a-mestizo) — Spaniard and Indian Produce a Mestizo (Miguel Cabrera, 1763)

**Theme:** Diversity and Cultural Blending in Americas

> Both pieces portray the mixing of Native and European cultures, albeit in different time periods.

---

## #166 — Black-on-black ceramic vessel

**Maria Martínez and Julian Martínez** (Tewa, Puebloan, San Ildefonso Pueblo, New Mexico) · c. mid-20th century C.E. · Blackware ceramic · 11⅛ × 13 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Maria_Martinez_pot.jpg/250px-Maria_Martinez_pot.jpg" width="240" alt="Black-on-black ceramic vessel">

### Summary

A **Pueblo revival olla** made for a non-Native fine-arts market, fired to a deep matte-and-glossy black.

- **Historical Context.** **Maria Martínez** (1887–1980) of San Ildefonso Pueblo trained in traditional Pueblo hand-coiling and revived ancient Pueblo **blackware** after excavations in what is now Bandelier National Monument revealed black-on-black pottery sherds of the 12th–17th centuries. The 1880 arrival of the railroad flooded New Mexico with cheap imported pots, displacing utilitarian Pueblo work — Maria responded by shifting toward the Art Deco–era fine-arts market. She won prizes at the St. Louis, New York, and Chicago World's Fairs. Julian, her husband, painted the matte designs.
- **Function.** Non-utilitarian — intentionally fired to be softer and not watertight, so it would be sold as art rather than used for cooking, storage, or ceremony. Its **Art Deco** geometry made it marketable to non-Native buyers.
- **Content.** Two bands of matte stylized natural forms — rain clouds, feathers, cornstalks, a bird in flight — possibly read as a **prayer for rain** rising to the sky.
- **Form.** **Blackware** olla: hand-coiled (no wheel), stone-burnished to a mirror gloss, painted in matte guaco (reduced wild-plant juice), then fired in a manure-smothered reduction fire that blackens the clay. The contrast between matte and burnished black is the signature Martínez effect.

**Vocabulary:** olla · matte · Art Deco · blackware · burnishing
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#5](#5--beaker-with-ibex-motifs) — Beaker with Ibex Motifs (Susa, c. 4200–3500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Stylization of outside world; funerary piece

> Abstract, stylized depictions of natural environment; both ceramic pieces that would not have held other items as their primary purpose

---

#### → [#165](#165--painted-elk-hide) — Painted Elk Hide (Cotsiogo, c. 1890–1900)

**Theme:** Depiction of traditional dances; commercially created art

> Native North American art created in part for consumption by non-Native audiences; meant to bring prestige to Native peoples

---

#### → [#216](#216--staff-god) — Staff god (Rarotonga, late 18th–early 19th c.)

**Theme:** Male/female labor/significance; importance as representation of ancestor or deity

> Labor to create both items was divided between different members of the community (men produced wooden core, while women produced tapa)

---

## #167 — Conical tower and circular wall of Great Zimbabwe

**Shona peoples** · c. 1000–1400 C.E. · Coursed granite blocks · Southeastern Zimbabwe · walls ~820 ft × 32 ft; tower ~30 ft × 18 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Conical_Tower_-_Great_Enclosure_III_%2833736918448%29.jpg/250px-Conical_Tower_-_Great_Enclosure_III_%2833736918448%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Conical tower of Great Zimbabwe">

### Summary

The largest stone ruins in sub-Saharan Africa and **royal center of the Shona**.

- **Historical Context.** Capital of a wealthy inland Shona kingdom that may have held 18,000 residents and produced up to two-fifths of the world's gold by taxing trade with Swahili port cities on Africa's east coast. Declined in the 15th century from soil exhaustion, gold depletion, and trade shifts. Misattributed by 18th- and 19th-century European explorers to the **Queen of Sheba** rather than to its African builders. After independence, its name was chosen for the modern nation.
- **Function.** **Royal center** — the walled Great Enclosure housed the ruler and elite; the **Conical Tower** may have functioned as a granary whose grain symbolized the king as "custodian of the harvest." Also a trading hub and possibly a religious site tied to the supreme god **Mwari**.
- **Content.** Three complexes: the **Hill Ruin** (sacred cave, 30-ft walls, monoliths), the **Great Enclosure** (enormous walls, turrets, monoliths, and a narrow inner passage to the **Conical Tower**), and the **Valley Ruins** with evidence of long-distance trade reaching as far as Southeast Asia.
- **Form.** **Ashlar granite masonry** with no mortar — stones cut from nearby hills and fitted so precisely that gravity alone holds the walls. Some wall sections carry geometric chevron patterns.

**Vocabulary:** ashlar masonry · Shona · Mwari · Great Enclosure
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#168](#168--great-mosque-of-djenné) — Great Mosque of Djenné (Mali, founded c. 1200; rebuilt 1906–1907)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg/250px-Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg" width="180" alt="Great Mosque of Djenné">

**Theme:** Religious

> Both these structures stand tall and don't use any sort of glue to hold them together. It has religious purposes, which the Walls in Zimbabwe did as well.

---

#### → [#177](#177--lukasa-memory-board) — Lukasa (memory board) (Luba, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Historical

> The way the board holds important information is similar to how important information and trades happened within the walls

---

#### → [#35](#35--acropolis-parthenon) — Acropolis / Parthenon (Iktinos and Kallikrates, 447–410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** religious

> The religious happenings that occured in this building can compare to the religious happenings that occured within Great Zimbabwe.

---

## #169 — Wall plaque, from Oba's palace

**Edo peoples, Benin (Nigeria)** · 16th century C.E. · Cast brass · H. 19⅜ × W. 16½ × D. 4½ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Benin_brass_plaque_03_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Benin_brass_plaque_03_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Benin wall plaque">

### Summary

A cast-brass plaque from the palace of the **Oba of Benin** — royal propaganda made from Portuguese trade brass.

- **Historical Context.** The **Edo peoples** of the Kingdom of Benin (modern Nigeria) had an established bronze-casting tradition by the 13th century. Hundreds of plaques decorated pillars in the palace of the **Oba** (king). Brass was sourced largely from **Portuguese trade** — melted-down European armbands and **manillas**. In 1897 a British expedition sacked Benin City, looted the palaces, and dispersed the plaques to European museums (the **Benin Bronzes**).
- **Function.** Architectural decoration arranged in visual sequences that recorded court rituals, royal genealogy, and imperial history — a kind of narrative relief.
- **Content.** The **Oba** (king) mounted on horseback, dominating the composition through **hierarchical scale**; attendants on either side raise shields to protect him from the sun and hold ceremonial sword and gift-box. **Coral beads** link the Oba to **Olokun**, god of the sea, who bestows wealth; **quatrefoil** "river-leaf" rosettes in the background also refer to Olokun.
- **Form.** **Lost-wax cast brass**; symmetrical central composition; hierarchical scale; dense background of quatrefoil flowers. Figures shown in composite view typical of Benin court style.

**Vocabulary:** Oba · Iwebo · Ibiwe · hierarchical scale · lost-wax casting
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#69](#69--david) — Donatello, David (c. 1440–1460)

**Theme:** Biblical David meant to illustrate youth, innocence, and virtue

> Sculpture made of bronze, use of sword, nudity, religious connection

---

#### → [#197](#197--todai-ji) — Buddha at Todai-ji (Japan, 752 C.E., recast later)

**Theme:** Created for Buddha's "eye-opening" ceremony.

> Sculpture made of bronze, religious, use of size to convey power

---

#### → [#202](#202--shiva-as-lord-of-dance-nataraja) — Shiva as Lord of Dance (Chola, c. 11th c.)

**Theme:** religious

> Sculpture made of bronze, divine energy, tells story
