## #170 — Sika dwa kofi (Golden Stool)

**Ashanti peoples** (south-central Ghana) · c. 1700 C.E. · Gold over wood and cast-gold attachments · 18 in h. × 24 in l. × 12 in w.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Golden_stool_31_January_1935.jpg/250px-Golden_stool_31_January_1935.jpg" width="240" alt="Golden Stool">

### Summary

The **sacred soul of the Asante nation** — a royal stool that may never be sat on or touch the ground.

- **Historical Context.** According to Asante tradition, the stool descended from the sky in the late 1600s onto the lap of King **Osei Tutu**, brought down by his priest **Okomfo Anokye** as a unifying object for the newborn Asante confederacy. In 1900 the British governor Frederick Hodgson demanded to sit on the stool, triggering the **War of the Golden Stool / Yaa Asantewaa War**. The Asante lost the war but kept the stool hidden; it was never captured.
- **Function.** A national palladium. The stool contains the **soul (sunsum)** of the Asante nation, is never placed on the ground, and only the king is allowed to touch it. Used in coronation (the new king is raised above but not onto the stool) and in major state ceremonies; bells attached to the sides alert the king of danger.
- **Content.** A crescent-shaped seat on a flat base; cast-gold scale, bells, and figural attachments; entire surface sheathed in gold.
- **Form.** A single block of **wood** carved and inlaid with sheet and cast **gold** attachments. Symmetrical and proportioned — lightweight (wood) but visually bearing the weight of solid gold.

**Vocabulary:** Ashanti · fetish · Sika Dwa Kofi · replica
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 2.1, 3.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#196](#196--gold-and-jade-crown) — Gold and Jade Crown (Silla Korea, 5th–6th c.)

**Theme:** Ceremonial

> Both the Gold and Jade Crown and the Golden Stool had limited use and were made for high ranking officials. The Gold and Jade Crown was made for the Queen while the Golden Stool was made for the leader of the Ashanti people.

---

#### → [#183](#183--the-kaaba) — The Kaaba (Mecca, pre-Islamic origin, rebuilt many times)

**Theme:** Religious/Spiritual Center

> Even though both objects, the Golden Stool (stool) and the Kaaba (black stone) do not visually seem sacred, the Ashanti society and the Islamic people respect and highly regard the items as honorable due to their beliefs.

---

#### → [#215](#215--'ahu-'ula-feather-cape) — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather Cape) (Hawaiian, late 18th c.)

**Theme:** Made for High Ranking Officials

> Like the Golden Stool that only permits the king to touch it, the Ahu Ula is worn only by high ranking chiefs and warriors of great ability. Like the Golden Stool that is encased with gold, the cap is also decorated with royal colors such as red and rare feathers.

---

## #171 — Ndop (portrait figure) of King Mishe miShyaang maMbul

**Kuba peoples** (Democratic Republic of the Congo) · c. 1760–1780 C.E. · Wood (*crossopterix febrifuga*), camwood powder · Brooklyn Museum · 19½ × 7⅝ × 8⅝ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg/250px-Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Ndop of King Mishe miShyaang maMbul">

### Summary

A **Kuba royal portrait figure** that serves as the king's spiritual double.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Kuba peoples** in present-day DRC (Kasai region). Tradition holds that each Kuba **nyim** (ruler) commissioned an *ndop* — "statue" — upon his investiture. Only one could be made per king, and he had to be physically present for the carving. If the king were injured in battle, a matching mark was said to appear on the statue. If the ndop decayed, it could be recarved.
- **Function.** A **spiritual double** of the king, believed to house his *double* or counterpart-soul. Kept in the royal quarters; placed near women of the royal harem during childbirth to ensure safe delivery; served as a surrogate for the king in his absence. After his death it was stored away and exhibited only on rare occasions.
- **Content.** The king sits **cross-legged** on a rectangular platform with geometric bands. His right hand rests on his knee; his left holds the *ikul*, a ceremonial peace knife. Headdress consists of a decorated board atop a cylindrical ring. A cylindrical drum on a small pedestal sits in front of him — the personal **ibol** (emblem) that distinguishes this king from other ndop. Closed eyes; calm, formal expression. Not a literal likeness but an idealized composite of royal characteristics.
- **Form.** Carved single block of hardwood, anointed with palm oil and red **camwood powder**. **One-to-three proportion** — the head is one-third of the entire figure, reflecting the Kuba belief that the head is the seat of intelligence.

**Vocabulary:** ndop · nyim · ikul · ibol
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#169](#169--wall-plaque-from-oba's-palace) — Wall plaque, from Oba's palace (Edo/Benin, 16th c.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Benin_brass_plaque_03_%28cropped%29.jpg/250px-Benin_brass_plaque_03_%28cropped%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Benin wall plaque">

**Theme:** ritual / belief systems / ceremony / socio-political / non-western traditions / representation of the human figure / symbolism

> Both represent idealized royal figures produced to commemorate, legitimize, and spiritually protect African rulers; both use hierarchical scale and symbolic attributes.

---

#### → [#51](#51--san-vitale) — Justinian and Theodora Panels, San Vitale (Byzantine, c. 547)

**Theme:** belief systems / socio-political / western vs. non-western traditions / representation of the human figure / symbolism

> Both depict rulers in highly idealized, frontal, hieratic form, meant to merge political authority with sacred authority.

---

#### → [#21](#21--mortuary-temple-of-hatshepsut) — Kneeling statue of Hatshepsut from her Mortuary Temple (Egyptian New Kingdom, c. 1479–1458 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** belief systems / socio-political / non-western vs. non-western traditions / representation of the human figure / symbolism

> Both are idealized royal sculpted portraits designed less as physical likenesses than as statements of the ruler's spiritual office.

## #172 — Power figure (Nkisi n'kondi)

**Kongo peoples, Yombe group** (Democratic Republic of the Congo) · c. late 19th century C.E. · Wood and metal · 36 × 18½ × 14¼ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/WLA_metmuseum_Power_Figure_Male_Nkisi.jpg/250px-WLA_metmuseum_Power_Figure_Male_Nkisi.jpg" width="240" alt="Power figure (Nkisi n'kondi)">
### Summary

A **Kongo power figure** bristling with nails — each one a sworn oath or broken promise.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Yombe** group of the Kongo peoples. The Yombe migrated south from present-day Gabon before the 15th century and came into extensive contact with the **Portuguese** beginning in the 16th century. **Ngoma Bunzi** was the supreme god, approached only through intermediary earth spirits (*Nzambi a Tsi*) and river spirits (*Simbi*).
- **Function.** A tool of justice and protection. Once the sculpture was carved, a ritual specialist, the **nganga**, activated it by packing medicinal substances (**bilongo**) into cavities in the head and stomach and sealing them with mirrors — glass through which spirits of the dead could see enemies. Every time someone needed to seal an oath, cure an illness, settle a dispute, or curse an enemy, a nail or blade was driven in — "waking" the spirit inside.
- **Content.** Carved in **anthropomorphic** standing form with wide eyes, imposing stance, belly-button cavity sealed with a **cowrie shell**. The outer surface is covered with nails, pegs, blades, cloth strips, rope, beads — added over a long period of use.
- **Form.** Wood core carved by the sculptor; the accumulation of nails, medicine bundles (*bilongo*), mirrors, and attached objects transforms the piece over time through ritual use rather than a single artistic gesture.

**Vocabulary:** nkisi · nganga · bilongo · mpemba
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#176](#176--ikenga-shrine-figure) — Ikenga (shrine figure) (Igbo, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Other world, spirits, guardian, protect

> The both connect to the 'other realm' and serve as a type of vessel between the spirits and the living. Just as the Power figure can be used to protect people the Ikenga is seen as a type of guardian. In form, they both represent stylized versions of the African man.

---

#### → [#179](#179--reliquary-figure-byeri) — Reliquary figure (byeri) (Fang, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Protect, spiritual ideas

> The Reliquary figure is a piece used to protect the remnants of ancestors. They are similar in that they are both used for protection; however they are different in the sense that the Reliquary figure is used for the protection of the dead whereas the Power figure is used for the protection of the living.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop portrait figure (Kuba, 1760–1780)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg/250px-Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Ndop portrait figure">

**Theme:** Spirits, supernatural

> The Ndop figure was used as a sprit double for Kuba people's kings and acted in the King's place in his absence. Both the Ndop figure and the Power figure represent some sort of spirit and supernatural force.

---

## #173 — Female (Pwo) mask

**Chokwe peoples** (Democratic Republic of the Congo / Angola) · Late 19th–early 20th century C.E. · Wood, fiber, pigment, and metal · 39.1 × 21.3 × 23.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Pwo_mask_Chokwe.jpg/250px-Pwo_mask_Chokwe.jpg" width="240" alt="Female (Pwo) mask">
### Summary

A Chokwe mask embodying the **idealized female ancestor** — worn by men in masquerade performance.

- **Historical Context.** The **Chokwe** are a matrilineal people of the DRC and Angola who honor young fertile women. The mask represents the **founding female ancestor** of the Chokwe lineage and is carved by a Chokwe man for the male dancer who performs the role.
- **Function.** Worn in masquerade performances to honor women who have passed through childbirth and retain wisdom and beauty; tied to coming-of-age rituals and to the veneration of the female ancestor.
- **Content.** A calm, closed-eyed, closed-mouthed female face with **scarification patterns**, a narrow chin, and a wide forehead. The closed eyes and mouth signal respect and inner knowledge; **white kaolin** around the eyes signals the spirit realm and the female capacity for second sight granted by childbirth. The fashionable hairstyle of the period is modeled on top.
- **Form.** Carved wood oiled to a smooth sheen for contrast against the softer, textured fiber hair and rougher headband. Idealized rather than individualized; symmetrical; small features (eyes, ears, mouth) symbolize the virtues of discretion.

**Vocabulary:** Pwo · matrilineal culture · Chihongo · kaolin
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2

---

#### → Roman Patrician (Republican Rome, 1st c. B.C.E.)

**Theme:** commemorative

> faces

**Theme:** initiation, ceremony

> Performance piece

#### → [#176](#176--ikenga-shrine-figure) — Ikenga (Igbo, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** initiation

> Initiation

## #174 — Portrait Mask (Mblo) of Moya Yanso

**Owie Kimou (carver), Baule peoples** · Late 19th–early 20th century C.E. · Wood, fiber, pigment, and metal · Côte d'Ivoire, West Africa · 16½ × 6 × 5 in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Mblo_mask_Baule.jpg/250px-Mblo_mask_Baule.jpg" width="240" alt="Portrait Mask (Mblo) of Moya Yanso">
### Summary

A rare **named portrait mask** of a specific Baule woman, carved in the idealized style of the Mblo masquerade.

- **Historical Context.** The **Baule** live in central Côte d'Ivoire and have a rich tradition of sculpture for personal shrines and masquerade. **Mblo** and **Goli** are the two main entertainment-mask traditions; in the village of **Kami** where this mask was made, the Mblo dance is called **Gbagba**. Remarkably, both the subject (**Moya Yanso**, the commissioner's wife) and the carver (**Owie Kimou**) are known — unusual for African masquerade masks of this period. A period photograph shows Moya's son holding the mask beside her.
- **Function.** Commissioned by Moya Yanso's husband **Kouame Ziarey** to honor her, worn by him and his sons in masquerade performances with drummers, singers, and dancers. Not decorative wall art — used to **"make something happen"** in ritual. Kept hidden between performances.
- **Content.** An idealized female face with **scarification** scars rendered in brass at the temples and cheeks. Half-slit eyes and high forehead denote modesty and wisdom; cheek folds and the beard-like extensions to the ears suggest age; triangular brass patterns denote health. Six tubular projections on top represent the hairstyle.
- **Form.** Carved wood oiled to a near-black sheen, with **brass** accents highlighting scarification and hair. Monochrome with gold highlights; strongly symmetrical with repeated triangles and cylinders; an idealized version of the real woman rather than a naturalistic likeness.

**Vocabulary:** Mblo · Goli · Gbagba · scarification
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu Mask (Sande Society, Mende, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** ritualistic, coming of age, spiritual connection to youth, beauty, and fertility

> Both use wood as their mediums and are danced in ceremonies in their respective societies. However, this mask is made and danced by women, whereas Mblo masks are made and danced by men.

---

#### → [#107](#107--la-grande-odalisque) — La Grande Odalisque (Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres, 1814)

**Theme:** sexual, idealistic, beauty

> Both depict the ideal female forms found in their respective societies, such as the elongation of the female figure in La Grande Odalisque, rather than capture the subject through anatomical realism.

---

#### → [#86](#86--henri-iv-receives-the-portrait-of-marie-de'-medici-from-the-marie-de'-medici-cycle) — Portrait of Marie de' Medici (Peter Paul Rubens, 1622–1625)

**Theme:** beauty, excitement, dramatic, theatrical, emotional

> Both depict not only the ideal woman of beauty, but are also portraits that are commissioned, and made to honor a woman's life and place in society. This series of Marie de'Medici chronicles her life and her marriage, elevating her status through the incorporation of Roman gods and angels as symbols, just as the portrait mask displays Moya Yanso's status in society through idealized features.

---

## #175 — Bundu mask

**Sande Society, Mende peoples** · 19th–20th century C.E. · Wood, cloth, and fiber · West African forests of Sierra Leone and Liberia

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Bundu_mask_Sande.jpg/250px-Bundu_mask_Sande.jpg" width="240" alt="Bundu mask">
### Summary

The only major African mask **carved, worn, and danced by women** — a Sande initiation mask.

- **Historical Context.** The **Mende** are likely descendants of the Mali Empire, now in Sierra Leone and Liberia. The **Sande Society** is a women's secret society that initiates girls into womanhood, teaches sexual and moral comportment, and confers fertility. Unique in West Africa for centering female power.
- **Function.** Worn during **Sande initiation rituals** celebrating the transition from girl to woman. Commissioned by women for women; conceals the dancer (the Sowei) and personifies an ideal of female beauty, health, and serenity.
- **Content.** An idealized female face: **elongated forehead**, **elaborate hairstyle**, small downturned lips, nearly shut eyes, and **neck rolls** — all signs of beauty. Neck rolls symbolize fertility; closed lips signal discretion; small eyes signal inwardness.
- **Form.** **Subtractive wood carving** polished to a shiny black (symbol of water and purity). Each mask is uniquely carved in its hairstyle; facial features are conventional.

**Vocabulary:** Sande Society · conceptual representation · subtractive sculpture · Sowei
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#164](#164--transformation-mask) — Transformation mask (Kwakwaka'wakw, late 19th c.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg/250px-Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg" width="180" alt="Transformation mask">

**Theme:** mask, idealized form, wooden, ritualistic

> Both masks are commissioned to be danced in ritualistic settings. The Transformation mask when fully opened reveals a realistic depiction of a tribe member or ancestor whereas the Bundu mask is a simply idealized face. Both are made of wood; the Transformation mask is commonly danced by men instead of women.

---

#### → [#174](#174--portrait-mask-mblo-of-moya-yanso) — Portrait Mask (Mblo) (Owie Kimou, Baule, late 19th–early 20th c.)

**Theme:** mask, depicts a human face, ritualistic, commissioned for a dance, African, highly decorative

> The Portrait Mblo Mask is similar to the Bundu Mask because it is a mask commissioned specifically in a celebratory ritual. Both masks celebrate a transition. The Bundu mask celebrates the transition into womanhood; the Portrait Mblo Mask celebrates the transition from life to death.

---

#### → [#222](#222--malagan-display-and-mask) — Malagan Mask (New Ireland, c. 20th c.)

**Theme:** mask, animalistic, zoomorphic, highly decorative

> The Malagan Mask is also an example of a mask commissioned for a ritual or dance. Like the Bundu mask, the Malagan Mask is more abstract and focuses on formal elements to convey meaning. One major difference is that the Malagan mask is made out of turtle shell.

---

## #176 — Ikenga (shrine figure)

**Igbo peoples** (Nigeria) · c. 19th–20th century C.E. · Wood · sizes vary (6 in to 3 ft)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/af/COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houten_beeld_van_een_man_met_ramshoorns_TMnr_5112-1.jpg/250px-COLLECTIE_TROPENMUSEUM_Houten_beeld_van_een_man_met_ramshoorns_TMnr_5112-1.jpg" width="240" alt="Ikenga (shrine figure)">
### Summary

A personal **shrine figure** embodying an Igbo man's personal god of achievement.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Igbo** of Nigeria. Each man received an Ikenga upon reaching adulthood; the figure was consecrated by a kinsman before use and could be joined by additional ones as the owner gained rank. Ikenga translates as "strong right arm."
- **Function.** Serves as the owner's **personal god (chi)** of success and status. Honors the right hand, which wields tools and weapons. Each figure encodes its owner's morality, prosperity, genealogy, and social rank. Kept in the household until the owner's death, then usually destroyed; rarely reused.
- **Content.** A hand-carved human figure with prominent **horns** (signaling power) and the right hand holding a tool or weapon. Warriors look militaristic; leaders look tall and imposing; the details personalize the figure to its owner.
- **Form.** **Subtractive wood carving**, usually symmetrical with two legs, a cylindrical torso, and upright horns. Varies from naturalistic to heavily stylized depending on the owner's story.

**Vocabulary:** Ikenga · Chi · ritual · kinsmen
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#179](#179--reliquary-figure-byeri) — Reliquary figure (byeri) (Fang, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Spiritual connection — however to the afterlife

> Individualized, Spiritual, Idealized

---

#### → [#173](#173--female-pwo-mask) — Female (Pwo) Mask (Chokwe, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Transition from childhood to adulthood, signifies fertility and success

> Individualized, Figural, Spiritual, Ritualistic, Idealized

---

#### → [#228](#228--androgyne-iii) — Androgyne III (Magdalena Abakanowicz, 1985)

**Theme:** Stylistic representation, encourages ambiguity

> Wood, Figural, Stylized

## #177 — Lukasa (memory board)

**Mbudye Society, Luba peoples** (Democratic Republic of the Congo) · c. 19th–20th century C.E. · Wood, beads, and metal · 54.8 × 21 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Lukasa_memory_board_Luba.jpg/250px-Lukasa_memory_board_Luba.jpg" width="240" alt="Lukasa (memory board)">
### Summary

A handheld **mnemonic device** read by touch to recall Luba royal history and cosmology.

- **Historical Context.** The **Luba Kingdom** was a powerful presence in central Africa from the 16th to the 20th centuries. Within Luba society the **Mbudye** were the "men of memory" — an elite association who preserved royal histories, cosmology, and political knowledge through embodied practice.
- **Function.** A **memory aid**, not a text. A Mbudye diviner held the board in the left hand and "read" it with the right index finger — the configuration of beads, shells, and incised patterns cued each recounting of origin myths, king lists, sacred enclosures, and royal relationships.
- **Content.** Hourglass-shaped wooden tablet covered on one side with colored beads, shells, and metal fragments in clusters; geometric carvings on the back and sides. Large beads surrounded by smaller beads stand for **culture heroes**; circles of beads for sacred enclosures; lines and clusters for events and relationships.
- **Form.** Rough-textured wood, small enough to hold in one hand. Multicolored beads pop against the darker wood background. Geometric designs incised on the reverse.

**Vocabulary:** Lukasa · Mbudye Society · mnemonic
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#19](#19--the-code-of-hammurabi) — Code of Hammurabi (Babylonian, c. 1792–1750 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Both are carved with great detail.

> Both show the importance of a kind of law making and cultural records.

---

#### → [#153](#153--chavín-de-huántar) — Chavín de Huántar (Chavín, 900–200 B.C.E.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg/250px-Chav%C3%ADn_de_Hu%C3%A1ntar_-_51149086708.jpg" width="180" alt="Chavín de Huántar">

**Theme:** Both are carved and contain symbols.

> Both are similar in that only certain people could view/read the pieces.

---

#### → [#165](#165--painted-elk-hide) — Painted Elk Hide (Cotsiogo, c. 1890–1900)

**Theme:** Although both pieces of work are written on different materials, they both show the importance of recording events.

> Both are used for record keeping.

## #178 — Aka elephant mask

**Bamileke peoples** (Cameroon, western grassfields region) · c. 19th–20th century C.E. · Wood, woven raffia, cloth, and beads · 57¾ × 20½ × 11½ in.

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Elephant_cap_mask_Bamileke.jpg/250px-Elephant_cap_mask_Bamileke.jpg" width="240" alt="Aka elephant mask">
### Summary

A **royal masquerade** mask worn by elite members of the Kuosi society to embody the spirit of the elephant.

- **Historical Context.** Made by the **Bamileke** of the Cameroon grasslands. Elephants and beadwork were reserved symbols of political power; only wealthy warriors and high-status elites of the **Kuosi society** could wear the mask. Rooted in the broadly African belief that ancestral spirits persist and can inhabit masked dancers.
- **Function.** Worn at **royal court ceremonies** and performances to transform the dancer into the elephant-spirit, conveying strength, wealth, and power. Economic and social signal of elite status.
- **Content.** An elongated cloth panel at the front represents the elephant's **trunk**; two large circular panels at the sides are its **ears**; beaded geometric patterns in black, white, red, and blue cover the whole. A humanoid face sits above the trunk — the fluidity between human and animal spirit.
- **Form.** Wood-and-textile mask covered in elaborate **beaded patterns** and woven raffia. Long, draped form built for dancing rather than static display.

**Vocabulary:** Aka · raffia · Kuosi society
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#164](#164--transformation-mask) — Transformation Mask (Kwakwaka'wakw, late 19th c.)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg/250px-Kwakwaka%27wakw_transformation_mask.jpg" width="180" alt="Transformation Mask">

**Theme:** Mythological

> Both masks transform the wearer into the mythological creature that the respective masks depict and honor the spiritual world.

---

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu Mask (Mende Society, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Ritual

> The bundu mask is used during initiation rituals of African girls, and the aka mask is used in rituals.

---

#### → [#173](#173--female-pwo-mask) — Female Pwo Mask (Chokwe, 20th c.)

**Theme:** Ritual

> The female pwo mask is used at initiation rituals, and the aka mask is used in performance rituals.

## #179 — Reliquary figure (byeri)

**Fang peoples** (southern Cameroon) · c. 19th–20th century C.E. · Wood · ~2 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/94/Reliquary_figure_Fang.jpg/250px-Reliquary_figure_Fang.jpg" width="240" alt="Reliquary figure (byeri)">
### Summary

A **Fang guardian figure** mounted atop a reliquary box holding ancestral bones.

- **Historical Context.** The **Fang** were historically nomadic, which reinforced a cultural emphasis on order and stability. Honorable deeds — killing an elephant, trading first with Europeans, bearing many children — marked one as a revered ancestor. Wood was sacred to Fang and to much of Africa; palm oil preserved the carving.
- **Function.** The byeri sat on top of a cylindrical **reliquary box** containing the skulls and bones of ancestors who had performed great deeds — clan leaders, warriors, craftsmen. Its role was to guard the relics from the **"forbidden gaze of women and uninitiated boys"** and from evil spirits, acting like a talisman. Elders consulted the figure at moments of important decision-making; young men were educated about lineage in front of it.
- **Content.** A highly stylized male figure (also made female): elongated torso, rounded head, closed calm eyes, protruding belly button (symbol of umbilical tie to ancestors), clasped hands, muscular limbs. Hairstyle of three crests reflects a high-status men's fashion.
- **Form.** Subtractive wood carving, abstracted into near-geometric forms. Small and portable to suit the migratory life of the Fang. Palm-oiled surface. Symmetrical, serene face; emphasis on internal spiritual balance rather than naturalism.

**Vocabulary:** reliquary · talisman · abstraction · byeri
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#58](#58--church-of-sainte-foy) — Reliquary Figure of Sainte-Foy (Romanesque, c. 9th–10th c.)

**Theme:** Reliquary figures

> Reliquaries~ both are associated with holding sacred items of important people.

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu mask (Sande Society, 19th–20th c.)

**Theme:** Abstraction, portrays ideals

> Depicts the ideals of the society. The Bundu mask shows female beauty standards, whereas the Reliquary figure shows values of the Fang peoples.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop figure (Kuba, 1760–1780)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg/250px-Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Ndop">

**Theme:** Portrays ideals, emphasis on spiritual aspects

> The Ndop figure portrays an idealized king and focuses on the king's spirituality, similar to the byeri figure which is abstracted to emphasize spirituality and values.

## #180 — Veranda Post of Enthroned King and Senior Wife (*Opo Ogoga*)

**Olowe of Ise** (Yoruba) · 1910–1914 C.E. · Wood and pigment · Nigeria · 10 ft 8 in × 11 × 14 in base

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Veranda_post_Yoruba.jpg/330px-Veranda_post_Yoruba.jpg" width="240" alt="Veranda Post of Enthroned King and Senior Wife (*Opo Ogoga*)">
### Summary

A carved **Yoruba veranda post** by the famous master **Olowe of Ise** — a statement of royal authority and female power.

- **Historical Context.** Carved by **Olowe of Ise (d. 1938)**, one of the most renowned Yoruba sculptors, as one of four posts for the **palace at Ikere**. The Yoruba live primarily in Nigeria and Benin; Olowe's identifiable style was itself a status symbol for the patron — only elite commissioners could afford his work.
- **Function.** Nominally holds up the roof of a veranda, but this particular post doesn't bear weight. Its real function is to project the political authority of the king and — pointedly — to assert that no king can rule without his senior wife. Olowe's signature presence also projects the patron's cultural sophistication.
- **Content.** A general Yoruba king seated on a throne. Behind him stands his **senior wife**, who is larger than the king and whose hands rest on his crown — she is **giving him the crown** and thus the legitimacy of his rule. In front, a smaller junior wife and the trickster god **Esu**; originally a (now lost) fan-bearer. The king's tall **crown** topped with a bird connects him to divine attention and protection.
- **Form.** Carved from a single wooden column; still holds traces of pigment. Columnar overall, with abundant negative space; strong diagonals in jaws, breasts, and arms. Hierarchical scale: the senior wife dwarfs the king, who in turn dwarfs the junior wife and Esu.

**Vocabulary:** Veranda · in the round · enthroned
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#18](#18--king-menkaura-and-queen) — King Menkaure and His Queen (Egyptian Old Kingdom, c. 2490 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Honoring a royal couple

> Both depict a royal couple with shows of power incorporated. In regards to the form, both are quite upright although Menkaure and his Queen is much more blocky and solid than the Veranda Post.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop Figure (Kuba, 1760–1780)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg/250px-Brooklyn_Museum_61.33_Ndop_Portrait_of_King_Mishe_miShyaang_maMbul_%285%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Ndop">

**Theme:** Commemoration of a king

> Both are representations of kings, though the Ndop shows a certain king while the Veranda Post shows a general king. Both are made of wood and include carved jewelry.

---

#### → [#208](#208--jahangir-preferring-a-sufi-shaikh-to-kings) — Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings (Bichitr, 1615–1618)

**Theme:** Scene depicting a king and other important figures

> Both include several royal figures and have positioning of them playing a large role in the interpretation.

## #181 — Petra, Jordan: Treasury and Great Temple

**Nabataean, Ptolemaic, and Roman** · c. 400 B.C.E.–100 C.E. · Cut rock · Jordan · Treasury façade ~130 ft; Great Temple ~57 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Treasury_petra_crop.jpeg/250px-Treasury_petra_crop.jpeg" width="240" alt="Petra Treasury">

### Summary

A rock-cut **Nabataean capital** where Hellenistic, Egyptian, and Arabian styles converge.

- **Historical Context.** Capital of the **Nabataean Kingdom** until the Roman conquest in 106 C.E. Wealth came from its control of the caravan trade in incense, spices, and luxury goods. Stylistic syncretism on the Treasury's façade reflects contact with the **Ptolemaic** Greeks in Alexandria and later the Romans. Petra declined as sea routes and cities like **Palmyra** took over the trade.
- **Function.** The Treasury (Al-Khazneh) was originally a **royal crypt and temple**. The Great Temple was the principal religious structure, probably dedicated to the Nabataean god **Dushara**, though a small theater inside may also mark it as a palace or assembly hall. The whole city functioned as a state capital and trading hub.
- **Content.** The Treasury is a shallow rock-cut façade with a **broken pediment**, central **tholos**, Corinthian columns, and relief figures including dancing Amazons and the Greco-Egyptian goddess **Isis-Tyche**. The Great Temple rises in two levels (only foundations and columns survive) and is enclosed by colonnades ending in a small apse.
- **Form.** Carved directly out of the pink-red sandstone cliff face — an Arabian innovation. Treasury façade fuses Greek pediment/tholos/Corinthian order, Egyptian obelisk bases, and Nabataean rock-cut technique. Entry from a narrow slot canyon dramatically reveals the façade.

**Vocabulary:** tholos · pediment · Ionic · Doric · Corinthian · mausoleum · crypt · façade
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#45](#45--forum-of-trajan) — Forum of Trajan (Roman, dedicated 112 C.E.)

**Theme:** City Planning

> Both Petra and the Forum of Trajan are examples of government-sponsored urban architecture. Both have a variety of structures and monuments dedicated to various functions.

---

#### → [#30](#30--audience-hall-apadana-of-darius-and-xerxes) — Persepolis (Achaemenid, c. 520–465 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** City Planning

> Both Petra and Persepolis are examples of centrally planned capitals that were the seats of great empires. Both were constructed at the height of their empires' wealth to display their power and trade to outsiders.

---

#### → [#206](#206--forbidden-city) — Forbidden City (Ming, begun 1406)

**Theme:** Ceremonial spaces, imperial capitals

> Both the Great Temple at Petra and the Forbidden City had a religious or cosmic significance of some sort: the Forbidden City was meant as a microcosm of the Chinese universe, while the Great Temple at Petra served as a house of worship. In addition, both Petra and the Forbidden City were used as the seats of empires.

---

## #182 — Buddha, Bamiyan

**Gandharan / Bamiyan culture** · c. 400–800 C.E. (destroyed 2001) · Cut rock with plaster and polychrome paint · Bamiyan Valley, Afghanistan · one 175 ft, other 115 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg/250px-Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg" width="240" alt="Buddha of Bamiyan">

### Summary

Two **colossal standing Buddhas** at a key waypoint on the Silk Road — destroyed by the Taliban in 2001.

- **Historical Context.** Carved between the 4th and 8th centuries at the western end of the **Silk Road**, Bamiyan was a major stop for Buddhist pilgrims and traders as Buddhism spread across Asia. The two Buddhas represented the largest standing Buddha figures in the world. In **2001 the Taliban destroyed them** as idolatrous, an act of deliberate iconoclasm; only the niches and outlines remain.
- **Function.** Public pilgrimage images that demonstrated the reach and prestige of Buddhism. Surrounding caves housed monks and Buddhist wall paintings; pilgrims could walk up through passages to the Buddhas' shoulders.
- **Content.** Two colossal standing Buddhas — one (the Western, "Salsal") traditionally identified as **Vairocana** (the universal Buddha), the other (the Eastern, "Shahmama") as **Sakyamuni** (the historical Buddha). Frontal, symmetrical, with a halo-shaped niche around each body.
- **Form.** **Rock-cut** figures carved from the sandstone cliff, finished in stucco and **polychrome paint**, originally gilded in places. Flowing drapery reflects **Hellenistic and Gandharan** influences (Roman toga) fused with Indian Buddhist iconography.

**Vocabulary:** Vairocana · Sakyamuni · iconoclasm
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2, 3.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#184](#184--jowo-rinpoche-enshrined-in-the-jokhang-temple) — Jowo Rinpoche (Yarlung, 7th c.)

**Theme:** Religious

> Both artworks support Buddhism and depict Sakyamuni. They also both disappeared (the Bamiyan Buddhas in 2001, and Jowo Rinpoche in the 1960s).

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (Maurya / Shunga, c. 300 B.C.E.–100 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious

> Both were important artworks in Buddhist history, but the Great Stupa was a Buddhist shrine while the Bamiyan Buddhas were for pilgrimages.

---

#### → [#198](#198--borobudur-temple) — Borobudur (Sailendra, c. 750–842 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religious

> Both pieces are massive Buddhist structures, as Borobudur contains 504 life-size Buddhas and many stupas carved out of stone.

---

## #183 — The Kaaba

**Islamic** · pre-Islamic origin; rebuilt 631–632 C.E. (and many times since) · Granite masonry, covered with silk Kiswa embroidered in gold and silver thread · Mecca, Saudi Arabia · 43 × 36.2 × 42.2 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg/250px-The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg" width="240" alt="The Kaaba">

### Summary

The **spiritual center of Islam** — the cube at the heart of the Masjid al-Haram in Mecca.

- **Historical Context.** Traditionally said to have been first built by **Ibrahim (Abraham)** as a simple open rectangle; rebuilt by Muhammad's **Quraysh** tribe c. 608 and repeatedly since. The **Black Stone**, said to have been given to Ibrahim by the angel Gabriel, is the only part of the original that still stands. Before Islam it housed pagan idols; these were destroyed when Muhammad returned to Mecca in 630. Since then the Kaaba has been the supreme focus of Islamic devotion; non-Muslims cannot enter the city.
- **Function.** The **qibla** — the direction of prayer — for all Muslims five times a day. The centerpiece of the **Hajj**, the pilgrimage that every able Muslim is required to make at least once. Pilgrims **circumambulate** (*tawaf*) the Kaaba seven times and try to touch or kiss the Black Stone.
- **Content.** A nearly cubic granite building covered each year by a new black silk **Kiswa** embroidered in gold with the **shahada** ("There is no god but Allah, and Mohammed is the Messenger of Allah") and other Quranic inscriptions in Arabic calligraphy. The Black Stone is set in the east corner. Interior holds pillars and gold plates.
- **Form.** Simple stone cube — the plainest geometry carrying the greatest spiritual value. Black Kiswa against the gold calligraphy creates sharp contrast.

**Vocabulary:** Kiswa · circumambulation · Hajj · qibla · Black Stone
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Innermost Coffin of Tutankhamun (Egyptian New Kingdom, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Building built on important site

> Both the Tomb of Tutankhamun and the Kaaba are still located in situ from when they were discovered.

---

#### → [#185](#185--the-dome-of-the-rock) — Dome of the Rock (Umayyad, 691–692)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jerusalem-2013%282%29-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%28SE_exposure%29.jpg/250px-Jerusalem-2013%282%29-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%28SE_exposure%29.jpg" width="180" alt="Dome of the Rock">

**Theme:** Building built on important site

> The Dome of the Rock surrounds a holy rock in situ, just how the Kaaba remains in its original stone.

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa (Sanchi, c. 300 B.C.E.–100 C.E.)

**Theme:** Shrine, circumambulation

> Both features are shrines for a valued god in a certain religion. Additionally, both structures are circumambulated by devouts.

---

## #184 — Jowo Rinpoche, enshrined in the Jokhang Temple

**Tibetan** (Yarlung Dynasty) · early–mid 7th century C.E. · Gilt metals with semiprecious stones, pearls, paint, and votive offerings · Jokhang Temple, Lhasa, Tibet · ~5 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Jowo_Rinpoche_Jokhang_Temple.jpg/330px-Jowo_Rinpoche_Jokhang_Temple.jpg" width="240" alt="Jowo Rinpoche, enshrined in the Jokhang Temple">
### Summary

The **most venerated Buddha image in Tibet** — a Shakyamuni Buddha said to have been modeled in the Buddha's own lifetime.

- **Historical Context.** According to tradition the celestial Indian architect **Vishwakarma** carved the Jowo Rinpoche during the life of the Buddha Shakyamuni (6th–5th century B.C.E.), so it preserves a true likeness. In the 7th century C.E., the Chinese princess **Wencheng Gongzhu** brought the sculpture to Tibet as part of her marriage dowry — the moment that aligns with the founding of **Vajrayana Buddhism** in Tibet. Renovated across 1,300 years; most recently after the **Cultural Revolution**; survived a 2018 fire at the Jokhang.
- **Function.** An **earthly proxy** for the Buddha after his *parinirvana*. Pilgrims visiting the Jokhang — Tibet's most sacred site — seek the Jowo's blessing as equivalent to being blessed by the Buddha himself.
- **Content.** Shakyamuni depicted at the moment of enlightenment, age 12. Seated in **padmasana** (lotus position) on a jeweled gold throne. Right hand in **bhumisparsha mudra** (calling earth to witness); left hand in **dhyana mudra** (meditation). A tall jeweled crown and blue hair top the gilt face; beneath ornate regalia he wears a simple monk's robe.
- **Form.** **Additive sculpture** — gilt metal core adorned with semiprecious stones, pearls, paint, and continual pilgrim offerings. Slightly over life-size.

**Vocabulary:** padmasana · rinpoche · dhyana mudra · bhumisparsha mudra
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#182](#182--buddha-bamiyan) — Buddha, Bamiyan (c. 400–800 C.E., destroyed 2001)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg/250px-Buddha_of_Bamiyan.jpg" width="180" alt="Buddha of Bamiyan">

**Theme:** Sculptures depicting the Buddha; similar time period

> Both are depictions of the Buddha from a similar time period. At least one of the Bamiyan Buddhas is a Buddha Shakyamuni, just like the Jowo Rinpoche. The Bamiyan Buddhas are much larger (175 and 120 ft vs. 5 ft) and are subtractive rock carvings, while the Jowo Rinpoche is an additive sculpture made of gilt metal.

---

#### → [#37](#37--winged-victory-of-samothrace) — Winged Victory (Nike) of Samothrace (Hellenistic, c. 190 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Sculptures depicting religious deities; over life sized pieces

> Both depict religious deities at more than life size. The Winged Victory depicts Nike in Greek polytheism; Buddhism is monotheistic. The Winged Victory is a subtractive marble sculpture; the Jowo is an additive gilt-metal sculpture made more for worship than for commemoration.

---

#### → [#202](#202--shiva-as-lord-of-dance-nataraja) — Shiva as Lord of Dance (Nataraja) (Chola, c. 11th c.)

**Theme:** Sculptures depicting religious deities; similar materials

> Both depict sacred deities of their religions with meaningful hand positions. Both are additive metal sculptures — gilt metal vs. cast bronze. The Jowo is over life size; Shiva as Lord of Dance is ~2 ft tall.

---

## #185 — The Dome of the Rock

**Umayyad Caliphate** (commissioned by Caliph **Abd al-Malik**) · 685–691 C.E. · Stone masonry and wooden roof with glazed ceramic tile and mosaics; gilt aluminum and bronze dome covered with gold leaf · Temple Mount, Jerusalem

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/Jerusalem-2013%282%29-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%28SE_exposure%29.jpg/250px-Jerusalem-2013%282%29-Temple_Mount-Dome_of_the_Rock_%28SE_exposure%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Dome of the Rock">

### Summary

The earliest surviving **Islamic monument** and one of the most politically charged sacred buildings in the world.

- **Historical Context.** Built by the Umayyad caliph **Abd al-Malik** on the Temple Mount in Jerusalem (called the Haram al-Sharif by Muslims) — a site holy to Judaism (the First and Second Temples), Christianity, and Islam (the Night Journey of Muhammad). Likely designed by the architects Radjâ ibn Haywâ and Yazid ibn Sallâm. Erected at a contested moment: the Umayyad caliphate competed with a rival caliphate in Mecca, so the monument asserted Islam's presence and prestige in the Judeo-Christian holy city.
- **Function.** A **shrine**, not a mosque — its central octagonal space surrounds and marks the **Foundation Stone**, the rock from which Muhammad is said to have ascended on the **Isra and Mi'raj** (Night Journey). Circumambulated by pilgrims.
- **Content.** A central octagonal plan with a golden dome over the rock; outer and inner ambulatories for circumambulation. Interior mosaics in vegetal and abstract patterns; Arabic **Kufic inscriptions** from the Quran running around the drum (some polemical, asserting Islamic theology over Christian doctrine).
- **Form.** Octagonal plan with a tall central drum and hemispherical **gold-leafed dome**. Exterior tiled in blue and turquoise glazed ceramic added later under Suleiman. Both mosaics and calligraphy strictly aniconic — no human or animal figures.

**Vocabulary:** qubba · Haram al-Sharif · Isra and Mi'raj · aniconism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#183](#183--the-kaaba) — The Kaaba (Islamic, Mecca)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/89/The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg/250px-The_Ka%27ba%2C_Great_Mosque_of_Mecca%2C_Saudi_Arabia_%284%29.jpg" width="180" alt="The Kaaba">

**Theme:** Building built on important site; circumambulation

> Both shelter a sacred stone on a site considered holy; in both, pilgrims circumambulate the sacred object.

---

#### → [#186](#186--great-mosque-masjid-e-jameh-isfahan) — Great Mosque (Masjid-e Jameh), Isfahan (Abbasid onward)

**Theme:** Islamic architecture, sacred space

> Both are Islamic sacred architecture with elaborate tilework; both use aniconic decoration (vegetal, geometric, and calligraphic motifs).

---

#### → [#51](#51--san-vitale) — Hagia Sophia (Byzantine, 537)

**Theme:** Monumental centrally-planned dome buildings in Jerusalem-Constantinople region

> Both are sixth–seventh-century centrally-planned buildings with large domes; the Dome of the Rock's builders drew on the earlier Byzantine tradition of dome engineering and mosaic.

---

## #186 — Great Mosque (Masjid-e Jameh), Isfahan

**Nizam al-Mulk, Taj al-Mulk** (Persian, under Seljuq patronage) · founded c. 700 C.E.; expanded 11th c.; restored 14th, 19th, 20th centuries · Stone, brick, wood, plaster, glazed ceramic tile · Isfahan, Iran · 20,000+ sq m

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Isfahan_Masjid.jpg/330px-Isfahan_Masjid.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Mosque (Masjid-e Jameh), Isfahan">
### Summary

A thousand-year **layered mosque** showing every major phase of Persian Islamic architecture.

- **Historical Context.** Originally founded under the **Abbasid** dynasty in present-day Iran. The 11th-century **Seljuq** expansion produced the two domed chambers — the south dome (over the **mihrab**) and the north dome — built a year apart by two rival architects, **Nizam al-Mulk** and **Taj al-Mulk**. Successive Mongol, Timurid, and **Safavid** patrons continued to add to it through the 20th century, so the complex records a thousand years of Persian architectural evolution.
- **Function.** A **congregational (Friday) mosque** with ongoing religious, educational (madrasa/library), and civic functions — still in use today, now also a tourist hub and UNESCO World Heritage Site.
- **Content.** A **four-iwan** hypostyle plan centered on a large open courtyard (**sahn**) with a fountain for ablutions. The south iwan leads to the mihrab and qibla; others open onto libraries, schools, and treasuries. Tilework in brilliant **turquoise** blue throughout. The muqarnas-vaulted ceilings, domes, and iwans are among the most celebrated examples of Persian-Islamic architecture.
- **Form.** **Hypostyle** plan with **four iwans** and two brick **domes**; glazed ceramic tile exteriors and **muqarnas** on many interior surfaces; vegetal **arabesque** and **calligraphic** decoration in keeping with Islamic aniconism.

**Vocabulary:** qibla · mihrab · minaret · muqarnas · arabesque · minbar · sahn · calligraphy · tessellations · iwan
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#46](#46--pantheon) — Pantheon (Roman, c. 118–125 C.E.)

**Theme:** Large scale Architecture

> Both created from a preexisting structure

---

#### → [#209](#209--taj-mahal) — Taj Mahal (Mughal, 1632–1653)

**Theme:** Large scale Architecture

> Both have context of Islamic architecture

---

#### → [#84](#84--mosque-of-selim-ii) — Mosque of Selim II (Sinan, 1568–1575)

**Theme:** Large scale architecture

> Both are mosques

## #187 — Folio from a Qur'an

**Arab, Abbasid Caliphate** · 8th–9th century C.E. · Ink and gold on parchment · probably modern-day Iraq · 230 × 320 mm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Quran_folio_Islamic.jpg/250px-Quran_folio_Islamic.jpg" width="240" alt="Folio from a Qur'an">
### Summary

An early **Kufic-script Qur'an folio** — calligraphy as the highest Islamic art.

- **Historical Context.** One of the earliest surviving Qur'ans, produced in the **Abbasid Caliphate**. The Abbasids formalized Arabic into a standardized **Kufic script** to ensure that the sacred text could be read consistently across a geographically vast Muslim world. Because Islam prohibits figural imagery in religious contexts, **calligraphy** became the highest and most prestigious form of Islamic art.
- **Function.** A page from the holy book, used in pious recitation and as a display of wealth in elite homes. The format — wider than it is tall, with generous spacing and few lines per page — makes it easy to read at a distance and distinguishes it from more modest copies.
- **Content.** The beginning of a sura (here *Al-Ankabut*), introduced by an elaborate gold **sura heading**. Fewer than nine lines per page signal a wealthy patron. Arabic reads right-to-left, accounting for the landscape format.
- **Form.** **Kufic script** in dark ink on vellum; vowels marked by small red diacritical **dots** (a recent innovation at the time to prevent misreading). Consonants rendered in thick, stately lines. Spacing and layout mathematically planned before inking — spare, geometric, monumental.

**Vocabulary:** Islam · calligraphy · Qur'an · Kufic · recitation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#55](#55--lindisfarne-gospels:-st.-matthew-cross-carpet-page;-st.-luke-portrait-page;-st.-luke-incipit-page) — Lindisfarne Gospels (Anglo-Saxon, c. 700)

**Theme:** geometric designs, religious manuscript, expensive material

> The Lindisfarne Gospel is also a religious manuscript where scholars took great pride in decorating a text that the patrons already had memorized. Both have large decorative letters that indicate the start of a new chapter.

---

#### → [#24](#24--last-judgment-of-hu-nefer-from-his-tomb-page-from-the-book-of-the-dead) — Last Judgment of Hu-Nefer (Egyptian, c. 1275 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** religious stories, expensive material

> Both are on elongated pieces of expensive parchment. Both tell religious stories, one through pictures and another through calligraphy.

---

#### → [#64](#64--golden-haggadah-the-plagues-of-egypt-scenes-of-liberation-and-preparation-for-passover) — Golden Haggadah (Late medieval Spain, c. 1320)

**Theme:** Abrahamic stories, gold (illuminated)

> Both were owned by wealthy families but the Golden Haggadah's visuals were vital to relating the stories to its western European owners while the calligraphy was meant more for decoration for the Muslim owners of the Folio.

---

## #188 — Basin (Baptistère de Saint Louis)

**Muhammad ibn al-Zain** (Mamluk) · 1320–1340 C.E. · Brass with silver and gold inlay · West Asia · h. 22.2 cm, diam. 50.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Baptistere_Saint_Louis_basin.jpg/330px-Baptistere_Saint_Louis_basin.jpg" width="240" alt="Basin (Baptistère de Saint Louis)">
### Summary

A masterpiece of **Mamluk metalwork** — a royal basin that became a French royal **baptismal font**.

- **Historical Context.** Made during the **Mamluk Period** (1250–1517), when the Mamluks — originally Turkic warrior-slaves — ruled Egypt and Syria and sponsored a flourishing of Islamic art in metalwork, glass, and textiles. Later captured by French crusaders or acquired in trade, the basin was reused in **France** as the baptismal font for children of the **royal house**, hence its name.
- **Function.** Originally a ceremonial basin — likely for **hand-washing before a royal meal** or related court ritual. Reappropriated later as the baptismal font in the Sainte-Chapelle de Vincennes.
- **Content.** Continuous horizontal **friezes** of Mamluk warriors, hunters, and courtly figures on horseback — the four horsemen encircle the outside. Interior shows water creatures (eels, fish). Unusually for a major Islamic work, no calligraphic inscription.
- **Form.** Cast brass with intricate **silver and gold inlay** forming the figures and decorative bands; cylindrical body flares slightly at the mouth. Horror-vacui surface packed with detail; symmetrical placement of the four horsemen.

**Vocabulary:** friezes · calligraphy (or lack thereof) · arabesque · Mamluk
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

## #189 — Bahram Gur Fights the Karg, folio from the Great Il-Khanid *Shahnama*

**Persian, Ilkhanate** · 1330–1340 C.E. · Ink and opaque watercolor on paper · Harvard Art Museums · 41.5 × 30 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/15/Bahram_Gur_Shahnama.jpg/330px-Bahram_Gur_Shahnama.jpg" width="240" alt="Bahram Gur Fights the Karg, folio from the Great Il-Khanid *Shahnama*">
### Summary

A folio of Persian heroic mythology from the **Great Il-Khanid Shahnama**, made for a Mongol-ruled court.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in **Tabriz**, capital of the Muslim-Mongol **Ilkhanate** (the Iranian successor state to the Mongol Empire). The Il-Khanids were Persian-Mongol rulers patronizing Persian cultural production as political legitimation after conversion to Islam. This particular manuscript is one of the most lavish Persian manuscripts ever made. Paper, rather than vellum, shows Islamic technological sophistication.
- **Function.** Illuminated pages from the **Shahnama** (Book of Kings) — the Persian national epic by Ferdowsi, tracing Persian kings from pre-history to Alexander. Educated young nobles in the glory and proper behavior of their Persian heritage; reinforced the Ilkhanid dynasty's claim to Persian legitimacy.
- **Content.** The Persian hero **Bahram Gur** has just slain the **karg**, a mythic wolf-rhinoceros monster. He stands in heroic pose with his stallion rearing on its hind legs and his mace slung over his shoulder, looking off into the sunset. A golden halo surrounds him. Bahram's features appear **Mongol**, signaling the cultural fusion of the Ilkhanate.
- **Form.** **Ink and opaque watercolor on paper**; composition dominated by the single figure and rearing horse; limited depth but strong compositional energy.

**Vocabulary:** folio · manuscript · Persian · Islamic texts · Ilkhanate
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#50](#50--rebecca-and-eliezer-at-the-well-and-jacob-wrestling-the-angel-from-the-vienna-genesis) — Vienna Genesis (Early Byzantine, early 6th c.)

**Theme:** Jacob wrestled the angel

> Religious nature, historical fiction

---

#### → [#64](#64--golden-haggadah-the-plagues-of-egypt-scenes-of-liberation-and-preparation-for-passover) — Golden Haggadah (Spain, c. 1320)

**Theme:** The plight of the Hebrews

> Historical fiction

---

#### → [#190](#190--the-court-of-gayumars-folio-from-shah-tahmasp's-*shahnama*) — The Court of Gayumars (Sultan Muhammad, 1522–1525)

**Theme:** Shahnama

> It's from the same book

## #190 — The Court of Gayumars, folio from Shah Tahmasp's *Shahnama*

**Sultan Muhammad** (Safavid Persia) · 1522–1525 C.E. · Ink, opaque watercolor, and gold on paper · Tabriz, Iran

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Court_Gayumars_Shahnama.jpg/330px-Court_Gayumars_Shahnama.jpg" width="240" alt="The Court of Gayumars, folio from Shah Tahmasp's *Shahnama*">
### Summary

A **Safavid** folio widely considered the single finest Persian miniature ever painted.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by the first **Safavid** shah, **Isma'il I**, and completed under his son **Tahmasp I**. The Safavids, claiming descent from Sufi shaikhs, sponsored the arts on a grand scale — this particular *Shahnama* (Book of Kings) manuscript is the most ambitious of the genre. **Gayumars** is the mythic first king of Iran; his son **Siyamak** will soon be murdered by the demon **Ahriman**'s son, Black Div.
- **Function.** Folio from a royally-commissioned manuscript that recorded all Persian kings to legitimize the Safavid dynasty and to educate royal heirs on ideal kingship.
- **Content.** King Gayumars enthroned on a **tiger-skin** throne at the top of a mountain landscape; his court, dressed in leopard pelts, forms a **semicircle** around him. **Siyamak** sits to his left; grandson **Hushang** to his right. An angel, **Surush**, appears to deliver the prophecy of Siyamak's murder. A garden spreads in front, a mountain rises behind — everyone is peaceful before the coming tragedy.
- **Form.** **Ink, opaque watercolor, and gold on paper**. Composition built around a radial semicircle. Faces and rock-cloud shapes show strong **Chinese / East Asian** influence absorbed via the Mongol period. Overall harmony between figures and landscape; no single detail overwhelms — a hallmark of Sultan Muhammad's style.

**Vocabulary:** folio · illuminated manuscript · Shahnama · Safavid
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.3, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#189](#189--bahram-gur-fights-the-karg-folio-from-the-great-il-khanid-*shahnama*) — Bahram Gur Fights the Karg (Ilkhanid Shahnama, 1330–1340)

**Theme:** Depiction of ideal king, power of the king

> Both works are folios from the Shahnama and depict the power of the king. Both use a Chinese landscape in the background, demonstrating cross-cultural influences.

---

#### → [#208](#208--jahangir-preferring-a-sufi-shaikh-to-kings) — Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings (Bichitr, 1615–1618)

**Theme:** Power of king, cross-cultural influence

> Both works were commissioned by kings in areas the Mongols conquered. Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh was created during the Mughal dynasty, which had roots to the Mongols. Both have Asiatic depictions of figures, use watercolor and gold on paper, feature floral themes, and achieve harmony.

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, c. 1000)

**Theme:** Depictions of nature

> The Court of Gayumars had Asiatic influences seen in the background of the work, with the mountains in the back and gardens in front of Gayumars. These depictions resemble the Chinese work Travelers among Mountains and Streams. Both pieces achieve harmony within nature, and nature blends into both pieces.

---

## #191 — The Ardabil Carpet

**Maqsud Kashani (inscribed name), Safavid Iran** · 1539–1540 C.E. · Wool and silk, knotted pile · Victoria and Albert Museum, London · 34′6″ × 17′6″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Ardabil_Carpet_Persian.jpg/330px-Ardabil_Carpet_Persian.jpg" width="240" alt="The Ardabil Carpet">
### Summary

The most celebrated Persian carpet in the world and an icon of the **Safavid dynasty**.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned for the **shrine of Sufi saint Safi al-Din** at Ardabil in northwest Iran, ancestor of the Safavid rulers. The Safavid Empire (founded 1501) tied Shi'a Islam to state identity; the court sponsored luxury workshops in Tabriz, Kashan, and Isfahan. An inscribed couplet by the poet Hafiz names **Maqsud Kashani**, a court official who likely oversaw production. One of a matched pair; its twin is in LACMA.
- **Function.** Placed in the shrine as a devotional furnishing honoring the saint and advertising Safavid wealth and piety. Too precious for daily use.
- **Content.** Non-figural Islamic design: a central sunburst medallion with 16 pendants, flanked by two mosque lamps of unequal size (a trick of perspective making them appear equal from the shrine entrance). Dense floral arabesques fill the red field; corner quadrants mirror the medallion.
- **Form.** Woven on a silk warp with wool pile; approximately **340 knots per square inch** (standard carpets have 80–160) yielding roughly 25 million knots. Natural dyes: indigo, madder, pomegranate. Symmetrical, balanced, flat.

**Vocabulary:** knots · Safi al-Din · Safavid · inscription · mosque lamp
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.3, 3.1, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#189](#189--bahram-gur-fights-the-karg-folio-from-the-great-il-khanid-*shahnama*) — Bahram Gur Fights the Karg, folio from the Great Il-Khanid Shahnama (Iran, c. 1330–1335)

**Theme:** Safavid Culture

> Both pieces are focused on honoring the person the piece is dedicated to. The Folio depicts the king in battle (honoring his military strength) and the carpet is in the shrine dedicated to a Sufi Mystic (who is important to the founding of the empire).

---

#### → [#186](#186--great-mosque-masjid-e-jameh-isfahan) — The Great Mosque (Masjid-i Jāmi'), Isfahan (Seljuk and later dynasties, c. 700–present)

**Theme:** Geometric Patterns

> Both the architectural structure and the Ardabil carpet are largely composed of ornate designs. Instead of depicting religious figures (like Allah), which the Islamic religion forbids, decorations on buildings feature patterns, shapes, and outlines.

---

#### → [#187](#187--folio-from-a-qur'an) — Folio from a Qur'an (Arab, North Africa, or Near East, c. 8th–9th century)

**Theme:** Calligraphy

> Religious texts such as the Quran often feature calligraphy. The words are counted not only as meaningful methods of communication, but are also dedicated as art.

---

## #192 — The Great Stupa at Sanchi

**Buddhist India (Mauryan through Shunga/Satavahana)** · 3rd century B.C.E.–1st century C.E. · Stone masonry · Sanchi, Madhya Pradesh, India · Dome ~120 ft diameter, ~54 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Great_Stupa_Sanchi.jpg/330px-Great_Stupa_Sanchi.jpg" width="240" alt="The Great Stupa at Sanchi">
### Summary

The earliest and most iconic surviving **Buddhist stupa**, built to enshrine relics of the Buddha.

- **Historical Context.** Originally commissioned by **Emperor Ashoka** (r. 269–232 B.C.E.) of the Mauryan dynasty, who converted to Buddhism after the Kalinga war and sponsored the spread of the religion through an extensive building campaign — legend credits him with 84,000 stupas. The core brick dome was enlarged in the 2nd century B.C.E. under the Shungas; the carved stone **toranas** (gateways) were added c. 1st century B.C.E.–1st century C.E.
- **Function.** A reliquary monument housing sacred relics of the Buddha. Pilgrims **circumambulate** the dome clockwise along a raised walkway, earning merit; the act re-enacts the cosmic order.
- **Content.** Solid hemispherical dome (*anda*) on a circular plinth, crowned by a square *harmika* and a triple parasol *yashti* (world axis). Four torana gateways at the cardinal directions carry dense narrative relief of **jataka tales** (Buddha's past lives) and yakshi tree-spirit figures. The Buddha himself is rendered only through symbols (footprints, empty throne, wheel) — aniconic.
- **Form.** Rubble-core dome faced with dressed stone. Low-relief and high-relief carving cover the toranas, bordering on horror vacui; the dome surface is unadorned. Axial symmetry at cardinal points.

**Vocabulary:** sepulchral · stupa · torana · yashti · harmika · jataka · circumambulation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#182](#182--buddha-bamiyan) — Bamiyan Buddhas (Gandharan culture, Bamiyan, Afghanistan, c. 400–800 C.E., destroyed 2001)

**Theme:** Grand, Buddhist, Devotion, International, Realism, Divine

> The Bamiyan Buddhas were also massive building projects for the purpose of providing a place for the congregation and worship of Buddhists.

---

#### → [#207](#207--ryoan-ji) — Ryoan-ji (Kyoto, Japan, c. 1480)

**Theme:** Buddhism, Harmony, Zen

> The Ryoan-Ji is a temple made for followers of Zen Buddhism. The ideals and teachings of Zen could be found within the temple and its rock garden, just as the teachings of Buddhism could be found on the Great Stupa at Sanchi.

---

#### → [#199](#199--angkor-wat) — Angkor Wat (Khmer, Cambodia, 12th century)

**Theme:** Religious, Monumental, Hindu, Lavish, Massive, Ambitious

> The Angkor Wat, literally City Temple, was created as a Hindu temple dedicated to Vishnu, the Hindu god of preservation of life. The temple was also commissioned by a powerful king to spread his religion and establish legitimacy of rule.

---

## #193 — Terracotta warriors from the mausoleum of the first Qin emperor of China

**Qin Dynasty, China** · c. 221–206 B.C.E. · Painted terracotta · Lintong, Xi'an, Shaanxi province, China · Figures 5′8″–6′2″ tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/51714-Terracota-Army.jpg/330px-51714-Terracota-Army.jpg" width="240" alt="Terracotta warriors from the mausoleum of the first Qin emperor of China">
### Summary

A buried life-sized army of roughly **8,000 individualized figures**, made to guard the First Emperor in the afterlife.

- **Historical Context.** **Qin Shi Huangdi** unified China in 221 B.C.E. after conquering the Warring States, founding the Qin Dynasty, standardizing script, currency, and measures, and beginning the Great Wall. Obsessed with immortality, he mobilized more than 700,000 conscripted laborers to build his vast mausoleum complex near modern Xi'an; the pit army was discovered by farmers in 1974.
- **Function.** A **funerary guard** to protect the emperor's spirit in the afterlife, part of an enormous simulated underworld city that reportedly included rivers of mercury and a star-map ceiling.
- **Content.** Infantry, archers, cavalry, charioteers, and officers arrayed in battle formation facing east. Each figure's face, hair, and armor differ — likely portraits or composites of actual soldiers. Weapons (swords, crossbow triggers, halberds) were real bronze.
- **Form.** Modular construction: heads, arms, and torsos molded separately, then assembled and individualized with layered clay; fired in kilns and painted in bright mineral pigments (the famous "Chinese purple" among them), most of which has flaked off. Figures average 300–400 lbs.

**Vocabulary:** mausoleum · terracotta · Qin Shi Huangdi · Qin Dynasty · Daoism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin (New Kingdom, 18th Dynasty, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** funerary, royal tomb, importance of creating a comfortable afterlife

> Both were tombs of powerful rulers that were discovered. Both of these rulers were buried with decorated with many lavish and ornate objects, with the goal of establishing a comfortable afterlife.

---

#### → [#48](#48--catacomb-of-priscilla) — Catacomb of Priscilla (Late Antique Europe, Rome, c. 200–400 C.E.)

**Theme:** funerary, underground cemetery, tomb

> Both were places of burial, as the Catacomb of Priscilla holds 4 million people, and the Mausoleum of Qin Shi Huangdi holds only him. Both were located underground.

---

#### → [#32](#32--tomb-of-the-triclinium) — Tomb of the Triclinium (Etruscan, Tarquinia, Italy, c. 480–470 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** funerary, brightly painted frescoes, celebratory

> Both were tombs that were extravagantly decorated. They also both contain images and objects that suggest a desire of the person buried to have a peaceful afterlife.

## #194 — Funeral banner of Lady Dai (Xin Zhui)

**Western Han dynasty, China** · c. 168 B.C.E. · Painted silk · Tomb 1, Mawangdui, Changsha, Hunan · 205 × 92 × 47.7 cm (T-shaped)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Funeral_banner_Lady_Dai.jpg/330px-Funeral_banner_Lady_Dai.jpg" width="240" alt="Funeral banner of Lady Dai (Xin Zhui)">
### Summary

A T-shaped silk banner mapping the **Han cosmos** — underworld, earth, and heaven — draped over the coffin of a marquise.

- **Historical Context.** **Li Cang**, first Marquis of Dai and prime minister of the Changsha kingdom, died 186 B.C.E.; his wife **Xin Zhui (Lady Dai)** was buried at Mawangdui around 168 B.C.E. Their tomb was rediscovered in 1972 during construction of a hospital — one of the greatest 20th-century Chinese archaeological finds, yielding Lady Dai's famously well-preserved body, lacquerwares, silks, and medical texts. Silk was a mark of Han elite status and the backbone of the Silk Road trade opened under the Han emperors.
- **Function.** A **mingjing** funerary banner carried at the head of the funeral procession, then draped over the innermost coffin to guide the *hun* (celestial soul) to heaven and keep the *po* (earthly soul) anchored to the tomb.
- **Content.** Three registers. **Top (heaven):** sun with crow, crescent moon with toad, the serpent-bodied Queen Mother **Xiwangmu** flanked by gate guards. **Middle (earth):** Lady Dai herself with three attendants on a platform, preparing her journey; below, mourners at a ritual feast before her coffin. **Bottom (underworld):** a giant holds up the earth above coiling fish and chthonic creatures.
- **Form.** Painted in mineral pigments on fine silk; fluid calligraphic line. The T-shape drapes over the coffin, the horizontal bar covering its head. Hierarchical scale: Lady Dai is larger than her attendants.

**Vocabulary:** banner · Yin and Yang · Western Han · silk
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#36](#36--grave-stele-of-hegeso) — Grave stele of Hegeso (Attributed to Kallimachos, Classical Greek, c. 410 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Grave marker, Historical

> Both had owners appear in the depicted scenes

#### → [#23](#23--tutankhamun's-tomb-innermost-coffin) — Tutankhamun's tomb, innermost coffin (New Kingdom, 18th Dynasty, c. 1323 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical, religious, Mythological

> Both functioned as tools to speed the journey to afterlife

---

#### → [#24](#24--last-judgment-of-hu-nefer-from-his-tomb-page-from-the-book-of-the-dead) — Last Judgment of Hu-Nefer (New Kingdom, 19th Dynasty, c. 1275 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Historical, Religious, Mythological

> Both depicted scene of the journey to afterlife

## #195 — Longmen Caves (Grottoes)

**Late Northern Wei through early Tang dynasties, China** · c. 493–1127 C.E. (main phase 5th–8th c.) · Limestone with remnants of pigment · Luoyang, Henan province, China · ~1 km along the Yi River

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/95/Longmen_Caves_Buddhist.jpg/330px-Longmen_Caves_Buddhist.jpg" width="240" alt="Longmen Caves (Grottoes)">
### Summary

A massive cliffside complex of Buddhist cave-shrines carved under **imperial patronage**, culminating in the colossal Vairocana Buddha of the Tang-era Fengxian Temple.

- **Historical Context.** Begun in 493 C.E. after **Emperor Xiao Wen** of the Northern Wei moved his capital south to **Luoyang** and embraced Chinese/Buddhist culture. Carving continued across the Sui and into the early Tang, reaching its peak under **Empress Wu Zetian** in the late 7th century. Over 2,300 niches, 2,800 inscriptions, and ~100,000 figures were cut into the limestone cliffs flanking the Yi River. Indian and Central Asian Buddhist styles arrived via the Silk Road.
- **Function.** Devotional cave-temples for meditation and merit-making; sponsors (emperors, monks, lay donors) dedicated niches and images to honor deceased relatives or pray for the dynasty.
- **Content.** The **Fengxian Temple** features a ~55-ft-high seated **Vairocana Buddha** flanked by bodhisattvas, disciples, heavenly kings, and vajrapani guardians — said to resemble Empress Wu. The earlier **Central Binyang Cave**, sponsored by Emperor Xuan Wu in memory of his father, shows a more linear, elongated Wei style. Pentads, haloes, celestial flying figures, and carved lotus ceilings.
- **Form.** High-relief colossal figures in the Tang caves have full, fleshy bodies and serene smiles; Wei-period figures are flatter and more abstract, with drapery stylized into rhythmic patterns. Originally painted in brilliant blue, red, ochre, and gold — mostly lost to weathering.

**Vocabulary:** limestone · Buddhism · stupa · stele · relics · imperial patronage · Vairocana
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#182](#182--buddha-bamiyan) — Bamiyan Buddhas (Gandharan culture, Bamiyan, Afghanistan, c. 400–800 C.E., destroyed 2001)

**Theme:** Stone carved, Buddha figures, Religion, form

> The form of the Bamiyan Buddhas are very similar to the Longmen Caves in China. Both have standing statues and was one painted.

---

#### → [#184](#184--jowo-rinpoche-enshrined-in-the-jokhang-temple) — Jowo Rinpoche, enshrined in the Jokhang Temple (Yarlung Dynasty, Lhasa, Tibet, believed 641 C.E., with later additions)

**Theme:** Buddha Statue, Religion, form

> The Jowo Rinpoche is similar to the Buddhas at the Longmen Caves in terms of the commissioned due to the spread of the religion and the respect and recognition to the religion. And also both are similar in the form of the Buddha Statues.

---

#### → [#197](#197--todai-ji) — Todai-ji (Nara, Japan, 743 C.E., rebuilt 1700)

**Theme:** Religion, Form, Cultural Evolution

> The Great Buddha statue in the Todaiji is similar to the ones at Longmen Caves because both depicts the cultural evolution in the two cultures and the acceptance of the religion.

---

## #196 — Gold and Jade Crown

**Silla Kingdom (Korea)** · Second half of 5th–6th century C.E. · Gold with jade (gogok) · Excavated Hwangnam Daechong, Gyeongju; National Museum of Korea · 27.3 cm tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Gold_Jade_Crown_Chinese.jpg/250px-Gold_Jade_Crown_Chinese.jpg" width="240" alt="Gold and Jade Crown">
### Summary

A tree-and-antler gold crown from a **Silla royal tomb**, uniting shamanic cosmology with the metallurgical traditions of the Eurasian steppes.

- **Historical Context.** Made during Korea's **Three Kingdoms Period** (57 B.C.E.–668 C.E.), when the small southeastern Silla kingdom was expanding its diplomatic reach; Silla eventually unified the peninsula in 668 with Tang Chinese assistance. Silla's capital Seorabeol (modern Gyeongju) was also known as **Geumseong**, "city of gold." Unlike Baekje and Goguryeo, which looked primarily to China, Silla absorbed **nomadic Eurasian steppe** influence visible in its goldwork. Excavated from Hwangnam Daechong, the "Great Tomb of Hwangnam," a double royal burial.
- **Function.** Funerary regalia marking royal status in the afterlife; increasingly believed to have also served in **shamanic rituals** during life, before Buddhism became the state religion in the 6th century.
- **Content.** Three upright tree-branch uprights (axis mundi / sacred tree from Gyeongju legend), two side **antler** uprights (steppe reindeer imagery), hung with flat gold spangles and comma-shaped jade **gogok** (fertility "fruits"). Six long pendants dangle from the band.
- **Form.** Sheet gold cut and bent into upright elements pierced, hammered, and riveted together; gogok of jade threaded with gold wire. Strongly vertical and symmetrical.

**Vocabulary:** Silla Kingdom · Three Kingdoms Period · shamanism · axis mundi · gogok · daegwan
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#53](#53--merovingian-looped-fibulae) — Merovingian looped fibulae (Early medieval Europe, mid-6th century C.E.)

**Theme:** abstract, zoomorphic, afterlife/funerary, nobility, metalwork

> Both the Merovingian looped fibula and the gold and jade crown were used to denote the status of its wearer and were created using advanced metallurgy; they were also buried with their owners.

---

#### → [#158](#158--ruler's-feather-headdress-probably-of-motecuhzoma-ii) — Ruler's feather headdress, probably of Motecuhzoma II (Mexica/Aztec, 1428–1520 C.E.)

**Theme:** royalty, featherwork, religious/ceremonial

> Both the ruler's feather headdress and the gold and jade crown were headwear items made for royalty to wear during ceremonial rituals.

---

#### → [#170](#170--sika-dwa-kofi-golden-stool) — Sika dwa kofi (Golden Stool) (Ashanti, c. 1700 C.E.)

**Theme:** religious/ceremonial, royalty, metalwork

> Both the Sika dwa kofi and the gold and jade crown were made using metalwork for royalty. They both had religious significance to their respective cultures.
