## #197 — Todai-ji

**Japanese Buddhist, various architects and sculptors (Unkei and Kaikei for Nio guardians)** · 743 C.E.; rebuilt after 1180 C.E. (Kamakura) and again c. 1700 · Wood, bronze, plaster · Nara, Japan · Daibutsuden 187 ft long, 160 ft high

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Todai-ji_Buddha_statue.jpg/330px-Todai-ji_Buddha_statue.jpg" width="240" alt="Todai-ji">
### Summary

The **Great Eastern Temple** at Nara — the head temple of Japanese Kegon Buddhism and once the world's largest wooden building, housing Japan's largest bronze Buddha.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Emperor Shomu** in 743 C.E. during the Nara period as the central temple of a nationwide Buddhist network, tying the state to the newly imperial religion. Construction required nearly all the copper in Japan for the **Daibutsu** (Great Buddha), a monumental undertaking that strained the treasury. The complex was destroyed during the **Genpei War** (1180); the Kamakura-era reconstruction introduced a more robust, Chinese-influenced (daibutsuyō) style and the **Nandaimon** (Great South Gate) with its pair of guardian kings carved by **Unkei and Kaikei** (1203).
- **Function.** Main temple for state Buddhism; center for scholarship, sutra translation, and lay worship. The network of provincial kokubunji temples radiated from it.
- **Content.** The **Daibutsuden** houses a 49-ft bronze seated Vairocana Buddha (current statue 17th c.) flanked by Bodhisattvas. The **Nandaimon** frames the 26-ft-tall **Ungyō and Agyō** guardian kings (Nio), muscular wood sculptures representing birth and death. Also on site: Nigatsudo, Hokke-do, and the Shoso-in treasury preserving Silk Road objects.
- **Form.** Post-and-beam wooden architecture with deep, tiled hip-and-gable roofs; bracketing systems hold up wide eaves. The Daibutsuden appears two-storied externally but is a single space inside.

**Vocabulary:** Daibutsu · Daibutsuden · Nandaimon · Nio guardian kings
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#182](#182--buddha-bamiyan) — Bamiyan Buddhas (Gandharan culture, Bamiyan, Afghanistan, c. 400–800 C.E., destroyed 2001)

**Theme:** Cultural Syncretism, Buddhism

> The Bamiyan Buddhas were in an area where there was much cultural syncretism, including religion. This is similar to Japanese use of Chinese/Korean architectural techniques and religious influences

---

#### → [#199](#199--angkor-wat) — Angkor Wat (Khmer, Cambodia, 12th century)

**Theme:** Religious and Political power

> Angkor Wat was dedicated to the god Vishnu, but also stood as the king's Mausoleum. Todaiji also stood as the center of Buddhism in Japan but showed the power of the Japanese Emperor

---

#### → [#207](#207--ryoan-ji) — Ryoan-ji (Kyoto, Japan, c. 1480)

**Theme:** Buddhism

> Ryoanji was created much later than the original Buddhist temples made by Emperor Shomu. However, it is also a sort of temple complex representing a chance in Buddhist temple design in Japan, with rock gardens.

---

## #198 — Borobudur Temple

**Sailendra Dynasty, Mahayana Buddhist (architect: Gunadharma, attributed)** · c. 750–842 C.E. · Volcanic stone masonry · Central Java, Indonesia · ~26,910 ft² footprint; ~115 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Borobudur_Temple.jpg/330px-Borobudur_Temple.jpg" width="240" alt="Borobudur Temple">
### Summary

The world's largest Buddhist monument — a **stone mandala** mountain that pilgrims climb as a spiritual ascent toward enlightenment.

- **Historical Context.** Built under the **Sailendra** rulers of central Java during a period of intense cultural exchange between India and Southeast Asia, when **Mahayana Buddhism** reached Java alongside indigenous ancestral worship. Abandoned by the 14th century (possibly due to volcanic eruption or conversion to Islam) and buried under ash and jungle; rediscovered in 1814 under the British administration of Thomas Stamford Raffles.
- **Function.** A **pilgrimage monument** without interior space. Devotees climb and circumambulate the terraces clockwise, reading the relief panels as they ascend — a physical journey through the three realms of Buddhist cosmology toward nirvana.
- **Content.** Nine stacked platforms: six square terraces below, three circular terraces above, crowned by a central stupa. The three levels represent **Kamadhatu** (desire), **Rupadhatu** (form), and **Arupadhatu** (formlessness). Over **1,400 narrative reliefs** tell Jataka tales and sutras; **504 Buddha statues** sit within niches and in 72 perforated bell-shaped stupas on the upper circular terraces.
- **Form.** Stepped-pyramid mandala aligned to the cardinal directions; axis mundi rising toward the central stupa. Dense narrative relief on the lower walls transitions to serene abstraction above as the pilgrim approaches enlightenment.

**Vocabulary:** Mahayana Buddhism · axis mundi · circumambulate · stupa · mandala
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#199](#199--angkor-wat) — Angkor Wat (Khmer, Cambodia, 12th century)

**Theme:** Religious monument

> Detailed Narrative Relief Decorations on walls. Axis Mundi, buildings point upward

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (Buddhist, India, 3rd c. B.C.E.–1st c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Buddhist architecture, Buddhist symbolism

> Stupa is the central focus. Circumambulation performed at the site

---

#### → [#195](#195--longmen-caves-grottoes) — Longmen Caves (Northern Wei–Tang, China, c. 493–1127 C.E.)

**Theme:** Buddhist architecture, Cross-cultural influences

> Extensive, monumental building. Numerous stupas and Buddha sculptures

---

## #199 — Angkor Wat

**Khmer Empire (patron: Suryavarman II)** · c. 1113–1150 C.E. (main construction); site occupied c. 800–1400 C.E. · Stone masonry, sandstone · Angkor, Cambodia · ~500 acres; central tower ~200 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Angkor_Wat_Cambodia.jpg/330px-Angkor_Wat_Cambodia.jpg" width="240" alt="Angkor Wat">
### Summary

The largest religious monument in the world — a Hindu temple-mountain that doubled as the **mausoleum of King Suryavarman II**, later converted to Buddhist use.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Suryavarman II** (r. 1113–c. 1150), who seized the Khmer throne by killing his great-uncle and sought legitimacy through unprecedented building. Unlike most Khmer temples (usually dedicated to Shiva), Angkor Wat honored **Vishnu**, with whom the king identified. Employed tens of thousands of workers and was never fully finished. Converted to a Theravada Buddhist site in the 14th century after the Khmer Empire's decline.
- **Function.** State temple housing the essence of Vishnu (and by identification, the king); astronomical observatory aligned to solstice and equinox sunrises; probable funerary monument for Suryavarman II, whose ashes may have been interred here.
- **Content.** Concentric galleries rise toward a central quincunx of five towers modeled on **Mount Meru**, cosmic home of the gods, surrounded by a vast moat (the cosmic ocean). Over 2,000 apsaras (celestial dancers) and long narrative bas-relief galleries illustrate Hindu epics — including the famous **Churning of the Sea of Milk**, where gods and demons pull the serpent Vasuki to extract **amrita** from the ocean.
- **Form.** Sandstone construction on a laterite core, oriented west (unusual for Khmer temples — supporting the funerary reading). The stepped pyramid form mirrors Mount Meru; the layout functions as a **mandala**. Corbelled vaults; no true arches.

**Vocabulary:** mandala · amrita · axis mundi · Mount Meru
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#56](#56--great-mosque-of-córdoba) — Great Mosque of Córdoba (Umayyad, Spain, begun c. 785–786 C.E.)

**Theme:** religion

> Converted from a mosque to a Catholic cathedral similarly to how Angkor Wat was converted from a Hindu temple to a Buddhist temple

---

#### → [#12](#12--white-temple-and-its-ziggurat) — White Temple and its Ziggurat (Sumerian, Uruk, c. 3500–3000 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Cosmology

> Both aligned with astronomical elements, such as the cardinal points or the equinox/moon positions

---

#### → [#200](#200--lakshmana-temple) — Lakshmana Temple (Chandela Dynasty, Khajuraho, India, c. 930–950 C.E.)

**Theme:** religion

> Both dedicated to Hindu god Vishnu and are for the purpose of housing Vishnu's essence

---

## #200 — Lakshmana Temple

**Chandela Dynasty (patron: Yashovarman)** · 930–950 C.E. · Sandstone · Khajuraho, Madhya Pradesh, India · ~98 ft long and tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Lakshmana_Temple_Hindu.jpg/330px-Lakshmana_Temple_Hindu.jpg" width="240" alt="Lakshmana Temple">
### Summary

A Nagara-style **Hindu temple-mountain** dedicated to Vishnu, famed for its dense figural sculpture including the frank erotic reliefs of Khajuraho.

- **Historical Context.** Part of the **Khajuraho Group of Monuments**, built between 950–1050 C.E. by the **Chandela Dynasty** during a Hindu resurgence in north-central India. Yashovarman commissioned the Lakshmana Temple to house a sacred image of Vaikuntha Vishnu received from a Pratihara overlord, using the temple to proclaim his dynasty's legitimacy and power.
- **Function.** Housed the Vishnu image in the innermost sanctuary (**garbhagriha**) and served as a pilgrimage destination. Devotees circumambulated the temple and its enclosed sanctum.
- **Content.** Multiple sculpted bands wrap the exterior: deities, celestial nymphs (surasundaris), couples in ordinary and erotic embraces (mithunas), mythic animals. The central shrine holds a four-headed Vishnu. Four subsidiary shrines mark the corners of the raised platform.
- **Form.** Nagara-style "mountain-peak" **shikhara** (tower) rising in a cluster of smaller replica peaks (**urushringas**), symbolizing Mount Meru. Axially planned: entrance porch → mandapa (hall) → antarala → garbhagriha. Densely carved exterior; dark, austere sanctum interior.

**Vocabulary:** Hinduism · dharma · sanctum · mandapa · garbhagriha · shikhara · urushringa · tirtha · Vishnu · Brahma
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#199](#199--angkor-wat) — Angkor Wat (Khmer, Cambodia, 12th century)

**Theme:** Religion, Temple/shrine, Vertical stacked structures

> (originally) Hindu temple

---

#### → [#202](#202--shiva-as-lord-of-dance-nataraja) — Shiva as Lord of Dance (Chola Dynasty, India, c. 11th century C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion

> Hinduism

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (Buddhist, India, 3rd c. B.C.E.–1st c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, Temple/shrine

> Religious temple

## #201 — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams

**Fan Kuan (Northern Song Dynasty, China)** · c. 1000 C.E. · Ink and colors on silk, hanging scroll · National Palace Museum, Taipei · 206.3 × 103.3 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Fan_Kuan_Travelers.jpg/330px-Fan_Kuan_Travelers.jpg" width="240" alt="Travelers Among Mountains and Streams">
### Summary

The quintessential **Northern Song landscape** and a foundational work of Chinese ink painting.

- **Historical Context.** Painted around 1000 C.E. by **Fan Kuan**, a recluse of the Taoist and Neo-Confucian tradition who reportedly lived in the Zhongnan and Taihua mountains to study nature directly. Northern Song intellectuals believed that patient observation of the natural world revealed underlying **li** (cosmic principles) — Fan Kuan's signature, found only in the 20th century hidden among the foliage, confirmed his authorship.
- **Function.** A hanging scroll for private contemplation, inviting the viewer to wander mentally through the landscape — a substitute for actual travel among mountains and a way to align oneself with nature's **qi** (vital energy).
- **Content.** A towering central mountain dominates the upper two-thirds; a thread-like waterfall plunges from a cleft. Below, mist separates the looming peak from a rocky foreground where a tiny mule train of travelers and a temple roof emerge among trees — barely visible, emphasizing human insignificance within the cosmos.
- **Form.** Monumental composition stacks foreground, middle ground, and background without linear perspective. Fan Kuan's signature "raindrop texture strokes" (*yudian cun*) build up the massive rock face; ink wash and careful gradations create atmosphere and depth.

**Vocabulary:** Buddhism · Bodhisattva · Confucianism · Daoism · li · Literati · Neo-Confucianism · qi
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#203](#203--night-attack-on-the-sanjo-palace) — Night Attack on the Sanjo Palace (Kamakura Japan, late 13th century)

**Theme:** East Asian painting, ink on silk/paper

> Japanese emaki scroll painting was influenced by Chinese painting tradition, with shared techniques of ink, color, and scroll format. (connection inferred — Travelers' snapshot truncated; related works commonly paired with Fan Kuan in image-set connections)

---

#### → [#210](#210--red-and-white-plum-blossoms) — Red and White Plum Blossoms (Ogata Kōrin, Japan, early 18th century)

**Theme:** Nature, East Asian painting

> Both foreground nature and a harmonious human relationship with it; both stem from a continuing East Asian tradition of ink painting and observed natural subjects.

---

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, Japan, c. 1830–1833)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg/500px-Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg" width="180" alt="Under the Wave off Kanagawa">

**Theme:** Human-nature relationship

> Both works depict humans as small, indicating the individuals insignificance relative to nature.

## #202 — Shiva as Lord of Dance (Nataraja)

**Chola Dynasty, India (Tamil Nadu)** · c. 11th century C.E. · Cast bronze · Tamil Nadu, India · 68.3 × 56.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d5/Shiva_Nataraja_Bronze.jpg/250px-Shiva_Nataraja_Bronze.jpg" width="240" alt="Shiva as Lord of Dance (Nataraja)">
### Summary

The canonical Chola-period bronze **Nataraja**, the dancing Shiva whirling the universe through cycles of creation and destruction.

- **Historical Context.** Cast during the height of the **Chola Dynasty** (c. 850–1250 C.E.) in southern India, whose rulers were fervent Shaivites and used bronze icons as instruments of state religion. Tamil religious poetry of the *Nayanars* saints shaped the iconography. Made by the lost-wax process, these sculptures were produced in royal workshops for temple festivals.
- **Function.** A **processional icon** carried from the inner shrine through city streets during festivals, dressed in real garments and bathed in offerings. Represents the cosmic dance of *Ananda Tandava* that destroys the universe and regenerates it.
- **Content.** Shiva dances inside a ring of flames (*prabha-mandala*) standing on the dwarf demon Apasmara (ignorance). His four arms hold the drum of creation (*damaru*), the flame of destruction, and strike protective and teaching mudras. His matted hair whips outward, bearing the goddess Ganga and a crescent moon.
- **Form.** Cast in solid bronze via lost-wax method. Idealized, elongated, lithe body frozen mid-movement; every attribute and gesture is codified iconography. Intended to be viewed in the round.

**Vocabulary:** Shiva · Nataraja · mudra · naturalistic · individualistic
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#200](#200--lakshmana-temple) — Lakshmana Temple (Chandela Dynasty, Khajuraho, India, c. 930–950 C.E.)

**Theme:** Hinduism, Indian religious art

> Both are Hindu devotional objects from medieval India; both served in ritual practice centered on a primary deity.

---

#### → [#199](#199--angkor-wat) — Angkor Wat (Khmer, Cambodia, 12th century)

**Theme:** Hindu religion, iconography

> Both share Hindu subject matter; Angkor Wat's narrative reliefs depict the same cosmological cycles and deities that Nataraja embodies.

---

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (Buddhist, India, 3rd c. B.C.E.–1st c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Indian religious art

> Both are devotional objects of South Asian religion; each served processional or circumambulatory worship.

## #203 — Night Attack on the Sanjo Palace

**Unknown Japanese artist, Kamakura period** · Second half of the 13th century C.E. · Handscroll; ink and color on paper · Museum of Fine Arts, Boston · 18¹⁄₁₆ × 304¹⁵⁄₁₆ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Night_Attack_Sanjo_Palace.jpg/330px-Night_Attack_Sanjo_Palace.jpg" width="240" alt="Night Attack on the Sanjo Palace">
### Summary

A vivid **Kamakura-period emaki** (handscroll) depicting the 1159 Heiji Rebellion, a foundational event in the samurai ascent to power.

- **Historical Context.** Painted roughly a century after the event, during the **Kamakura period** when military rule had replaced aristocratic government. The scroll depicts the 1159 **Heiji Disturbance** in Kyoto, where Fujiwara Nobuyori and Minamoto Yoshitomo abducted the retired Emperor Go-Shirakawa and burned the Sanjo Palace. An exemplar of the Yamato-e style and otoko-e ("men's pictures") warrior subject, it became a prized possession of nobles by the 15th century.
- **Function.** Narrative entertainment and commemoration — read by unrolling right to left, telling a moving story of chaos, violence, and the triumph of the conspirators.
- **Content.** From right to left: Nobuyori forcing the retired emperor into an ox-cart; panicked women and bystanders fleeing the burning palace; warriors hacking and shooting Taira loyalists; finally, at far left, order restored as the warriors surround the imperial cart.
- **Form.** Vivid red flames and black smoke dominate the central section; figures are outlined in black and overlap chaotically. The long horizontal format allows the viewer to experience the event as continuous time. Parts of the scene are seen from a high, oblique viewpoint (*fukinuki yatai*, "blown-off roof").

**Vocabulary:** otoko-e · Kamakura · emaki
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** Stretched paintings depicting mountains

> Both artworks are of similar form: East Asian scrolls stretched vertically or horizontally

---

#### → [#210](#210--red-and-white-plum-blossoms) — Red and White Plum Blossoms (Ogata Kōrin, Japan, early 18th century)

**Theme:** Depicting nature and scenery

> Similarities in form, both Japanese styled painting although #210 is woodblock painting

---

#### → [#211](#211--under-the-wave-off-kanagawa-the-great-wave-from-*thirty-six-views-of-mount-fuji*) — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (Katsushika Hokusai, Japan, c. 1830–1833)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg/500px-Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg" width="180" alt="Under the Wave off Kanagawa">

**Theme:** Depicts the struggle between two sides, nature and men

> In both works, the viewer is a bystander viewing and in awe at a catastrophic event.

---

## #204 — The David Vases

**Yuan Dynasty, China (made in Jingdezhen)** · 1351 C.E. · White porcelain with cobalt-blue underglaze · British Museum, London · 63.8 cm tall, 19.6 cm diameter

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/The_David_Vases.jpg/330px-The_David_Vases.jpg" width="240" alt="The David Vases">
### Summary

A pair of dated **blue-and-white porcelain** altar vases — the benchmark for understanding 14th-century Yuan ceramic production and the global cobalt trade.

- **Historical Context.** Made in **Jingdezhen**, the imperial kiln city of south China, in 1351 during the Mongol **Yuan Dynasty**. Yuan-era blue-and-white depended on **cobalt imported from Iran** along Silk Road trade routes, a spectacular case of cross-cultural exchange. The vases bear a dated inscription naming donor **Zhang Wenjin** of Yushan, who commissioned them along with an incense burner as offerings to a Daoist temple dedicated to a deified general.
- **Function.** Altar vases for a **Daoist temple** — religious votive objects. Later collected by **Sir Percival David** (early 20th c.), whose collection became the foundation of Chinese ceramic connoisseurship in the West.
- **Content.** Tall *yuhuchunping*-derived form scaled up to altar size. Registers of ornament: stylized dragons chasing pearls, phoenixes, cranes among clouds, scrolling peonies and lotus, plantain leaves; elephant-head handles on the neck. A dated dedicatory inscription runs around the neck's upper side.
- **Form.** Hard white porcelain body covered with clear glaze; decoration painted in cobalt-blue pigment *under* the glaze, fired at high temperature. Shape echoes ancient bronze ritual vessel proportions.

**Vocabulary:** underglaze · porcelain · cobalt blue
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#166](#166--black-on-black-ceramic-vessel) — Black-on-black ceramic vessel (Maria Martínez and Julian Martínez, San Ildefonso Pueblo, mid-20th c.)

**Theme:** Used for trading and everyday purposes.

> Like The David Vases, the Black on Black ceramic vessel was traded among the Puebloan people and also has designs that were important and meaningful to their culture.

---

#### → [#33](#33--niobides-krater) — Niobid Krater (Niobid Painter, Classical Greek, c. 460–450 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Place to mix water and wine.

> They are both similar in that both include in a way complex compositions and both have exceptional character.

---

#### → [#5](#5--beaker-with-ibex-motifs) — Beaker with ibex motifs (Susa, Iran, c. 4200–3500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Found among funerary objects in a cemetery.

> Both pieces of art are similar in that they are both decorative pieces that depict animals and other things important to their societies.

## #205 — Portrait of Sin Sukju

**Korean (Joseon Dynasty)** · 15th century C.E. · Hanging scroll, ink and color on silk · Cheongwon, Korea · 167 × 109.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Sin_Sukju_portrait_Korean.jpg/250px-Sin_Sukju_portrait_Korean.jpg" width="240" alt="Portrait of Sin Sukju">
### Summary

A formal court portrait of a **Joseon meritorious subject**, honoring the scholar-statesman Sin Sukju for his loyalty to the king.

- **Historical Context.** **Sin Sukju** (1417–1475) was a Joseon scholar and politician who rose to the post of chief state councillor (prime minister). Portraits of meritorious subjects (*gongsin*) were painted as official rewards, preserved by descendants, and displayed in memorial halls. Joseon painters prized exacting physiognomic likeness — the face was thought to reveal inner character. Western modeling techniques entered Korean portraiture only in the 18th century via Jesuit intermediaries.
- **Function.** A state-sponsored **honor portrait** commemorating Sin Sukju's loyalty; it celebrated the sitter while reinforcing Confucian values of service and propriety.
- **Content.** Sin Sukju is shown three-quarter view, seated stiffly in a red chair, hands folded in his sleeves. He wears the green and dark blue robes of a civil official with an embroidered **rank badge** at the chest. His gaze meets the viewer; his features, including age lines, are rendered with unblinking precision.
- **Form.** Ink and mineral color on silk. Flat, decorative treatment of drapery contrasts with the finely modeled face. Angular folds of the robes; pure yellow background. No depth or atmospheric perspective.

**Vocabulary:** hanging scroll · hwaji · Joseon dynasty
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

#### → [#104](#104--george-washington) — George Washington (Jean-Antoine Houdon, 1788–1792, Marble)

**Theme:** historical

> This piece also expresses as much detail of the person without idealizing their features.

**Theme:** portraiture, historical

> This piece also depicts a historically important political figure and in also from an Asian country.

---

#### → [#118](#118--the-valley-of-mexico-from-the-hillside-of-santa-isabel-el-valle-de-méxico-desde-el-cerro-de-santa-isabel) — The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel (José María Velasco, 1882)

**Theme:** Landscape

> This piece also serves as a propaganda for the country of the artwork's origin.

## #206 — Forbidden City

**Chinese (Ming Dynasty, commissioned by the Yongle Emperor)** · 1406–1420 C.E. · Stone masonry, marble, brick, wood, ceramic tile · Beijing, China · 178 acres (~720,000 m²)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Forbidden_City_Beijing.jpg/330px-Forbidden_City_Beijing.jpg" width="240" alt="Forbidden City">
### Summary

The imperial palace city of **Ming and Qing China** — a vast axial complex aligned to cosmic order, forbidden to commoners for nearly 500 years.

- **Historical Context.** After overthrowing the Mongol Yuan dynasty, the Ming restored Han Chinese rule; the **Yongle Emperor** moved the capital from Nanjing to Beijing and built the Forbidden City (1406–1420). It housed 24 emperors through the Ming and Qing dynasties until 1912. Construction mobilized roughly a million laborers and consumed vast quantities of Phoebe zhennan nanmu wood and specially quarried "golden bricks."
- **Function.** **Administrative center** and imperial residence. The outer court held ceremonial halls for state ritual and reception; the inner court was the private domain of the emperor, his family, and eunuch staff.
- **Content.** Roughly 980 buildings arranged on a strict north–south axis with the Meridian Gate (south) and Hall of Supreme Harmony at its heart. Yellow-glazed roof tiles (reserved for the emperor) and marble balustrades dominate. Dragon iconography symbolizes imperial divinity; pairs of guardian lions flank thresholds.
- **Form.** Post-and-beam timber architecture on raised stone platforms; hierarchical scaling of courtyards and halls; strict bilateral symmetry. Largest structure is the Hall of Supreme Harmony, the tallest in Beijing at the time.

**Vocabulary:** Forbidden · Ming Dynasty · eunuch
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#30](#30--audience-hall-apadana-of-darius-and-xerxes) — Audience Hall (apadana) of Darius and Xerxes (Persian, Persepolis, c. 520–465 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Administrative

> Like the Forbidden City, the Audience Hall of Darius and Xerxes was an administrative center for a large empire. It was also newly created to accommodate for a growing empire.

---

#### → [#193](#193--terracotta-warriors-from-the-mausoleum-of-the-first-qin-emperor-of-china) — Terracotta Warriors (Qin Dynasty, c. 221–206 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary, commemorative

> Both of these art accomplishments emphasize the power of the emperor. Qin Shi Huang Di was the first to consolidate this power and used his prowess to build his Terracotta warriors. The Forbidden City is a similar display of China's power.

---

#### → [#209](#209--taj-mahal) — Taj Mahal (Mughal India, 1632–1653)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit3.jpg/500px-Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit3.jpg" width="180" alt="Taj Mahal">

**Theme:** Funerary, commemorative

> The Taj Mahal was built for the wife of Shah Jahan after she died. The grandeur and scale of this was greatly emphasized like the Forbidden City.

---

## #207 — Ryoan-ji

**Japanese (Muromachi period; patron: Hosokawa Katsumoto)** · c. 1480 C.E.; current garden design c. 18th c. · Rock garden, stone; wood architecture · Kyoto, Japan · Rock garden ~2,600 sq ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Ryoan-ji_garden_kyoto.jpg/330px-Ryoan-ji_garden_kyoto.jpg" width="240" alt="Ryoan-ji">
### Summary

The most famous **Zen dry-landscape garden** in Japan — fifteen stones set in raked gravel as a meditation on emptiness.

- **Historical Context.** Part of a large Rinzai **Zen Buddhist** temple complex in Kyoto, built by **Hosokawa Katsumoto**, a shogunal deputy, in 1450 and rebuilt after fire. Zen traveled from China to Japan in the 12th century and fused with native Shinto reverence for nature and Chinese monochrome ink-painting aesthetics. The dry-landscape (*karesansui*) garden's designer is unknown.
- **Function.** A **visual aid for meditation**. Practitioners sit on the veranda of the abbot's quarters (*hojo*) and contemplate the stones in silence — the garden encourages introspection and the realization of Zen's core concept of emptiness.
- **Content.** Fifteen carefully placed stones in five groups, partially wrapped in moss, set in a bed of raked white gravel. An earthen wall encloses the space. The stones have been interpreted as mountain peaks in mist, tigers crossing a river, or islands in a sea — their meaning is deliberately open. From any vantage point, only **14 of the 15 stones** are visible, implying enlightenment is still to come.
- **Form.** Austere minimalism: three materials (stone, gravel, moss), one color family, strict rectangular enclosure. The raked gravel is maintained daily. Low oil-boiled earth wall ages with patina.

**Vocabulary:** Zen Buddhism · wabi / sabi · hojo · karesansui
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#197](#197--todai-ji) — Todai-ji (Nara, Japan, 743 C.E., rebuilt c. 1700)

**Theme:** Buddhism, religious sites

> Both Todaiji and Roanji are sites for the practicing of Buddhism in Japan, but they may be contrasted in the monumental scale of Todaiji, while Ryoanji focuses on minimalism.

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** Nature, introspection

> Both works are meant to encourage introspection, especially through the contemplation of nature.

---

#### → [#65](#65--alhambra) — Alhambra (Nasrid Dynasty, Granada, Spain, 1354–1391 C.E.)

**Theme:** Gardens, architectural compounds

> Alhambra, like Ryoanji, features innovation in its use of gardens. However, the two pieces may be compared in the overall effect of their respective gardens. While Alhambra is meant to show political power, Ryoanji is much more spiritual.

---

## #208 — Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings

**Bichitr (Mughal India)** · c. 1620 C.E. · Watercolor, gold, and ink on paper · Freer Gallery of Art, Washington, D.C. · 18 × 25.3 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Jahangir_Mughal_painting.jpg/330px-Jahangir_Mughal_painting.jpg" width="240" alt="Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings">
### Summary

A **Mughal allegorical portrait** mixing Islamic, Hindu, and European iconography to proclaim Emperor Jahangir's spiritual superiority over worldly kings.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by the court artist **Bichitr** under **Jahangir** ("Seizer of the World"), fourth Mughal emperor (r. 1605–1627). Akbar, Jahangir's father, had consulted the Sufi saint Shaikh Salim Chishti for a son; Jahangir took his father's advisor's name. Jahangir was a major art patron, especially of portraiture and allegory. His court was deeply cosmopolitan, receiving European diplomats such as Sir Thomas Roe, ambassador of King James I.
- **Function.** **Imperial propaganda**: Jahangir publicly rejects earthly kings in favor of a Sufi holy man, projecting both piety and supreme authority.
- **Content.** Jahangir, enlarged and haloed by a double solar/lunar nimbus, sits on an hourglass throne and hands a book to the Sufi shaikh. Below him, descending in rank: an **Ottoman sultan**, **King James I of England** (copied from an English portrait), and the painter **Bichitr** himself holding his own self-portrait. Two putti hover with a broken bow and weep; a third, on the hourglass, writes a wish for a thousand-year reign.
- **Form.** Flat Persianate composition on a blue ground, with arabesque borders. European elements — putti, nimbus, modeled face of King James — are grafted onto the Mughal miniature idiom. Hierarchical scale and frontal portrait conventions.

**Vocabulary:** Jahangir · Putti · Sufi · Mughal Empire · Nur ud-Din
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#63](#63--arena-scrovegni-chapel-including-lamentation) — Arena (Scrovegni) Chapel, including Lamentation (Giotto, Padua, c. 1305)

**Theme:** Religion, Portrait

> Nimbus, Putti, Plain background, Multiple individuals, religious symbolism

---

#### → [#68](#68--the-arnolfini-portrait) — The Arnolfini Portrait (Jan van Eyck, 1434)

**Theme:** Portrait

> High attention to detail, high contrast, Facing audience, hand gestures

---

#### → [#55](#55--lindisfarne-gospels:-st.-matthew-cross-carpet-page;-st.-luke-portrait-page;-st.-luke-incipit-page) — Lindisfarne Gospels, St. Luke portrait page (Early medieval Hiberno-Saxon, c. 700 C.E.)

**Theme:** Religion, Scripture

> Nimbus, Portrait facing the audience, hand gestures, sitting, symbolism in the background to represent passing of time

---

## #209 — Taj Mahal

**Mughal India (patron: Shah Jahan; chief architect: Ustad Ahmad Lahauri)** · 1632–1653 C.E. · Stone masonry and marble with inlay of precious and semiprecious stones; gardens · Agra, Uttar Pradesh, India · Mausoleum ~240 ft tall on 1,902 × 1,002 ft complex

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit3.jpg/500px-Taj_Mahal%2C_Agra%2C_India_edit3.jpg" width="240" alt="Taj Mahal">

### Summary

A white-marble mausoleum built by **Shah Jahan for his wife Mumtaz Mahal** — widely regarded as the pinnacle of Mughal architecture and a "paradise on Earth."

- **Historical Context.** **Shah Jahan**, fifth Mughal emperor, commissioned the mausoleum after **Mumtaz Mahal** died in childbirth in 1631. Some 20,000 laborers and craftsmen worked for two decades; materials came from across the empire and beyond — white marble from Makrana (Rajasthan), jade from China, lapis from Afghanistan. Shah Jahan was later deposed by his son Aurangzeb and imprisoned at Agra Fort, where he could see the Taj across the river.
- **Function.** **Tomb** for Mumtaz Mahal (and, later, Shah Jahan himself — he was never able to build his rumored matching black tomb). A congregational mosque stands to the west of the mausoleum; a matching "answer" building mirrors it to the east.
- **Content.** Central tomb chamber holds the cenotaphs of Mumtaz and Shah Jahan; the actual sarcophagi are in a crypt below. Exterior bears pietra-dura floral inlays and Qur'anic inscriptions in black stone calligraphy. Set within a **charbagh** (four-part paradise garden) bisected by watercourses at the end of a long reflecting pool.
- **Form.** Central bulbous dome with four **minarets** anchoring the corners of the marble plinth. Perfectly symmetrical; the mausoleum is offset to the end of the garden rather than its center, so the river becomes part of the composition. Iwans (arched recesses) pierce each face.

**Vocabulary:** Mughal Empire · minaret · cenotaph · charbagh · pietra dura
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#167](#167--conical-tower-and-circular-wall-of-great-zimbabwe) — Conical tower and circular wall of Great Zimbabwe (Shona peoples, c. 1000–1400 C.E.)

**Theme:** Royalty, extravagant buildings

> Both are representations of royal money and power through extravagant construction

---

#### → [#29](#29--sarcophagus-of-the-spouses) — Sarcophagus of the Spouses (Etruscan, c. 520 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Love, afterlife

> A representation of care and love in the afterlife

---

#### → [#207](#207--ryoan-ji) — Ryoan-ji (Kyoto, Japan, c. 1480)

**Theme:** Religion, harmony

> A building founded on religious ideas with a focus on harmony

---

## #210 — Red and White Plum Blossoms

**Ogata Kōrin (Japan, Edo period)** · Early 18th century C.E. · Ink, color, and gold leaf on paper (pair of two-fold byōbu screens) · MOA Museum of Art, Atami, Japan · 156 × 172.2 cm each

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Red_White_Plum_Blossoms_Japanese.jpg/330px-Red_White_Plum_Blossoms_Japanese.jpg" width="240" alt="Red and White Plum Blossoms">
### Summary

A pair of gold-ground **Rinpa folding screens** by Ogata Kōrin — the canonical expression of Japanese decorative naturalism.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by **Ogata Kōrin** (1658–1716) during the peace and prosperity of the Edo period. Kōrin, born to a wealthy Kyoto textile family, inherited and systematized the decorative tradition of Hon'ami Kōetsu and Tawaraya Sōtatsu; the style is named **Rinpa** after his name. His works defined a visual language used later by his brother Kenzan, Sakai Hōitsu, and into the 20th century.
- **Function.** **Byōbu** (folding screens) were luxury furnishings in elite homes — dividing interior space, blocking drafts, and displaying wealth. Often brought out seasonally; plum blossoms signal late winter into early spring.
- **Content.** Two aged plum trees stand on the outer edges: red blossoms at right, white at left. Between them, a stylized black stream swirls with silver patterns of eddying water. Gold-leaf ground flattens space and abstracts the scene to its essentials.
- **Form.** Signature Rinpa techniques: **tarashikomi** (dripping fresh ink/color into still-wet pigment to create mottled textures in the tree trunks), **mokkotsu** ("boneless" painting without outlines), and the use of gold and silver leaf. Bold two-panel composition unified by the central stream.

**Vocabulary:** tarashikomi · rinpa · mokkotsu · byōbu · naturalism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#34](#34--doryphoros-spear-bearer) — Doryphoros (Spear Bearer) (Polykleitos, original c. 450–440 B.C.E.; Roman copy)

**Theme:** Natural World

> Compared to older Greek works, *Doryphoros (Spear Bearer)* (it's a Roman work) represents a more natural position of the body. Techniques such as contrapposto helped achieve such natural-looking positions. Like the *Doyphoros (Spear Bearer)*, *Red and White Plum Blossoms* also reaches a level of naturalism that is unrivaled by previous works of its culture. Though not perfect in representing nature, the *Red and White Plum Blossoms* displayed a considerably realistic depiction of flowers and a river, similarly to how *Doryphoros (Spear Bearer)* portrayed the ideal human body doing everyday activities.

---

#### → [#83](#83--hunters-in-the-snow) — Hunters in the Snow (Pieter Bruegel the Elder, 1565)

**Theme:** Natural World

> In both *Red and White Plum Blossoms* and *Hunters in the Snow*, a diagonal composition (to some extend for Ogata's work) draws the viewer's eyes to a certain point on the artwork. Both also use the color white in their works. *Hunters in the Snow*, however, depicts normal life (when put in context, it represents the dream lifestyle of the people during the time), and *Red and White Plum Blossom* represented connection with nature. Not all rinpa works depict nature; they also often presented daily lifestyle activities in the compositions.

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** Natural World

> Both of these works of art capture a scene of nature, symbolizing the bond humans have with nature and how we should respect it. *Red and White Plum Blossoms* differ in that it uses vibrant and contrasting colors in it. In *Travelers Among Mountains and Streams*, the work is very subdued, filled with detail, minimalistic, and lacking color. Rather than sticking to Chinese tradition, the Japanese took Chinese art and added on it to link it with their culture.

---

## #211 — Under the Wave off Kanagawa (The Great Wave), from *Thirty-six Views of Mount Fuji*

**Katsushika Hokusai (Japan, Edo period)** · 1830–1833 C.E. · Polychrome woodblock print; ink and color on paper · Japan · 10⅛ × 14¹⁵⁄₁₆ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a5/Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg/500px-Tsunami_by_hokusai_19th_century.jpg" width="240" alt="Under the Wave off Kanagawa">

### Summary

Japan's most iconic print — Hokusai's cresting wave over fishing boats, with Mount Fuji diminished in the distance.

- **Historical Context.** Made by **Katsushika Hokusai** (1760–1849) in his seventies as part of *Thirty-six Views of Mount Fuji*. Despite Edo-period isolationism, Dutch trade brought **Prussian blue** pigment and Western linear perspective to Japan; both are key to this print. After Perry forced Japan open in the 1850s, these prints flooded Europe and sparked **Japonisme**, influencing Van Gogh, Whistler, Monet, and Debussy.
- **Function.** An inexpensive, mass-produced **ukiyo-e** ("floating world") print sold as a domestic tourist souvenir — feeding popular interest in pilgrimages to sacred Mount Fuji.
- **Content.** A colossal wave with claw-like crest looms over three **oshiokuri-bune** fishing boats; in the distance, under the wave's trough, tiny snow-capped **Mount Fuji** sits low on the horizon. The composition juxtaposes nature's overwhelming force with the serene eternity of the mountain.
- **Form.** Woodblock print with separate blocks for each color, all outlined by a final black key block. Hokusai exploits the new synthetic **Prussian blue** for the deep sea and sky. Western-style low horizon and diminishing perspective; native flattening of space and bold outline. The wave's foam spray mirrors the snow on Fuji.

**Vocabulary:** Japanese woodblock · Japonisme · polychrome · Prussian blue · Ukiyo-e
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#111](#111--slave-ship-slavers-throwing-overboard-the-dead-and-dying-typhoon-coming-on) — Slave Ship (J.M.W. Turner, 1840)

**Theme:** Power of the Ocean

> Both place the ocean at the center of focus, choosing to emphasize the strength of the sea.

---

#### → [#109](#109--the-oxbow-view-from-mount-holyoke-northampton-massachusetts-after-a-thunderstorm) — The Oxbow (Thomas Cole, 1836)

**Theme:** Nature

> Both focus on nature and the natural world, not humans.

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** Human-nature relationship

> Both works depict humans as small, indicating the individuals insignificance relative to nature.

---

## #212 — Chairman Mao en Route to Anyuan

**Liu Chunhua (China)** · 1967 C.E. · Color lithograph based on an oil painting · Guangzhou, China (private collection) · 29.5 × 21.6 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Chairman_Mao_Chinese_propaganda.jpg/330px-Chairman_Mao_Chinese_propaganda.jpg" width="240" alt="Chairman Mao en Route to Anyuan">
### Summary

The most widely reproduced image of the **Cultural Revolution**, depicting a youthful Mao striding into history with messianic purpose.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in 1967 by **Liu Chunhua**, a student at the Central Academy of Arts and Crafts and a member of the **Red Guard**, for an exhibition commemorating the 1922 **Anyuan Miners' Strike**. The strike was the Communist Party's first major labor action; later Maoist historiography exaggerated Mao's role in it at the expense of Liu Shaoqi. At the height of the Cultural Revolution, the painting was reproduced an estimated **900 million times** in posters, pins, and stamps.
- **Function.** **Propaganda** — builds the Mao personality cult and legitimizes the Cultural Revolution by casting Mao's early career as heroic, predestined struggle.
- **Content.** Mao, depicted as a 28-year-old, strides down a mountain path in a traditional blue **cheongsam** gown (rather than a Western suit). His left fist is clenched in determination; his right hand holds a worn paper umbrella, symbol of humble persistence. Wind ruffles his hair and robes; dark storm clouds rise behind him.
- **Form.** Mao-era **Socialist Realism** with Chinese inflections. Atmospheric perspective places Mao against luminous light that haloes him as a sun-like figure; cool palette suggests pre-revolutionary dawn. Western oil-painting techniques adapted to Chinese political iconography.

**Vocabulary:** atmospheric perspective · propaganda · socialist realism · color lithograph
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#205](#205--portrait-of-sin-sukju) — Portrait of Sin Sukju (Korean, 15th century)

**Theme:** propaganda, rulers, portraiture

> though vastly differing in time period, both works depict the rulers of a country and tries to validate them for their causes (the embellished symbol on Sin Sukju's clothes and Mao's heroic stance)

---

#### → [#13](#13--palette-of-king-narmer) — Palette of King Narmer (Predynastic Egypt, c. 3000–2920 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** propaganda, rulers, commemoration

> drastically differing in style, both works commemorates the accomplishment of their respective rulers that support them in their own countries

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** landscape, contrast

> the two works masterfully used the technique of atmospheric perspective to emphasize opposite things: the insignificance of humans in *Travelers among Mountains and Streams* and the significance of an individual in *Chairman Mao en Route to Anyuan*

---

## #213 — Nan Madol

**Saudeleur Dynasty, Pohnpei (Micronesia)** · c. 700–1600 C.E. · Basalt boulders and prismatic columns · Pohnpei Island, Micronesia · ~0.29 sq mi; walls up to 25 ft tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Nan_Madol_Pohnpei.jpg/330px-Nan_Madol_Pohnpei.jpg" width="240" alt="Nan Madol">
### Summary

A city of nearly **100 stone-built islets** in a tidal lagoon — the ritual and political capital of the Saudeleur Dynasty.

- **Historical Context.** Built on and around Temwen Island by the **Saudeleur Dynasty**, which unified Pohnpei c. 1100 C.E. and ruled until c. 1628. The construction required quarrying and rafting prismatic basalt columns from the other side of the island without metal tools or wheeled transport. After the Saudeleur fall to the semi-mythical conqueror Isokelekel, the city gradually depopulated.
- **Function.** A combined **royal, religious, and mortuary complex**: elite residences, priestly quarters, food preparation islands, and tombs for Saudeleur rulers. Its watery channels and tidal causeways controlled access and reinforced hierarchy.
- **Content.** Nearly 100 artificial islets connected by canals; the central royal tomb complex, **Nandauwas**, has walls 25 ft high. Individual islets served specialized functions (meetinghouse, priests, anointing rites).
- **Form.** Massive log-cabin-style walls of naturally prismatic columnar basalt stacked in **header-and-stretcher courses**, filled with coral rubble, raising platforms above tidal waters.

**Vocabulary:** header courses · stretcher courses · megalith · nahnmwarki · Pohnpei
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#161](#161--city-of-machu-picchu) — Great Serpent Mound (Mississippian, Ohio, c. 1070 C.E.)

**Theme:** Sacred site, monumental earth/stone construction

> Both complexes are massive, culturally central ceremonial sites built without metal tools and set in a specific landscape; both carry ritual and funerary associations.

---

#### → [#8](#8--stonehenge) — Stonehenge (Neolithic, Wiltshire, UK, c. 2500–1600 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Megalithic construction

> Both sites are megalithic — Nan Madol's walls use massive basalt columns comparable in engineering feat to Stonehenge's sarsens.

---

#### → [#167](#167--conical-tower-and-circular-wall-of-great-zimbabwe) — Conical tower and circular wall of Great Zimbabwe (Shona peoples, c. 1000–1400 C.E.)

**Theme:** Royal/ritual capital, stacked masonry

> Both are dressed-stone royal complexes built without mortar by pre-colonial non-European societies to project the authority of a ruling dynasty.

---

## #214 — Moai on Platform (ahu)

**Rapa Nui people** · c. 1100–1600 C.E. · Volcanic tuff figures on basalt base · Rapa Nui (Easter Island) · Average ~7.94 × 3.15 × 1.54 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Moai_Easter_Island.jpg/330px-Moai_Easter_Island.jpg" width="240" alt="Moai on Platform (ahu)">
### Summary

Monumental ancestral portrait figures set on ceremonial **ahu platforms** — the defining art of Polynesian Rapa Nui.

- **Historical Context.** Carved by the Rapa Nui, Polynesians who settled the remote volcanic island perhaps c. 800–1200 C.E. Nearly 1,000 **moai** were produced, largely at the **Rano Raraku** quarry, and transported (probably "walked" upright with ropes) to coastal ahu. Production declined after c. 1600 with deforestation, clan warfare, and eventual European contact. Many moai were toppled during intraclan conflicts and, later, after Christianization; a number have been re-erected since the 20th century.
- **Function.** Ancestor effigies generating **mana** (spiritual power) for the community. Moai face inland with their backs to the sea, guarding and blessing the living descendants.
- **Content.** Over-large heads on blocky torsos with arms at the sides and hands resting on the belly. Elongated earlobes, heavy brows, pursed lips, prominent nose. Some bear red scoria **pukao** (topknots) and once had inset coral-and-obsidian eyes (inserted only during ritual "activation").
- **Form.** Carved from soft volcanic **tuff** using basalt picks. Austere geometric style with minimal detail. Set on rectangular ahu platforms of cut basalt blocks.

**Vocabulary:** moai · ahu · mana · tuff
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#9](#9--ambum-stone) — Ambum Stone (New Guinea, c. 1500 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Stone carving, Pacific, spiritual power

> Both are carved stone figures from the Pacific imbued with spiritual significance; both reduce natural form to abstracted essentials.

---

#### → [#161](#161--city-of-machu-picchu) — Great Serpent Mound (Mississippian, Ohio, c. 1070 C.E.)

**Theme:** Site-specific, monumental, ritual landscape

> Both are massive site-specific works set into a ritually meaningful landscape; both used the mobilized labor of an entire community.

---

#### → [#213](#213--nan-madol) — Nan Madol (Saudeleur Dynasty, Pohnpei, c. 700–1600 C.E.)

**Theme:** Pacific monumental construction

> Both are colossal pre-contact Pacific monuments built by societies that moved stone over great distances without metal tools.

---

## #215 — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather cape)

**Polynesia (Hawaii)** · before 1850 C.E. · Feathers and olonā fiber · National Maritime Museum · 64 × 15½ in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/Ahu_ula_Hawaiian_feather_cape.jpg/250px-Ahu_ula_Hawaiian_feather_cape.jpg" width="240" alt="'Ahu 'ula (Feather cape)">
### Summary

A feather cape of extraordinary rarity and labor — a **Hawaiian chiefly garment** embodying sacred mana.

- **Historical Context.** 'Ahu 'ula were made exclusively for the **ali'i** (chiefs) of the Hawaiian Islands. The cape came to symbolize the unification of Hawaii under **King Kamehameha** in the late 18th century; many were given as diplomatic gifts to visiting European captains, including Captain Cook, beginning a long dispersal into Western collections. Making a cape required roughly **500,000 feathers** from native forest birds (now largely extinct), each harvested laboriously.
- **Function.** A **rank garment** worn only by ali'i during ceremonies and battle; transmitted across generations to carry the wearer's lineage of mana (spiritual authority). Length and density signaled status.
- **Content.** Semicircular in later examples (trapezoidal earlier). Vivid red feathers of the 'i'iwi bird fill the ground; yellow (rare, harvested one feather at a time) and black feathers from the extinct 'ō'ō form geometric bands, crescents, and triangles.
- **Form.** Tiny feather bundles tied in overlapping rows onto a netted base of hand-twisted **olonā** bast fiber. Red was the royal color across Polynesia; yellow was scarce and doubly prestigious.

**Vocabulary:** tropical honeycreepers · cloak · scarcity · ali'i · mana · olonā
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#158](#158--ruler's-feather-headdress-probably-of-motecuhzoma-ii) — Ruler's feather headdress, probably of Motecuhzoma II (Mexica/Aztec, 1428–1520 C.E.)

**Theme:** Spiritual, commemorative

> Similarly to the 'ahu 'ula, the ruler's feather headdress is worn by royal members of society and links them to gods and allows for supernatural powers. Both pieces are also completely made of feathers.

---

#### → [#196](#196--gold-and-jade-crown) — Gold and jade crown (Silla Kingdom, Korea, 5th–6th century C.E.)

**Theme:** Funerary, commemorative

> The Gold and Jade Crown, like the 'ahu 'ula, was dedicated to the royal people and had materials that were of extreme importance. For instance the crown had gold and the cape had rare yellow feathers. The only difference is the crown was buried with the royals.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop (portrait figure) of King Mishe miShyaang maMbul (Kuba, c. 1760–1780 C.E.)

**Theme:** Commemorative

> Both the 'ahu 'ula and the Ndop represent rank. For instance, the Ndop is depicted with the symbol of the rulers rank and it was created to honor the spirit of the certain ruler. The 'ahu 'ula's length is what demonstrates the ranking and importance of those who wear it. Although they are not the same object wise they contain many similarities.

---

## #216 — Staff God

**Cook Islanders (Rarotonga, Polynesia)** · Late 18th to early 19th century C.E. · Wood carving wrapped in barkcloth · Cook Islands · 396 cm long (156 × 23.5 × 23.5 in)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Staff_God_Polynesian.jpg/250px-Staff_God_Polynesian.jpg" width="240" alt="Staff God">
### Summary

A wrapped wooden staff-god from **Rarotonga** combining male and female imagery — probably dedicated to the creator god **Tangaroa** and one of the last surviving examples of its kind.

- **Historical Context.** Made on Rarotonga (Cook Islands) before European missionaries from the **London Missionary Society** arrived in 1821. The missionaries denounced such figures as idolatrous and pressured converts to destroy or deface them; this is the only known complete wrapped staff-god to survive. Its phallus, formerly at the base, was removed, likely by missionaries. The figure later entered the British Museum.
- **Function.** Devotional object, probably tied to **fertility**, lineage, and domestic prosperity. Likely invoked the creator god Tangaroa or a specific ancestral deity.
- **Content.** A large carved head at one end; the shaft carries alternating small male figures in profile (carved in relief) and female figures incised into the surface, suggesting both sexes and the cycle of reproduction. Wrapped extensively in decorated barkcloth — a woman's craft.
- **Form.** Long cylindrical wood staff carved by men; wrapped in **tapa** barkcloth painted and beaten by women, reflecting complementary gender roles. Originally richly decorated at both ends; now missing the phallus.

**Vocabulary:** barkcloth · mana · Tangaroa · idolatry
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#173](#173--female-pwo-mask) — Female (Pwo) mask (Chokwe peoples, late 19th–early 20th century)

**Theme:** Relationship Between Male and Female Societal Roles

> Both pieces portray the manner in which men and women occupied separate spheres in their respective societies while also respecting one another for their unique skills and purposes.

---

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu mask (Sande Society, Mende peoples, 19th–20th century)

**Theme:** Veneration of Fertility

> Both pieces depict a deep reverence of human sexuality and fertility, although the Bundu Mask focuses more on the female condition while the Staff God includes both sexes.

---

#### → [#157](#157--templo-mayor-main-temple) — Templo Mayor (Mexica/Aztec, Tenochtitlan, 1375–1520 C.E.)

**Theme:** Changes to Devotional Works Post-European Contact

> Both pieces portray how religious icons/structures functioned in their original contexts and how they changed post-contact.

---

## #217 — Female Deity from Nukuoro

**Nukuoro, Caroline Islands, Micronesia (Polynesian Outlier)** · 18th–19th century C.E. · Wood · Nukuoro, Micronesia · 220 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Female_Deity_Nukuoro.jpg/250px-Female_Deity_Nukuoro.jpg" width="240" alt="Female Deity from Nukuoro">
### Summary

A serenely abstracted wood **tino aitu** figure from the Polynesian-outlier atoll of Nukuoro, believed to house ancestral and divine spirits.

- **Historical Context.** Made on **Nukuoro**, a remote atoll in the Caroline Islands whose population speaks a Polynesian language — a Polynesian outlier within Micronesia. Figures of this kind were carved for specific families and deities and placed in the main *amalau* (cult house) of the atoll. Central to the annual **Mataariki** festival, which marked the start of the harvest year. Very few examples survive; most were destroyed or removed after Christian conversion in the late 19th century.
- **Function.** A **tino aitu** — a body for a deity or ancestral spirit. During rituals, the spirit "entered" the figure; decorated with cloth, loom bands, and feathers for festivals; received offerings of food and prayers for agricultural success, health, and protection.
- **Content.** Idealized but anonymous standing female figure. Spiritual identification came from ritual dressing and naming, not carved detail.
- **Form.** Subtractive wood carving with sleek, polished surface. Minimal facial features (brow ridge, hint of eyes, small mouth), elongated torso, swelling hips tapering to bent legs. Pronounced abstraction and simplification — a geometry of human form.

**Vocabulary:** tino aitu · subtractive sculpture · deity
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop (portrait figure) of King Mishe miShyaang maMbul (Kuba, c. 1760–1780 C.E.)

**Theme:** African art

> religion, statue, figurine

---

## #218 — Buk Mask

**Torres Strait Islanders (Mabuiag Island, between Australia and Papua New Guinea)** · Mid to late 19th century C.E. · Turtle shell, raffia, feathers, and shell · Torres Strait · 54.6 × 63.5 × 57.8 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/61/Secret_Society_Mask_Torres_Strait.jpg/250px-Secret_Society_Mask_Torres_Strait.jpg" width="240" alt="Buk Mask">
### Summary

A rare **Torres Strait turtle-shell mask** surmounted by a frigate-bird totem, almost certainly used in dance ceremonies for death, fertility, or initiation.

- **Historical Context.** Made by Torres Strait Islanders in the mid–late 1800s using polished plates of **sea-turtle shell** lashed together — a technique unique in the world. By the 1870s, **London Missionary Society missionaries** arrived and prompted the destruction or surrender of traditional religious objects, making surviving masks few; most in museums today were collected by Europeans during that transitional period.
- **Function.** Worn in male-performed dances connected to **death, fertility, or initiation**, accompanied by drums, chants, and fire to invoke mythical ancestors.
- **Content.** A human face with shell eyes and a latticework "beard"; surmounted by a **frigate bird** totem with outstretched wings ornamented with disks that resemble ceremonial pendants. Raffia hair and feather plumes hang and move in the wind.
- **Form.** Plates of turtle shell heated, bent, and sewn together with vegetable fibers — a subtractive/additive technique. Dynamic, intended to be seen in motion. The composite human–bird imagery is typical of Torres Strait ceremonial art.

**Vocabulary:** Torres Strait · effigy · totem
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#175](#175--bundu-mask) — Bundu mask (Sande Society, Mende peoples, 19th–20th century)

**Theme:** Initiation ceremony, idealized

> Both the Bundu Mask and Buk Masks were masks worn in their respective societies for initiation ceremonies. Both masks also include the use of raffia as a material.

---

#### → [#222](#222--malagan-display-and-mask) — Malagan display and mask (New Ireland, Papua New Guinea, 20th century)

**Theme:** Funerary, ceremonial

> Like the Buk Mask, the Malagan Mask was used in ceremonies to commemorate the dead and also included a variety of materials, giving it multiple textures.

---

#### → [#178](#178--aka-elephant-mask) — Aka elephant mask (Bamileke, Cameroon, 19th–20th century)

**Theme:** Performance, Ceremonial

> The Aka Elephant Mask and the Buk Mask both feature animals that are significant to their society. They also both include multiple materials, including raffia, and were worn by dancers in a performance.

---

## #219 — Hiapo (Tapa)

**Polynesia (Niue)** · 1850–1900 C.E. · Tapa cloth (beaten and decorated mulberry bark) · Te Papa, New Zealand · 64.5 × 129 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Hiapo_tapa_Polynesian.jpg/330px-Hiapo_tapa_Polynesian.jpg" width="240" alt="Hiapo (Tapa)">
### Summary

A densely patterned **Niuean barkcloth** — a cross-Polynesian textile tradition made by collaborating women and saturated with mana.

- **Historical Context.** Tapa (*hiapo* in Niuean) is made throughout Polynesia, each island developing distinctive motifs. Production is a communal **women's art**: bark of the paper mulberry is stripped, soaked, and beaten with wooden mallets into thin sheets, then painted with vegetal dyes. Niuean hiapo of this period show European contact — the introduction of the botanical drawing aesthetic by missionaries led to fine-line, naturalistic leaves and labeled names being integrated into traditional geometric grids.
- **Function.** Ceremonial gift exchange at weddings, funerals, and diplomatic presentations; bedding, clothing, and wall hangings. Tapa carried **mana** and signaled wealth and prestige.
- **Content.** Dense geometric bands and grids of triangles, circles, spirals, and concentric shapes; occasional human figures, plants, or written names mark Niuean interaction with missionaries. Fine-line drawing and decreasing-scale motifs are distinctive to Niue.
- **Form.** Beaten mulberry bark (*uto*) layered and felted into a malleable sheet. Hand-painted with mineral and vegetable dyes. Very thin, flexible, warm.

**Vocabulary:** dye · tapa · Niue · mana · mulberry
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#130](#130--the-portuguese) — The Portuguese (Georges Braque, Analytic Cubism, 1911)

**Theme:** Abstraction

> Utilization of geometric shapes to express an emotion or to achieve a purpose

---

#### → [#223](#223--presentation-of-fijian-mats-and-tapa-cloths-to-queen-elizabeth-ii) — Presentation of Fijian Mats and Tapa Cloths to Queen Elizabeth II (Fiji, 1953/2013)

**Theme:** Similarity of Materials

> Both the art pieces illustrate the importance of tapa cloth in society. Each of the images show the presentation of tapa cloth to important figures. The tapa cloths are made in a similar method and the cultural aspects of each island have a lot in common.

---

#### → [#215](#215--'ahu-'ula-feather-cape) — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather cape) (Hawaii, before 1850 C.E.)

**Theme:** Precious Material

> Both are meant to be worn, symbolize Mana & radiating authority. The capes are created from rare, expensive materials put together in an arduous process.

---

## #220 — Tamati Waka Nene

**Gottfried Lindauer (Bohemian-born, working in New Zealand)** · 1890 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Auckland Art Gallery, Auckland, New Zealand · 101.1 × 82.4 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Tamati_Waka_Nene_Maori.jpg/250px-Tamati_Waka_Nene_Maori.jpg" width="240" alt="Tamati Waka Nene">
### Summary

A **posthumous European-style portrait** of the Ngāpuhi chief Tamati Waka Nene that fuses Māori identity markers with Victorian oil painting.

- **Historical Context.** **Gottfried Lindauer** (1839–1926), a Czech-born painter patronized by dealer Henry Partridge, produced scores of Māori portraits based on photographs and earlier likenesses. **Tamati Waka Nene** (c. 1780s–1871) was a Ngāpuhi rangatira who fought as a British ally in the 1845–46 Flagstaff War and helped broker the Treaty of Waitangi. He had converted to the Wesleyan faith in the 1830s and supported European trade. This portrait was painted from photographs nearly two decades after his death.
- **Function.** In Māori culture, portraits of ancestors act as vessels for the subject's **spirit and mana**, treated as living presences by descendants. Simultaneously, the painting served Pākehā (European New Zealander) collectors' taste for "ethnographic" portraits.
- **Content.** Nene is shown in three-quarter view holding a ceremonial **tewhatewha** staff hung with feathers and pressing a bone dagger to his lips. He wears a **kiwi feather cloak**, a large greenstone earring, and full facial **tā moko** tattoos — each a signifier of chiefly rank.
- **Form.** Conventional European oil technique: modeled volumes, **atmospheric perspective**, rich cool palette. Traditional Māori regalia is rendered with careful descriptive realism.

**Vocabulary:** portrait · tattooing (tā moko) · mana · atmospheric perspective · posthumous · rangatira
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#104](#104--george-washington) — George Washington (Jean-Antoine Houdon, 1788–1792)

**Theme:** Rulers

> George Washington by Jean-Antoine Houdon is a statue of George Washington. It depicts Washington in a classicized way, and emphasizes his rank and honor, just as Tamati Waka Nene's rank is emphasized.

---

#### → [#169](#169--wall-plaque-from-oba's-palace) — Wall Plaque, from Oba's Palace (Edo peoples, Benin, 16th century)

**Theme:** Rulers

> The Wall Plaque, from Oba's Palace is also a depiction of a ruler, however it is not solely focused on the oba (king) as there are soldiers and animals included in the plaque. This work also is an example of different cultural interactions-horses were introduced to Benin by the Portuguese.

---

#### → [#43](#43--augustus-of-prima-porta) — Augustus of Prima Porta (Imperial Roman, early 1st century C.E.)

**Theme:** Rulers

> Augustus of Primaporta is a statue of the leader Augustus, who exemplifies the ideals of beauty at the time: strength, military know-how, youth, fitness, and more.

## #221 — Navigation Chart

**Micronesian (Marshall Islands)** · Late 19th to early 20th century C.E. · Wood and fiber (coconut midrib, sennit, shells) · Marshall Islands · ~75.5 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a3/Marshall_Islands_navigation_chart.jpg/330px-Marshall_Islands_navigation_chart.jpg" width="240" alt="Navigation Chart">
### Summary

A Marshallese **stick chart** — a geometric map not of islands, but of the **ocean swells** that Micronesian navigators used to travel between atolls.

- **Historical Context.** Made by **Marshallese navigators** (*ri-meto*) as mnemonic and teaching aids for the centuries-old Micronesian open-ocean navigation tradition. Unique to the Marshall Islands; similar seaworthiness skills existed elsewhere in the Pacific but without these physical diagrams. Three chart types existed: *mattang* (abstract teaching diagrams), *meddo* (specific island groups), and *rebbelib* (larger sections of the archipelago). Charts were personal instruments — each navigator's chart was his own.
- **Function.** **Training and memory aid** for reading ocean swell patterns. A navigator memorized the chart on land, then sailed without it, lying in the canoe to feel how distinctive reflected, refracted, and crossing swells interacted with islands.
- **Content.** Curved sticks trace **swell patterns**; straight sticks mark dominant swell directions and wind currents; small **cowrie shells** mark the positions of **islets and atolls**. Emphasis is on forces and movement, not coastal geography.
- **Form.** Lashed coconut-leaf midribs and sennit fiber; loose, flexible construction. Flat, diagrammatic, non-pictorial — a form of information design.

**Vocabulary:** islet · swell · atoll · ri-meto · mattang · meddo · rebbelib
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#213](#213--nan-madol) — Nan Madol (Saudeleur Dynasty, Pohnpei, c. 700–1600 C.E.)

**Theme:** Micronesia, maritime culture

> Both are Micronesian works that reflect a maritime culture organized around the sea — Nan Madol as a sea-built city, the Navigation Chart as an instrument for crossing open ocean.

---

#### → [#214](#214--moai-on-platform-ahu) — Moai on Platform (Rapa Nui, c. 1100–1600 C.E.)

**Theme:** Pacific navigation, settlement

> Both artifacts are products of the long Pacific voyaging tradition that populated the most remote inhabited islands on earth without written language.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Black-on-black ceramic vessel (Maria Martínez and Julian Martínez, San Ildefonso Pueblo, mid-20th century)

**Theme:** Indigenous craft, geometry

> Both works are handcrafted using locally sourced materials and abstract geometric patterning that carries encoded cultural knowledge.

---

## #222 — Malagan display and mask

**People of northern New Ireland Province, Papua New Guinea** · 20th century C.E. · Wood, pigment, fiber, and shell · New Ireland · 28 × 16 × 18 in (mask)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Malanggan-Masken_Berlin-Dahlem.jpg/330px-Malanggan-Masken_Berlin-Dahlem.jpg" width="240" alt="Malagan display and mask">
### Summary

Ephemeral **mortuary sculptures** of northern New Ireland — carved to hold the souls of the dead during ritual and, in most cases, destroyed afterward.

- **Historical Context.** **Malangan** (or malagan) refers to a cycle of elaborate, months-long mortuary ceremonies practiced on New Ireland in the Bismarck Archipelago. Preparation begins after a funeral and can take months or years; families pool resources to commission sculptures and masks from specialist carvers and to host feasts and dances. Ceremonies affirm clan identity and the transfer of land rights as well as dismiss the dead.
- **Function.** Sculptures temporarily house the **souls of the deceased** before dispatching them to the afterworld; they are treated as spiritually dangerous. After the climactic ceremony most malangan sculptures are **burned or left to rot** — only masks and musical instruments are retained for future use. **Tatanua masks** are worn by male dancers honoring the dead.
- **Content.** Composite images of humans, fish, birds, snakes, and celestial motifs; every element evokes clan myth and genealogy. Designs belong to specific lineages; the clan headman retains the designs in memory.
- **Form.** Carved wood painted in ritually ordered **black, white, yellow, and red** pigments; decorated with fiber, shell, and lime. Asymmetrical paint on left and right sides of the mask so the dancer's turn reveals a new face.

**Vocabulary:** Malangan · Tatanua dance · Tatanua masks
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#218](#218--buk-mask) — Buk Mask (Torres Strait, mid–late 19th century)

**Theme:** Supernatural beliefs, use in ceremonies

> Both the Buk mask and Malagan masks are purposed for specific ceremonies, especially funerary ones. Both are celebratory and have positive connotations of the supernatural.

---

#### → [#171](#171--ndop-portrait-figure-of-king-mishe-mishyaang-mambul) — Ndop (portrait figure) of King Mishe miShyaang maMbul (Kuba, c. 1760–1780 C.E.)

**Theme:** Spiritual/religious purpose, commemorative. Idealized representation, not from direct observation.

> The ndop figure and malagan sculptures both are stylized representations of an individual used for spiritual beliefs. Both are associated with a certain individual's spirit and are believed to hold the soul of the person.

---

#### → [#164](#164--transformation-mask) — Transformation mask (Kwakwaka'wakw, Northwest Coast, late 19th century)

**Theme:** Decorative items used in ceremonies. Portrays the religious traditions of the culture.

> The transformation masks and malagan masks were both used in ritual dances and ceremonies. Both masks were carved from wood and painted with unique designs. They represent the traditions and culture of the people.

---

## #224 — The Gates

**Christo and Jeanne-Claude** · 1979–2005 C.E. (installed February 12–27, 2005) · Mixed-media installation: vinyl frames, steel footings, saffron fabric · Central Park, New York City · 7,503 gates, each 16 ft high, spanning 23 miles of walkways

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/The_Gates_Christo.jpg/330px-The_Gates_Christo.jpg" width="240" alt="The Gates">
### Summary

A brief, saffron-colored invasion of **Central Park** — a **$21-million temporary public artwork** that reframed an urban landscape for sixteen days.

- **Historical Context.** Proposed in 1979 by the artist duo **Christo and Jeanne-Claude**, the project was rejected multiple times by New York City over concerns about damage to the park. It was finally approved under Mayor Bloomberg and installed from **February 12 to 27, 2005**. Christo and Jeanne-Claude **self-funded the entire $21 million cost** through sales of preparatory drawings, refusing sponsorships. About four million visitors came during the installation.
- **Function.** Public installation art reimagining the **designed landscape** of Central Park (by Frederick Law Olmsted and Calvert Vaux, 1858) and making viewers aware of the walkways, of the park as construct, and of art as ephemeral experience.
- **Content.** 7,503 rectangular **gates**, each 16 ft high and of varying widths (5½–18 ft) matching their path, threaded along 23 miles of walkways. From each hung a panel of saffron nylon fabric that fluttered in the winter wind.
- **Form.** Repetition and seriality — identical frames in rhythm. Brilliant saffron color against leafless winter trees and snow-covered ground. After deinstallation, materials were recycled.

**Vocabulary:** Earthworks · site-specific
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#148](#148--narcissus-garden) — Narcissus Garden (Yayoi Kusama, 1966)

**Theme:** Meaning

> Both works are outdoor installations that directly engage the viewer, forcing viewers to confront beliefs surrounding the nature of art.

---

#### → [#151](#151--spiral-jetty) — Spiral Jetty (Robert Smithson, 1970)

**Theme:** Earthworks

> Spiral Jetty and The Gates are both earthworks, using the existing landscape to create art forms that are site-specific. Both are not entirely permanent in their forms; The Gates billowed in the wind and were taken down after a 15-day exhibition period, while Spiral Jetty emerges during times of drought and disappears during periods of high precipitation levels.

---

#### → [#225](#225--vietnam-veterans-memorial) — Shibboleth → actually #248 Shibboleth (Doris Salcedo, Tate Modern, 2007–2008)

**Theme:** Creation

> Both works alter preexisting public locations and change the viewers' experience of the space as well as traditional notions of art.

---

## #225 — Vietnam Veterans Memorial

**Maya Lin (USA)** · 1982 C.E. · Black polished granite · Washington, D.C. · Two walls each 246′9″ long, max 10.1 ft high

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Vietnam_Veterans_Memorial.jpg/330px-Vietnam_Veterans_Memorial.jpg" width="240" alt="Vietnam Veterans Memorial">
### Summary

A V-shaped black granite **cut into the earth** inscribed with the names of over 58,000 American dead — a deliberately austere counter-monument by a 21-year-old undergraduate.

- **Historical Context.** Designed by **Maya Lin** as a 21-year-old Yale undergraduate, selected anonymously from 1,421 entries in a national competition. Deeply controversial on completion — critics, including some veterans, called it a "black gash of shame" for breaking with heroic monument traditions. A figurative sculpture (*Three Soldiers*, Frederick Hart, 1984) and a Women's Memorial were added nearby as compromises. The Wall rapidly became one of the most visited memorials in the U.S.
- **Function.** A commemorative monument listing the **58,000+ Americans killed or missing** in Vietnam (1959–1975), chronologically rather than alphabetically, allowing each name to reflect its moment. Visitors touch names, leave offerings, and see themselves reflected in the polished stone.
- **Content.** No figurative imagery. The walls meet at a 125° angle, one arm pointing to the Washington Monument, the other to the Lincoln Memorial — literally embedding the loss within the symbolic axis of the nation.
- **Form.** Minimalist: two tapering wings of polished **black granite** descend into a cut in the earth. Reflective surface doubles the viewer into the monument. Names engraved in Optima typeface.

**Vocabulary:** Minimalism · medium · architectural style
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#151](#151--spiral-jetty) — Spiral Jetty (Robert Smithson, 1970)

**Theme:** Earthworks, minimalism, site-specific

> Both are minimalist American works of the 1970s–1980s that integrate into the landscape rather than impose over it; both invite bodily, meditative experience.

---

#### → [#123](#123--where-do-we-come-from?-what-are-we?-where-are-we-going?) — The Burghers of Calais (Auguste Rodin, 1884–1895)

**Theme:** Memorial sculpture, anti-heroic

> Both memorials deliberately reject triumphal convention — Rodin's hollow-eyed burghers and Lin's chronological list humanize sacrifice rather than glorify it.
