## #226 — Horn Players

**Jean-Michel Basquiat (USA)** · 1983 C.E. · Acrylic and oil paintstick on canvas panels (triptych) · The Broad, Los Angeles · 96 × 75 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Horn_Players_Romare_Bearden.jpg/330px-Horn_Players_Romare_Bearden.jpg" width="240" alt="Horn Players">
### Summary

A **Neo-Expressionist triptych** honoring bebop pioneers Charlie Parker and Dizzy Gillespie — and a model of Basquiat's graffiti-infused painting practice.

- **Historical Context.** **Jean-Michel Basquiat** (1960–1988), born in Brooklyn to Puerto Rican and Haitian parents, rose from late-1970s New York street art — tagging sardonic poetry as "**SAMO©**" — to gallery stardom in the early 1980s. He was painted into Andy Warhol's Factory circle. A self-taught painter, Basquiat fused Abstract Expressionism's gestural energy with graffiti, Black cultural history, and medical/anatomical imagery. Died at 27 of an overdose.
- **Function.** An **homage** to Black musicians whose music shaped Basquiat; also a manifesto for a distinctly Black avant-garde, uniting "high" art with street idiom.
- **Content.** Left panel depicts **Charlie Parker** ("Bird") with saxophone; right panel, **Dizzy Gillespie** with his signature bent trumpet. Scrawled text scatters across all three panels: "**ORNITHOLOGY**" (Parker's famous tune and his nickname), **"DIZZY GILLESPIE,"** "Chan" and "Pree" (Parker's wife and daughter), the abstract word "SOAP"; anatomical heads. Words crossed out to make them "more visible."
- **Form.** Bold black ground; figures rendered in distinct blocks of unmodeled color (especially Parker's face). Oil-paintstick and acrylic. Improvisational, layered, graffiti-like — mirroring bebop's velocity and freedom.

**Vocabulary:** Abstract Expressionism · Neo-Expressionism · bebop · triptych
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#76](#76--school-of-athens) — School of Athens (Raphael, 1509–1511)

**Theme:** Commemoration of Important Figures

> Both pieces were intended as a way to show admiration towards highly influential artists/thinkers.

---

#### → [#132](#132--improvisation-28-second-version) — Improvisation 28 (Wassily Kandinsky, 1912)

**Theme:** Heavy Focus on Improvisation, plus the Mirroring of Music in the Artwork.

> Both pieces were created via improvisation, with little attention paid to the deeper meaning of what's being painted. In both cases, this is done purposefully and is intended at least partially to reflect a musical composition. In Kandinsky's case, his paintings were intended to reflect the sounds of a symphony, while in the case of Horn Players, it's likely that Basquiat intended for the art to mirror Bebop music/Free Jazz, which is intentionally unstructured and reliant on improvisation.

---

## #227 — Summer Trees

**Song Su-nam (South Korea)** · 1979 C.E. · Ink on paper · Korea · ~2 ft 1⅝ in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Frankenthaler_Summer_Trees.jpg/330px-Frankenthaler_Summer_Trees.jpg" width="240" alt="Summer Trees">
### Summary

A **contemporary Korean ink painting** that revives traditional East Asian brushwork as an assertion of cultural identity against 20th-century Westernization.

- **Historical Context.** Painted by **Song Su-nam** (1938–2013) during the height of South Korea's industrialization and political tension between globalized Western art and homegrown traditions. Song led the **Sumukwha** (Oriental Ink) movement in 1980s Korea, advocating for a revival of East Asian ink painting informed by modern abstraction and Zen-influenced spirituality.
- **Function.** **Aesthetic and nationalist**: to reassert the expressive power of the ink brush as an uniquely East Asian medium at a moment when Korean artists were expected to adopt Western idioms.
- **Content.** Vertical bands of varying ink density suggest a stand of trees in summer — without depicting specific trunks, branches, or leaves. A minimalist landscape in the tradition of *literati* painting, pared to essentials.
- **Form.** Grayscale tonal variation produced by layering wet ink on wet paper (bleeding, feathery edges) and wet ink on dry paper (crisp, spotted brush marks). Velvety blacks descend into silvery grays; no color; vertical format.

**Vocabulary:** abstraction · contemporary · Sumukwha / Oriental Ink Movement
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#210](#210--red-and-white-plum-blossoms) — Red and White Plum Blossoms (Ogata Kōrin, Japan, early 18th century)

**Theme:** Nature, Trees

> Both depict nature and are ink paintings on paper

---

#### → [#234](#234--earth's-creation-i) — Earth's Creation (Emily Kame Kngwarreye, 1994)

**Theme:** Abstraction

> Both are abstract and stylized depictions of nature and also contemporary

---

#### → [#201](#201--travelers-among-mountains-and-streams) — Travelers Among Mountains and Streams (Fan Kuan, Northern Song China, c. 1000 C.E.)

**Theme:** Nature, Trees

> Both are black and white depictions of nature

## #228 — Androgyne III

**Magdalena Abakanowicz (Poland)** · 1985 C.E. · Burlap, resin, wood, nails, and string · Metropolitan Museum of Art, New York · 50 × 60 × 20 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1f/Androgyne_III_Kiki_Smith.jpg/250px-Androgyne_III_Kiki_Smith.jpg" width="240" alt="Androgyne III">
### Summary

A **hollowed, headless body** on wooden stretcher-poles — Abakanowicz's meditation on dehumanization under war and totalitarianism.

- **Historical Context.** **Magdalena Abakanowicz** (1930–2017) grew up in an aristocratic Polish family forced into hiding during World War II and later survived Soviet-imposed Socialist Realism in communist Poland. Her direct experience of war, communist repression, and the anonymous crowd drove a career-long meditation on the fragility of human identity. She is the namesake of the **Abakan** — her monumental, semi-abstract woven fiber sculptures.
- **Function.** No practical use; a **witness-object**. Abakanowicz often made such figures in large series (**Backs**, **Seated Figures**) so the accumulation of headless, limbless bodies would impress the viewer with "the countless" victims of 20th-century violence.
- **Content.** A seated, life-sized human torso — genderless (hence "**androgyne**"), hollow, missing head, arms, and legs, placed on a wooden stretcher whose poles substitute for the absent legs. The burlap surface reads as wrinkled skin or scarred flesh.
- **Form.** Built by stiffening **burlap sackcloth** with **resin** around a wooden frame. The back appears solid; the front reveals the figure as a shell. Highly textured — reminiscent of bones, veins, and muscle. Sculpture in the round.

**Vocabulary:** androgynous · social realism · Abakans
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#244](#244--the-swing-after-fragonard) — The Swing (after Fragonard) (Yinka Shonibare, 2001)

**Theme:** War, featureless figures

> Both The Swing (After Fragonard) and Androgyne III reference themes of war. The Swing depicts a beheaded woman (referencing the French Revolution), while the limblessness of Androgyne III references WWII.

---

#### → [#179](#179--reliquary-figure-byeri) — Reliquary figure (byeri) (Fang peoples, 19th–20th century)

**Theme:** Human figure

> Both works are depictions of human figures, with emphasis on death and spirituality.

---

#### → [#41](#41--seated-boxer) — Seated Boxer (Hellenistic Greek, c. 100 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Human figure, emotion

> Both works have a poignant sense of loss and suffering. The Seated Boxer looks to be in contemplation, and Androgyne III, in a similar pose, expresses patience as well as hollowness.

---

## #229 — A Book from the Sky (Tianshu)

**Xu Bing (China)** · c. 1987–1991 C.E. · Hand-printed books and wall/ceiling scrolls printed from hand-carved wood letterpress type; ink on paper · Installation, Metropolitan Museum of Art · Books 18⅛ × 20 in each; ceiling scrolls 38 in × ~115 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5a/Book_from_Sky_Xu_Bing.jpg/330px-Book_from_Sky_Xu_Bing.jpg" width="240" alt="A Book from the Sky (Tianshu)">
### Summary

An immersive library of **invented, unreadable Chinese characters** — a post-Cultural-Revolution meditation on language, meaning, and cultural rupture.

- **Historical Context.** **Xu Bing** (b. 1955) grew up in Beijing during the **Cultural Revolution**, a period when language was politicized — characters were simplified and countless books destroyed. Both his parents were scholars; he was exposed to classical texts forbidden to most. After the Revolution's end, Xu hand-carved **over 4,000 invented, meaningless Chinese characters** using the standard Kangxi radicals, and printed them with traditional wood letterpress type into bound books and giant scrolls. First exhibited in Beijing in 1988; it shocked Chinese intellectuals.
- **Function.** **Conceptual critique**. By making characters look authoritative yet deliver no meaning, Xu confronts viewers with the fragility of language and the experience of Chinese readers cut off from their textual heritage by the Revolution's reforms. Western viewers who can't read Chinese experience exclusion either way.
- **Content.** Hand-bound books in the traditional **thread-binding** style, enormous wall scrolls, and suspended ceiling scrolls, all covered in carefully set rows of nonsense characters.
- **Form.** Strict, authoritative black-on-white columns modeled on classical print culture. Gives the illusion of sacred scripture; the scale dwarfs the viewer. Impeccable traditional craft at the service of meaninglessness.

**Vocabulary:** wood letterpress · radical · Kangxi radicals
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#250](#250--kui-hua-zi-sunflower-seeds) — Sunflower Seeds (Ai Weiwei, 2010)

**Theme:** post Cultural Revolution China

> Ai Wei Wei and Xu Bing influenced each other. They shared identity and artistic vision.

---

#### → [#212](#212--chairman-mao-en-route-to-anyuan) — Chairman Mao en Route to Anyuan (Liu Chunhua, 1967)

**Theme:** The Cultural Revolution in China

> This piece's imagery propaganda starkly contrasts with Xu Bing's emphasis on literary history

---

#### → [#203](#203--night-attack-on-the-sanjo-palace) — Night Attack on the Sanjo Palace (Kamakura Japan, late 13th century)

**Theme:** Chinese calligraphy

> Both pieces include Chinese calligraphy. 203 is brush painted, while 229 is printed.

---

## #230 — Pink Panther

**Jeff Koons (USA)** · 1988 C.E. · Glazed porcelain · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 41 × 20½ × 19 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Pink_Panther_Koons.jpg/330px-Pink_Panther_Koons.jpg" width="240" alt="Pink Panther">
### Summary

A glossy porcelain **kitsch icon** pairing a blonde Hollywood starlet with a cartoon character — part of Koons's provocative **Banality** series.

- **Historical Context.** Made by **Jeff Koons** (b. 1955) for his **Banality** series (1988), which elevated mass-culture ephemera to fine-art status using traditionally high-art craft. Porcelain — once an imperial luxury — was now a mass commodity, a contradiction Koons exploited. The series drew fierce debate about commodification, taste, and originality (Koons was later sued for copyright infringement over another Banality piece). The **Pink Panther**, a cartoon character from the 1960s Blake Edwards films, is wrapped around the figure of film star **Jayne Mansfield**, whose trademark color was pink.
- **Function.** **Critique (or embrace) of kitsch** — a deliberately provocative collapse of adult sexuality and childhood cartoons, of high and low culture. Also a marketable luxury object for the 1980s art market.
- **Content.** A partially nude, idealized female figure (Mansfield-like) cradles the pink-furred Pink Panther; her head tilts back, her eyes closed, her red-lipped smile broad. The cartoon's weariness contrasts with her ecstatic passivity.
- **Form.** Glazed porcelain produced in an edition of three plus one artist's proof in a German workshop. Saccharine pastel palette (pink, mint, lemon). Highly polished surfaces; deliberately commercial sheen.

**Vocabulary:** contemporary art · kitsch · porcelain · Jayne Mansfield · Pink Panther · consumerism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 1.4

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#147](#147--marilyn-diptych) — Marilyn Diptych (Andy Warhol, 1962)

**Theme:** popular culture, depiction of Hollywood icon

> Both the Marilyn Diptych and the Pink Panther depict Hollywood icons, Marilyn Monroe and Jayne Mansfield. Both of these actresses were famous around the same time and were often compared to each other. The two artworks highlight the well-known features of the two celebrities.

---

#### → [#204](#204--the-david-vases) — The David Vases (Yuan Dynasty, 1351)

**Theme:** use of porcelain

> Both the David Vases and the Pink Panther were created out of porcelain, with porcelain holding symbolism for both artworks. The use of porcelain in the Pink Panther shows the continuation of the use of a historical material.

---

#### → [#150](#150--lipstick-ascending-on-caterpillar-tracks) — Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks (Claes Oldenburg, 1969–1974)

**Theme:** sculpture, desire, sensuality, combination of male and female forms

> The Lipstick (Ascending) on Caterpillar Tracks and the Pink Panther both contain the ideas of desire and sensuality with the depiction of lipstick and the depiction of Jayne Mansfield, respectively. Both sculptures were thought to have been made with cheap materials.

---

## #231 — Untitled #228, from the History Portraits series

**Cindy Sherman (USA)** · 1990 C.E. · Chromogenic color photograph · Museum of Modern Art, New York · 6′10″ tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Kehinde_Wiley_portrait.jpg/330px-Kehinde_Wiley_portrait.jpg" width="240" alt="Untitled #228, from the History Portraits series">
### Summary

Cindy Sherman **dressed as the Old Testament heroine Judith**, photographed as a monumental, ironic restaging of the Renaissance and Baroque Judith-and-Holofernes tradition.

- **Historical Context.** Made during **Sherman's History Portraits series (1988–1990)**, painted in Rome and photographed after returning to New York. Sherman (b. 1954) had already established herself with **Untitled Film Stills** (late 1970s), all staged self-portraits. In the History Portraits she restages Old Master paintings — not to flatter them but to expose the artifice and gender coding of "great art." Aligned with feminist art theory of the 1970s–90s that interrogated the "male gaze."
- **Function.** A **feminist critique** of both the canon of Western painting (where women are either saints, seductresses, or victims) and the tropes of heroism. It challenges viewers to see the mask underneath supposedly naturalistic portrayals.
- **Content.** Sherman poses as **Judith** standing over the severed head of **Holofernes** — a story from the Book of Judith retold countless times by Caravaggio, Artemisia Gentileschi, and others. Sherman's Judith wears red silk, has grotesquely oversized hands and feet, a sickly pallor, and a cold, masculine stare. Holofernes' head is comically fake.
- **Form.** Large-scale color photograph shot to mimic Old Master lighting and composition, yet obviously artificial: visible prosthetic body parts, green-tinged theatrical lighting, cheap fabrics. Photography performing as painting.

**Vocabulary:** contemporary · photography · Old Testament · Judith · Holofernes · Feminist Movement
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#99](#99--portrait-of-sor-juana-inés-de-la-cruz) — Portrait of Sor Juana Inés de la Cruz (Miguel Cabrera, c. 1750)

**Theme:** Women's roles, Religion, Portrait

> Both are portraits of women that challenge traditional women's roles by portraying them in an unusual way (woman posed as a scholar, woman hero placed in negative light).

---

#### → [#105](#105--self-portrait) — Self-Portrait (Elisabeth Louise Vigée Le Brun, 1790)

**Theme:** Women's roles, Portrait

> Both are portraits featuring women in the context of their occupations (LeBrue painting, Sor Juana at a desk with quills), which was not conventional of women at their times.

---

#### → [#107](#107--la-grande-odalisque) — La Grande Odalisque (Jean-Auguste-Dominique Ingres, 1814)

**Theme:** Women, Sexuality

> Both portray women in a rather sickly, gloomy light, and both women have dark circles around their eyes. Both also use drapes in the backgrounds.

---

## #232 — Dancing at the Louvre, from The French Collection (Part I, #1)

**Faith Ringgold (USA)** · 1991 C.E. · Acrylic on canvas, tie-dyed, pieced fabric border · New York · 73.5 × 80 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a1/Shahzia_Sikander_Louvre.jpg/330px-Shahzia_Sikander_Louvre.jpg" width="240" alt="Dancing at the Louvre, from The French Collection (Part I, #1)">
### Summary

A **story-quilt** reimagining art history from a Black woman's perspective — a mother and children dance joyously in front of Leonardo's Mona Lisa.

- **Historical Context.** **Faith Ringgold** (1930–2024), Harlem-born painter and activist, began making "story quilts" in the 1980s after encountering Nepali cloth-bordered paintings at the Rijksmuseum. Quilting connected her to African-American women's traditions — especially the legacy of **enslaved quilters** — and to her own mother, fashion designer Willi Posey. *Dancing at the Louvre* is the first of twelve quilts in **The French Collection**, a narrative series following fictional Willia Marie Simone, a young Black American artist who moves to 1920s Paris.
- **Function.** A **counter-history** of modern art. By inserting Black women and children into the Louvre — joyously dancing before Leonardo da Vinci's *Mona Lisa*, *Virgin of the Rocks*, and *Virgin and Child with St. Anne* — Ringgold challenges the exclusion of African-American and women artists from the Western canon.
- **Content.** Willia Marie and her friend Marcia with three small girls hold hands and dance in a gallery of the Louvre. A narrative text, handwritten in the cloth borders, tells the story. Leonardo's paintings recede to the background.
- **Form.** **Mixed-media quilt**: a central acrylic painting on canvas, bordered by strips of tie-dyed and pieced fabric, backed with plain cotton and batted in traditional quilt fashion. The hand-written text was inscribed in black marker on the cloth border.

**Vocabulary:** quilt · story quilt
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#59](#59--bayeux-tapestry) — Bayeux Tapestry (Romanesque English or Norman, c. 1066–1080)

**Theme:** narrative art

> works as part of a series, woven art

---

#### → [#98](#98--the-tête-à-tête-from-marriage-à-la-mode) — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode (William Hogarth, c. 1743)

**Theme:** Narrative Art, social commentary

> Works as part of a series, criticism of the status quo

---

#### → [#243](#243--darkytown-rebellion) — Darkytown Rebellion (Kara Walker, 2001)

**Theme:** African American art

> Artist draws on identity as an African American

---

## #233 — Trade (Gifts for Trading Land with White People)

**Jaune Quick-to-See Smith (Salish, Kootenai, and French-Cree)** · 1992 C.E. · Oil paint and mixed media, collage, objects, canvas · Chrysler Museum of Art, Norfolk, Virginia · 60 × 170 in (triptych)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Trade_Gifts_Tania_Bruguera.jpg/330px-Trade_Gifts_Tania_Bruguera.jpg" width="240" alt="Trade (Gifts for Trading Land with White People)">
### Summary

A massive **Native American counter-monument** to Columbus's quincentenary, indicting five centuries of exploitative "trade" with Indigenous peoples.

- **Historical Context.** **Jaune Quick-to-See Smith** (b. 1940), raised on the Flathead Reservation in Montana, created *Trade* for the **1992 quincentenary** of Columbus's arrival — part of her "Quincentenary Non-Celebration" series. She countered the triumphal narrative with a work about land theft, stereotyping, and the commodification of Native identity. Earned an MA in art from the University of New Mexico, 1980.
- **Function.** A **political protest** and historical reckoning. Makes explicit the unequal trade — blankets, trinkets, alcohol — for enormous tracts of Native land, and the continuing commodification of Native imagery in American consumer culture.
- **Content.** A **triptych canvas** collaged with newspapers, comics, photos, tobacco and gum wrappers, and ads stereotyping Native life. Drips of red paint recall blood. A dark silhouette of a canoe overlays the collage. Above the canvas hangs a clothesline of cheap, mass-produced **"Indian" kitsch** — toy tomahawks, a Washington Redskins hat, feather headdresses, Red Man chewing tobacco tins — offered as if for trade.
- **Form.** Large horizontal triptych. Aggressive layering of collage, drips, and outline contrasts with the commodity objects suspended above. Dominant red palette.

**Vocabulary:** collage · triptych · mixed media
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#141](#141--the-migration-of-the-negro-panel-no.-49) — The Migration of the Negro, Panel no. 49 (Jacob Lawrence, 1940–1941)

**Theme:** Segregation, stereotyping, prejudice, injustice, racial pride

> Both artists explore their cultural history through their art. Both pieces demonstrate the marginalization of a minority, and at the same time, fortify their culture's identity and show pride in their heritage.

---

#### → [#142](#142--the-jungle) — The Jungle (Wifredo Lam, 1943)

**Theme:** Abstraction, unsettling, disillusionment

> The two pieces both seek to challenge the United States' stereotypes of their cultures. The Jungle was created to reveal the reality of laboring Cubans in the sugar cane industry versus the American idea that Cuba was a vacation spot or beach resort. Trade's function is similar – to reveal the commoditization and oppression of Natives by the American government.

---

#### → [#245](#245--old-man's-cloth) — Old Man's Cloth (El Anatsui, 2003)

**Theme:** Tradition, colonization, trade

> These pieces are very similar in that they are making a commentary on European colonization, which both greatly affected African and Native American traditions and cultures. The Old Man's cloth references the transatlantic slave trade through liquor bottle caps, and Trade references the unfair trading of trinkets for Native land.

---

## #235 — Rebellious Silence, from the Women of Allah series

**Shirin Neshat (Iran/USA); photograph by Cynthia Preston** · 1994 C.E. · Ink on gelatin silver print · Various collections · Variable dimensions

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Rebellious_Silence_Neshat.jpg/330px-Rebellious_Silence_Neshat.jpg" width="240" alt="Rebellious Silence, from the Women of Allah series">
### Summary

A stark self-portrait bisected by a rifle — Neshat's most famous photograph, from a series confronting **Iranian Muslim women's identity** after the Islamic Revolution.

- **Historical Context.** **Shirin Neshat** (b. 1957) left Iran in 1974 to study in California; when she returned in 1990, the **1979 Islamic Revolution** had transformed the country she remembered. Founded by Ayatollah Khomeini, the revolution imposed strict dress codes including the mandatory chador, reshaped women's legal status, and was followed by the devastating Iran–Iraq War (1980–1988). The **Women of Allah** series (1993–1997), photographed by Cynthia Preston, addresses these upheavals from the perspective of a diasporic Iranian woman.
- **Function.** **Political and personal confrontation** with stereotypes of Muslim women as either oppressed victims or revolutionary martyrs; refuses both simplistic Western and Iranian narratives. Opens space for ambiguity.
- **Content.** Neshat faces the viewer directly in black chador; a vertical rifle barrel bisects her face between the eyes. Her exposed skin is covered in **Farsi calligraphy** — poems by Iranian poet **Tahereh Saffarzadeh** about female martyrdom and revolutionary duty. Text becomes veil.
- **Form.** Black-and-white gelatin silver print, overlaid with black ink calligraphy by the artist's own hand. Rigid frontal composition, strong geometric division by the gun.

**Vocabulary:** Muslim · Islamic Revolution · Chador · Farsi calligraphy · Women of Allah
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#167](#167--conical-tower-and-circular-wall-of-great-zimbabwe) — Conical tower and circular wall of Great Zimbabwe (Shona peoples, c. 1000–1400 C.E.)

**Theme:** Cultural identity

> Both works assert the enduring cultural identity of non-Western societies against Western frames; both use local materials and idioms (stone masonry / Farsi calligraphy) to do so.

---

#### → [#143](#143--dream-of-a-sunday-afternoon-in-the-alameda-park-*sueño-de-una-tarde-dominical-en-la-alameda-central*) — Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in the Alameda Park (Diego Rivera, 1947–1948)

**Theme:** Political art, national history

> Both works embed political critique into personal/national memory; both use text or emblematic imagery to encode messages not legible to all viewers.

---

#### → [#208](#208--jahangir-preferring-a-sufi-shaikh-to-kings) — Jahangir Preferring a Sufi Shaikh to Kings (Bichitr, Mughal India, c. 1620)

**Theme:** Islamic calligraphy, portraiture

> Both combine a frontal Islamic-world portrait with calligraphic text as a signifier of authority and meaning.

---

## #236 — En la Barberia no se Llora (No Crying Allowed in the Barbershop)

**Pepón Osorio (Puerto Rican-American)** · 1994 C.E. · Mixed-media installation · Originally installed on Park Street, Hartford, Connecticut · Installation scale

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/En_la_Barberia_no_se_Llora.jpg/330px-En_la_Barberia_no_se_Llora.jpg" width="240" alt="En la Barberia no se Llora (No Crying Allowed in the Barbershop)">
### Summary

An immersive installation reconstructing a **Puerto Rican barbershop** pink-walled, overloaded with machismo kitsch, and screening videos of men weeping.

- **Historical Context.** **Pepón Osorio** (b. 1955 in Puerto Rico) trained as a social worker before becoming an installation artist. Commissioned by **Real Art Ways** and installed in a storefront in the heavily Puerto Rican community on Park Street in Hartford. Osorio deliberately placed the work within the community rather than in a museum, inviting Latino men (and their families) to confront expectations of **machismo** on their own turf. The Puerto Rican barbershop is a key site of male socialization.
- **Function.** **Community intervention**. Confronts machismo and homophobia by juxtaposing hypermasculine iconography with the taboo of men crying on camera. Most visitors were women — offering a moment of reflection on the men in their lives.
- **Content.** A barbershop lined with pink rose-patterned walls, plastic flowers, religious portraits, sports memorabilia, a Puerto Rican flag, toy action figures, baseballs, **chucherías** (trinkets), clear plastic car mats, and a red velour automobile-seat barber chair. **TV monitors** embedded in the mirrors loop videos of Latino men weeping, with their neighbors' reactions.
- **Form.** Osorio calls the style **"Nuyorican Baroque"** — excessive, saturated, ornamented. Every surface is loaded. The installation engages sight, sound, and enclosed space.

**Vocabulary:** Nuyorican Baroque · machismo · chucherías
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#98](#98--the-tête-à-tête-from-marriage-à-la-mode) — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode (William Hogarth, c. 1743)

**Theme:** societal criticism series

> This piece reveals the nature of marriage that the artist wants to criticize, like Osorio's piece in revealing the exaggeration of machismo.

---

#### → [#250](#250--kui-hua-zi-sunflower-seeds) — Sunflower Seeds (Kui Hua Zi) (Ai Weiwei, 2010)

**Theme:** installation, Mao Zedong, Sunflower seeds, locals

> Ai Weiwei, like Osorio used locals to help in the installation, which contributed to the overall meaning of the piece.

---

#### → [#106](#106--y-no-hai-remedio-and-there's-nothing-to-be-done-from-los-desastres-de-la-guerra-plate-15) — Y no hai remedio, from Los Desastres de la Guerra (Francisco de Goya, 1810–1823)

**Theme:** private artwork, political criticism

> Instead of placing it in a museum, Osorio's installation was local. De Goya also created The Disasters of War as private artwork. Both have criticisms of the contemporary environment.

---

## #237 — Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000)

**Michel Tuffery (Samoan–Cook Islands–Tahitian–Pākehā New Zealand)** · 1994 C.E. · Flattened tin cans, steel, mixed media · Museum of New Zealand Te Papa Tongarewa · 1150 × 2170 × 650 mm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg/330px-Pisupo_lua_afe_%28Corned_beef_2000%29.jpg" width="240" alt="Pisupo Lua Afe (Corned Beef 2000)">
### Summary

A life-sized **bull assembled from flattened corned-beef cans**, critiquing globalization's impact on Pacific diets and ecosystems.

- **Historical Context.** **Michel Tuffery** (b. 1966 in Wellington, NZ) draws on his **Samoan, Cook Islands, and Tahitian** heritage. Imported canned food reached Samoa in the 1960s as canned pea soup — **pisupo** — and "pisupo" came to mean all canned foods, especially **corned beef**, which replaced traditional foods and became a required gift at Pacific ceremonies. "Lua afe" means "two thousand." Cattle introduced by colonial powers have also damaged fragile Pacific ecosystems.
- **Function.** **Environmental and cultural critique**. Exposes the health crisis caused by imported processed foods, the loss of local foodways, and the ecological cost of cattle. Can be rolled on hidden wheels into public performances.
- **Content.** A life-sized bull — symbol both of the beef industry and of the cultural import replacing native food. Recycled cans turn colonial trade goods back into art about that trade.
- **Form.** Overlapping, fish-scale tiling of flattened corned-beef tin cans riveted and welded onto a steel armature. Dense, hard, heavy, colorful (red and yellow of the Libby's label). Mobile on concealed wheels.

**Vocabulary:** assemblage
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#147](#147--marilyn-diptych) — Marilyn Diptych (Andy Warhol, 1962)

**Theme:** Human Identity; repetition; pop-culture; mass production

> Criticize society; mournful; both works are bright and bold, colorful; both comment on mass production + industrialization

---

#### → [#233](#233--trade-gifts-for-trading-land-with-white-people) — Trade (Gifts for Trading Land with White People) (Jaune Quick-to-See Smith, 1992)

**Theme:** Human Identity; global trade

> Both are commentaries on foreign intervention

---

#### → [#245](#245--old-man's-cloth) — Old Man's Cloth (El Anatsui, 2003)

**Theme:** Human Identity; colonial past; Global trade

> Both are assemblages of objects; Old Man's Cloth is made of liquor bottle caps and copper wire; both comment on effects of Global trade + colonial past on native cultures; materials reflect history

---

## #238 — Electronic Superhighway: Continental U.S., Alaska, Hawaii

**Nam June Paik (Korean-American)** · 1995 C.E. · Fifty-one-channel video installation with custom electronics, neon, steel, wood · Smithsonian American Art Museum, Washington, D.C. · 15 × 40 × 4 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Electronic_Superhighway_Paik.jpg/330px-Electronic_Superhighway_Paik.jpg" width="240" alt="Electronic Superhighway: Continental U.S., Alaska, Hawaii">
### Summary

A map of the United States built from **336 televisions and neon tubing** — the "father of video art" visualizing the pre-internet information age.

- **Historical Context.** **Nam June Paik** (1932–2006), born in Seoul and trained as a composer in Tokyo and Germany, joined John Cage and the **Fluxus** avant-garde in the 1960s and pioneered video as an art form. Paik himself coined the term "electronic superhighway" in 1974 to predict a global information network. This installation, made for the Smithsonian in 1995, was created on the verge of mass internet adoption.
- **Function.** An **immersive, nation-scale diagram** of American media culture. Each state becomes a constellation of looping television images — a portrait through media consumption. Both celebratory and cautionary about information overload.
- **Content.** Fifty-one channels of video, one for each state (plus the District of Columbia), loop clips tied to that state's identity: *The Wizard of Oz* for Kansas, civil-rights footage for Alabama, presidential primaries for Iowa. A live camera in D.C. shows the viewer staring back. Colored neon tubing traces the state borders.
- **Form.** 336 cathode-ray TVs embedded in a steel-and-wood map. Dense grid of flashing color and image. Must be seen frontally, like a wall. Meant to overwhelm.

**Vocabulary:** Fluxus · Superhighway · information overload
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3, 3.2, 3.5

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#227](#227--summer-trees) — Summer Trees (Song Su-nam, 1979)

**Theme:** Traditional art in the face of globalism and modern technology

> Stark contrasts between these two works- both from Korean artists, one moved to America and integrated in a global society with technology; the other, the stark opposite remaining in Korea and a staunch defender of the past traditions.

---

#### → [#239](#239--the-crossing) — The Crossing (Bill Viola, 1996)

**Theme:** Temporalism, technology, futurism

> Similar video formats, innovation, similar positive outlook on future and modern technology

---

#### → [#224](#224--the-gates) — The Gates (Christo and Jeanne-Claude, 2005)

**Theme:** Public art, modern capabilities

> Similar contemporary art usage of advancing technology and modernism to innovate new techniques and representations of art

---

## #240 — Guggenheim Bilbao Museo

**Frank Gehry (Canadian-American)** · 1997 C.E. · Titanium, glass, and limestone · Bilbao, Spain · ~260,000 sq ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Guggenheim_Bilbao.jpg/330px-Guggenheim_Bilbao.jpg" width="240" alt="Guggenheim Bilbao Museo">
### Summary

Frank Gehry's titanium-clad, sculpturally exuberant museum — the flagship of **Deconstructivist architecture** and the origin of the "**Bilbao Effect**."

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by the **Basque regional government** as the centerpiece of an urban-renewal program for **Bilbao**, a shipping-and-steel port in severe decline since the 1980s. The Guggenheim Foundation, seeking a European outpost, partnered with the city. Opening in 1997, the museum drew over a million tourists in its first year, spawned the term "**Bilbao Effect**" (signature architecture driving regional revival), and won multiple major awards. Designed with the CAD program **Catia** (originally developed for aerospace).
- **Function.** A contemporary-art museum and an **economic/urban catalyst**. Houses Abstract Expressionism, Pop, Conceptual, Arte Povera, and Neo-Expressionist work, alongside massive site-specific pieces by Richard Serra.
- **Content.** From across the Nervión River the building reads as a ship, or a fish (a recurring Gehry motif), or a bouquet of chrome petals. The interior centers on a soaring "**Boat Gallery**" atrium with glazed elevators, irregular walls, catwalks, and clerestory light.
- **Form.** Cluster of **curvilinear volumes** clad in ~33,000 thin titanium shingles that shimmer and respond to weather. Glass curtain walls and Spanish limestone accents. Non-rectilinear throughout.

**Vocabulary:** Bilbao effect · Catia · Deconstructivism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#88](#88--san-carlo-alle-quattro-fontane) — San Carlo alle Quattro Fontane (Francesco Borromini, Rome, 1638–1646)

**Theme:** Baroque, curvilinear, drama

> The alternating convex and concave patterns on the façade resemble the curvilinear patterns on the exterior of the Museum.

---

#### → [#159](#159--city-of-cusco-including-qorikancha-santo-domingo-and-walls-at-saqsa-waman) — Walls at Saqsa Waman, City of Cusco (Inka, c. 1440 C.E.)

**Theme:** Hefty, curvilinear, puma

> The walls follow a non-rigid path, as well as the stones themselves, which are also smooth, rounded objects rather than rigid blocks. Curvy theme is similar to the Bilbao Museo.

---

#### → [#167](#167--conical-tower-and-circular-wall-of-great-zimbabwe) — Conical tower and circular wall of Great Zimbabwe (Shona peoples, c. 1000–1400 C.E.)

**Theme:** Grandiose, curvilinear, unusual, grain

> The form of the structure is also quite curvilinear, with the large walls surrounding a conical tower.

---

## #241 — Pure Land

**Mariko Mori (Japan/USA)** · 1998 C.E. · Color photograph on glass · Museum of Contemporary Art, Tokyo · 305 × 610 × 2.2 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0f/Pure_Land_Shahzia.jpg/330px-Pure_Land_Shahzia.jpg" width="240" alt="Pure Land">
### Summary

A **techno-Buddhist paradise** — Mariko Mori casts herself as a serene deity floating above the Dead Sea, surrounded by alien musician-bodhisattvas.

- **Historical Context.** **Mariko Mori** (b. 1967 in Tokyo), a former model turned artist, trained in London and New York. *Pure Land* is part of Mori's mid-1990s **Nirvana** series — a 3-D video installation of the same scene whose still-photograph sibling stands alone as an art object. Mori draws from **Pure Land Buddhism**, a sect promising rebirth in Amida Buddha's western paradise. Photographed on site at the Dead Sea — associated with purification in both Shinto and Judaic traditions. Inspired in part by Japanese pop aesthetics, anime, and sleep-paralysis experiences.
- **Function.** Blends **religious icon and science-fiction fantasy**. The larger Nirvana installation immersed viewers in 3-D video and scented wind; the photograph distills that experience into a meditative still moment.
- **Content.** Mori poses as **Kichijoten**, a Japanese goddess of fortune, at center, seated on a lotus. Around her float six cartoonish-alien **bodhisattva musicians**. A Tibetan stupa-cum-spaceship floats at right. Distance is compressed; background figures blur as if rushing toward us.
- **Form.** Large cibachrome photograph laminated on glass. Pastel palette (pinks, mauves, aqua). Digital composite of body photography, CGI characters, and painted digital background.

**Vocabulary:** Tibetan stupa · Nirvana · Amida Buddha · Pure Land Buddhism · Bodhisattvas
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#192](#192--the-great-stupa-at-sanchi) — Great Stupa at Sanchi (Buddhist, India, 3rd c. B.C.E.–1st c. C.E.)

**Theme:** Visualization and meditation through self introspection given a subject to focus on

> Since a stupa is a solid mound, one would circumambulate the stupa and experience feelings of samsara and the journey of the eightfold path. Like Pure Land, the audience would have had to use the artwork itself (meditation) to understand the spiritual aspect that was meant to be portrayed rather than the artwork directly depicting the meanings.

---

#### → [#177](#177--lukasa-memory-board) — Lukasa (memory board) (Mbudye Society, Luba peoples, 19th–20th century)

**Theme:** Creating relationships with the audience

> The elite Luba people who read the Lukasa boards and recounted the myths to their audience often changed the manner of delivery of the stories based on his audience. This is similar to Pure Land because the photograph and the animated video wished to immerse the audience so that the person viewing the video could feel their own sense of nirvana rather than a specific meaning.

---

#### → [#90](#90--angel-with-arquebus-asiel-timor-dei) — Angel with Arquebus, Asiel Timor Dei (Master of Calamarca, c. 17th century)

**Theme:** Combination of two different cultures

> In Angel with Arquebus the angel holds a gun which is definitely uncharacteristic of what angels should be portrayed with, a sword. This is very similar to Pure Land, as the bodhisattvas are now colorful, technological, alien like figures, but still portray the same meaning as past traditions.

---

## #243 — Darkytown Rebellion

**Kara Walker (USA)** · 2001 C.E. · Cut paper silhouettes and projection on walls · Collection Musée d'Art Moderne Grand-Duc Jean, Luxembourg · 14 × 37 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8e/Darkytown_Rebellion_Walker.jpg/330px-Darkytown_Rebellion_Walker.jpg" width="240" alt="Darkytown Rebellion">
### Summary

A room-sized tableau of **black paper silhouettes** of antebellum Southern violence, bathed in colored light — Walker's confrontational excavation of American racial memory.

- **Historical Context.** **Kara Walker** (b. 1969 in Stockton, CA) moved to Georgia at age 13 and experienced Southern racism directly. She is known for mural-scale cut-paper silhouettes — a genre associated with genteel 19th-century women's craft — used to depict brutal, grotesque scenes of slavery, rape, lynching, and rebellion. Her work has drawn sharp controversy from critics who argue she reinscribes racial stereotypes and from supporters who value her confrontation of suppressed histories. *Darkytown* extends the silhouette tradition by adding **colored overhead projectors** whose beams are interrupted by visitors' bodies, implicating the viewer in the scene.
- **Function.** An **immersive history lesson** forcing viewers to complete and interpret violent and sexually charged scenes that the silhouette reduces to stereotype; the colored projections literally cast the viewer's shadow into the tableau.
- **Content.** Figures of men, women, and children in 19th-century dress: a man with a severed leg, a woman giving birth, others brandishing weapons or falling wounded. References to racist 19th-century minstrel-show tropes (the title alludes to an 1899 "coon song"). Walker never names specific events; her scenes are composites.
- **Form.** Black cut-paper silhouettes adhered with wax to white gallery walls. Colored gel projectors wash the wall in lurid reds, greens, and blues; the viewer's shadow joins the scene.

**Vocabulary:** racism · stereotypes · silhouettes · shadows · narrative · controversy · concealment · historical representation
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#141](#141--the-migration-of-the-negro-panel-no.-49) — The Migration of the Negro, Panel no. 49 (Jacob Lawrence, 1940–1941)

**Theme:** Discrimination and racism

> African Americans are the subjects of the works and the societal differences are portrayed

---

#### → [#75](#75--sistine-chapel-ceiling-and-altar-wall-frescoes) — Sistine Chapel ceiling (Michelangelo, 1508–1512)

**Theme:** Narrative

> Both are done on a flat wall surface. Sistine Chapel Ceiling shows a narrative of biblical events such as the coming of Christ. Darkytown Rebellion contains narratives of lives of African Americans in the South.

---

#### → [#2](#2--great-hall-of-the-bulls) — Great Hall of the Bulls (Lascaux, France, Paleolithic, 15,000–13,000 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Wall painting

> Both are done directly done on a wall surface, have an eerie feel due to its surroundings

---

## #244 — The Swing (after Fragonard)

**Yinka Shonibare (British-Nigerian)** · 2001 C.E. · Life-size fiberglass mannequin, Dutch wax printed cotton textile, swing, artificial foliage · Tate, London · 10.8′ × 11.5′ × 7.2′

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/The_Swing_after_Fragonard_Walker.jpg/330px-The_Swing_after_Fragonard_Walker.jpg" width="240" alt="The Swing (after Fragonard)">
### Summary

A life-sized, **headless remake of Fragonard's Rococo painting** in African-print fabric — Yinka Shonibare's postcolonial confrontation with European aristocracy and the entangled histories of trade and identity.

- **Historical Context.** **Yinka Shonibare** (b. 1962 in London, raised in Lagos, returned to London) self-identifies as a "**post-colonial hybrid**." He is best known for using **Dutch wax printed cotton** — a fabric whose patterns originated in Indonesian batik, were appropriated by Dutch manufacturers, produced in European factories, and marketed to West Africa, where they became synonymous with African identity — as a metaphor for layered, imposed cultural authenticity. This work directly remakes **Jean-Honoré Fragonard's 1767 painting** *The Swing* (image-set #101). Fragonard's painting depicts an aristocrat peeking beneath the dress of a swinging lady; Shonibare's revision removes both her head (evoking the **Reign of Terror** a generation later) and the voyeur (making the viewer complicit).
- **Function.** **Postcolonial institutional critique**. Displayed in London museums, it forces European audiences to see their cultural heritage through the lens of colonial exploitation.
- **Content.** A life-sized mannequin in Fragonard's pose mid-swing — shoe flying off, petticoats fluttering — but headless and dressed in vivid Dutch wax prints instead of pale silk. Hung from a real swing amid artificial dark-green foliage.
- **Form.** 3-D reconstruction of a 2-D painting; the viewer circles the mannequin, physically stepping into the role once occupied by the hidden lover. Bright pattern pops against dark foliage and blank white gallery wall.

**Vocabulary:** Postmodernism · Rococo · installation art · Dutch wax · hybridity
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 2.1

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#106](#106--y-no-hai-remedio-and-there's-nothing-to-be-done-from-los-desastres-de-la-guerra-plate-15) — Y no hai remedio (And There's Nothing to Be Done), from Los Desastres de la Guerra (Francisco de Goya, 1810–1823)

**Theme:** oppression, political protest, violence

> Both pieces protest the policies of an invading government: brutality, disruption, and erasure.

---

#### → [#143](#143--dream-of-a-sunday-afternoon-in-the-alameda-park-*sueño-de-una-tarde-dominical-en-la-alameda-central*) — Dream of a Sunday Afternoon in the Alameda Park (Diego Rivera, 1947–1948)

**Theme:** class struggle, national history

> Both pieces address the complex history of their nation(s) as well as the divide between the privileged and underprivileged.

---

#### → [#148](#148--narcissus-garden) — Narcissus Garden (Yayoi Kusama, 1966)

**Theme:** commercialism, social critique

> Both pieces criticize society for its flaws and the way those flaws have affected the artist (self-absorption, capitalism, cultural appropriation, superiority).

---

## #245 — Old Man's Cloth

**El Anatsui (Ghanaian, working in Nigeria)** · 2003 C.E. · Aluminum (flattened liquor-bottle caps and labels) and copper wire · Harn Museum of Art, Gainesville · 16′ × 17′1″

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5c/Old_Mans_Cloth_El_Anatsui.jpg/330px-Old_Mans_Cloth_El_Anatsui.jpg" width="240" alt="Old Man's Cloth">
### Summary

A shimmering "metal cloth" of **flattened liquor-bottle caps** — El Anatsui's signature contemporary reimagining of West African Kente cloth and the transatlantic slave trade.

- **Historical Context.** **El Anatsui** (b. 1944 in Ghana) studied in the 1960s during the height of African **decolonization**; he became a central figure at the University of Nigeria, Nsukka, and a leader of the pan-African **Sankofa** revival of traditional forms. His metal textiles of the early 2000s — made from bottle caps collected near his studio in Nsukka — reference the **triangular trade** in which European liquor was exchanged for enslaved Africans, while simultaneously evoking West African **Kente** cloth.
- **Function.** A contemporary display work made for museums and biennials. Conceptually, a **commemorative textile** — folding the history of the slave trade, colonial consumerism, and Akan craft into a single shimmering surface.
- **Content.** Thousands of flattened aluminum tops and labels from schnapps and other liquor bottles, stitched with copper wire into interlocking rectangular blocks of gold, red, black, and silver — mimicking the color and weave of Kente. Draped over the wall, its folds catch the light.
- **Form.** Enormous, flexible metal textile. Made in **communal workshop** with apprentices, echoing Akan cloth-making traditions. Installs differently each time, never twice in exactly the same folds.

**Vocabulary:** decolonization · Sankofa · sunsum · Kente
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#233](#233--trade-gifts-for-trading-land-with-white-people) — Trade (Gifts for Trading Land with White People) (Jaune Quick-to-See Smith, 1992)

**Theme:** Historic

> Both depict historic unequal economic exchanges between European colonialists and native peoples

---

#### → [#244](#244--the-swing-after-fragonard) — The Swing (after Fragonard) (Yinka Shonibare, 2001)

**Theme:** Historic

> The artists of both works are West African artists depicting the effects of imperialism.

---

#### → [#246](#246--stadia-ii) — Stadia II (Julie Mehretu, 2004)

**Theme:** Non-object

> Both works offer indirect commentary on globalization and commercialism.

## #246 — Stadia II

**Julie Mehretu (Ethiopian-American)** · 2004 C.E. · Ink and acrylic on canvas · Carnegie Museum of Art, Pittsburgh · 108 × 144 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Stadia_II_Eliasson.jpg/330px-Stadia_II_Eliasson.jpg" width="240" alt="Stadia II">
### Summary

A monumental **abstract painting** overlaying architectural plans of global stadiums with floating flags, logos, and confetti — one panel of a triptych on nationalism, sport, and mass spectacle.

- **Historical Context.** **Julie Mehretu** (b. 1970 in Addis Ababa, Ethiopia; raised in Michigan) earned an MFA at RISD in 1997. Her mature work builds dense palimpsests of maps, architectural drawings, and abstract marks that read as atmospheric, tectonic, and political at once. *Stadia II* is part of a 2004 triptych commissioned while Mehretu was artist-in-residence at Houston's Glassell School; it drew on the Russian Constructivist tradition and on stadium architecture as a site of nationalism, corporate sponsorship, and propaganda.
- **Function.** Large-scale contemporary painting for museum display. Invites viewers to lose themselves in cascading lines and shards of color while meditating on the **choreography of crowds** at sports events, political rallies, and acts of terror.
- **Content.** Curved architectural shells of **stadium interiors** drawn in ink at the bottom; a centrifugal eruption of color — small circles and dots (confetti), triangles and blocks (flag fragments, corporate logos, religious symbols) — bursts outward from the center. Gray painterly marks rise like smoke.
- **Form.** Layered technique: projected architectural plans traced in ink, sealed under acrylic-and-silica ground, then further drawing and paint layered above. Vast scale. Limited color palette of red, black, white, and blue dominates.

**Vocabulary:** centrifugal · stratified · tectonic geology · constructivism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.2, 1.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#245](#245--old-man's-cloth) — Old Man's Cloth (El Anatsui, 2003)

**Theme:** Artist from utopia, historical movements

> Stadia II also represents national identities in abstract shapes and colors.

---

#### → [#244](#244--the-swing-after-fragonard) — The Swing (after Fragonard) (Yinka Shonibare, 2001)

**Theme:** "Indonesian motifs, made by Europeans, sold in West Africa"

> Stadia II also shows a variety of countries and cultures

---

#### → [#227](#227--summer-trees) — Summer Trees (Song Su-nam, 1979)

**Theme:** Material use represented the culture of the artist and the history of the country, use of solid shapes, rectangle

> Stadia II also uses shapes to represent the flags of different countries, religions, and revolutions.

---

## #247 — Preying Mantra

**Wangechi Mutu (Kenyan-American)** · 2006 C.E. · Mixed media on Mylar · Brooklyn Museum, New York · 73.25 × 54.25 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Preying_Mantra_Xu_Bing.jpg/330px-Preying_Mantra_Xu_Bing.jpg" width="240" alt="Preying Mantra">
### Summary

A **collaged Afrofuturist Eve** reclining on a Kuba-cloth blanket with a green snake — Mutu's layered interrogation of race, gender, and the art-historical female body.

- **Historical Context.** **Wangechi Mutu** (b. 1972 in Nairobi, Kenya) trained in Wales and at Yale; she lives and works in New York and Nairobi. She is a leading voice in **Afrofuturism**, using collage, sculpture, and film to imagine hybrid Black female identities. Her work responds to European colonial imagery of African women as exotic, primitive, or grotesque, and to the long Western tradition of the reclining female nude.
- **Function.** **Feminist and postcolonial critique**. Evokes biblical Eve with her snake, praying-mantis predation, and reclining odalisque conventions, then collages them into a single hybrid being who is simultaneously predator and prey.
- **Content.** A dark-skinned, nude **female figure** reclines in a tree. Her body parts are collaged from many sources — her left ear, for example, has chicken feet and insect pincers. She holds a green snake in her left hand and looks out at the viewer. She rests on a brightly patterned blanket resembling Kuba cloth from the DRC. The title puns "**praying mantis**" on "**preying mantra**."
- **Form.** Mixed-media collage on translucent Mylar — ink, acrylic, magazine cutouts, photographs. Saturated magenta, yellow, and blue. Visibly layered, textured surface. Diagonal composition from lower left to upper right.

**Vocabulary:** Afrofuturism · collage · hybridity · Mylar
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 3.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#10](#10--tlatilco-female-figurine) — Tlatilco female figurine (Central Mexico, c. 1200–900 B.C.E.)

**Theme:** Idealized woman

> The Tlatilco female figure embodies the idealized, exaggerated female bodies that Mutu encourages viewers to ponder in the Preying Mantra. Both also contain influences from earlier cultures: the Tlatilco female figure contains Olmec imagery while the Preying Mantra incorporates Kuba cloth.

---

#### → [#105](#105--self-portrait) — Self-Portrait (Elisabeth Louise Vigée Le Brun, 1790)

**Theme:** Power of women, sensuality

> In her self-portrait, Le Brun portrays herself as a powerful woman of high status, showing off her wealth, independence, and professional skills as the court painter for Marie Antoinette. The female figure in Preying Mantra also possesses hidden power similar to that of a praying mantis. Both pieces incorporate female sensuality, with Le Brun's parted mouth and the female figure's nudity.

---

#### → [#115](#115--olympia) — Olympia (Édouard Manet, 1863)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg/500px-Edouard_Manet_-_Olympia_-_Google_Art_ProjectFXD.jpg" width="180" alt="Olympia">

**Theme:** Commentary on female nude, direct gaze

> Both pieces provide modern commentary on female nudes by depicting them in sensual positions. Both women gaze directly at the viewer without submission.

---

## #248 — Shibboleth

**Doris Salcedo (Colombian)** · 2007–2008 C.E. · Installation: concrete and steel-reinforced fissure in the floor · Tate Modern Turbine Hall, London (permanent scar remains) · 548 ft long, variable width and depth

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Shibboleth_Salcedo.jpg/330px-Shibboleth_Salcedo.jpg" width="240" alt="Shibboleth">
### Summary

A jagged **crack running 548 feet through the floor of the Tate Modern** — a memorial to the exclusion of immigrants and the poor from Western modernity.

- **Historical Context.** **Doris Salcedo** (b. 1958 in Bogotá), shaped by the violence of Colombia's civil war, builds memorials to the disappeared and marginalized. Commissioned for the Tate Modern's **Unilever Series** in the Turbine Hall, *Shibboleth* opened in October 2007. Salcedo secretly cast the fissure from a mold over a year; the piece broke the ground of one of the world's most powerful art institutions. The title is a biblical word (Judges 12:6) for a shibboleth — a word used by one group to identify outsiders and kill them. Salcedo's subject: the **racialized exclusion** underpinning European modernity.
- **Function.** A **political/philosophical installation**. Visitors walked along, straddled, and peered into the fissure, feeling the instability of the ground beneath them. After the exhibition, the Tate filled the crack, but the repair line remains — a permanent scar.
- **Content.** A fissure begins as a hairline at one end and gradually widens and deepens as it crosses the hall. Steel mesh is visible inside the crack — a surrogate for borders, fences, walls.
- **Form.** **Subtractive sculpture / installation** — cast concrete inserted into the existing floor, using industrial fabrication. Entirely monochromatic gray; no applied color; only the wall of the museum and the crack itself.

**Vocabulary:** shibboleth · Spazialismo · transgressive
**Learning Objectives:** LO 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#98](#98--the-tête-à-tête-from-marriage-à-la-mode) — The Tête à Tête, from Marriage à la Mode (William Hogarth, c. 1743)

**Theme:** symbolism, historical and societal contexts

> artists show their view on society and politics that they view as an issue — their art draws attention and awareness to this issue. class distinction for The Tete, cultural/racial distinction for the Shibboleth.

---

#### → [#151](#151--spiral-jetty) — Spiral Jetty (Robert Smithson, 1970)

**Theme:** large scale, part of its surroundings

> large, temporary, installation meant to be viewed from higher up but can also be appreciated from regular human point of view

---

#### → [#224](#224--the-gates) — The Gates (Christo and Jeanne-Claude, 2005)

**Theme:** meaningful public art

> message about humans and their behavior: immigrant experience for the Shibboleth, religious appreciation for The Gates. complex project/installment.

---

## #249 — MAXXI National Museum of XXI Century Arts

**Zaha Hadid (Iraqi-British)** · 2009–2010 C.E. · Glass, steel, and reinforced concrete · Rome, Italy · ~27,000 m²

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/16/MAXXI_Museum_Rome.jpg/330px-MAXXI_Museum_Rome.jpg" width="240" alt="MAXXI National Museum of XXI Century Arts">
### Summary

Zaha Hadid's sinuous, board-formed-concrete **contemporary art museum** in Rome — a flowing, non-rectilinear response to the ancient city's classical order.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned in 1998 via international competition, won by **Zaha Hadid** (1950–2016), the first woman to win the Pritzker Prize. Completed in 2009 and opened in 2010; won the **Stirling Prize** the same year. Italy's first major **publicly funded contemporary-art institution** in decades, and a landmark in Rome's shift from pure classical heritage toward contemporary identity. Hadid drew on Russian **Constructivism**, Malevich's Suprematism, and the ribboning minaret at Samarra. Her other major works include the Guangzhou Opera House and the London Aquatics Centre.
- **Function.** Multidisciplinary museum: **MAXXI Arte** (contemporary art) and **MAXXI Architettura** (contemporary architecture), with auditorium, library, bookshop, café, and temporary-exhibition galleries. Hadid described the building as a "**campus for art**" rather than an object container.
- **Content.** Intersecting concrete tubes and ribboning corridors form flowing, overlapping gallery spaces. Black steel staircases hang suspended over the main atrium; an open truss-and-louver ceiling floods the interior with daylight. Exterior walls of board-formed concrete curve to embrace the former barracks site.
- **Form.** Continuous, non-rectilinear architecture blurring interior and exterior. Neutral gray-and-white palette; vast glazing; sweeping pathways instead of fixed rooms. CAD-enabled complex curves.

**Vocabulary:** curvilinear · modernism · constructivism
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 2.2

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#240](#240--guggenheim-bilbao-museo) — Guggenheim Bilbao Museo (Frank Gehry, 1997)

**Theme:** no boundary of spaces, modern & contemporary art

> both are museums displaying modern and contemporary art, eliminate the typical boundaries of space

---

#### → [#45](#45--forum-of-trajan) — Forum of Trajan (Apollodorus of Damascus, Rome, 106–112 C.E.)

**Theme:** public space

> large areas of publicly owned space where many interactions take place

---

#### → [#135](#135--villa-savoye) — Villa Savoye (Le Corbusier, 1929)

**Theme:** international style, modernism, architectural designs

> simplicity, subtle colors, open interior free from distinct traditional walls

## #250 — Kui Hua Zi (Sunflower Seeds)

**Ai Weiwei (Chinese)** · 2010–2011 C.E. · Sculpted and painted porcelain · Installed in the Turbine Hall, Tate Modern, London · 100 million+ pieces; variable display

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Sunflower_Seeds_Ai_Weiwei.jpg/330px-Sunflower_Seeds_Ai_Weiwei.jpg" width="240" alt="Kui Hua Zi (Sunflower Seeds)">
### Summary

**Over 100 million hand-painted porcelain sunflower seeds** carpeting the Tate's Turbine Hall — Ai Weiwei's mass-collaborative meditation on China, propaganda, and conformity.

- **Historical Context.** **Ai Weiwei** (b. 1957 in Beijing) is China's most prominent contemporary artist and political dissident. *Sunflower Seeds* was commissioned for the Tate Modern's Unilever Series and opened in 2010. Each seed was hand-sculpted and hand-painted by a team of roughly **1,600 artisans in Jingdezhen** — the imperial porcelain city, whose workshops had been decimated by mass production. The project revived traditional craft at scale and provided years of employment to Jingdezhen workers. Sunflower seeds were the **motif of Maoist propaganda** (Mao as sun, the people as sunflowers turning toward him); they are also shared casually among friends in everyday Chinese life.
- **Function.** Installation art meets **social project**. Originally visitors could walk on and touch the carpet of seeds; after silicosis concerns from porcelain dust, the Tate cordoned off the surface and required observation from the edges or a raised walkway.
- **Content.** A vast rectangle of seed-shaped porcelains. Each looks nearly identical from a distance but reveals individual handwork up close — each one distinct yet part of an overwhelming mass, a metaphor for Chinese citizens under a homogenizing state.
- **Form.** Hand-thrown porcelain forms, hand-painted black stripes, fired and distributed like grain. The installation itself is simple (a rectangle), but the **scale** and **collective labor** behind it are the point.

**Vocabulary:** political art · interactive art · hyper-realistic
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 2.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#236](#236--en-la-barberia-no-se-llora-no-crying-allowed-in-the-barbershop) — En la Barberia no se Llora (Pepón Osorio, 1994)

**Theme:** contemporary, symbolic objects, culture critique

> Both use iconic objects from respective cultures to critique a social norm in installations.

---

#### → [#229](#229--a-book-from-the-sky-tianshu) — A Book from the Sky (Xu Bing, c. 1987–1991)

**Theme:** communism, knowledge, existential, culture critique

> Both are from Chinese artists and both protest communist china by making multiples of the same objects.

---

#### → [#244](#244--the-swing-after-fragonard) — The Swing (after Fragonard) (Yinka Shonibare, 2001)

**Theme:** "life-like" objects, thought-provoking, non-Western artist

> Both ate installations at the Tate by non-Western artists about realistically sized objects (sunflower seeds) and figures (mannequin).

## Works without connections
