## #15 — Seated Scribe

**Egyptian, Old Kingdom, Fourth Dynasty** · c. 2620–2500 B.C.E. · Painted limestone (with rock crystal, magnesite, and copper inlaid eyes; wooden nipples) · Saqqara, Egypt

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8d/Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg/500px-Le_Scribe_accroupi_-_Mus%C3%A9e_du_Louvre_Antiquit%C3%A9s_%C3%A9gyptiennes_E_3023.jpg" width="240" alt="Seated Scribe">

### Summary

A vivid painted limestone statuette of a scribe in cross-legged pose — one of the most lifelike surviving portraits of a non-royal individual from the Old Kingdom.

- **Historical Context.** From the necropolis at **Saqqara**, made during the **Fourth Dynasty** of the Old Kingdom. **Scribes** were revered in Egyptian society for their literacy; funerary statues of scribes commemorated that role in the afterlife.
- **Function.** A **tomb statue** honoring the deceased scribe; likely placed above a descriptive funerary text about him. It served as a permanent substitute for the scribe's **ka**.
- **Content.** A middle-aged man **seated cross-legged** holding a partially unrolled papyrus scroll; alert, slightly paunchy body. He is **not** the idealized youthful body of royal statuary.
- **Form.** **Painted limestone** with striking **inlaid eyes** (rock crystal, magnesite, copper) that give the gaze extraordinary presence. The seated pose is a departure from the rigid standing poses of royal Egyptian sculpture.

---

## #22 — Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and three daughters

**New Kingdom Egypt (Amarna), 18th Dynasty** · c. 1353–1335 B.C.E. · Limestone · Amarna, Egypt

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Akhenaten_Nefertiti_daughters.jpg/330px-Akhenaten_Nefertiti_daughters.jpg" width="240" alt="Akhenaton, Nefertiti, and three daughters">
### Summary

A small Amarna-period **sunken relief** of the royal family beneath the rayed disk of **Aten** — a famously intimate image of pharaonic life that documents Akhenaten's religious revolution.

- **Historical Context.** **Akhenaten** imposed a radical **monotheistic cult of Aten**, the sun disk, and moved Egypt's capital from Thebes to **Amarna**. He and **Nefertiti** presented themselves as the sole intermediaries between Aten and humanity. The experiment did not outlive him.
- **Function.** A **household shrine** for private worship in an Amarna home, reinforcing the royal family as the conduit to Aten — the rays of the sun disk terminate in hands offering **ankhs** to the king and queen.
- **Content.** Akhenaten kisses a daughter while Nefertiti holds two more; above them the **Aten** disk radiates hands bearing life. An informal, domestic royal scene unprecedented in pharaonic imagery.
- **Form.** **Sunken relief** in limestone. Bodies are **curvilinear and elongated** — swollen thighs, narrow shoulders, distinctive long faces — a radical break from the rigidly geometric proportions of earlier Egyptian art. The sun-disk's rays are strictly linear, a direct emblem of divine presence.

---

## #48 — Catacomb of Priscilla

**Late Antique Europe** · c. 200–400 C.E. · Excavated tufa and fresco · Rome, Italy

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/Catacomb_Priscilla_Madonna.jpg/330px-Catacomb_Priscilla_Madonna.jpg" width="240" alt="Catacomb of Priscilla">
### Summary

An **Early Christian** underground burial complex on the Via Salaria — its plastered-over tufa walls hold some of the **earliest Christian paintings**, including an iconic image of the **Good Shepherd**.

- **Historical Context.** Begun in the second century C.E., the Catacomb of Priscilla is the oldest part of the Roman Christian catacombs — dug into the soft volcanic **tufa** outside the city walls. Early Christians could not build above-ground churches, so burial and occasional clandestine worship happened here.
- **Function.** **Christian burial site** and occasional meeting place. Stacked passageways ("galleries") line niches (**loculi**) that held wrapped bodies; larger chambers (**cubicula**) provided space for funerary rites and imagery.
- **Content.** Wall paintings of Old and New Testament scenes — Jonah, the Three Young Men in the Furnace, the Good Shepherd — meant to provide hope for salvation. The famous **Greek Chapel** decoration includes the earliest known depiction of the Virgin and Child.
- **Form.** **Plaster** over rock, then **fresco** painting in a late-Roman style with loose brushwork and flat color fields that prefigures Byzantine iconography. The complex is five miles long and holds some **40,000 tombs**.

---

## #49 — Santa Sabina

**Late Antique Europe** · c. 422–432 C.E. · Brick and stone, wooden roof · Rome, Italy

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Santa_Sabina_Rome.jpg/330px-Santa_Sabina_Rome.jpg" width="240" alt="Santa Sabina">
### Summary

One of the earliest and most intact **Early Christian basilicas** in Rome — an elegant reuse of the Roman basilica plan for Christian worship, adorned with carved **cypress-wood doors** that include one of the oldest depictions of the Crucifixion.

- **Historical Context.** Built in the early 5th century C.E., after Christianity had become the Empire's official religion. Named after **Saint Sabina**, a Roman matron stoned to death for her conversion. Santa Sabina marks the early transition from catacomb-era Christianity to public monumental church architecture.
- **Function.** A **congregational basilica** for Christian worship, sited on the Aventine Hill. The plan — long nave with side aisles terminating in an apse — would remain the standard Christian church form for centuries.
- **Content.** A tall, sober nave lit by **clerestory windows**, lined by 24 reused Corinthian columns. The famous **wooden doors** carry carved biblical scenes, including a very early **Crucifixion**. Spandrel mosaics and the chi-rho over the apse add color above the white walls.
- **Form.** **Timber-roofed brick basilica** with stone columns. The unadorned white walls and open nave emphasize light and space; Roman engineering (clerestory, arcaded nave) is adapted to Christian liturgical axis.

---

## #53 — Merovingian looped fibulae

**Early medieval Europe (Merovingian)** · Mid-sixth century C.E. · Silver gilt worked in filigree, with inlays of garnets and other stones · Musée d'Archéologie nationale, Saint-Germain-en-Laye

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Merovingian_fibulae_brooch.jpg/250px-Merovingian_fibulae_brooch.jpg" width="240" alt="Merovingian looped fibulae">
### Summary

A pair of richly inlaid **bow fibulae** (decorative safety pins) from a Merovingian grave — miniature masterpieces of migration-era **cloisonné** metalwork.

- **Historical Context.** Made in 6th-century **Merovingian Gaul**, a successor kingdom to the western Roman Empire. Fibulae of this type were first popularized by **Roman military campaigns** and were adapted by "barbarian" peoples who had been in long cultural contact with Rome. They survive largely because they were buried with their wealthy owners.
- **Function.** Functional **garment clasps** that also advertised status. Their presence in grave goods suggests personal identification and rank in the afterlife.
- **Content.** Symmetrical **eagle** motifs — a symbol adopted from the Roman empire for its associations with power and status — set with garnets and colored glass. Fine **filigree** and tight geometric patterning cover the surface.
- **Form.** **Silver gilt** with **cloisonné**-style inlay (enamel, glass, or gemstones separated by soldered metal strips). Dense, all-over ornamentation dominates over representational form — characteristic of migration-era art's preference for **animal style** and pattern.

---

## #57 — Pyxis of al-Mughira

**Umayyad (Islamic Iberia)** · c. 968 C.E. · Ivory · Musée du Louvre, Paris · ~6 in tall

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/Pyxis_al-Mughira.jpg/250px-Pyxis_al-Mughira.jpg" width="240" alt="Pyxis of al-Mughira">
### Summary

A small carved **ivory box** made as an eighteenth-birthday gift for the Umayyad prince **al-Mughira** — the finest surviving example of the ivory-carving tradition of Islamic Spain, dense with princely iconography.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in the royal ivory workshops at **Madinat al-Zahra** for **al-Mughira**, son of the caliph Abd al-Rahman III. Combines Islamic themes with an Iberian carved-ivory tradition and motifs borrowed from the broader Mediterranean princely repertoire.
- **Function.** A **pyxis** — a lidded container — probably for perfumes, cosmetics, or jewels. A precious, intimate object marking al-Mughira's coming of age and his place in the Umayyad court.
- **Content.** Four lobed medallions wrap the cylinder with princely scenes: a lion attacking a bull (a standard metaphor of power), hunters on horseback, falconers, and — most famously — **three enthroned figures** (perhaps al-Mughira flanked by musicians) being entertained with a lute and flywhisks. An **Arabic inscription** in Kufic script runs around the lid identifying the owner and the date.
- **Form.** Carved **elephant ivory**, once polychromed. Dense, all-over carving balances figural and vegetal motifs — peacocks, vine scrolls, hunting animals — in a hierarchical, symmetrical arrangement that asserts princely authority.

---

## #64 — Golden Haggadah (The Plagues of Egypt, Scenes of Liberation, and Preparation for Passover)

**Late medieval Spain (Jewish, likely Barcelona workshop)** · c. 1320 C.E. · Illuminated manuscript (pigments and gold leaf on vellum) · British Library, London

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Golden_Haggadah_Plagues.jpg/330px-Golden_Haggadah_Plagues.jpg" width="240" alt="Golden Haggadah (The Plagues of Egypt, Scenes of Liberation, and Preparation for Passover)">
### Summary

A lavishly illustrated **Jewish Haggadah** — the liturgical book read at the **Passover seder** — whose gold-ground miniatures narrate the Exodus story with Gothic courtly elegance.

- **Historical Context.** Produced in **late-medieval Christian Spain** (likely Barcelona), where a significant Jewish population coexisted — often uneasily — with Catholic rulers before the expulsions of 1492. Although Jewish tradition discouraged figurative imagery in synagogue books, private devotional manuscripts like the Haggadah freely featured figural scenes.
- **Function.** A **Haggadah** is read aloud during the **Passover seder**, retelling the Israelites' liberation from Egyptian slavery. The Golden Haggadah was a wealthy family's deluxe copy, meant to be used at the family Passover table.
- **Content.** Fourteen full-page miniatures — four to a page, framed in Gothic architecture — narrate **Genesis and Exodus**: Adam naming the animals, the Ten Plagues, Pharaoh's pursuit, the parting of the Red Sea, and **preparations for Passover** (baking matzah, sacrificing lambs, cleaning houses of leaven).
- **Form.** **Pigment and gold leaf on vellum**. The gold-diapered backgrounds give the book its name. The figures are in the **Gothic** courtly style — elongated, elegant, with expressive gestures — showing the integration of Jewish content into the surrounding Christian artistic idiom.

---

## #66 — Annunciation Triptych (Merode Altarpiece)

**Workshop of Robert Campin (Northern Renaissance)** · 1427–1432 C.E. · Oil on wood · The Cloisters, Metropolitan Museum of Art, New York

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Merode_Altarpiece.jpg/330px-Merode_Altarpiece.jpg" width="240" alt="Annunciation Triptych (Merode Altarpiece)">
### Summary

A small **Northern Renaissance triptych** by the **Workshop of Robert Campin** relocating the **Annunciation** into a meticulously detailed Flemish **bourgeois interior** — an emblem of how private devotion and a rising **merchant class** reshaped religious art.

- **Historical Context.** Early-15th-century Flanders was a booming trade center; a new **merchant middle class** commissioned small devotional works for home use, not church display. The Campin workshop (often identified with the Master of Flémalle) pioneered the **Early Netherlandish oil** technique and its hyper-detailed naturalism.
- **Function.** A private **devotional altarpiece** for a wealthy patron (a donor couple, probably Peter Engelbrecht and Margarete Scrynmaker, kneel at left). Meant for use in a home oratory, not a public church.
- **Content.** **Center panel:** the **Annunciation** — Gabriel appears to Mary as she reads; a tiny **Christ-child bearing a cross** slides down rays of light toward her ear (the **Incarnation**). Symbolic objects saturate the room: extinguished candle (God's presence), lilies (purity), towel (Mary's role as vessel). **Left panel:** the donors kneel at the door; **right panel:** **Joseph** in his carpentry shop, making **mousetraps** (a metaphor: Christ sprung on the devil, per Augustine).
- **Form.** **Oil on wood**, a Northern innovation that permits micro-detail, glazes, and subtle atmosphere. Inconsistent perspective and domestic (anachronistic) setting; hyperreal textures — wood, metal, cloth, light.

---

## #70 — Palazzo Rucellai

**Leon Battista Alberti (architect)** · c. 1450 C.E. · Stone, masonry · Florence, Italy

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Palazzo_Rucellai_Florence.jpg/330px-Palazzo_Rucellai_Florence.jpg" width="240" alt="Palazzo Rucellai">
### Summary

The **Palazzo Rucellai** by **Leon Battista Alberti** — the first Florentine palace façade to import the **stacked classical orders** of the Roman **Colosseum** to private domestic architecture, translating imperial Rome into early-Renaissance bourgeois grandeur.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by the wealthy Florentine merchant **Giovanni Rucellai**, rival-allied to the Medici, as a demonstration of family prestige. Alberti had visited Rome in 1430 and was inspired by ancient Roman buildings, particularly the **Colosseum**; he wrote the Renaissance's foundational architectural treatise, *De re aedificatoria*.
- **Function.** An urban **private palazzo** for the Rucellai family. Like other Florentine palaces, it served as residence, business headquarters, and public face of the family.
- **Content.** A three-story façade organized into three tiers by **horizontal entablatures**. Windows are round-arched with bifora (twin) lights; family emblems (the Rucellai sails) punctuate the frieze; the bottom floor features large **rusticated stone** and square windows to express strength at street level.
- **Form.** **Stone masonry** with finely cut blocks. Each story carries a different superimposed **classical order**: **Doric/Tuscan** below, **Ionic** in the middle, **Corinthian** above — a direct quotation of the Colosseum's order stacking, applied to domestic architecture for the first time.

---

## #73 — Last Supper

**Leonardo da Vinci** · c. 1494–1498 C.E. · Oil and tempera on plaster · Santa Maria delle Grazie, Milan · 15 ft × 29 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg/500px-%C3%9Altima_Cena_-_Da_Vinci_5.jpg" width="240" alt="The Last Supper">

### Summary

Leonardo's **psychologically charged** rendering of the moment Christ announces his betrayal.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by Duke **Ludovico Sforza** of Milan for the refectory (dining hall) of the Dominican convent of **Santa Maria delle Grazie**. Painted during the High Renaissance; Leonardo's experimental oil-and-tempera-on-dry-plaster technique caused the work to deteriorate within decades.
- **Function.** A refectory mural situating the friars' own meals beneath Christ's final meal. It structures the room as an extension of the painted space.
- **Content.** Christ sits at the center of a long table; the twelve apostles react in shock to the words "one of you will betray me," arranged in four groups of three. Judas clutches a money bag and recoils into shadow.
- **Form.** Strict **one-point linear perspective** with the vanishing point at Christ's temple; symmetrical architecture and a pyramidal Christ anchor the composition. Each apostle is individuated through gesture and expression — a study in psychological realism.

**Vocabulary:** linear perspective · fresco secco · High Renaissance
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---

## #80 — Venus of Urbino

**Titian** · c. 1538 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Uffizi, Florence · 119 × 165 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Tiziano_-_Venere_di_Urbino_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/330px-Tiziano_-_Venere_di_Urbino_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="Venus of Urbino">
### Summary

A reclining female nude whose **pointed domestic setting** transforms a classical Venus into a marital allegory.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by **Guidobaldo II della Rovere**, Duke of Urbino, likely on the occasion of his marriage to Giulia Varano. Titian, the leading Venetian painter, drew on Giorgione's *Sleeping Venus* (c. 1510), which he may have helped complete, but brought the goddess indoors and awake.
- **Function.** A private bedroom painting — an *allegory of marriage* celebrating fidelity, fertility, and sensual love. The dog at the feet symbolizes fidelity; the *cassoni* (marriage chests) in the background allude to dowry and domestic virtue.
- **Content.** A nude woman reclines on a rumpled bed in a Venetian palazzo interior; she gazes directly at the viewer, left hand covering her pudenda, right hand holding roses. A maid and a young girl rummage in *cassoni* in the background; a small dog sleeps.
- **Form.** Warm Venetian **colorito** — glowing flesh, rich reds, deep greens. Strong diagonal of the body cutting across the picture plane. Space divided by a vertical curtain into near/far zones. Later referenced by Manet's *Olympia* (#115).

**Vocabulary:** Venetian Renaissance · colorito · reclining nude · cassoni
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 3.5

---

## #81 — Frontispiece of the Codex Mendoza

**Viceroyalty of New Spain** · c. 1541–1542 C.E. · Ink and color on paper · Bodleian Library, Oxford

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/09/Codex_Mendoza_1.jpg/330px-Codex_Mendoza_1.jpg" width="240" alt="Frontispiece of the Codex Mendoza">
### Summary

A **post-Conquest Aztec codex** that grafts indigenous iconography onto a European book format to transmit Mexica history to the Spanish crown.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned around 1541 by Antonio de **Mendoza**, first Viceroy of New Spain, as a gift for Emperor **Charles V**. Native tlacuilos (scribes) used pre-conquest pictographic conventions while Spanish annotators added glosses in alphabetic script. The ship carrying it was seized by French privateers, and it ended up in England via André Thevet.
- **Function.** Ethnographic and political report: it details the founding of **Tenochtitlan**, the reigns of Aztec rulers, tribute lists, and daily life — equal parts history, census, and legitimizing document under Spanish rule.
- **Content.** The frontispiece shows the founding of Tenochtitlan: an **eagle perched on a cactus** at the center of a blue X-shaped canal system dividing the city into four quarters, framed by ten founding chieftains, turquoise fire-drill glyphs for each year of the founding cycle, and conquered cities at the bottom (Colhuacan, Tenayuca).
- **Form.** Bound like a European codex but rendered in Aztec style: flat color fields, glyphic place-names, stylized profile figures. The water-and-land glyph forms an X (*atl-tlachinolli*) at center; outer band shows 51 year glyphs.

**Vocabulary:** codex · tlacuilo · glyph · Tenochtitlan · viceroyalty
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.3, 3.5

---

## #86 — Henri IV Receives the Portrait of Marie de' Medici, from the Marie de' Medici Cycle

**Peter Paul Rubens** · 1621–1625 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Louvre, Paris · 394 × 295 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Rubens_Marie_Medici.jpg/330px-Rubens_Marie_Medici.jpg" width="240" alt="Henri IV Receives the Portrait of Marie de' Medici, from the Marie de' Medici Cycle">
### Summary

Rubens' **Flemish Baroque** allegory transforms a royal marriage negotiation into a Mount-Olympus pageant.

- **Historical Context.** Commissioned by the Queen Mother **Marie de' Medici** for her gallery at the Palais du Luxembourg, Paris, after she was exiled by her son Louis XIII and then reconciled. One of 24 monumental canvases narrating — and glorifying — her life. Rubens was the leading Flemish painter, trained on Titian and the Venetians; he ran a large Antwerp workshop and served as a diplomat.
- **Function.** **Dynastic propaganda.** The cycle legitimizes Marie's regency and recasts her messy political history as divinely ordained destiny.
- **Content.** Winged **Cupids of Marriage and Love** present a portrait of Marie to **Henry IV** of France, who gazes at it, smitten. **Jupiter** and **Juno**, gods of marriage, approve from clouds above; a personified **France** (helmeted) leans into Henri to counsel political union. Marie and a cherub make eye contact with the viewer.
- **Form.** Typical Rubens Baroque: writhing bodies in dramatic diagonals, warm Venetian palette with shimmering flesh, swirling drapery, and lavish sensuousness. Real event mixed freely with mythological allegory.

**Vocabulary:** Baroque · allegory · dynastic propaganda
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.2

---

## #93 — The Palace at Versailles

**Louis Le Vau and Jules Hardouin-Mansart (architects)** · Begun 1669 C.E. · Masonry, stone, wood, iron, gold leaf; marble and bronze sculpture; gardens · Versailles, France

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Versailles_Chateau_Cour_Royale.jpg/330px-Versailles_Chateau_Cour_Royale.jpg" width="240" alt="The Palace at Versailles">
### Summary

**Louis XIV's absolutist stage** — the definitive French Baroque royal residence, and Europe's template for palace architecture for the next century.

- **Historical Context.** **Louis XIV** — the "Sun King" — transformed his father's modest hunting lodge into the seat of his absolute monarchy, relocating his court in 1682 to remove nobles from Parisian power and place them under his watchful eye. **Le Vau**, **Hardouin-Mansart**, painter **Charles Le Brun**, and landscape architect **André Le Nôtre** led the teams.
- **Function.** Royal residence, seat of government, courtly theater, and propaganda. A machine for the cult of the Sun King, with daily rituals (*lever*, *coucher*) playing out for rotating audiences of nobles.
- **Content.** The palace's **Hall of Mirrors** (Galerie des Glaces) — 17 mirror-clad arches facing 17 windows onto the gardens — narrates Louis's reign in Le Brun's ceiling paintings. **Le Nôtre's gardens** stretch the axis to the horizon with parterres, fountains, and allées identifying the king with Apollo.
- **Form.** Long classical façade punctuated with pilasters, balustrades, and sculpture; sprawling axial plan (axis mundi) radiating from the king's bedchamber. Interior mixes classical order (columns, arches) with flamboyant gilded ornament, allegorical painting, and crystal chandeliers.

**Vocabulary:** Baroque · absolutism · axial plan · parterre
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 1.4, 3.2

---

## #105 — Self-Portrait

**Elisabeth Louise Vigée Le Brun** · 1790 C.E. · Oil on canvas · Uffizi, Florence · 100 × 81 cm

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8f/Goya_Self-portrait.jpg/250px-Goya_Self-portrait.jpg" width="240" alt="Self-Portrait">
### Summary

A self-confident **self-portrait** by the most successful female painter of 18th-century France — a testimony to her professional status.

- **Historical Context.** Vigée Le Brun was **court painter to Marie Antoinette** and one of very few women admitted to the **French Royal Academy** (1783). She fled Paris at the outbreak of the **French Revolution** (1789) and lived in exile across Europe; this painting was commissioned by the **Uffizi Gallery** in Florence for its famous collection of artists' self-portraits, a high honor.
- **Function.** Self-advertisement asserting her skill on equal terms with male peers, and cementing her international reputation while in exile.
- **Content.** Vigée Le Brun turns from her easel toward the viewer, palette and brushes in hand, in the act of painting a portrait of Marie Antoinette on her canvas. She wears a white muslin dress and a turban-like scarf — fashionable, intellectual, and faintly exotic.
- **Form.** Soft **Rococo-to-Neoclassical transitional** style: warm creamy flesh tones, loose brushwork in the dress, crisp detail in the tools of her trade. The gaze is direct, unforced, confident — claiming her place as painter-subject.

**Vocabulary:** self-portrait · French Royal Academy · Rococo
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 1.3, 3.5

---

## #118 — The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel (El Valle de México desde el Cerro de Santa Isabel)

**José María Velasco** · 1882 C.E. · Oil on canvas

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/e2/Jos%C3%A9_Mar%C3%ADa_Velasco_-_The_Valley_of_Mexico_from_the_Santa_Isabel_Mountain_Range_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" width="240" alt="The Valley of Mexico from the Hillside of Santa Isabel">

### Summary

A sweeping **Neoclassical Mexican landscape** painted by Velasco in his characteristic naturalist-scientific manner.

- **Historical Context.** Painted in Mexico. Velasco was the preeminent Mexican landscape painter of the later 19th century; the student notes place the work in a tradition of continuity rather than rupture.
- **Function.** A landscape celebrating place — it focuses on environmental elements rather than city life.
- **Content.** A scene of a woman with her children and their dog walking along the valley; abundant vegetation is shown across a vast landscape depicting the entire valley.
- **Form.** Oil on canvas; carefully observed natural detail paired with broad panoramic composition.

---

## #151 — Spiral Jetty

**Robert Smithson** (Environmental / Land Art) · 1970 C.E. · Earthwork: mud, precipitated salt crystals, rocks, and water coil · Rozel Point, Great Salt Lake, Utah

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Spiral-jetty-from-rozel-point.png/330px-Spiral-jetty-from-rozel-point.png" width="240" alt="Spiral Jetty">

### Summary

The defining **Land Art / earthwork** of the American 1970s.

- **Historical Context.** Built at Rozel Point on the **Great Salt Lake** by Smithson with a construction crew — the many hands involved deliberately refused the idea that art is a unique product of a single artist. Part of the broader 1960s–70s move to pull art out of galleries.
- **Function.** To relocate the work of art from the museum into the landscape. Meaning comes from the work's **location** and from its changing state over time rather than from any fixed content.
- **Content.** A 1,500-foot counterclockwise spiral coil extending into the pink salt waters of the Great Salt Lake, inspired partly by the microbial red-pink water color and the mythic sense of the site.
- **Form.** Basalt rocks, mud, and salt crystals. Originally black basalt, now encrusted white with precipitated salt against the pink water. Submerged and re-exposed as lake levels change.

**Vocabulary:** earthwork · Land Art · environmental art
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 3.1

---

## #152 — House in New Castle County

**Robert Venturi, John Rauch, and Denise Scott Brown** (Postmodernism) · 1978–1983 C.E. · Wood frame and stucco · New Castle County, Delaware

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/House_New_Castle_County_Delaware.jpg/330px-House_New_Castle_County_Delaware.jpg" width="240" alt="House in New Castle County">
### Summary

A **Postmodernist** house that rejects the severe International Style for reference and wit.

- **Historical Context.** Designed in northern Delaware by **Venturi, Rauch and Scott Brown**, the architects whose book *Learning from Las Vegas* launched American **Postmodern** architecture. A direct repudiation of the stripped-down modernism of Mies, Corbusier, and their heirs.
- **Function.** A private house for a family of three — a musician wife and a birdwatcher husband — with rooms shaped around their specific interests.
- **Content.** Exterior deliberately awkward and whimsical; painted **white to reference American barn vernacular**; centered on a large fireplace inside, with rooms in unexpected and irregular scales.
- **Form.** Wood frame and stucco; traditional American sources filtered through a Postmodern lens — complex, contradictory, ornamented, full of historical references, with surprising interior volumes.

**Vocabulary:** Postmodernism · vernacular · contradiction
**Learning Objectives:** LO 2.2, 2.3

---

## #156 — Great Serpent Mound

**Mississippian / Fort Ancient culture (Eastern Woodlands)** · c. 1070 C.E. · Earthwork / effigy mound · Adams County, southern Ohio · ~1,300 ft long, 800+ ft of winding body

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/The_Great_Serpent_Mound.jpg/330px-The_Great_Serpent_Mound.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Serpent Mound">

### Summary

The largest surviving **effigy mound** in North America — an 800-foot serpent built into the Ohio landscape.

- **Historical Context.** Two leading attributions: the earlier **Adena culture** (c. 800 B.C.E.) and the later **Fort Ancient culture** (c. 1070 C.E.), with radiocarbon dating favoring the latter. The head of the serpent is aligned with the **summer solstice sunset**, and the curves of its body appear to align with lunar events, implying an astronomical function.
- **Function.** Likely ceremonial, astronomical, or calendrical rather than funerary — no burials are inside the serpent itself, though nearby conical mounds contain burials.
- **Content.** A winding serpent effigy with a coiled tail at one end and an open mouth at the other, appearing to swallow (or release) an oval form.
- **Form.** Yellow-brown clay and ash built up over a rock core and finished with soil, now grassed over. Visible in plan from above; the coiled body is rhythmic and flowing rather than geometric.

**Vocabulary:** effigy mound · earthwork · solstice alignment
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.4, 3.1

---

## #160 — Maize cobs

**Inka** · c. 1440–1533 C.E. · Sheet metal / repoussé, metal alloys (gold, silver, copper)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Corn_cobs.jpg/250px-Corn_cobs.jpg" width="240" alt="Maize cobs">
### Summary

Life-size **gold-and-silver maize cobs** from the gardens of the Inka **Qorikancha**.

- **Historical Context.** Produced during the Inka empire's height, when tribute from across the Andes flowed into **Cusco**. Part of the legendary garden attached to the Qorikancha temple — the Spanish described an entire garden of gold and silver llamas, women, men, and plants that performed imperial power and wealth. Most of it was melted down after conquest.
- **Function.** A political and religious display: evidence of the empire's reach and of the Inka ruler's divine descent from the sun god **Inti**. Corn was the sacred, life-sustaining staple of Andean civilization.
- **Content.** Ears of **maize** rendered at natural size, each kernel individually formed. Unusually naturalistic — Inka art generally favored stylized or geometric form, so the realism here is a stylistic choice.
- **Form.** Beaten **sheet metal / repoussé**, combining silver and copper alloys to mimic the colors and textures of actual corn. Surviving examples often show husks peeled back to reveal kernels.

**Vocabulary:** repoussé · sheet metal · Qorikancha · Inti
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 2.2

---

## #168 — Great Mosque of Djenné

**Mali** · Founded c. 1200 C.E.; rebuilt 1906–1907 · Adobe · Djenné, Mali

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg/250px-Djenne_great_mud_mosque.jpg" width="240" alt="Great Mosque of Djenné">

### Summary

The largest **adobe (mud-brick)** building in the world and the centerpiece of West African **Sudano-Sahelian** architecture.

- **Historical Context.** Djenné was a major city of the Mali Empire and a stop on the trans-Saharan trade routes. The original mosque was founded around 1200 C.E.; it was rebuilt in its present form in 1906–1907 under French colonial rule using traditional techniques. Only Muslims may enter the mosque; non-Muslims are excluded.
- **Function.** The central mosque of Djenné and historically a center of Islamic learning where students from across West Africa studied the **Quran** during the Middle Ages. Also a community gathering place.
- **Content.** Three tall minaret-towers on the qibla (east) wall; the central tower houses the **mihrab** — the niche marking the direction of Mecca. **Ostrich eggs** top the pillars as symbols of fertility and purity. Annual community plastering festivals renew the mud surface.
- **Form.** **Adobe** (sun-dried mud brick) coated with mud plaster. Protruding wooden beams called **torons** serve both as decorative rhythmic elements and as permanent scaffolding for annual replastering. Massive walls taper and are buttressed against heat and seasonal rains.

**Vocabulary:** adobe · mihrab · toron · Sudano-Sahelian
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.1, 1.3, 1.4

---

## #223 — Presentation of Fijian Mats and Tapa Cloths to Queen Elizabeth II

**Fijian artists; photograph/video documentation** · 1953 C.E. (original event); ongoing documentation · Multimedia performance with photographic and filmic documentation · Fiji and archives · Variable

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/35/Fijian_Mats_Tapa_Queen_Elizabeth.jpg/330px-Fijian_Mats_Tapa_Queen_Elizabeth.jpg" width="240" alt="Presentation of Fijian Mats and Tapa Cloths to Queen Elizabeth II">
### Summary

A documentary record of the 1953 Fijian ceremonial **presentation of mats (*masi*) and barkcloth tapa** to Queen Elizabeth II during her post-coronation tour — an enactment of Pacific gift-exchange diplomacy.

- **Historical Context.** Queen Elizabeth II toured Fiji in December 1953 as part of her six-month Coronation Commonwealth tour. Fijian chiefs and communities received her with a formal presentation of finely woven pandanus **mats** and barkcloth **masi/tapa** — traditional ceremonial wealth. Fiji was then still a British Crown Colony (it gained independence in 1970). The event was filmed and photographed; the textiles themselves entered the Royal Collection.
- **Function.** The presentation enacts Pacific protocols of **tribute and exchange**: offering mats and tapa binds giver and recipient in a reciprocal relationship. Within colonial politics it simultaneously performed loyalty and asserted the sovereign dignity of Fijian chiefs.
- **Content.** Fijian men and women in ceremonial dress ritually present stacks of woven mats and long rolls of barkcloth to the seated Queen. The textiles are the real "content" — carefully produced women's art patterned with geometric designs specific to Fijian clans and regions.
- **Form.** Documentary still photography and newsreel footage freeze an ephemeral ceremony. The textiles themselves are soft, flexible, sometimes monumental in scale.

**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.2, 3.3

---

### Connections to other works in the image set

#### → [#219](#219--hiapo-tapa) — Hiapo (Tapa) (Polynesia, Niue, 1850–1900 C.E.)

**Theme:** Barkcloth, Polynesian textile tradition

> Both works are products of the pan-Polynesian tapa tradition — women's communal production of beaten mulberry bark as diplomatic and ceremonial wealth.

---

#### → [#215](#215--'ahu-'ula-feather-cape) — 'Ahu 'ula (Feather cape) (Hawaii, before 1850 C.E.)

**Theme:** Gift to European royalty, Pacific prestige textile

> 'Ahu 'ula capes were famously presented by Hawaiian chiefs to Captain Cook and other European visitors as diplomatic gifts — the same logic at work in the 1953 Fijian presentation.

---

## #234 — Earth's Creation I

**Emily Kame Kngwarreye (Anmatyerre, Australia)** · 1994 C.E. · Synthetic polymer paint (acrylic) on canvas · Private collection · 275 × 632 cm (set of four panels)

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Earth_Creation_contemporary_art.jpg/330px-Earth_Creation_contemporary_art.jpg" width="240" alt="Earth's Creation I">
### Summary

A monumental Aboriginal painting of the **Dreaming** rendered as lush abstract dabs — at the time of its 2007 sale, the most expensive painting by a female Australian artist.

- **Historical Context.** **Emily Kame Kngwarreye** (c. 1910–1996) was a senior Anmatyerre woman of the **Utopia community** in Australia's Northern Territory. She began painting on canvas only at about age 80, after decades of practicing **awelye** (women's ceremonial body painting) and batik. In less than a decade she produced roughly 3,000 paintings and became internationally recognized. *Earth's Creation* relates to the rainy season and the emergence of life from the desert after storms — a vivid moment of the **Dreaming** (Tjukurrpa), the ancestral creative force in Aboriginal cosmology.
- **Function.** A **ceremonial and ancestral painting** rooted in *awelye*. It embodies the artist's custodial knowledge of her Country — specifically the yam (*anooralya*) and pencil-yam (*kame*) Dreaming for which she was named.
- **Content.** Non-figurative: dense, overlapping layered dabs of bright color — greens, yellows, pinks, reds — evoke the desert landscape springing to life after rain. The title refers to the primordial moment of the world's making.
- **Form.** Four large panels totaling more than 20 feet wide. Thick, rapid layering of paint applied with a stippling, dabbing technique in a dense, all-over composition. No horizon, no figure — an aerial/spiritual view of country.

**Vocabulary:** Aboriginal Dreaming (Tjukurrpa) · awelye · Utopia community
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.3

---

## #239 — The Crossing

**Bill Viola (USA)** · 1996 C.E. · Video/sound installation (two channels of color video projection onto screens mounted back-to-back, amplified stereo sound) · Collection of the Bill Viola Studio and museums worldwide · Variable; projections roughly 16 ft × 23 ft

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/The_Crossing_contemporary_art.jpg/330px-The_Crossing_contemporary_art.jpg" width="240" alt="The Crossing">
### Summary

A monumental video diptych in which a man is progressively consumed by **fire on one screen and engulfed by water on the other** — a contemporary elemental meditation on death, purification, and rebirth.

- **Historical Context.** **Bill Viola** (1951–2024) is a pioneering video artist whose work fuses new-media technology with deeply traditional subjects drawn from Christian mysticism, Sufi poetry, and Zen Buddhism. *The Crossing* premiered in 1996 during the maturation of single-channel video installation as a museum medium. Viola's slow time, devotional subjects, and theatrical scale made him the Old Master of video art.
- **Function.** A contemplative **sacramental object**. Visitors walk around the back-to-back screens and experience both destructions simultaneously; the accompanying thunderous soundtrack amplifies the body's response. Viola treats video as a vehicle for spiritual encounter.
- **Content.** A man slowly approaches the camera against a black ground. On one screen, a flame ignites at his feet, grows, and eventually engulfs him; on the other, a drip of water becomes a torrent that drowns him. Both reduce him to nothing; both last about ten minutes before looping.
- **Form.** Two high-resolution color video projections mounted back to back; each projected onto a large screen; amplified stereo sound. Extreme slow motion stretches moments of transformation.

**Vocabulary:** video art · installation · elements
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.2, 3.2

---

## #242 — Lying with the Wolf

**Kiki Smith (USA)** · 2001 C.E. · Ink and pencil on paper · Private collection · 72 × 88 in

<img src="https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Lying_with_the_Wolf.jpg/330px-Lying_with_the_Wolf.jpg" width="240" alt="Lying with the Wolf">
### Summary

**Kiki Smith**'s large drawing of a nude woman reclining peacefully with a wolf — a revision of the predator-prey dynamic of Red Riding Hood and a feminist reimagining of St. Genevieve.

- **Historical Context.** **Kiki Smith** (b. 1954, Nuremberg; raised in New Jersey), trained as an EMT before becoming an artist. Her works are centered on the body, folklore, and feminism. *Lying with the Wolf* is part of a series reworking fairy tales (especially Little Red Riding Hood) and female saints (St. Genevieve, who was said to have tamed wolves). Drawn in the wake of decades of feminist art theory challenging male depictions of the female nude.
- **Function.** Reverses the expected narrative of female victimhood. In place of the wolf devouring the girl, Smith's woman embraces the wolf calmly — a meditation on **reconciliation between humanity and the wild, between woman and the "beast."**
- **Content.** A life-sized, naked woman reclines on the floor holding a large wolf in a near-embrace. Both are rendered in warm, sensitive contour lines. Calm, meditative, intimate — neither menacing nor erotic.
- **Form.** Large-format drawing in ink and pencil on multiple joined sheets of Nepalese paper. Minimal ground; figures outlined with graceful line and soft tonal shading. Scale approaches that of a history painting.

**Vocabulary:** feminist art · folklore revision · St. Genevieve
**Learning Objectives:** LO 1.3, 2.1, 3.2

---
